Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
The Eternal Cannot Originate from a Free Agent
Argument that intention entails non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for why an eternal effect cannot be brought about by a free agent, and why this is a foundational principle in kalam.

Core Idea
Intention requires non-existence — so a free agent’s effect must be originated.

This lesson presents the core argument: an eternal existent cannot be an act of a free agent. The author establishes this by analyzing the concept of intention (qasd) and its necessary link to non-existence. The argument is simple but decisive in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and its justification
Read the opening statement: 'القديم بالزمان يمتنع استناده إلى المختار' and the reason given: 'لأن القصد إلى الإيجاد مقارن للعدم ضرورة'.
Notice the term 'القديم بالزمان' means eternal in time (beginningless).
2
Understand the concept of قصد (intention)
The author explains that intention is directed to obtaining what is not already present. This implies the intended effect must be preceded by non-existence.
3
Recognize agreement among theologians
The author states this view is agreed upon by theologians, and opposition is mere obstinacy. This reinforces the certainty of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم بالزمان يمتنع استناده إلى المختار لأن القصد إلى الإيجاد مقارن للعدم ضرورة، والمنازع مكابر. يعني أن أثر المؤثر المختار لا يكون إلا حادثاً مسبوقاً بالعدم، لأن القصد إنما يتوجه إلى تحصيل ما ليس بحاصل، وهذا متفق بين المتكلمين والنزاع فيه مكابرة، وما نقل في المواقف عن الآمدي أنه قال: سبق الإيجاد قصدا كسبق الإيجاد إيجابا في جواز كونهما بالذات دون الزمان، وفي جواز كون أثرهما قديماً فلا يوجد في كتاب الأبكار إلا ما قال على سبيل الاعتراض، من يمتنع أن يكون وجود العالم أزلياً، مستنداً إلى الواجب تعالى، ويكونان معاً في الوجود، لا تقدم إلا بالذات، كما في حركة اليد والخاتم، وهو لا يشعر بابتنائه على كون الواجب مختاراً، لا موجباً ولذا مثل بحركة اليد والخاتم واقتصر في الجواب على دفع السند قائلاً: لا نسلم استناد حركة الخاتم إلى حركة اليد، بل هما معلولان لأمر خارج. نعم. صرح في شرح الإشارات بأن الفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إلى أن القديم يمتنع أن يكون فعلاً لفاعل مختار، ولا إلى أن المبدأ الأول ليس بقادر مختاراً، بل إلى أن قدرته واختياره لا يوجبان كثرة في ذاته، وأن فاعليته ليست كفاعلية المختارين من الحيوان ولا كفاعلية المحبورين من ذوي الطبائع الجسمانية، وإلى أنه أزلي تام في الفاعلية وأن العالم أزلي مستند إليه، وأنت خبير بأن هذا احتراز عن شناعة نفي القدرة، والاختيار عن الصانع.
The eternal in time is impossible to be attributed to the free agent, because the intention to bring into existence is concomitant with non-existence necessarily, and the disputant is obstinate. It means that the effect of the free agent cannot be but originated, preceded by non-existence, because the intention is only directed to obtaining what is not attained, and this is agreed upon among the theologians and the dispute in it is obstinacy. And what is transmitted in al-Mawaqif from al-Amidi that he said: The precedence of bringing into existence by intention is like the precedence of bringing into existence by necessitation in the permissibility of both being by essence but not by time, and in the permissibility of their effect being eternal, so it is not found in the book al-Abkar except what he said by way of objection: one for whom it is impossible that the existence of the world be eternal, dependent on the Necessary Exalted, and both are together in existence, no precedence except by essence, like the movement of the hand and the ring, and he is not aware that it is built upon the Necessary being free (mukhtar), not necessitating (mujib). Hence he gave the example of the movement of the hand and the ring, and restricted the answer to repudiating the basis, saying: we do not accept the dependence of the movement of the ring on the movement of the hand, rather both are effects of an external thing. Yes. It is stated in Sharh al-Isharat that the philosophers did not hold that the eternal is impossible to be an act of a free agent, nor that the First Principle is not powerful and free, but that His power and free choice do not necessitate multiplicity in His essence, and that His agency is not like the agency of free animals nor like the agency of the compelled of physical natures, and that He is eternal perfect in agency and that the world is eternal dependent on Him. And you are aware that this is a precaution against the ugliness of negating power and free choice from the Maker.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by stating the thesis: the eternal in time cannot be attributed to a free agent. The reason given is that the intention (qasd) to bring into existence is necessarily associated with non-existence—one only intends to bring about what does not yet exist. The author notes that the opposing view is obstinate. He then unpacks the meaning: the effect of a free agent must be originated (hadith) and preceded by non-existence ('adam), because intention is directed only to obtaining what is not attained. This is agreed upon by theologians. The author then addresses a quote from al-Amidi transmitted in al-Mawaqif, where al-Amidi suggests that the precedence of bringing into existence by intention (qasdan) is like precedence by necessitation (ijaban) in that both can be by essence without time, implying their effect could be eternal. The author explains that al-Amidi's words are only by way of objection, and that al-Amidi's example of the hand and ring (where the ring moves simultaneously with the hand) is based on the assumption that the Necessary is free (mukhtar), not necessitating (mujib). The reply to this objection is that the dependence of the ring's movement on the hand is not conceded; rather both are effects of something external. The author then cites Sharh al-Isharat to clarify the philosophers' actual position: they do not deny that the eternal can be an act of a free agent, nor that the First Principle is powerful and free. Rather, they hold that His power and choice do not imply multiplicity in His essence, and that His agency is unlike that of animals or compelled beings. He is eternal and perfect in agency, and the world is eternal and dependent on Him. The author concludes that this is a precaution to avoid the scandal of denying power and choice to the Maker. Thus the passage defends the Ash'ari position that an eternal effect from a free agent is impossible, and refutes the attempt to reconcile the eternity of the world with divine freedom.

Key Terms
القديم بالزمان
المختار
القصد إلى الإيجاد
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a free agent can create something eternal because intention can precede the effect in essence without time.
Correction: Intention is directed to bringing about what is not yet attained; if the effect is eternal, non-existence never preceded it, so intention would have no object.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say an eternal effect cannot come from a free agent?
A) Because the free agent would then be limited by time.
B) Because intention is directed only to obtaining what does not yet exist.
C) Because the philosophers have proven it impossible.
D) Because God's power is only over originated things.

Key Takeaway
A free agent's deliberate act requires that its object does not yet exist, so the effect must be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do something, do you ever pause to realize that your intention itself proves that thing does not yet exist? How does this simple truth shape your trust that God’s creation of the universe was a deliberate, meaningful act rather than an automatic process?
?
If someone argued that God’s will is eternal, so He could have eternally willed a temporal universe, how would you respond based on the necessity of non-existence for intention? Have you ever struggled to accept that God creates freely without being forced by any prior cause?
?
The author calls opposition to this argument ‘mere obstinacy.’ Is there any area of your faith where you cling to an idea out of stubbornness rather than sound reasoning? Will you honestly examine that today?

intermediate

Refuting Objections and Philosophers' Position
Rebuttal of al-Amidi and clarification of philosophers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes al-Amidi's objection that intention can precede essence-wise, and how he clarifies the philosophers' actual view on divine agency and the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Precedence by intention is not like precedence by necessitation; the hand-and-ring analogy fails.

After establishing the principle, the author tackles an objection from al-Amidi that uses an analogy of simultaneous movement. He then addresses the philosophers' position to prevent misunderstanding: they affirm divine free agency but deny it requires temporal creation. The lesson shows the weakness of the objection and the subtlety of the philosophical claim.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's objection
Al-Amidi suggests that precedence by intention (qasdan) could be like precedence by necessitation (ijaban) – both can be by essence without time, so the effect could be eternal.
He uses the example of a hand and a ring moving together.
2
Understand the response
The author notes that al-Amidi's example assumes the agent is free, not necessitating. The reply is that the ring's movement does not depend on the hand; both are caused by an external factor.
3
Examine the philosophers' claim
From Sharh al-Isharat: Philosophers do not deny that an eternal effect can be from a free agent, nor that God is powerful and free. They hold that His agency is unique and does not imply multiplicity, and the world is eternal and dependent on Him.
4
Note the author's final warning
The author concludes that this philosophical position is a precaution to avoid denying God's power and choice, but it does not reconcile with the earlier proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم بالزمان يمتنع استناده إلى المختار لأن القصد إلى الإيجاد مقارن للعدم ضرورة، والمنازع مكابر. يعني أن أثر المؤثر المختار لا يكون إلا حادثاً مسبوقاً بالعدم، لأن القصد إنما يتوجه إلى تحصيل ما ليس بحاصل، وهذا متفق بين المتكلمين والنزاع فيه مكابرة، وما نقل في المواقف عن الآمدي أنه قال: سبق الإيجاد قصدا كسبق الإيجاد إيجابا في جواز كونهما بالذات دون الزمان، وفي جواز كون أثرهما قديماً فلا يوجد في كتاب الأبكار إلا ما قال على سبيل الاعتراض، من يمتنع أن يكون وجود العالم أزلياً، مستنداً إلى الواجب تعالى، ويكونان معاً في الوجود، لا تقدم إلا بالذات، كما في حركة اليد والخاتم، وهو لا يشعر بابتنائه على كون الواجب مختاراً، لا موجباً ولذا مثل بحركة اليد والخاتم واقتصر في الجواب على دفع السند قائلاً: لا نسلم استناد حركة الخاتم إلى حركة اليد، بل هما معلولان لأمر خارج. نعم. صرح في شرح الإشارات بأن الفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إلى أن القديم يمتنع أن يكون فعلاً لفاعل مختار، ولا إلى أن المبدأ الأول ليس بقادر مختاراً، بل إلى أن قدرته واختياره لا يوجبان كثرة في ذاته، وأن فاعليته ليست كفاعلية المختارين من الحيوان ولا كفاعلية المحبورين من ذوي الطبائع الجسمانية، وإلى أنه أزلي تام في الفاعلية وأن العالم أزلي مستند إليه، وأنت خبير بأن هذا احتراز عن شناعة نفي القدرة، والاختيار عن الصانع.
The eternal in time is impossible to be attributed to the free agent, because the intention to bring into existence is concomitant with non-existence necessarily, and the disputant is obstinate. It means that the effect of the free agent cannot be but originated, preceded by non-existence, because the intention is only directed to obtaining what is not attained, and this is agreed upon among the theologians and the dispute in it is obstinacy. And what is transmitted in al-Mawaqif from al-Amidi that he said: The precedence of bringing into existence by intention is like the precedence of bringing into existence by necessitation in the permissibility of both being by essence but not by time, and in the permissibility of their effect being eternal, so it is not found in the book al-Abkar except what he said by way of objection: one for whom it is impossible that the existence of the world be eternal, dependent on the Necessary Exalted, and both are together in existence, no precedence except by essence, like the movement of the hand and the ring, and he is not aware that it is built upon the Necessary being free (mukhtar), not necessitating (mujib). Hence he gave the example of the movement of the hand and the ring, and restricted the answer to repudiating the basis, saying: we do not accept the dependence of the movement of the ring on the movement of the hand, rather both are effects of an external thing. Yes. And you are aware that this is a precaution against the ugliness of negating power and free choice from the Maker.

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by stating the thesis: the eternal in time cannot be attributed to a free agent. The reason given is that the intention (qasd) to bring into existence is necessarily associated with non-existence—one only intends to bring about what does not yet exist. The author notes that the opposing view is obstinate. He then unpacks the meaning: the effect of a free agent must be originated (hadith) and preceded by non-existence ('adam), because intention is directed only to obtaining what is not attained. This is agreed upon by theologians. The author then addresses a quote from al-Amidi transmitted in al-Mawaqif, where al-Amidi suggests that the precedence of bringing into existence by intention (qasdan) is like precedence by necessitation (ijaban) in that both can be by essence without time, implying their effect could be eternal. Thus the passage defends the Ash'ari position that an eternal effect from a free agent is impossible, and refutes the attempt to reconcile the eternity of the world with divine freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers deny free choice, al-Amidi's objection is valid, and the hand-ring analogy proves eternal effects from free agents.
Correction: Philosophers affirm free agency; al-Amidi's analogy fails because the ring's movement does not depend on the hand; both are effects of an external cause.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute al-Amidi's hand-and-ring analogy?
A) He says the ring's movement is caused by the hand.
B) He says both movements are effects of an external cause.
C) He says the hand's movement is not a free act.
D) He says the example only works for necessitating causes.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers affirm God's free agency but deny that it requires temporal creation; however, the author shows this is a specious defense and the earlier proof stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will is free and not constrained by any necessity—even the necessity of His own nature? How does your daily reliance on God either reflect that belief or betray a subtle assumption that He is bound by natural laws?
?
The philosophers in this passage verbally affirmed God's power and choice while still holding the world eternal. Are you ever satisfied with a mere verbal confession of God's attributes while your actions imply something else? In what area of your life do you functionally treat God as bound by causality rather than as the absolutely free Creator?
?
This lesson shows how easily we can protect our assumptions with 'precautions' that sound orthodox but avoid the real confrontation with truth. Where in your own faith are you using theological jargon to avoid facing a hard implication about God's sovereignty or your own accountability?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Why an Eternal Effect Cannot Originate from a Necessitating Agent
Proves that an eternal effect cannot arise from a necessitating agent and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments against the philosophical claim that an eternal effect can proceed from a necessitating agent, including the problem of preponderance without a preponderator and the refutation of 'bringing the existent into existence.'

Core Idea
An eternal agent necessitates eternal effects; non-eternal effects would require a preponderator.

This passage tackles a core dispute between Islamic theologians and philosophers: can an eternal, necessary being (Allah) produce an eternal effect by His essence? The philosophers held that the First is a necessitating agent (mujib bi al-dhat), and thus the universe is eternal. The theologians argue that this leads to absurdities. Here, the author presents the problem and responds to a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Introduce the philosophers' claim
Read the opening lines: the philosophers assert that the First is a necessitating agent, not a free agent. Note what this implies for the origin of the universe.
The philosophers' view is more famous than disputable.
2
Understand the first argument (preponderator)
The text argues: if a necessitating agent were possible, its effect must be eternal. If not, then existence after non-existence is a preponderance without cause. Use the commentary to grasp why this is impossible.
Tarjih bila murajjih is a fundamental principle.
3
Examine al-Razi's objection (creating the existent)
The author presents an argument: the agent's influence cannot be during the effect's persistence, or else that would be 'creating the already existent.' Understand why this is problematic.
This is a key objection to the Ash'ari position.
4
See the Ash'ari response
The answer clarifies that 'bringing into existence' during persistence means dependence on the agent, not acquiring a new existence. This resolves the objection.
The response saves the possibility of an agent sustaining the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا فكونه عندهم موجباً بالذات لا فاعلاً بالاختيار أشهر من أن يقال: دون الموجب لو أمكن إذ في التخلف عن تمام العلة ترجح بلا مرجح، وما يقال إن تأثير حال البقاء إيجاد للموجود مدفوع لما سبق. أي لو أمكن مؤثر قديم موجب بالذات على ما يدعيه الفلاسفة لم يمتنع استناد الأثر القديم إليه، بل وجب أن يكون معلوله الأول، وسائر ما يصدر عنه بالذات، أو بالوسائط القديمة قديماً، وإلا لكان وجوده بعد ذلك ترجحاً بلا مرجح، حيث لم يوجد في الأزل، ووجد فيما لا يزال مع استواء الحالين نظراً إلى تمام العلة واستدل الإمام على امتناع استناد القديم إلى الموجب أيضاً، بأن تأثيره في شيء يمتنع أن يكون حال بقائه وإلا لزم إيجاد الموجود، فتعين أن يكون حال حدوثه أو عدمه، فيكون حادثاً لا قديماً. وجوابه: ما سبق أن الممتنع إيجاد الموجود بوجود حاصل بغير هذا الإيجاد هو غير لازم، وأن معنى تأثير المؤثر في الشيء، وإيجاده إياه حال بقائه هو أن وجوده يفتقر إلى وجود المؤثر، ويدوم بدوامه، من غير أن يكون هناك تحصيل ما لم يكن حاصلاً ليلزم حدوثه.
Otherwise, their view that He is a necessitating agent (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar), is more famous than that it is said: Without the necessitating agent if possible, for in the non-occurrence of the effect from a complete cause there is preponderance without a preponderator. And what is said that the effect during the state of persistence is bringing into existence the existent is refuted by what preceded. That is: if an eternal necessitating agent were possible as the philosophers claim, the attribution of an eternal effect to Him would not be impossible; rather it would be necessary that His first effect, and all that proceeds from Him by essence or through eternal intermediaries, be eternal. Otherwise, its existence after that would be a preponderance without a preponderator, since it did not exist in pre-eternity and existed in what never ceases while the two states are equal with regard to the complete cause. And the Imam argued for the impossibility of attributing the eternal to the necessitating agent also: that its influence on a thing cannot be during its persistence, for that would entail bringing into existence the existent; so it must be during its origination or non-existence, so it is originated, not eternal. And its answer: that what preceded is that the impossibility of bringing into existence the existent with an existence obtained by other than this bringing into existence is not necessary; and that the meaning of the influence of the agent on the thing and its bringing it into existence during its persistence is that its existence depends on the existence of the agent and endures by its endurance, without there being any obtaining of what was not attained so as to necessitate its origination.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of the possibility that an eternal effect could originate from a necessitating agent. The author begins by noting that the philosophers' position that the First is a necessitating agent (mujib bi al-dhat) rather than a free agent is more famous. He then states that if a necessitating eternal agent were possible, then an eternal effect would not be impossible; in fact, it would be necessary that its first effect and all subsequent effects (by essence or through eternal intermediaries) be eternal. Otherwise, if the effect came into existence after not existing in pre-eternity, that would be a preponderance without a preponderator (tarjih bila murajjih), which is impossible. He then presents an argument from the Imam (likely al-Razi) to show the impossibility of an eternal effect from a necessitating agent: the agent's influence on a thing cannot be during the thing's continued existence (because that would be creating the already existent), so it must be at the moment of origination or nonexistence, making the effect originated, not eternal. The answer to this argument is that what was previously stated holds: the impossibility of creating the existent (ijad al-mawjud) with an existence obtained by other than this act of creation is not necessary. The true meaning of the agent's influence on a thing during its persistence is that the thing's existence depends on the agent's existence and continues by its continuation, without any new acquisition of what was not already attained, so the charge of 'creating the existent' does not apply. Thus the passage defends the Ash'ari view that an eternal effect from a necessary agent is impossible, and it refutes the philosophical counterargument.

Key Terms
الموجب بالذات
الفاعل بالاختيار
التخلف عن تمام العلة
ترجح بلا مرجح
إيجاد الموجود
حال البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an eternal effect from a necessitating agent is possible because the agent's causal action is essential and eternal, but this leads to preponderance without a preponderator and the absurdity of creating an already existent thing.
Correction: An eternal agent necessitates an eternal effect; a non-eternal effect implies a change (preponderance) without cause. Also, the charge of 'creating the existent' is avoided by understanding causal dependence during persistence.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument from the Imam (al-Razi) against an eternal effect from a necessitating agent?
A) The agent would be imperfect if it had an eternal effect.
B) The effect would require a preponderator at all times.
C) The agent's influence cannot be during the effect's persistence, as that would entail creating the already existent; it must be at origination.
D) The philosophers have agreed that an eternal effect is impossible.

Key Takeaway
An eternal agent necessitates an eternal effect; any non-eternal effect would require a preponderator, and the charge of 'creating the existent' is avoided by understanding causal dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence depends on God at every moment, as this lesson's response implies, why do you still act as if you are self-sufficient in your daily anxieties? What would change if you truly felt your dependence on Him moment by moment?
?
Do you sometimes treat God as a 'necessitating agent' in your mind, expecting Him to act according to your plans and timings, rather than as a free agent who can choose otherwise? How can you realign your heart to embrace His absolute freedom, even when His choices contradict your desires?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Philosophers' Argument for Matter and Duration
Presentation and rebuttal of the philosophers' argument for the pre-eternity of matter and time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that every temporal event requires matter and duration, and the Ash'ari rebuttal that distinguishes between essential and receptive contingency.

Core Idea
Every temporal event requires matter and time? Not if contingency is essential, not receptive.

The philosophers argue that for anything to come into existence, it must have a substrate (matter) and a duration (time). However, the Ash'ari response shows that this argument confuses two different types of contingency. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation, clarifying key concepts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the philosophers' claim that every temporal event is preceded by matter and duration.
Notice the footnotes (١)-(٤) indicating references in the edition.
2
Understand the argument for matter
The philosophers argue that before existence, the event is possible, and that possibility is existential, thus requiring a substrate. Identify why they think this substrate cannot be the event itself.
Contrast with the contingency of the eternal.
3
Examine the rebuttal
The author replies by distinguishing between essential contingency (al-imkān al-dhātī) and receptive contingency (al-imkān al-isti‘dādī). See how each type affects the need for a substrate.
Essential contingency is not existential; receptive contingency is not conceded for all events.
4
Review the definitions
The passage defines matter and duration. Matter can be a subject for accident, hyle for form, or relation for soul. Duration is time.
These definitions clarify what the philosophers mean.
5
Consider the conclusion
The philosophers build on this to claim the pre-eternity of matter and time, but the author limits this: not that specific substrata are eternal, nor that there is an infinite regress of matter.
The argument is cut off, but the direction of refutation is clear.

المبحث الثاني في زعم الفلاسفة أن الحادث له مادة ومدة (قال): زعمت الفلاسفة أن كل حادث مسبوق بمادة ومدة (١) . أما المادة (٢) . فلأنه قبل الوجود (۳) ممكن، وإمكانه وجودي يفتقر إلى الحل، وليس هو الحادث لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه، بخلاف إمكان لقديم . ورد بأنه إن أريد الإمكان الذاتي فلا نسلم أنه وجودي، وإن أريد الاستعدادي المخالف له في اقتضاء الرجحان والتفاوت والتحقق . فلا نسلم أن كل حادث ممكن . أي موجود بعدم العدم مسبوق بمادة ومدة. وعنوا بالمادة ما يكون موضوعاً للحادث إن كان عرضاً ، أو هيولا إن كان صورة، أو متعلقة إن كان نفساً، وبالمدة الزمان وبنوا على ذلك، قدم المادة والزمان، لا بمعنى أن محل هذا السواد، ومحل هذه مثلاً وبدون هذه النفس (٤) مثلاً قديم لظهور إستحالته، ولا بمعنى أن قبل كل مادة مادة لا إلى بداية كما في الحركة والزمان (لأنه يستلزم اجتماع المواد الغير متناهية في الوجود ضرورة أن كلاً منها جزء ما
The second investigation: concerning the claim of the philosophers that the temporal event has a matter and a duration. (He said): The philosophers claimed that every temporal event is preceded by a matter and a duration (1). As for matter (2), because before existence (3) it is contingent, and its contingency is existential, requiring a substrate, and it is not the temporal event itself because a thing cannot precede itself, unlike the contingency of the eternal. And it was replied: If what is meant is essential contingency, then we do not concede that it is existential; and if what is meant is receptive contingency, which differs from it in requiring preponderance, differentiation, and actualization, then we do not concede that every temporal event is contingent. That is, it exists after non-existence, preceded by matter and duration. By matter they meant that which is the subject of the temporal event if it is an accident, or the hyle if it is a form, or that which it is related to if it is a soul; and by duration they meant time. And upon that they built the pre-eternity of matter and time—not in the sense that the substratum of this blackness and the substratum of that [؟] and without this soul, for example, are pre-eternal because that is obviously impossible; nor in the sense that before every matter there is another matter without beginning, as in motion and time (because it entails the coexistence of infinite matters in existence, necessarily each of them is part of ...)
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage opens the second investigation and presents the philosophers' claim that every temporal event (ḥādith) must be preceded by a matter (mādda) and a duration (mudda, i.e., time). The philosophers argue for the necessity of matter because before existence, the temporal event is possible, and that possibility is an existential attribute that requires a substrate (ḥall). This substrate cannot be the event itself because that would involve self-priority. They contrast this with the contingency of the eternal. The author then gives a rebuttal: if by 'possibility' they mean essential contingency (al-imkān al-dhātī), it is not existential; if they mean receptive contingency (al-imkān al-isti‘dādī), which differs in implying preponderance, differentiation, and actualization, then it is not conceded that every temporal event is contingent in that sense. The passage then clarifies the definitions: matter is the subject for an accident, hyle for a form, or relation for a soul, and duration is time. The philosophers use this to argue for the pre-eternity of matter and time, but the author notes that this does not mean that the specific substratum of an accident is eternal (obviously false) nor that there is an infinite regress of matter (which would involve infinite coexistent matters, impossible). The argument is cut off, but the direction is a refutation of the philosophers' claim.

Misconception: One might think that the philosophers' argument successfully proves that matter is eternal in the sense that every specific matter (e.g., the substratum of a particular blackness) is eternal.
Correction: The author shows that this is obviously false, and the argument only suggests that matter in general is eternal, not individual matters.

Checkpoint

B) Because the event's possibility before existence requires a substrate
C) Because the event is an accident

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the philosophers' argument for the pre-eternity of matter and time hinges on distinguishing between essential and receptive contingency, showing that the philosophers' premise is not universally valid.

?

Where in your life do you need to repent of that subtle shirk?

Objection and Response: Temporal from Eternal
Objection against temporal from eternal, response via divine free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a temporal cannot depend on an eternal cause is answered by the principle of divine free will.

Core Idea
Divine free will breaks the necessity linking eternal cause to temporal effect.

This passage presents a classic objection against a temporal universe arising from an eternal Creator. The objection claims that if the cause is complete and eternal, its effect must also be eternal. The author offers a concise response rooted in divine freedom, a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (the objection) and understand its logic: a complete cause necessitates an eternal effect, so a temporal effect cannot come from an eternal cause without prior temporal events.
Notice the terms 'complete cause' (العلة التامة) and 'effect' (المعلول).
2
Identify the objection's conclusion
The objection concludes that a temporal being must be preceded by a chain of temporal events that prepare a substrate. This reflects a causal principle from philosophy.
3
Read the response
The response is brief: 'the eternal is free (mukhtar); He brings the temporal into existence whenever He wills.' Notice the pivot from necessity to will.
The term 'mukhtar' (اختيار) is key—it implies volition without constraint.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how divine free will overcomes the apparent causal necessity. This is a core doctrine in Ash'ari occasionalism, where God acts by will, not by nature.
This response preserves God's absolute sovereignty over creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قيل : دوام المعلول بدوام علته التامة ضروري فيمتنع استناد الحادث إلى القديم بل لا بد له من سبق حوادث متعاقبة مفيدة استعدادات متفاوتة مفتقرة إلى محل متعلق به . قلنا : القديم مختار يوجد الحادث متى شاء .
He said: For if it is said: The permanence of the effect with the permanence of its complete cause is necessary, so it is impossible for the temporal to be dependent on the eternal; rather, it must have prior successive temporal events producing varying preparations, which need a substrate related to them. We say: The eternal is free (mukhtar); He brings the temporal into existence whenever He wills.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection argues that if a cause is complete and eternal, its effect must be eternal as well (since the effect is simultaneous with the complete cause). Therefore, a temporal effect cannot arise from an eternal cause; rather, it must be preceded by a series of temporal events that gradually prepare a substrate, such as a material undergoing successive changes. The answer is straightforward: the eternal agent is free (mukhtar) and creates the temporal at the moment He wills, without needing a chain of preparatory events. This asserts the principle of divine will overriding causal necessity.

Correction: The author shows that divine free will allows a temporal effect from an eternal agent, as God creates by choice, not by necessity.

Checkpoint

B) By necessity, as the effect must accompany the cause.
C) By His free will, creating it whenever He wills.
D) Through an intermediary that is itself temporal.

Key Takeaway
Divine free will allows a temporal effect to arise from an eternal cause, refuting the objection that causal necessity requires the effect to be co-eternal with the cause.

Temporal Effects and the Eternal Cause: Objection and Response
Presents objection and kalam response on temporal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that a temporal effect cannot arise from an eternal cause and the Ash'ari response based on divine free will.

Core Idea
God's free will breaks the chain of necessary causation.

This passage presents a classic objection from philosophers who argue that an eternal cause would necessarily produce a perpetual effect, making temporal creation impossible. The Ash'ari response is concise but powerful: God is a free agent who creates whenever He wills, without needing preconditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the key terms: 'complete cause' (علة تامة) and 'perpetuity of the effect' (دوام المعلول).
The objector is using a principle from Aristotelian causality.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The objector argues that an effect must be simultaneous with its complete cause. Therefore, a temporal effect (hadith) cannot be grounded in an eternal cause (qadim).
This would seem to deny creatio ex nihilo.
3
Study the kalam response
The response is: 'The Eternal is free; He brings the temporal thing into existence whenever He wills.' This rejects the necessitarian framework.
Freedom (ikhtiyar) is the key attribute here.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
This exchange shows how Ash'ari theology prioritizes divine will and power over philosophical necessity.
It's a foundational argument against the philosophers.

Instead, it must be preceded by a series of temporal events that prepare the subject. The response is concise: God is a free agent (mukhtar); He creates the temporal thing whenever He wills, without needing a temporal precondition. This establishes the Ash‘ari principle of divine freedom against philosophic necessitarianism.

Essential Possibility: A Relational Attribute
First philosophical answer: essential possibility and its need for a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that the possibility of a temporal thing before its existence is a relational attribute (essential possibility) requiring a subject, and the weaknesses of this view.

Core Idea
Essential possibility is not an independent thing but a relation that still needs a subject.

The philosophers attempt to resolve the problem by arguing that 'possibility' before existence is not a separate entity but a relation to a subject. This passage details their argument, distinguishing between essential and accidental existence, and then exposes its flaws. Pay attention to how they use the categories of substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recall the central problem: If a temporal thing is possible before it exists, that possibility seems to be an existing attribute. But if it exists, where does it inhere? The philosophers propose that possibility is merely a relation (essential possibility).
2
Follow the existence distinction
The passage explains existence as either essential (in itself) or accidental (through another). It then examines possibility in relation to each type, concluding that for dependent things (accidents, forms), possibility requires a subject. For self-subsistent things, possibility before existence would be self-subsistent, which is impossible because only substances are self-subsistent.
Notice the example of whiteness existing in a body versus a self-subsistent thing.
3
Examine the critique
The author states that this view is weak because the original problem remains and because the argument that possibility must be an existing thing is itself flawed. Even if one accepted that, it would not require the detailed subdivision given.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Avoid thinking that 'essential possibility' means possibility is a real entity. The text clarifies it is relational, but even as a relation it needs a subject—which still poses a problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم أن للفلاسفة في التقصي عن هذا الإشكال وجهين : أحدهما: أن المراد الإمكان الذاتي ومعنى كون إمكان الحادث قبل وجوده وجودياً، تعلقه بموضوع موجود في الخارج، وتقريره أن الإمكان لا محالة يكون بالقياس إلى وجود والوجود إما بالذات، كوجود البياض في نفسه، وإما بالعرض كوجود الجسم أبيض، أما الإمكان بالقياس إلى وجود بالعرض، وهو إمكان أن يوجد شيء شيئاً آخر، أو يوجد له شيء آخر، كالبياض للجسم، والصورة للمادة والنفس للبدن ولا خفاء في احتياجه إلى وجود شيء حتى يوجد له شيء آخر، وأما الإمكان بالقياس إلى وجود بالذات، وهو إمكان وجود الشيء في نفسه فذلك الشيء إن كان مما يتعلق وجوده بالغير، أي يكون بحيث إذا وجد كان موجوداً في غيره كالعرض والصورة، أو مع غيره كالنفس، فهو كالأول في الاحتياج إلى موضوع يقوم به إمكان ذلك الشيء قبل وجوده بمعنى كون ذلك الشيء في ذلك الموضوع أو معه بالقوة، فهو صفة للموضوع من حيث هو فيه كعرض في موضوع وصفة للشيء من حيث هو بالقياس إليه كإضافة المضاف إليه، وإن لم يكن ذلك الشيء مما يتعلق وجوده بالغير، بل يكون قائماً بنفسه من غير تعلق بموضوع، أو مادة، فمثله لا يجوز أن يكون حادثاً، وإلا لكان إمكانه قبل حدوثه قائماً بنفسه، إذ لا علاقة له بشيء من الموضوعات ليقوم به، وهو محال لأنه عرض لا جوهر، وضعف هذا الوجه ظاهر لأن الإشكال عائد ولأنه لو ثبت أن إمكان الحادث عرضي يستدعي محلاً، أو استدل على ذلك استدلالاً فاسداً بأنه لو لم يكن إمكان الحادث أمراً موجوداً لم يكن للحادث إمكان وجود، فلم يكن ممكن الوجود على ما في الشفاء، لم يحتج إلى ذكر من التفاصيل .
Know that the philosophers have two ways of dealing with this problem: First: that what is intended is essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati), and the meaning of the possibility of a temporal thing being existential before its existence is its relation to a subject existing externally. Its explanation: possibility necessarily relates to existence, and existence is either essential (bi-al-dhat), like the existence of whiteness in itself, or accidental (bi-al-‘arad), like the existence of a body as white. As for possibility in relation to accidental existence—that is, the possibility that a thing exist as something else, or that something else exist for it, like whiteness for a body, form for matter, and soul for the body—there is no doubt that it needs a thing to exist for the sake of another thing. As for possibility in relation to essential existence—that is, the possibility of the existence of a thing in itself—if that thing is such that its existence depends on another (i.e., when it exists it exists in something else, like accident and form, or with something else, like the soul), then it is like the first in needing a subject to sustain its possibility before its existence, meaning that thing being in that subject or with it in potency. Thus it is a quality of the subject in terms of being in it, like an accident in a subject, and a quality of the thing in terms of its relation to it, like a relation of the related. But if that thing is not such that its existence depends on another, but is self-subsistent without attachment to a subject or matter, then such a thing cannot be temporal; otherwise its possibility before its occurrence would be self-subsistent, since it has no relation to any subject to sustain it, which is impossible because it is an accident, not a substance. The weakness of this view is clear because the problem returns, and because if it were established that the possibility of a temporal thing is accidental requiring a subject—or one deduces such a corrupt deduction that if the possibility of the temporal thing were not an existing thing, then the temporal thing would not have possibility of existence, and thus would not be possible of existence, according to what is in al-Shifa—then there would be no need to mention such details.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical answer to the problem. The philosophers argue that when we speak of the 'possibility' (imkan) of a temporal thing before its existence, what is meant is essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati), which is not a separate entity but a relation to a subject. The text distinguishes two types of existence: existence in itself (essential) and existence through another (accidental). Possibility in relation to accidental existence requires a subject. For example, whiteness exists in a body; its possibility before existence is a quality of that body. For possibility in relation to essential existence: if the thing itself depends on another (like an accident), it similarly needs a subject. But if the thing is self-subsistent (qaim bi-nafsihi), it cannot be temporal, otherwise its possibility before existence would be self-subsistent—an impossibility, as possibility is an accident. The author then criticizes this view: the problem remains, and the argument that possibility must be an existing thing is flawed; even if one accepts that, it does not require the detailed explanation given.

وجود بالذات
وجود بالعرض
عرض
جوهر
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'essential possibility' means possibility is a real, independent attribute.
Correction: The passage explains that essential possibility is a relational attribute that does not exist independently but requires a subject.

Checkpoint
According to the first philosophical answer, why can't a self-subsistent thing be temporal?
A) Because it would need a subject.
B) Because its possibility before existence would be self-subsistent, which is impossible for an accident.
C) Because self-subsistent things are eternal.
D) Because the philosophers deny temporal creation.

Key Takeaway
Essential possibility is a relational attribute that requires a subject, but this view does not solve the original problem and is logically flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might that distort your understanding of your own dependence on Allah? Today, identify one concept you treat as a thing and resolve to see it as a relation instead.
?
The philosophers offered a detailed classification to solve the problem of possibility before existence, but the author shows it fails. When you face a deep theological puzzle, do you settle for a clever-sounding answer, or do you press until the problem is truly resolved? Think of a time you accepted an explanation that later felt incomplete. What will you do differently now?
?
Essential possibility means everything temporal depends on a subject. In your daily life, do you ever act as if you are self-subsistent—independent of Allah? Identify one area where you unconsciously rely on yourself. Commit today to acknowledging your contingency before Allah in that area.

The Second Philosophical Answer: Preparatory Possibility
Explains the second answer: preparatory possibility through successive temporal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi) and why successive temporal conditions are necessary for temporal things.

Core Idea
Temporal things require a chain of successive preparatory conditions, not an eternal cause alone.

This passage presents the second philosophical answer to the problem of temporal origination: preparatory possibility. The argument shows that the complete cause of a temporal thing cannot be the Eternal alone, but requires a series of successive temporal conditions that prepare the effect without coexisting. This resolves the infinite regress issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is cut off at the end due to page break, but the argument is structurally complete up to that point.
2
Identify the main claim and its justification
The passage argues that preparatory possibility is what is intended. Note the proof: the complete cause of a temporal thing cannot be the Eternal alone, else the effect would be eternal.
Look for the phrase 'وإلا لزم قدم الحادث' which gives the key reasoning.
3
Understand the resolution of infinite regress
The passage rejects dependence on an infinite chain collectively and instead proposes a series of successive temporal conditions, each preparing the next without coexisting. This resolves the regress.
Think of celestial motions as an example of such successive preparations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: أن المراد الإمكان الاستعدادي والدليل قائم على ثبوته لكل حادث وتقريره أن العلة التامة للحادث، لا يجوز أن تكون ذات القديم وحده، أو مع شرط قديم، وإلا لزم قدم الحادث، لأن المعلول دائم بدوام علته التامة بالضرورة، لما في التخلف من الترجح بلا مرجح، بل لا بد من شرط حادث، وحدوثه يتوقف على شرط آخر حادث، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، ويمتنع توقف الحادث على تلك الحوادث جملة لامتناع التسلسل، ولأن مجموعها بحدوثه، يفتقر إلى شرط آخر حادث، فيكون داخلاً خارجاً، وهو محال بل لابد من حوادث متعاقبة، يكون كل سابق منها معداً لللاحق من غير اجتماع في الوجود كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية، ويحصل بحسبها للحادث حالات مقربة إلى الفيضان من العلة استعداداته المتفاوتة في
Second: that what is intended is preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti‘dadi), and the proof stands for its being established for every temporal thing. Its explanation: the complete cause of a temporal thing cannot be the Essence of the Eternal alone, or together with an eternal condition; otherwise the temporality of the temporal thing would be necessary, because the effect is perpetual with the perpetuity of its complete cause, necessarily, due to the need for a preponderator without preponderator in case of delay. Rather, there must be a temporal condition, and its occurrence depends on another temporal condition, and so on ad infinitum. It is impossible for the temporal thing to depend on those temporal things collectively, because of the impossibility of infinite regress, and because their totality, in its occurrence, would need another temporal condition, thus it would be both inside and outside, which is impossible. Rather, there must be successive temporal things, each preceding one being a preparation for the following, without coexisting in existence, like celestial motions and positions. According to them, for the temporal thing there occur states bringing it closer to the emanation from the cause, its varying preparatory states.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical answer: preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti‘dadi). The argument holds that the complete cause of a temporal thing cannot be the Eternal alone or with an eternal condition, because then the effect would be eternal. Instead, a temporal condition is necessary. But that condition itself requires another temporal condition, leading to an infinite regress. However, the temporal thing cannot depend on an infinite series collectively, as that would regress further and lead to paradox. The solution: there must be a series of successive temporal conditions (like celestial motions), each preparing the next, never coexisting, and through them the temporal thing acquires varying states of preparation that bring it closer to actualization from the cause.

Key Terms
إمكان استعدادي
علة تامة
حوادث متعاقبة
استعداد
فيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That temporal things can be created directly by the Eternal without any temporal intermediary conditions.
Correction: The argument shows that a temporal condition is necessary to avoid the effect being eternal; otherwise, the effect would be co-eternal with the cause.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the complete cause of a temporal thing be the Eternal alone?
A) Because the Eternal cannot cause temporal things.
B) Because the effect would then be eternal.
C) Because the temporal thing needs a material subject.
D) Because the Eternal is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
The second answer resolves the issue of temporal causation by positing a chain of successive preparatory conditions, each preparing the next without coexisting, thereby avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you acknowledge that each step is a preparation from Allah, or do you assume your own will is sufficient? What would change if you saw every condition as a divine preparation?
?
The lesson teaches that temporal conditions must be successive, not all at once. Do you ever demand that Allah bring all His blessings at once, impatient with the gradual nature of His provision? How can you trust His timing tonight?
?
Consider a difficulty you are facing now. Instead of seeing it as the final cause, can you trace the chain of preparatory states Allah has placed before it? Does that perspective shift your reliance from the secondary cause to the Primary Cause?

Preparedness Possibility and Its Refutation by Divine Free Will
Taftazani presents the proof for preparedness possibility and refutes it using divine free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of preparedness possibility (imkan isti'dadi) is rejected in Sunni kalam because it presupposes God acts by necessity, whereas He acts by free will.

Core Idea
Preparedness possibility assumes God is a necessary cause; free will eliminates the need for it.

Taftazani continues his discussion of possibility by examining the second interpretation: preparedness possibility. He first presents the rational argument that seems to require a chain of preparatory conditions, but then reveals its fatal flaw. The argument ultimately fails because it wrongly assumes that God's creative act is necessary rather than voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal English translation to understand the argument. Notice the structure: first the proof for imkan isti'dadi, then the critique.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Trace the logical steps of the proof
Identify the steps: (a) The complete cause of a contingent cannot be eternal alone. (b) So a contingent condition is needed. (c) That leads to infinite regress, which is impossible. (d) So there must be successive preparatory conditions, each preparing the next, requiring a substrate (matter). This is the 'preparedness' argument.
Note that the proof is presented as the opponent's view.
3
Identify the refutation
Taftazani declares the argument weak because it is based on the assumption that the Creator is an agent by essence (mujib bi al-dhat) – i.e., acting by necessity. In contrast, a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar) can create directly whenever His will attaches, without needing any contingent condition.
This is the key turn in the argument.
4
Contrast the two assumptions
Understand the difference between 'mujib bi al-dhat' (necessary causality) and 'fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar' (free agency). The first requires a chain of causes; the second does not.
This distinction is central to Islamic theology.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Why does this matter for Sunni kalam? It upholds God's absolute freedom and power, and avoids the necessity of an eternal world or a chain of preparatory causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: أن المراد الإمكان الاستعدادي والدليل قائم على ثبوته لكل حادث وتقريره أن العلة التامة للحادث، لا يجوز أن تكون ذات القديم وحده، أو مع شرط قديم، وإلا لزم قدم الحادث، لأن المعلول دائم بدوام علته التامة بالضرورة، لما في التخلف من الترجح بلا مرجح، بل لا بد من شرط حادث، وحدوثه يتوقف على شرط آخر حادث، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، ويمتنع توقف الحادث على تلك الحوادث جملة لامتناع التسلسل، ولأن مجموعها بحدوثه يفتقر إلى شرط آخر حادث، فيكون داخلاً خارجاً، وهو محال بل لابد من حوادث متعاقبة، يكون كل سابق منها معدا للاحق من غير اجتماع في الوجود كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية، ويحصل بحسبها للحادث حالات مقربة إلى الفيضان من العلة استعداداته المتفاوتة في القرب والبعد المفتقر إلى محل ليس هو نفس الحادث، ولا أمراً منفصلاً عنه متعلقاً به هو المعنى بالمادة وهذا أيضاً ضعيف، لابتنائه لما تقدم، بل على كون الصانع القديم موجباً بالذات إذ الفاعل بالاختيار يوجد الحادث متى تعلق به إرادته القديمة التي من شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص، من غير توقف على شرط حادث.
And the second [interpretation]: that what is meant is preparedness possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi), and the proof stands for its being established for every contingent. Its exposition is that the complete cause (al-'illa al-tammah) of the contingent cannot be the essence of the Eternal alone, or with an eternal condition; otherwise the contingency would be eternal (qadim), because the effect is perpetual with the perpetuity of its complete cause by necessity, since delay would involve preponderance without a preponderator. Rather, there must be a contingent condition (shart hadith), and its coming-to-be depends on another contingent condition, and so on to infinity. And it is impossible for the contingent to depend on those contingents as a whole because of the impossibility of infinite regress, and because their totality, by its coming-to-be, depends on another contingent condition, thus [the condition] would be both inside and outside, which is impossible. Rather, there must be successive contingents, each preceding one being a preparation (mu'idd) for the succeeding one without coexisting in existence, like celestial movements and positions. And by means of them, states closer to emanation from the cause occur for the contingent, its varying preparednesses (isti'dadat) in nearness and distance, which require a substrate that is neither the contingent itself nor something separate from it, attached to it—that is what is meant by matter (madda). And this also is weak, because it is based on what preceded, indeed on [the assumption] that the Eternal Maker is an agent by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), whereas the free agent (al-fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar) brings the contingent into existence whenever His eternal will, which is characterized by preponderance and specification, attaches to it, without depending on a contingent condition.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the second interpretation of possibility: preparedness possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi). The author first states the proof: every contingent must have preparedness possibility. The reasoning is that the complete cause of a contingent cannot be the Eternal essence alone or with an eternal condition, because that would necessitate the effect being eternal (qadim) due to the principle that the effect is simultaneous with its complete cause. Therefore, a contingent condition (shart hadith) is needed. But that contingent condition itself depends on another, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Since infinite regress is impossible, and the totality of all conditions would itself be contingent and require another condition (leading to circularity), the only solution is a series of successive contingents that do not coexist, each preparing (mu'idd) the next, like celestial motions. These create varying states of preparedness requiring a substrate (madda). The author then declares this argument weak because it assumes the Creator is an agent by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), i.e., acting by necessity. In contrast, the free agent (al-fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar) can create directly whenever His eternal will specifies without needing any contingent condition. Thus, the whole chain of preparedness is unnecessary.

Key Terms
إمكان استعدادي
علة تامة
شرط حادث
تسلسل
معد
مادة
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that every causal chain requires an infinite regress of preparatory conditions or a substrate of matter for the world to exist.
Correction: This is only required if God acts by necessity (mujib bi al-dhat). In Sunni theology, God is a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar) and can bring about the contingent directly by His will, without any chain of preparedness.

Checkpoint
On what assumption does the proof for preparedness possibility rely, according to the author?
A) That the Creator is a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar).
B) That the Creator is an agent by essence (mujib bi al-dhat).
C) That infinite regress is possible.
D) That matter is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Preparedness possibility is rejected because it assumes God's action is necessary, whereas Sunni kalam affirms God's free will, making any chain of preparatory causes unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever behave as if God's will alone is not enough for your situation to change—as if you need certain worldly circumstances to 'prepare the way' first? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If you truly believed that God's eternal will can bring anything into existence without any preparatory cause, what would you ask Him for today that you have been holding back because you thought it was not 'ready' or 'possible'?
?
The author argues that the free agent creates whenever His will attaches, without needing conditions. When was the last time you acted as if God needed something else to happen before He could answer your prayer? What does this reveal about your view of God's power and your own trust in Him?

Time and Contingents: Claim and Refutation
Taftazani presents and refutes the claim that every contingent must be preceded by time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some argue that every contingent must be preceded by time, and how Taftazani refutes this by introducing precedence by essence (bi al-dhat).

Core Idea
Not all precedence requires time; essential precedence exists within time itself.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a key theological argument about time and contingency. The opponent claims that every contingent being must be preceded by time, based on the succession of contingents and the priority of non-existence over existence. The author refutes this by pointing to essential precedence that does not depend on an external temporal framework, using the parts of time as an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the compact style and the use of technical terms like 'mudda', 'sabq', and 'zaman'.
The passage ends mid-sentence; this is a natural break in the manuscript.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that time must precede every contingent. The passage gives two reasons: the succession of contingents and the priority of non-existence over existence. Find these in the text.
The claim is presented in the first part of the passage before 'wa radda bi'.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation begins with 'wa radda bi' and argues that the opponent's premises are not universally valid. Taftazani then offers his own view: precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat), as in the parts of time themselves.
The refutation targets the opponent's classification of precedence.
4
Examine the types of precedence
The passage lists several types of precedence: by causality, nature, time, honor, and sensible or intellectual order. Taftazani adds essential precedence as a distinct category.
This classification is key to understanding the argument.
5
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology. If precedence can be essential, then the need for an external time is removed, preserving the possibility of a beginningless series of events.
This connects to broader debates on the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فلأن تعاقب الحوادث، وسبق العدم على الوجود، لا يتصور إلا بالزمان. ورد بأن مبنى الأول على ما مر والثاني على ما زعموا أن السبق ومقابلته يكون إما بالعلية، أو بالطبع، أو بالزمان أو بالشرف، أو بالرتبة الحسية، أو العقلية طبعاً أو وضعاً. وعندنا قد يكون بالذات كما في أجزاء الزمان من غير افتقار إلى زمان آخر، ولا يضرنا تسميته زمانياً على ما قال بعضهم إن السبق بالزمان قسمان وبعضهم إن الحقيقي منه ليس إلا الذي فيما بين أجزاء الزمان وإنما يعرض للغير بواسطته، حتى إن مضى تقدم الأب على الابن، تقدم زمانه على زمانه، وقد يرجع الرتبي والشرفي أيضاً إلى الزماني، والزماني ما
And as for time (al-mudda): because the succession of contingents and the priority of non-existence over existence cannot be conceived except by time. And it is refuted by [the argument] that the basis of the first [premise] is as has passed, and the second [premise] is based on what they claimed: that precedence and its opposite are either by causality, or by nature, or by time, or by rank, or by sensible or intellectual order, natural or positional. In our view, precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat) as in the parts of time, without needing another time. And it does not harm us to call it temporal (zamani) according to what some said: that precedence by time is of two types, and some said that the real [precedence] is only that which is among the parts of time, and it accidentally attaches to other things through it. Thus if the precedence of the father over the son passes, [it is] the precedence of his time over his time. And rank-based and honorific precedence may also reduce to temporal precedence. And temporal [precedence] is what
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that every contingent must be preceded by time (mudda). The argument is that the succession of contingents (one after another) and the priority of non-existence over existence cannot be conceived without time. The author refutes this by noting that the first premise (succession) relies on earlier assumptions, and the second premise (priority of non-existence) depends on the opponents' classification of precedence: by causality, nature, time, rank, sensible or intellectual order. In the author's view (uindana, 'in our view'), precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat), as in the parts of time themselves: a part of time precedes another part of time directly, without requiring a separate time. He then discusses that calling this 'temporal' is not problematic, because some scholars divide temporal precedence into two types: the real type is that between parts of time, and other types (like father-son) derive from it. He also notes that rank-based and honorific precedence can reduce to temporal precedence. The passage ends mid-sentence, suggesting a continuation on the next page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All precedence is temporal, so every contingent must be preceded by time.
Correction: The author shows that precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat) without requiring an external time, as in the parts of time themselves.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The kalam position holds that essential precedence (bi al-dhat) obviates the need for an external time to precede every contingent, as exemplified by the parts of time themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The source shows that honorific precedence can reduce to temporal precedence. Are there ways you seek honor or status that are ultimately tied to worldly time—like achievements that fade? What would it look like to seek honor that is based on essence—pleasing Allah—regardless of when it comes?

The Necessity of Duration Preceding Every Originated Thing
States the claim and records an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that duration must precede every originated thing and the objection raised against it.

Core Idea
Duration must precede every event, but is time truly necessary?

This lesson introduces a key claim in kalam: that every originated thing requires a preceding duration. The author presents the reasoning based on succession and non-existence, then records an objection that challenges the assumptions. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the Islamic view of time and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about duration
Read the Arabic passage that states the necessity of duration preceding every originated thing. Notice the reasoning: succession of events and precedence of non-existence require time.
2
Examine the objection
Read the objection which argues that the premises rely on earlier assumptions and on a specific classification of precedence. Understand the five/six types mentioned.
The objection is a common counterargument against making time a necessary condition.
3
Reflect on the issue
Ask yourself: why does this matter? It touches on the nature of time and whether time is an independent entity or a relation.
This connects to the broader kalam debate on temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب سبق المدة لكل حادث
وأما المدة فلأن تعاقب الحوادث، وسبق العدم على الوجود، لا يتصور إلا بالزمان.
ورد بأن مبنى الأول على ما مر والثاني على ما زعموا أن السبق ومقابلته يكون: إما بالعلية، أو بالطبع، أو بالزمان أو بالشرف، أو بالرتبة الحسية، أو العقلية طبعاً أو وضعاً.
وعندنا: قد يكون بالذات كما في أجزاء الزمان من غير افتقار إلى زمان آخر، ولا يضرنا تسميته زمانياً على ما قال بعضهم إن السبق بالزمان قسمان وبعضهم إن الحقيقي منه ليس إلا الذي فيما بين أجزاء الزمان وإنما يعرض للغير بواسطته، حتى إن مضى تقدم الأب على الابن، تقدم زمانه على زمانه، وقد يرجع الرتبي والشرفي أيضاً إلى الزماني، والزماني ما [؟] إلى بالطبع فينحصر التقدم بالحقيقة فيما بالذات وما بالطبع.
The necessity of duration preceding every originated thing. As for duration, because the succession of originated things and the precedence of non-existence over existence are only conceivable through time. Objection: the first premise is based on what has passed, and the second on their assumption that precedence and its opposite are either by causation, or by nature, or by time, or by honor, or by sensory or intellectual rank by nature or position. According to us: it may be by essence as in parts of time without needing another time, and it does not harm us to call it temporal according to what some said that precedence by time is of two types, and some said that the true one is only that between parts of time and it occurs to others through it, such that even if the precedence of the father over the son is considered, it is the precedence of his time over his time. And the rank-based and honor-based may also reduce to the temporal, and the temporal is that which [?] to nature, so true precedence is confined to what is by essence and what is by nature.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessity of duration (al-mudda) preceding every originated thing (hadith). It begins by stating the claim that duration must precede every hadith because the succession of events and the precedence of non-existence over existence are only conceivable through time. Then an objection is raised: the first premise relies on an earlier assumption, and the second relies on their claim that precedence and its opposite are only of five types: causation, nature, time, honor, and rank. According to the author's school, precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat), as in the parts of time themselves without needing another time. He then notes that some scholars divide temporal precedence into two categories, while others restrict true temporal precedence to the relation between parts of time, attributing it to others only by extension. For example, the father's precedence over the son is actually his time's precedence over the son's time. Rank-based and honor-based precedence may also reduce to temporal precedence. The passage concludes that true precedence is confined to what is by essence and what is by nature, thereby challenging the necessity of time for all originated things.

الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time is an absolute necessity for all precedence.
Correction: The objection shows that there are other types of precedence (by causation, nature, etc.) that do not require time.

Checkpoint

A) Duration is not necessary for originated things.
B) Duration must precede every originated thing.
C) Time is an illusion.
D) Precedence can only be by time.

Key Takeaway
The claim that duration precedes every originated thing is defended by succession and non-existence arguments, but an objection points to alternative types of precedence.

Types of Precedence and True Precedence
Presents the author's refinement of precedence types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the objection, distinguishing between precedence by essence, by nature, and the reduction of other types to temporal precedence.

Core Idea
True precedence is by essence or nature, not all temporal.

After the objection, the author presents his school's view: precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat), as in the parts of time themselves. He then discusses how other forms of precedence relate to temporal precedence and concludes that true precedence is confined to essence and nature. This clarifies the nuanced Islamic position on the relationship between time and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's response
Read from 'وعندنا' to the end. Notice that the author says precedence may be by essence, as in parts of time itself.
This is a key point: time does not need another time to precede.
2
Understand the reduction of types
The author notes that some scholars divide temporal precedence into two, others restrict true temporal precedence to parts of time. Then rank-based and honor-based precedence reduce to temporal.
The final conclusion: true precedence is only by essence and by nature.
3
Reflect on the overall debate
Consider how this refines the initial claim: duration is necessary but not all precedence is temporal. The author preserves the necessity while acknowledging other types.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب سبق المدة لكل حادث
وأما المدة فلأن تعاقب الحوادث، وسبق العدم على الوجود، لا يتصور إلا بالزمان.
ورد بأن مبنى الأول على ما مر والثاني على ما زعموا أن السبق ومقابلته يكون: إما بالعلية، أو بالطبع، أو بالزمان أو بالشرف، أو بالرتبة الحسية، أو العقلية طبعاً أو وضعاً.
وعندنا: قد يكون بالذات كما في أجزاء الزمان من غير افتقار إلى زمان آخر، ولا يضرنا تسميته زمانياً على ما قال بعضهم إن السبق بالزمان قسمان وبعضهم إن الحقيقي منه ليس إلا الذي فيما بين أجزاء الزمان وإنما يعرض للغير بواسطته، حتى إن مضى تقدم الأب على الابن، تقدم زمانه على زمانه، وقد يرجع الرتبي والشرفي أيضاً إلى الزماني، والزماني ما [؟] إلى بالطبع فينحصر التقدم بالحقيقة فيما بالذات وما بالطبع.
The necessity of duration preceding every originated thing. As for duration, because the succession of originated things and the precedence of non-existence over existence are only conceivable through time. Objection: the first premise is based on what has passed, and the second on their assumption that precedence and its opposite are either by causation, or by nature, or by time, or by honor, or by sensory or intellectual rank by nature or position. According to us: it may be by essence as in parts of time without needing another time, and it does not harm us to call it temporal according to what some said that precedence by time is of two types, and some said that the true one is only that between parts of time and it occurs to others through it, such that even if the precedence of the father over the son is considered, it is the precedence of his time over his time. And the rank-based and honor-based may also reduce to the temporal, and the temporal is that which [?] to nature, so true precedence is confined to what is by essence and what is by nature.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessity of duration (al-mudda) preceding every originated thing (hadith). It begins by stating the claim that duration must precede every hadith because the succession of events and the precedence of non-existence over existence are only conceivable through time. Then an objection is raised: the first premise relies on an earlier assumption, and the second relies on their claim that precedence and its opposite are only of five types: causation, nature, time, honor, and rank. According to the author's school, precedence can be by essence (bi al-dhat), as in the parts of time themselves without needing another time. He then notes that some scholars divide temporal precedence into two categories, while others restrict true temporal precedence to the relation between parts of time, attributing it to others only by extension. For example, the father's precedence over the son is actually his time's precedence over the son's time. Rank-based and honor-based precedence may also reduce to temporal precedence. The passage concludes that true precedence is confined to what is by essence and what is by nature, thereby challenging the necessity of time for all originated things.

Misconception: That all precedence is temporal.
Correction: The author argues that true precedence is by essence or nature, and temporal precedence is a subset that itself reduces to natural precedence.

Checkpoint

A) By time and by honor
B) By essence and by nature
C) By causation and by time
D) By rank and by honor

Key Takeaway
True precedence is by essence (as in parts of time) or by nature; temporal, rank-based, and honor-based precedence are derivative.
