First Proof for the Precedence of Time and Its Refutation
First proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument that every originated thing requires preceding successive events implying time, and how the refutation points out its reliance on a previously rejected premise.

Core Idea
Time is not automatically required for every originated thing.

The first argument the opponents raise is that an originated thing necessarily involves a succession of prior events, which implies time. But this argument depends on a premise that has already been discussed and rejected. Let's examine the logic and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Carefully read the Arabic passage stating the first proof. Note how it argues that any originated thing requires a sequence of prior events, which can only be explained by time.
The proof assumes that every originated thing must have preceding originated events.
2
Examine the refutation
Read the refutation that points out this proof rests on the need for preceding successive events, a claim that has been discussed and rejected earlier in the text.
The refutation is concise; it references a previous discussion.
3
Reflect on the assumption
Consider why the refutation is effective: it shows that the proof's premise is not necessarily true, thus the argument for time's precedence fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على كون الحادث مسبوقاً بالزمان بوجهين:
أحدهما: أنه لا بد له من سبق حوادث متعاقبة بمعنى حصول هذا بعد حصول ذاك، بحيث لا يجتمع المتقدم والمتأخر، وما ذاك إلا بالزمان.
وثانيهما: أنه لا معنى للحادث إلا ما يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم، وظاهر أن سبق عدم الشيء على وجوده لا يعقل إلا بالزمان، وهذا التقرير لا يبتني على أن التقدم أمر وجودي، وأنه هو الزمان حتى يرد الاعتراض بأنا لا نسلم أنه وجودي، بل اعتباري يعرض للعدم أيضاً، والحاكم بثبوته الوهم وحكمه مردود كما في تحيز الباري حيث يحكم به الوهم بناء على أن ما يشاهد من الموجودات متحيزة، وإنما يبتنى على صحة الحكم بأن هذا متقدم على ذلك كقدم الحادث على وجوده ولا خفاء في أنه حكم عقلي ضروري والزمان معروض التقدم لا نفسه.
والجواب: أن مبنى الأول على افتقار كل حادث إلى سبق حوادث متعاقبة، وقد مر ما فيه.
ومبنى الثاني: على ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من أن أقسام التقدم والتأخر والمعية منحصرة بحكم الاستقراء في خمسة، بمعنى أن كلا منها يكون إما بالعلية، كتقدم حركة اليد على حركة المفتاح وإما بالطبع كتقدم الجزء على الكل، وإما بالزمان كتقدم الأب على الابن وإما بالشرف كتقدم المعلم على المتعلم، وإما بالرتبة وهي قد تكون حسية بأن يكون الحكم بالترتيب، وتقدم البعض على البعض مأخوذاً من الحس لكونه في الأمور
They argued that the originated thing is preceded by time in two ways: First: it inevitably has preceding successive originated things, meaning that this occurs after that occurs, such that the earlier and later do not coincide, and that is only through time. Second: The meaning of originated is nothing but that its existence is preceded by non-existence, and it is apparent that the precedence of non-existence of a thing over its existence is only conceivable through time. This presentation does not depend on that precedence is an existential matter and that it is time itself such that an objection could be made that we do not concede it is existential, but rather a relational concept that also applies to non-existence, and the judge of its existence is illusion and its judgment is rejected as in the case of God's spatial occupation where illusion judges based on observed existents being spatially located. Rather, it depends on the validity of the judgment that this precedes that, like the precedence of the originated thing over its existence, and there is no doubt that it is a necessary intellectual judgment, and time is the locus of precedence, not precedence itself. The answer: the first proof is based on every originated thing needing preceding successive originated things, and what was said about it has already passed. The second proof is based on what the philosophers hold: that the divisions of precedence, posteriority, and simultaneity are confined by induction to five: either by causation (like the precedence of hand movement over key movement), or by nature (like the precedence of a part over the whole), or by time (like the precedence of a father over a son), or by honor (like the precedence of a teacher over a student), or by rank (which may be sensory, such that the judgment of order and precedence of some over others is taken from sense, because it is in matters...).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that every originated thing (hadith) must be preceded by time, followed by their refutation. The first proof argues that an originated thing requires a succession of prior originated events, which implies time. The second proof defines the originated as that whose existence is preceded by non-existence, arguing that such precedence of non-existence over existence is only conceivable through time. The author notes that this second proof does not rely on precedence being an existential attribute (otherwise it could be countered as illusionary), but rather on the necessary intellectual judgment of precedence itself. The response refutes the first proof by pointing out that it depends on the assumption that every originated thing needs prior originated things, which has already been discussed and rejected earlier. The second proof is refuted by showing that it rests on the philosophers' claim that the types of precedence are limited to five: causation, nature, time, honor, and rank. The author implies that this classification is not exhaustive and that precedence may be by essence or nature as earlier discussed, thus the necessity of time is not proven.

A) Every originated thing must have a cause.
B) Every originated thing requires preceding successive events.
C) Time is the locus of precedence.
D) Precedence is a necessary intellectual judgment.

Key Takeaway
The first argument fails because it assumes without justification that every originated thing needs preceding events.

?
When you hear arguments about the necessity of time for creation, do you tend to accept them without scrutinizing their foundations? After this lesson, what will you look for in similar arguments to avoid being misled by unproven premises?

Second Proof for the Precedence of Time and Its Refutation
Second proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the precedence of non-existence over existence requires time, and its refutation based on the philosophers' fivefold division of precedence being non-exhaustive.

Core Idea
Precedence of non-existence does not necessitate time.

The second proof argues that an originated thing's existence is preceded by its non-existence, and such precedence is only conceivable through time. The refutation shows this argument relies on a limited classification of precedence types that is not exhaustive, thus time is not necessarily involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the second proof carefully. Note how it defines an originated thing as that whose existence is preceded by non-existence, and asserts that this precedence is only conceivable through time.
The proof claims the precedence is a necessary intellectual judgment.
2
Understand the fivefold division
The refutation mentions the philosophers' claim that all types of precedence (causation, nature, time, honor, rank) are confined to five. The author implies this classification is not exhaustive.
The passage cuts off; the refutation likely continues that precedence can be by essence or nature, not necessarily time.
3
Analyze the refutation's logic
The refutation points out that the second proof builds on this limited classification. Since the classification is not exhaustive, the proof does not establish time as necessary.
The key is that the opponents' own framework does not force time as the only explanation.

The Philosophers' Fivefold Classification and Its Refutation
Responds to second proof with fivefold classification and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that precedence is limited to five types and the mutakallimun's refutation by counterexample.

Core Idea
The fivefold classification is incomplete – time itself can precede time.

In this passage, the author responds to the second proof by presenting the philosophers' division of precedence into five types: causality, nature, time, dignity, and rank. However, the mutakallimun reject the claim that this classification is exhaustive. They offer a compelling counterexample: the precedence of yesterday over today, which does not fit any of the five types.

Guided Path
1
Dismiss first proof
The first proof is briefly dismissed as already addressed. Focus on the second proof, which is based on the philosophers' classification.
2
Learn the five types
Read the classification: causality, nature, time, dignity, and rank. Notice examples for each.
Rank is further divided into sensory/intellectual and natural/conventional.
3
See the refutation
The mutakallimun deny exclusivity and completeness. Find their counterexample: precedence of parts of time.
They argue that yesterday's precedence over today is not by time because both are time itself.
4
Understand the conclusion
This counterexample shows the classification is not exhaustive, thus the second proof fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن مبنى الأول على افتقار كل حادث إلى سبق حوادث متعاقبة، وقد مر ما فيه . ومبنى الثاني : على ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من أن أقسام التقدم والتأخر والمعية منحصرة بحكم الاستقراء في خمسة، بمعنى أن كلا منها يكون إما بالعلية، كتقدم حركة اليد على حركة المفتاح وإما بالطبع كتقدم الجزء على الكل، وإما بالزمان كتقدم الأب على الابن وإما بالشرف كتقدم المعلم على المتعلم ، وإما بالرتبة وهي قد تكون حسية بأن يكون الحكم بالترتيب، وتقدم البعض على البعض مأخوذاً من الحس لكونه في الأمور المحسوسة، وقد تكون عقلية بأن يكون ذلك بحكم العقل لكونه في الأمور المعقولة، وكل منهما قد يكون بحسب الطبع، وقد يكون بحسب الوضع، وذلك كتقدم الرأس على الرقبة، وتقدم الإمام على المأموم، وتقدم الجنس على النوع، وتقدم بعض مسائل العلم على البعض ومعلوم أن تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده ليس إلا بالزمان والمتكلمون منعوا الحصر، وتمام الاستقرار. ونقضوه بتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض، كتقدم الأمس على اليوم، فإنه كما ليس بالعلية والطبع والرتبة والشرف ليس بالزمان، لأن الأمس واليوم زمان لا أمر يقع في الزمان.
The answer: The basis of the first proof is the dependence of every originated thing on preceding successive events, and what is in it has already been discussed. The basis of the second proof is what the philosophers have held, that the divisions of precedence, posteriority, and simultaneity are limited, by virtue of induction, to five, meaning that each of them is either by causality, like the precedence of hand movement over key movement; or by nature, like the precedence of the part over the whole; or by time, like the precedence of the father over the son; or by dignity, like the precedence of the teacher over the student; or by rank, which may be sensory, such that the judgment of order and the precedence of some over others is taken from the senses because it is in sensory matters, or intellectual, such that it is by the judgment of the intellect because it is in intellectual matters, and each of them may be according to nature or according to position, such as the precedence of the head over the neck, the precedence of the imam over the follower, the precedence of the genus over the species, and the precedence of some sciences over others. And it is known that the precedence of the non-existence of an originated thing over its existence is only by time. The mutakallimun have denied the exclusivity and completeness of the induction. And they refuted it by the precedence of some parts of time over others, like the precedence of yesterday over today, for it is just as it is not by causality, nature, rank, or dignity, so too it is not by time, because yesterday and today are time, not something occurring in time.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a response (jawab) to the second proof presented in the previous lesson. The first proof (mabna al-awwal) is dismissed as already addressed. The second proof is based on the philosophers' claim that the types of precedence (taqaddum), posteriority (ta'akhkhur), and simultaneity (ma'iyya) are limited, by induction (istiqra'), to five: 1) by causality (bil-'illiyya), e.g., hand movement precedes key movement; 2) by nature (bil-tab'), e.g., part precedes whole; 3) by time (bil-zaman), e.g., father precedes son; 4) by dignity (bil-sharaf), e.g., teacher precedes student; 5) by rank (bil-rutba), which subdivides into sensory and intellectual, and each may be natural or conventional, with examples like head over neck, imam over follower, genus over species, etc. The philosophers argue that the precedence of non-existence over existence can only be temporal. The mutakallimun, however, reject the exclusivity (al-hasr) and completeness (tamam al-istiqra') of this classification. They refute it by giving a counterexample: the precedence of some parts of time over others, e.g., yesterday over today. Such precedence is not by causality, nature, rank, or dignity, and crucially, it is not by time either, because yesterday and today are time itself, not things occurring in time. Thus the philosophers' classification fails to account for this type, proving that the induction is incomplete.

Key Terms

بالطبع
بالزمان
بالشرف
بالرتبة
الحصر
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun reject the five types of precedence entirely.
Correction: They accept the types as valid but argue that the classification is not exhaustive.

Checkpoint
How do the mutakallimun refute the philosophers' claim that precedence is limited to five types?
A) By denying that any of the five types are valid.
B) By showing that the precedence of parts of time over one another does not fit any of the five, including time.
C) By arguing that the philosophers' induction is based on sense perception, which is unreliable.
D) By asserting that precedence of non-existence over existence is not temporal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' fivefold precedence classification is not exhaustive, as demonstrated by the precedence of parts of time over each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confidently assumed your own reasoning (like the philosophers' induction) was complete, only to realize later you had overlooked a crucial exception? How does this lesson warn you about the limits of human induction when it comes to matters of the Unseen?
?
The mutakallimun used a seemingly simple counterexample – yesterday before today – to dismantle a sophisticated philosophical framework. Is there an area of your faith where you have been overcomplicating what is actually a clear, simple truth? What would it mean for you to return to that simplicity today?
?
The philosophers built their argument on ‘induction’ – observing the world and generalizing. But the mutakallimun showed that induction fails when applied to time itself. How much of your daily certainty rests on patterns you have observed rather than on divine revelation? Could you humbly hold your convictions as possibly incomplete?

Two Opinions on Temporal Priority
Two opinions on the nature of temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on temporal priority and the distinction between priority that requires a time interval and priority that does not.

Core Idea
Temporal priority can exist without a measurable time interval.

This passage explores a subtle point in the philosophy of time: is all priority temporal? The author presents two scholarly opinions that distinguish between priority that involves a time gap and priority that does not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for arguments about the temporal nature of events and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument about temporal priority.
Notice that the passage begins by discussing a type of priority that does not require time.
2
Identify the two opinions
The first opinion divides temporal priority into two types; the second opinion restricts genuine temporal priority to parts of time only.
Contrast the father-son example (with time) with yesterday-today example (without time).
3
Analyze the key distinction
Understand that some forms of priority, like non-existence over existence, do not require a time interval. This challenges the idea that every event is preceded by time.
This has implications for arguments about the pre-eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزمان، فيكون من التقدم بغير العلية والطبع والرتبة والشرف تقدم لا يفتقر إلى زمان يقع فيه المتقدم والمتأخر، فيجوز أن يكون تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده من هذا القبيل. فلا يثبت كون كل حادث مسبوقاً بالزمان ولا يضرنا عن الزمان تسمية مثل هذا التقدم زمانياً على ما قال بعضهم : أن التقدم الزماني على وجهين : أحدهما : أن يكون المتقدم حاصلاً في زمان قبل زمان المتأخر كما بين الأب والابن. وثانيهما: أن يكون تحقق المتقدم قبل تحقق المتأخر من غير أن يكون في زمان كما بين الأمس واليوم . وقال بعضهم : إن التقدم الزماني بالحقيقة هو الذي بين أجزاء الزمان وإنما يعرض الغير بواسطته، إذ لا معنى لتقدم الأب على الابن، إلا تقدم زمانه على زمانه، حتى لو أريد بالتقدم الحقيقي، ما يستغني عن الواسطة لم يتناول هذا القسم
Time, so there is from priority without causality, nature, rank, and honor a priority that does not require a time in which the prior and posterior occur. So it is permissible that the priority of the non-existence of an event over its existence be of this kind. So it is not established that every event is preceded by time, and it does not harm us regarding time to call such priority temporal according to what some said: that temporal priority is of two types: one: that the prior occurs in a time before the time of the posterior, as between father and son. And the second: that the realization of the prior occurs before the realization of the posterior without being in time, as between yesterday and today. And some said: that temporal priority in reality is what is between parts of time, and it occurs to other things only by mediation, since there is no meaning to the priority of father over son except the priority of his time over his time, such that if what is meant by real priority is that which dispenses with mediation, it would not include this category.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents two opinions concerning the nature of temporal priority (al-taqaddum al-zamānī). The first opinion, attributed to some, holds that temporal priority can be of two types. The first type occurs when the prior entity exists in a time that is before the time of the posterior entity, as in the case of a father and his son. The second type occurs when the realization of the prior entity occurs before the realization of the posterior entity, but without there being a measurable time interval between them, as in the case of yesterday and today. The point is that some forms of priority do not require a stretch of time. The second opinion, attributed to others, states that genuine temporal priority is only that which exists between the parts of time itself. When we apply the term 'temporal priority' to other things, such as the priority of a father over his son, it is only by virtue of their relation to time: the father's time is prior to the son's time. Therefore, if one defines real priority as that which does not require a mediating factor, then this second category (the priority of father over son) would not be considered genuine temporal priority.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all priority involves time.
Correction: The passage shows there are forms of priority, like non-existence over existence, that do not require a time interval.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion mentioned, how many types of temporal priority are there?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The passage clarifies that temporal priority can be understood in two ways: with or without a time interval, and only the pure temporal priority between time parts is considered real by some scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says the non-existence of an event can be prior to its existence without any time gap. How does that affect your urgency to live purposefully today?
?
If true temporal priority only exists between parts of time, and your relationship with your parents is merely mediated priority, what does that say about the true nature of your connection to them? Does your gratitude hinge on time or on something more essential?
?
The passage undermines the assumption that every event is preceded by time. Think of the most significant event in your life: is your understanding of it tied to a timeline, or can you conceive of it as having a priority that transcends time? How does that change the weight you give to it now?

Reducing Priority in Rank and Honor to Time and Nature
The author reduces rank, honor, and temporal priority to nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how priority in rank, honor, and time are reducible to priority by nature, leaving only two genuine types of priority.

Core Idea
All apparent forms of priority ultimately depend on causality or nature.

This lesson presents a key argument from classical kalam: that many commonly recognized forms of priority—such as rank, honor, and time—are not independent but can be traced back to a more fundamental type: priority by nature. The author uses concrete examples to show how each reduces to time, and time itself reduces to nature. This sets the stage for understanding the two true priorities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the examples
The passage gives three examples: place, genus/species, and teacher/student. Notice how each is linked to time.
The author argues that the priority of a place is about the time of reaching it; genus priority is about the time of conceptualization; teacher priority is about precedence in gathering or starting.
2
Follow the reduction to time
Each example is argued to involve time, either directly (place) or indirectly (genus, teacher). Understand why the author claims they return to time 'with or without mediation'.
3
Understand why temporal priority reduces to nature
The author says the earlier part of time prepares for and is a condition for the later, like movement. This makes temporal priority a case of priority by nature.
The concept of 'nature' here refers to a non-causal but essential dependence.
4
Conclude: only two true priorities remain
After the reduction, the only genuine forms are priority by causality and priority by nature. The passage marks this conclusion explicitly.
This conclusion is the foundation for the next lesson's detailed discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحصر بعضهم التقدم في الذي بالعلية والذي بالطبع ذاهباً إلى أن التقدم بالرتبة والتقدم بالشرف، راجعان إلى الزمان لأن معنى تقدم مكان على آخر أن زمان الوصول إليه قبل زمان الوصول إلى الآخر، ومعنى تقدم الجنس على النوع أن زمان الأخذ، والشروع في ملاحظته قبل زمان الأخذ في النوع، ومعنى تقدم المعلم على المتعلم أن فيه صفة توجب تقدمه في المجلس أو في الشروع في الأمور، فيعود إلى الزمان بوسط أو بلا وسط وأن التقدم الزماني راجع إلى التقدم بالطبع، لأن السابق من الأجزاء المفروضة للزمان معد لوجود اللاحق وشرط له كالحركة، فالتقدم الحقيقي هو الذي بالعلية أو بالطبع والمعنى المشترك بينهما كون المتأخر محتاجاً في تحققه إلى المتقدم من غير احتياج للمتقدم إليه، إلا أن المتقدم في الذي بالعلية هو المفيد لوجود المتأخر، ولا كذلك في الذي بالطبع، والمعتبر هل هو العلة التامة أم الفاعلية؟ فيه تردد، فعلى الأول يكون المتقدم والمتأخر بالعلية متلازمين وجوداً وعدماً. وعلى الثاني: قد توجد ذات المتقدم بدون ذات المتأخر بأن ينتفي بعض شروط التأثر، والمتقدم بالطبع لا يستلزم المتأخر وجوداً بل عدماً، والمتأخر يستلزمه وجوداً لا عدماً، وأما بالنظر إلى وصفي التقدم والتأخر فبين كل متقدم ومتأخر تلازم وجوداً وعدماً، لكونهما متضايفين، لكن إذا اعتبرا من قسم واحد فإن تضايف المتقدم بالطبع مثفلا إنما هو مع المتأخر بالطبع لا بالعلية أو الزمان أو الرتبة أو الشرف، وعلى هذا قياس سائر الأقسام، والمعنى المشترك بين التقدم بالعلية، والتقدم بالطبع، قد يقال له التقدم بالذات، وقد يقال له التقدم بالطبع، ويخص ما بالعلية باسم الذاتي، ويسمى التقدم بالطبع تقدماً بالذات. بمعنى أن المتقدم مقوم محتاج إليه باعتبار الذات. والحقيقة دون مجرد الموجود كما في العلية، فإن ذات الاثنين لا تتم ولا تعقل بدون الواحد ولا خفاء في أن هذا إنما هو في الجزء دون الشرط، فالحكم ليس بكلي على ما يشعر به ظاهر عبارة المواقف.
And some restricted priority to that by causality and that by nature, going to the view that priority in rank and priority in honor are reducible to time, because the meaning of the priority of one place over another is that the time of reaching it is before the time of reaching the other, and the meaning of the priority of genus over species is that the time of taking and beginning to observe it is before the time of taking the species, and the meaning of the priority of teacher over student is that in him there is a quality that necessitates his precedence in the gathering or in beginning matters, so it returns to time with or without mediation, and that temporal priority is reducible to priority by nature, because the preceding among the supposed parts of time is a preparation for the existence of the subsequent and a condition for it, like movement. So true priority is that by causality or by nature, and the common meaning between them is that the posterior is in need of the prior in its realization, without the prior needing the posterior, except that the prior in that by causality is the one that benefits the existence of the posterior, and not so in that by nature. And the criterion: is it the complete cause or the efficient cause? There is hesitation. On the first, the prior and posterior by causality are mutually entailing in existence and non-existence. On the second, the essence of the prior may exist without the essence of the posterior, by some conditions of being affected being negated. And the prior by nature does not entail the posterior in existence, but in non-existence, and the posterior entails it in existence, not in non-existence. And as for considering the two descriptions of priority and posteriority, there is mutual entailment in existence and non-existence between every prior and posterior, because they are correlatives. But if they are considered from one category, then the correlativity of the prior by nature, for example [?], is only with the posterior by nature, not with causality or time or rank or honor. And upon this analogy all other categories. And the common meaning between priority by causality and priority by nature may be called priority by essence, and may be called priority by nature, and what is by causality is specified by the name 'essential', and priority by nature is called priority by essence, meaning that the prior is a constituent that is needed with respect to essence. And the reality is not merely existence, as in causality, for the essence of two does not become complete nor is it intelligible without the one. And it is not hidden that this is only in the part, not in the condition, so the judgment is not universal, as is indicated by the apparent wording of al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage restricts true priority to only two types: priority by causality (al-taqaddum bi al-‘illiyya) and priority by nature (al-taqaddum bi al-ṭab‘). The author, following the view of some, first argues that priority in rank (al-taqaddum bi al-rutba) and priority in honor (al-taqaddum bi al-sharaf) are reducible to temporal priority, which in turn is reducible to priority by nature. For example, the priority of one place over another is ultimately about the time of reaching it; the priority of genus over species is about the time of beginning to consider it; and the priority of teacher over student is about precedence in gathering or starting matters. These all involve time, either directly or indirectly. Then, temporal priority itself is reduced to priority by nature because the earlier part of time prepares for and is a condition for the later part, similar to how a movement prepares for a subsequent movement. Thus, the only genuine forms of priority are those where the posterior depends on the prior. In priority by causality, the prior is the cause that gives existence to the posterior, while in priority by nature, the prior does not give existence but is still necessary for the posterior in a non-causal sense. The author then addresses whether the criterion for such priority is the complete cause (al-‘illa al-tāmma) or merely the efficient cause (al-fā‘iliyya), noting a hesitation among scholars. This distinction has implications for whether the prior and posterior are mutually entailing: on the view of the complete cause, they entail each other's existence and non-existence; on the efficient cause view, the prior may exist without the posterior. Furthermore, the priority by nature does not entail the posterior in existence but in non-existence, while the posterior entails the prior in existence. The passage concludes by discussing the correlative nature of priority and posteriority, emphasizing that they must be within the same category. Finally, it introduces the term 'priority by essence' (al-taqaddum bi al-dhāt) and notes that it can apply to both causality and nature, but specifically to causality it is called 'essential', and to nature it is also called 'by essence' in the sense that the prior is a constituent that is needed for the essence of the posterior. The author notes that this applies to parts, not conditions, and that this is not universal, as indicated by al-Mawaqif.

Key Terms
التقدم بالطبع
التقدم بالزمان
التقدم بالرتبة
التقدم بالشرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That priority in rank, honor, and time are independent, fundamental types of priority.
Correction: The passage shows they are reducible to temporal priority, which in turn reduces to priority by nature. Hence they are not independent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the priority of a teacher over a student reduced to time?
A) The teacher is taller.
B) The teacher was born earlier.
C) The teacher has a quality that makes him precede in gathering or starting matters.
D) The teacher knows more.

Key Takeaway
All non-causal priorities reduce to time, and time reduces to nature, leaving only causality and nature as true priorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you consider 'higher in rank' or 'more honorable' than yourself. Can you identify the temporal circumstances—such as birth, opportunity, or social convention—that give rise to their priority? How might this realization humble your own desire for status?
?
You often attribute importance to being 'first' in time—first to arrive, first to speak, first to succeed. If such temporal priority is merely a natural condition (like a prior movement enabling a later one), does it still hold the weight you give it? Will you rethink your attachment to being 'first'?
?
When you feel slighted by someone who is 'above' you in rank, does it help to remember that their precedence is ultimately rooted in natural causes—not absolute worth? How can you respond with deeper recognition of divine causality and nature rather than resentment?

Causality and Created Things

Priority by Causality and Nature: Core Concepts
The author defines true priority, distinguishes causality from nature, debates the criterion, and discusses correlativity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common meaning of true priority, the key difference between causality and nature, the debate over complete vs efficient cause, and the correlative nature of priority and posteriority.

Core Idea
True priority means the later depends on the prior, but causality and nature differ in how the prior relates to the later.

Having established that only causality and nature are true priorities, the author now dives into their precise definitions and implications. He explains their shared core—the dependence of the posterior on the prior—and then highlights the crucial difference: in causality, the prior actively gives existence; in nature, it is merely a necessary condition. The lesson also examines a scholarly debate about whether the criterion is the complete cause or merely the efficient cause, and explores the logical entailments between prior and posterior.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the common meaning of true priority
The common meaning is that the posterior depends on the prior for its realization, without the reverse. This applies to both causality and nature.
Dependence is one-way.
2
Distinguish causality from nature
In causality, the prior gives existence to the posterior (e.g., a creator). In nature, the prior does not give existence but is still necessary (e.g., parts of time).
Note the asymmetry.
3
Analyze the complete cause vs efficient cause debate
If the complete cause is considered, prior and posterior are mutually entailing. If only the efficient cause, the prior can exist without the posterior. This has implications for necessity.
The author notes hesitation among scholars.
4
Understand entailment relations
Priority by nature does not entail the posterior's existence, only non-existence. But the posterior entails the prior's existence. Also, correlatives of priority and posteriority entail each other, but only within the same category.
5
Learn about 'priority by essence'
Both causality and nature can be called 'priority by essence', but causality is specifically called 'essential'. This means the prior is a constituent needed for the posterior's essence.
Applies to parts, not conditions, so not universal.

Seven Topics on Causality
Enumerates seven topics of the fifth methodology on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven key topics that make up the fifth methodology on causality in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Causality is explored through seven distinct but interconnected topics.

This passage serves as a roadmap for the fifth methodology, which deals with causality and effectuality. The author lists seven topics that will be examined in detail. Each topic addresses a fundamental question about the nature of cause and effect, agent and recipient, matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the numbered list of seven topics.
The Arabic uses 'الأول' through 'السابع' to enumerate.
2
Identify each topic
Go through each of the seven topics one by one. What is the main claim of each?
Topics range from definition to specific metaphysical rules.
3
Trace the logical sequence
Notice the progression: from definition (first), to necessity (second), unity (third), mutual exclusivity (fourth), bodily forces (fifth), impossibility of reciprocal causation (sixth), and matter-form relationship (seventh).
This order is not random; it builds from general to specific.
4
Consider the theological importance
Reflect on why these topics matter in Islamic theology. They address debates between theologians and philosophers about causation.
For example, the fourth topic challenges philosophical views on agent and recipient.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the seventh topic's content.
Use the passage directly to find the answer.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه مباحث :
الأول : في تعريف العلة
المنهج الخامس في العلية والمعلولية
الثاني : يجب وجود المعلول عند وجود الفاعل
الثالث : وحدة المعلول توجب وحدة الفاعل
الرابع : الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا عند الفلاسفة
الخامس : لا تأثير للقوى الجسمانية
السادس : يستحيل تراقي عروض العلة والمعلول
السابع : المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل
And within it are topics: First: On the definition of the cause. The fifth methodology on causality and effectuality. Second: The existence of the effect is necessary when the agent exists. Third: The unity of the effect necessitates the unity of the agent. Fourth: The one cannot be both receptive and active according to the philosophers. Fifth: There is no effect for bodily forces. Sixth: Reciprocal occurrence of cause and effect is impossible. Seventh: Matter for form is a locus, receptacle, and bearer.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven topics (mabāḥith) that fall under the fifth methodology (al-manhaj al-khāmis) concerning causality (al-illiyya) and effectuality (al-maʿlūliyya). The author lists them in order: First, the definition of the cause (al-illa). Second, the necessity of the effect (al-maʿlūl) existing when the agent (al-fāʿil) exists. Third, the unity of the effect necessitates the unity of the agent. Fourth, according to the philosophers (al-falāsifa), one and the same thing cannot be both receptive (qābil) and active (fāʿil). Fifth, bodily forces (al-quwā al-jismāniyya) have no effect (lā taʾthīr). Sixth, it is impossible for cause and effect to occur reciprocally (tarāqī ʿurūḍ). Seventh, matter (al-mādda) serves as a locus (maḥall), receptacle (qābil), and bearer (ḥāmil) for form (al-ṣūra). Each of these points is a distinct doctrinal issue that will be elaborated in subsequent sections.

The third topic says that unity of effect necessitates unity of agent. Are you consistent in seeing only one Source behind every event in your life? Where do you slip into seeing multiple independent causes? Commit now to one specific change in how you perceive daily events.

Causality and Created Things

Definition of Cause and Effect as Correlative Concepts
Al-Taftazani defines cause and effect as correlative rational considerations without external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that causality and effectuality are rational, correlative concepts with no external reality, and why they must involve two distinct things.

Core Idea
Cause and effect exist only in the mind, not in the world.

Al-Taftazani introduces the fifth methodology, focusing on causality and effectuality. He emphasizes that these are relational rational considerations, not independent realities. This lesson unpacks his definition and the logical reasoning behind it, including the necessity of involving two different things and the intermediate cause example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation to grasp the full meaning.
2
Understand the key concept
Cause and effect are correlative considerations (i'tibaran mutadha'ifan) – they exist only in relation to each other, not as independent properties.
3
Why no external existence?
If cause and effect had external reality, infinite regress would follow. They are secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thaniyah).
Think about why an actual causal chain cannot exist in the real world without regress.
4
The condition of two distinct things
Cause and effect cannot be in the same thing in the same respect; otherwise a thing would be prior and posterior to itself. They must be in relation to two different things.
The intermediate cause (al-'illah al-mutawassitah) illustrates this: it is a cause for its effect and an effect of its cause – two different relations.
5
Apply to the intermediate cause
Examine the intermediate cause example. Notice that the same thing can be both cause and effect, but under two different aspects.
This confirms the relational nature and avoids contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهج الخامس
العلية والمعلولية
قال : المنهج الخامس في العلية والمعلولية (١)، وهما اعتباران متضايفان (۲) لا يجتمعان في شيء إلا بالقياس إلى شيئين (۳)، وبيانهما في مباحث .
من لواحق الوجود والماهية العلية والمعلولية وهما من الاعتبارات العقلية، التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان، وإلا لزم التسلسل على ما مر غير مرة بل هما من المعقولات الثانية وبينهما تقابل التضايف، إذ العلة لا تكون إلا بالنسبة إلى المعلول وبالعكس، فلا يجتمعان في شيء واحد إلا باعتبارين، كالعلة المتوسطة التي هي علة لمعلولها، معلولة لعلتها .
(۱) أي في كون الشيء علة وكونه معلولاً .
(۲) لا وجود لهما خارجاً ومعنى كونهما متضايفين أن كون الشيء علة لا يعقل إلا بالإضافة إلى كون شيء آخر معلولاً له والعكس .
(۳) متباينين إذ لو كان الشيء علة لشيء ومعلولاً لذلك الشيء، والعلة أصل والمعلول فرع والأصل مقدم على الفرع لزم كون الشيء متقدماً ومتأخراً عن نفسه بخلاف ما إذا كان علة لشيء ومعلولا لشيء آخر، فإنما يلزم تقدمه عن معلوله وتأخره عن علته التي هي غير ذلك المعلول .
ولا استحالة فيه
The Fifth Methodology: On Causality and Effectuality. He said: The fifth methodology is on causality and effectuality (1), and they are two correlative considerations (2) that do not coexist in one thing except in relation to two (different) things (3). Their explanation is in sections. From the concomitants of existence and quiddity are causality and effectuality, and they are among the rational considerations which have no realization in external entities; otherwise, infinite regress would follow, as has passed more than once. Rather, they are from the secondary intelligibles, and between them is the correlation of correlativity, for the cause is only in relation to the effect and vice versa. Thus they do not coexist in one thing except under two aspects, like the intermediate cause which is a cause for its effect and an effect of its cause. (1) That is, in something's being a cause and its being an effect. (2) They have no external existence, and the meaning of their being correlative is that something's being a cause is not conceived except in addition to something else being an effect for it, and vice versa. (3) Two distinct things, for if something were a cause for something and an effect of that same thing – while the cause is original and the effect is derivative, and the original is prior to the derivative – then it would necessitate that something be prior and posterior to itself. Contrary to if it is a cause for something and an effect of something else, for then it would only necessitate its priority over its effect and its posteriority to its cause, which is other than that effect. And there is no impossibility in that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines causality (al-illiyyah) and effectuality (al-ma'luliyyah) as two correlative considerations (i'tibaran mutadha'ifan). The author first states that they are relational: they cannot coexist in one thing except with respect to two different things, as they are relative concepts. He then explains that they are among the rational considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyyah) that have no realization in external existence (a'yan), otherwise an infinite regress would follow. They are secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thaniyah) that exist only in the mind. He emphasizes the correlation: a cause is only a cause relative to its effect, and vice versa. Therefore, they cannot be united in a single thing except under two different aspects, as in the case of an intermediate cause (al-'illah al-mutawassitah) which is a cause for its effect and an effect of its cause. The footnotes further clarify: (1) specifies the meaning of the terms, (2) explains that they have no external existence and the nature of their correlation, (3) elaborates that if cause and effect were in the same respect, it would lead to a thing being prior and posterior to itself, which is impossible; but when the cause and effect are in relation to different things, no contradiction arises.

معلول
تضايف
اعتبارات عقلية
معقولات ثانية
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect are real properties existing independently in objects.
Correction: They are purely rational correlations with no external reality; they only exist as mental considerations.

Checkpoint
Why must cause and effect be in relation to two different things rather than the same thing?
A) Because otherwise the thing would be prior and posterior to itself in the same respect.
B) Because they do not exist in the external world.
C) Because they are secondary intelligibles.
D) Because of the principle of infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Cause and effect are not real properties but rational correlations that require two distinct relata.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If cause and effect are merely mental considerations, then the only true cause is God. In what areas of your life do you still attribute power or effect to created things as if they have independent causality? How can you actively shift that reliance to God alone today?
?
The intermediate cause is both a cause and an effect under different aspects. In your own life, you are both a cause (influencing others) and an effect (dependent on God and others). How does this realization humble your pride when you succeed and console your despair when you fail?
?
This lesson says that if cause and effect had external existence, it would lead to infinite regress. Have you ever felt that your problems or blessings are caused by an endless chain of worldly factors? How can remembering that all causes are mental considerations—and that God is the only real cause—free you from anxiety and deepen your trust in Him?

Defining 'Illa (Cause)
Author defines 'illa and its customary usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of 'illa as both that which a thing depends on and that from which it proceeds.

Core Idea
The cause is both the necessary support and the source of a thing.

This lesson unpacks the opening lines of the section, where the author establishes the basic meaning of 'illa. We will see how the term carries both a general sense of dependence and a more specific customary usage referring to the source of a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note that the author defines 'illa as 'what the thing needs' but then mentions that its usage typically refers to 'that from which the thing proceeds.'
Pay attention to the shift from general need to specific source.
2
Understand the two aspects
Recognize that 'illa has two related meanings: the condition for existence (need) and the agent of existence (source). The latter is the common usage in philosophical discussions.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this distinction matters: it sets the stage for dividing causes into types based on their relation to the thing caused.
This is a foundational concept for the rest of the section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
تعريف العلة
المادة
:(قال): المبحث الأول : العلة (۱) ما يحتاج إليه الشيء (۲)، وإن كان إطلاقها ينصرف إلى ما يصدر عنه الشيء، ثم إن كانت داخلة في الشيء، فوجوبه معها، إما بالفعل ،فصورية، وإما بالقوة فمادية، ويدخل فيهما الجزء من الصورة (۳) والمادة، وبذكر الوجوب يندفع أن الوجود قد يكون مع بالفعل لا بالقوة، إلا أنه يرد الجزء الغير الأخير من الصورة جمعاً منعاً، إن كانت خارجة، فالشيء إما بها ،ففاعلية أو لها فغائية، ويقال للأولين علة الماهية وللآخرين علة الوجود ومرجع الشروط والآلات إلى الفاعل، ومن الشروط ما هو عدمي كزوال المانع ، ولا يستحيل دخوله في علة الوجود بمعنى أن العقل، إذا لاحظ وجود المعلول، لم يجده حاصلاً بدونه .
الحادث من
وقد يقال : إنه في التحقيق كاشف عن شرط وجودي كزوال الرطوبة لاحتراق الخشبة ينبيء عن اليبوسة التي هي الشرط وليس عدم مباديه إلا بالعرض.

(۱) العلة في اللغة: عرض مؤدي للبنية الجسمية ولما كانت بهذا المعنى سبباً في تأثير الجسم بالأذى وفي اتصافه بذلك الأذى نقلت عرفاً لمطلق ما يكون سبباً في تحقق الشيء فهو عرفاً .
(۲) سقط من (أ) و (ب) لفظ (الشيء).
(۳) إذ لا يجب حصوله مع ذلك الجزء لصحة حصوله بلا وجود للمعلول وذلك كجزء صورة السرير المقصود حصوله على التربيع مثلاً فإن أحد أضلاعه الأربعة جزء من مجموع ما تحصل به صورته ولا يجب معه السرير لصحة حصوله مع التثليث ولا يجب السرير على ذلك التقدير حتى يحصل مجموع التربيع فيجب بالفعل.
First section: Definition of the cause. (Author) said: The first section: The cause (1) is that which the thing (2) needs, although its absolute usage refers to that from which the thing proceeds. Then, if it is internal to the thing, its existence (or necessity) is with it; either actually, so it is formal; or potentially, so it is material. And included in these two are the part of the form (3) and the matter. By mentioning existence (or necessity), it is refuted that existence may be with actuality and not potentiality, except that the non-final part of the form is rejected as a comprehensive prevention. If it is external, then the thing exists either by it, so it is efficient; or for it, so it is final. The first two are called the cause of quiddity, and the last two the cause of existence. The conditions and instruments are referred back to the efficient cause. Among conditions is what is privative, like the removal of the impediment; it is not impossible for it to enter into the cause of existence in the sense that the intellect, when it observes the existence of the effect, does not find it occurring without it. The contingent from [???] And it may be said: In reality it is indicative of an existential condition, like the removal of moisture for the burning of wood indicates the dryness which is the condition; the absence of its principles is only accidental. (1) The cause in language: an accident leading to the bodily structure; and since it is in this sense a cause of the body's being affected by harm and of its being described by that harm, it was transferred customarily to any absolute cause of the realization of a thing; so it is customarily [defined as such]. (2) The word 'the thing' is missing from copies (A) and (B). (3) Because its occurrence is not necessary with that part due to the validity of its occurrence without the existence of the effect, such as a part of the form of a bed intended to be made on a square shape; for one of its four sides is a part of the totality by which its form is obtained, and the bed is not necessary with it due to the validity of its occurrence with a triangle, and the bed is not necessary on that estimation until the totality of the square is obtained; then it becomes necessary actually.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'illa (cause) and divides it into types. The author begins by stating that the cause is that which a thing depends on, but its customary usage refers to that from which the thing proceeds (i.e., the source). He then classifies cause based on whether it is internal or external to the thing. Internal causes are either actual (formal) or potential (material). Formal cause is the actual essence that makes the thing what it is; material cause is the potential substrate. Included in these are the part of the form and the matter. The author notes that by mentioning 'wujub' (existence/necessity) we avoid the misconception that existence must always be actual and not potential; however, a non-final part of the form cannot count as a proper cause. External causes are either efficient (by which the thing is brought about) or final (for the sake of which the thing exists). The first two (formal and material) are called causes of quiddity (mahiyya); the latter two (efficient and final) are called causes of existence (wujud). Conditions and instruments are traced back to the efficient cause. Among conditions is the privative condition, like the removal of an impediment, which can be considered part of the cause of existence in the sense that the intellect does not find the effect occurring without it. The author then discusses a contingent example and explains that some conditions are existential, such as dryness for burning, where the removal of moisture indicates the presence of dryness. The footnotes provide additional clarification: the linguistic origin of 'illa, a textual variant, and an example for the part of the form not being a sufficient cause.

Key Terms
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'illa only means 'cause' in the sense of a producing agent, ignoring the dependency aspect.
Correction: The passage shows that 'illa includes both dependency (need) and origination (source), though customary emphasis is on the latter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the absolute usage of 'illa refer to?
A) That which the thing needs
B) That from which the thing proceeds
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The term 'illa encompasses both dependence and origination, but its conventional meaning is the source of a thing's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a cause is both what something depends on and what it comes from. In your own life, do you treat Allah as merely the origin of your existence, or do you also depend on Him every moment for your breath, your faith, and your next heartbeat? When was the last time this dependence actually made you cry out to Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between the broad meaning of 'illa (necessary support) and its customary focus (origin). Which meaning do you habitually reduce Allah to—just the One who started everything? What would change if you began your day remembering that you need Him not only for your beginning but for your very continuation?
?
If 'illa includes both need and emanation, then nothing in creation is self-sufficient. Name one area of your life where you still act as if you are the cause of your own success or righteousness. What would it look like to truly attribute that to Allah as both the sustainer and the source?

Internal Causes: Formal and Material
Classification of internal causes into formal and material, with clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between formal (actual) and material (potential) causes as internal causes, and the condition of actual necessity.

Core Idea
Internal causes are either actual (formal) or potential (material), and only the complete whole has actual necessity.

This lesson focuses on the internal causes, those that are within the thing itself. The author explains that internal causes are either actual (formal) or potential (material), and clarifies that the existence of a part does not imply the existence of the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the internal cause classification
Read the Arabic text from 'then if it is internal' to 'as a comprehensive prevention'. Identify the two types: formal (actual) and material (potential).
Note the phrase 'its existence (or necessity) is with it'.
2
Understand formal and material
Formal cause is the actual essence that makes the thing what it is; material cause is the potential substrate. They are both within the thing.
The author mentions that parts of form and matter are included.
3
Examine the exception
The passage notes that a non-final part of the form cannot be considered a cause that necessitates the existence of the whole. Consider the example of a bed: one side does not necessitate the bed.
This illustrates that only the complete set of parts achieves actual necessity.

The author begins by stating that the cause is that which a thing depends on, but its customary usage refers to that from which the thing proceeds (i.e., the source). He then classifies cause based on whether it is internal or external to the thing. Internal causes are either actual (formal) or potential (material). Formal cause is the actual essence that makes the thing what it is; material cause is the potential substrate. Included in these are the part of the form and the matter. The author notes that by mentioning 'wujub' (existence/necessity) we avoid the misconception that existence must always be actual and not potential; however, a non-final part of the form cannot count as a proper cause. External causes are either efficient (by which the thing is brought about) or final (for the sake of which the thing exists). The first two (formal and material) are called causes of quiddity (mahiyya); the latter two (efficient and final) are called causes of existence (wujud). Conditions and instruments are traced back to the efficient cause. Among conditions is the privative condition, like the removal of an impediment, which can be considered part of the cause of existence in the sense that the intellect does not find the effect occurring without it. The author then discusses a contingent example and explains that some conditions are existential, such as dryness for burning, where the removal of moisture indicates the presence of dryness. The footnotes provide additional clarification: the linguistic origin of 'illa, a textual variant, and an example for the part of the form not being a sufficient cause.

Key Terms
صورية
مادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any part of a form is enough to cause the existence of the thing.
Correction: Only the final or complete set of parts achieves actual necessity; a partial form does not suffice.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an internal cause according to this passage?
A) Formal cause
B) Material cause
C) Efficient cause
D) Part of the form

Key Takeaway
Internal causes are formal (actual) and material (potential), and only the complete form necessitates the thing's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the causes behind your own existence and actions, do you tend to focus on a single 'formal' reason (like your intention) while ignoring the 'material' potentialities (like your circumstances)? How might ignoring the whole cause distort your self-understanding?
?
If only the complete whole has actual necessity, what does that imply about your own incomplete efforts? Are there areas in your life where you expect a result from a partial cause? How will you adjust your expectations and actions to seek completeness?
?
The lesson warns against mistaking a part for the whole cause. In your relationship with God, do you sometimes treat a single act of worship as sufficient while neglecting the whole of your spiritual life? What would it mean to have a 'complete' internal cause in your faith?

External Causes: Efficient and Final
Classification of external causes and the distinction between cause of quiddity and cause of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of external causes into efficient and final, and how they relate to cause of quiddity and cause of existence.

Core Idea
External causes are either by which (efficient) or for which (final), and together with internal causes they form two pairs: cause of quiddity and cause of existence.

This lesson covers the external causes: efficient (by which the thing is made) and final (for which the thing is made). The author also introduces the important division into causes of quiddity (internal) and causes of existence (external).

Guided Path
1
Read the external cause classification
Read the Arabic text from 'if it is external' to 'cause of existence'. Identify the two external causes: efficient (fā'iliyya) and final (ghā'iyya).
Note that the thing exists 'by' the efficient cause and 'for' the final cause.
2
Understand cause of quiddity vs. existence
The author says the first two (formal and material) are called cause of quiddity, and the last two (efficient and final) are called cause of existence. Grasp why internal causes define the essence (quiddity) and external causes bring it into existence.
This is a key conceptual distinction in Islamic philosophy.
3
Reflect on the pairing
Consider why this pairing matters: it shows that causes are not just four separate kinds but two complementary pairs, each explaining a different aspect of a thing.

Source Text

:(قال): المبحث الأول : العلة (۱) ما يحتاج إليه الشيء (۲)، وإن كان إطلاقها ينصرف إلى ما يصدر عنه الشيء، ثم إن كانت داخلة في الشيء، فوجوبه معها، إما بالفعل ،فصورية، وإما بالقوة فمادية، ويدخل فيهما الجزء من الصورة (۳) والمادة، وبذكر الوجوب يندفع أن الوجود قد يكون مع بالفعل لا بالقوة، إلا أنه يرد الجزء الغير الأخير من الصورة جمعاً منعاً، إن كانت خارجة، فالشيء إما بها ،ففاعلية أو لها فغائية، ويقال للأولين علة الماهية وللآخرين علة الوجود ومرجع الشروط والآلات إلى الفاعل، ومن الشروط ما هو عدمي كزوال المانع ، ولا يستحيل دخوله في علة الوجود بمعنى أن العقل، إذا لاحظ وجود المعلول، لم يجده حاصلاً بدونه .

(۱) العلة في اللغة: عرض مؤدي للبنية الجسمية ولما كانت بهذا المعنى سبباً في تأثير الجسم بالأذى وفي اتصافه بذلك الأذى نقلت عرفاً لمطلق ما يكون سبباً في تحقق الشيء فهو عرفاً .
(۲) سقط من (أ) و (ب) لفظ (الشيء).
(۳) إذ لا يجب حصوله مع ذلك الجزء لصحة حصوله بلا وجود للمعلول وذلك كجزء صورة السرير المقصود حصوله على التربيع مثلاً فإن أحد أضلاعه الأربعة جزء من مجموع ما تحصل به صورته ولا يجب معه السرير لصحة حصوله مع التثليث ولا يجب السرير على ذلك التقدير حتى يحصل مجموع التربيع فيجب بالفعل.
First section: Definition of the cause. (Author) said: The first section: The cause (1) is that which the thing (2) needs, although its absolute usage refers to that from which the thing proceeds. Then, if it is internal to the thing, its existence (or necessity) is with it; either actually, so it is formal; or potentially, so it is material. And included in these two are the part of the form (3) and the matter. By mentioning existence (or necessity), it is refuted that existence may be with actuality and not potentiality, except that the non-final part of the form is rejected as a comprehensive prevention. If it is external, then the thing exists either by it, so it is efficient; or for it, so it is final. The first two are called the cause of quiddity, and the last two the cause of existence. The conditions and instruments are referred back to the efficient cause. Among conditions is what is privative, like the removal of the impediment; it is not impossible for it to enter into the cause of existence in the sense that the intellect, when it observes the existence of the effect, does not find it occurring without it. The contingent from [???] And it may be said: In reality it is indicative of an existential condition, like the removal of moisture for the burning of wood indicates the dryness which is the condition; the absence of its principles is only accidental. (1) The cause in language: an accident leading to the bodily structure; and since it is in this sense a cause of the body's being affected by harm and of its being described by that harm, it was transferred customarily to any absolute cause of the realization of a thing; so it is customarily [defined as such]. (2) The word 'the thing' is missing from copies (A) and (B). (3) Because its occurrence is not necessary with that part due to the validity of its occurrence without the existence of the effect, such as a part of the form of a bed intended to be made on a square shape; for one of its four sides is a part of the totality by which its form is obtained, and the bed is not necessary with it due to the validity of its occurrence with a triangle, and the bed is not necessary on that estimation until the totality of the square is obtained; then it becomes necessary actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Can you identify one habit where you are neglecting the final cause—pleasing Allah—and instead acting for a fleeting efficient cause? ?

Do you live as if your existence is a gift from your Creator, or do you behave as if your essence alone makes you self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that your existence is not self-given? Pick one area of life (e.g., wealth, health, relationships) and consciously affirm its existence from Allah.

Conditions and Privative Conditions
Explanation of conditions, especially privative conditions, and their relation to causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of conditions (including privative ones) in causality, how they relate to the efficient cause, and the example of burning wood.

Core Idea
Privative conditions, like removal of an impediment, are valid parts of the causal framework and indicate existential conditions.

This lesson focuses on conditions (shurūṭ) and instruments, tracing them back to the efficient cause. The author explains that even privative conditions—such as the absence of an impediment—can be considered part of the cause of existence, and illustrates with the example of burning wood.

Guided Path
1
Read the section on conditions
Read the Arabic text from 'the conditions and instruments' to the end. Identify the statement that conditions are referred back to the efficient cause.
2
Understand privative conditions
The author mentions 'among conditions is what is privative, like the removal of the impediment'. Understand that this is a condition by absence, not presence.
He argues it is not impossible for such a privative to enter into the cause of existence.
3
Analyze the example
The example of burning wood: removal of moisture indicates the presence of dryness, which is an existential condition. The privative condition (removal of moisture) points to a positive condition (dryness).
This shows that privative conditions are not real causes but indicators.

The Four Causes: Definition and Classification
Defines cause and effect and classifies causes into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general definition of cause and effect, the classification of causes into internal (material, formal) and external (efficient, final), and why they are grouped as causes of the essence vs. causes of existence.

Core Idea
Causes are either internal to essence or external; internal define what a thing is, external bring it into being.

This passage from al-Sanusi's theological primer lays the foundation for understanding causality in Islamic thought. Before discussing how God acts in the world, we need a precise vocabulary for what a cause is and how it relates to its effect. The author defines cause generally and then classifies causes into four Aristotelian types, grouping them into two fundamental pairs: causes of the essence and causes of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source Arabic once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own definitions and structure come through before you analyse.
Notice that the passage opens with a general definition of cause and effect.
2
Identify the two main divisions and the four causes
Find where the passage divides causes into internal (formal, material) and external (efficient, final). Label each cause based on the description given.
The formal cause is 'in act', material cause is 'in potentiality'.
3
Understand the grouping into 'cause of essence' and 'cause of existence'
The passage explains that material and formal causes are called 'cause of the essence' because the thing depends on them for both essence and existence, while efficient and final causes are 'cause of existence' because the thing depends on them only for existence.
Think of essence as 'what it is' and existence as 'that it is'.
4
Reflect on why this classification matters
This taxonomy helps distinguish between what makes a thing what it is (internal principles) and what brings it into being (external agents and purposes). In theology, this will be used to discuss God's relationship to creation.
The efficient cause here corresponds to the agent (fāʿil), which is the primary meaning of 'cause' in everyday usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يراد بالعلة ما يحتاج إليه الشيء. وبالمعلول ما يحتاج إلى الشيء، وإن كانت العلة عند إطلاقها منصرفة إلى الفاعل، وهو ما يصدر عنه الشيء بالاستقلال، أو بانضمام الغير إليه ، ثم علة الشيء أعني ما يحتاج هو إليه إما أن تكون داخلة فيه أو خارجة عنه فإن كانت داخله فوجوب الشيء معها، إما بالفعل وهي العلة الصورية، وإما بالقوة وهي العلة المادية، وإن كانت خارجة عن الشيء. فإما أن يكون الشيء بها وهي العلة الفاعلية، أو لأجلها العلة الغائية، ويخص الأوليان أعني المادية والصورية باسم علة الماهية ، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في ماهيته ، كما في وجوده ، ولذا لا يعقل إلا بهما، أو بما ينتزع عنهما كالجنس والفصل، ويخص الآخر بأنه أعني الفاعلية والغائية باسم علة الوجود، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في الوجود فقط، ولذا يعقل بدونهما.
Cause may be meant as that which the thing has need of. And effect as that which has need of the thing, even though cause when it is used unrestrictedly is diverted to the agent, which is that from which the thing issues either independently or by the joining of another. Then the cause of a thing, I mean that which it needs, is either internal to it or external to it. If it is internal, then the obligation of the thing is with it, either actually—which is the formal cause—or potentially—which is the material cause. And if it is external to the thing, then either the thing is through it—which is the efficient cause—or for its sake—which is the final cause. And the first two, namely the material and formal, are specified with the name 'cause of the essence' (ʿillat al-māhiyya), because the thing depends on them in its essence, just as in its existence, and therefore it is not conceived except through them, or through what is derived from them like genus and differentia. And the other two, namely the efficient and final, are specified with the name 'cause of existence' (ʿillat al-wujūd), because the thing depends on them in existence only, and therefore it is conceived without them.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'cause' (ʿilla) and 'effect' (maʿlūl) and then classifies causes into four types. It begins by noting that the term 'cause' is often diverted to the agent (fāʿil), but here it is used more generally. The author then states that a thing's cause is either internal or external. Internal causes are either formal (ṣūriyya) – the cause in act – or material (mādiyya) – the cause in potentiality. External causes are either efficient (fāʿiliyya) – that through which the thing is – or final (ghāʾiyya) – that for the sake of which the thing is. The material and formal causes are called 'cause of the essence' (ʿillat al-māhiyya) because the thing depends on them both in its essence and in its existence, so it is inconceivable without them or their derivatives like genus and differentia. The efficient and final causes are called 'cause of existence' (ʿillat al-wujūd) because the thing depends on them only for its existence, and can be conceived without them.

معلول
علة صورية
علة مادية
علة فاعلية
علة غائية
علة الماهية
علة الوجود
جنس
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think the term 'cause' always refers to the agent (efficient cause) and ignore other types.
Correction: In this passage, 'cause' is defined broadly as what a thing needs; it includes internal (material, formal) and external (efficient, final) causes, though common usage often narrows it to the agent.

Checkpoint
Which pair of causes is called 'cause of the essence' (ʿillat al-māhiyya)?
A) Efficient and final
B) Material and formal
C) Material and efficient
D) Formal and final

Key Takeaway
Material and formal causes constitute the essence of a thing, while efficient and final causes bring it into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that your essence (what you are) is defined by material and formal causes, while your existence (that you are) comes from efficient and final causes. Which of these aspects do you tend to neglect in your daily self-reflection, and how might that lead you to live without purpose?
?
Consider the efficient cause of your own existence—ultimately, Allah. How does acknowledging that you are brought into being by an external Agent change the way you view your own autonomy and responsibility?
?
Every action you take has a final cause—a purpose for which you do it. Are your daily actions driven by a conscious final cause, or merely by immediate efficient triggers? This lesson forces you to ask: what is the ultimate final cause that gives coherence to your life?

Inductive Basis of Causal Division and Status of Conditions
Explains the inductive basis of the fourfold division and refutes including conditions in material cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourfold division of causes is based on induction, and why conditions are not parts of the material cause.

Core Idea
The division of causes rests on induction, not proof; conditions are external to the effect.

Sanusi completes his discussion of causality by addressing the basis for the fourfold division and a common confusion about conditions. He explains that the restriction of external causes to efficient and final is founded on induction, not demonstrative proof. He then critiques al-Ghazālī's view that conditions are parts of the material cause, arguing that conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the inductive basis
Note that there is no logical proof confining external causes to efficient and final; the division is accepted based on induction (istiqrāʾ). This is the foundation of the fourfold causal classification.
Induction here means empirical survey, not a priori reasoning.
2
See how conditions and instruments are included
Conditions (shurūṭ) and instruments (ālāt) are included under the efficient cause because they revert to 'that by which the thing exists' (mā bihi al-shayʾ). This clarifies their place in the scheme.
3
Refute al-Ghazālī's position on conditions
Al-Ghazālī considered conditions as parts of the material cause, arguing that the recipient is only receptive with them. Sanusi rejects this because conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal. Even al-Ghazālī himself admitted the material cause is internal.
This shows careful attention to consistency in causal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتمام هذا الكلام ببيان أمور أن ما لا ذكر في بيان الحصر، وجه ضبط لأنه لا دليل على انحصار الخارج فيما بـه الشيء، وما لأجله الشيء سوى الاستقراء أن المراد بالصورية والمادية وما ينسب إليهما من الأجزاء لصدق التعريف عليها وكذا في الفاعلية والغائية، وبهذا الاعتبار يندرج الشروط والآلات في الأقسام لكونها راجعة إلى ما به الشيء وما ذهب إليه الإمام من أن الشروط من أجزاء العلة المادية، بناء على أن القابل إنما يكون قابلاً بالفعل معها ليس بمستقيم، لأنها خارجة عن المعلوم، وقد صرح هو أيضاً بأن المادية داخلة
And the completion of this discussion is by explaining matters: that which was not mentioned in the explanation of the division, and the manner of its control, because there is no proof for the restriction of the external to that by which the thing [exists] and that for which the thing [exists] other than induction. That what is meant by the formal and material and what pertains to them of the parts is due to the truth of the definition applying to them, and likewise in the efficient and final. And by this consideration, conditions and instruments are included in the divisions because they revert to 'that by which the thing [exists]'. And what the Imām [al-Ghazālī] held, that conditions are parts of the material cause, based on the idea that the recipient is only actually receptive with them, is not sound, because they are external to the effect, and he himself also explicitly stated that the material cause is internal.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses the completeness of the classification of external causes into efficient and final. The author notes that there is no proof (dalīl) for this restriction except induction (istiqrāʾ). He then explains that through this consideration, conditions (shurūṭ) and instruments (ālāt) are included under the efficient cause because they revert to 'that by which the thing exists' (mā bihi al-shayʾ). The author criticizes the view of Imam al-Ghazālī that conditions are parts of the material cause, arguing that since conditions are external to the effect, they cannot be material causes, which are internal. He further points out that al-Ghazālī himself admitted that the material cause is internal.

Key Terms

ما به الشيء
ما لأجله الشيء
شرط
آلة
علة مادية
الإمام
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conditions are parts of the material cause because they are necessary for the recipient to be receptive.
Correction: Conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal. Therefore conditions cannot be parts of the material cause, even though they are necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are conditions not parts of the material cause?
A) Because they are internal to the effect
B) Because they are included under efficient cause
C) Because they are external to the effect
D) Because there is no proof for the division

Key Takeaway
The division of external causes into efficient and final is based on induction, not a priori proof, and conditions are external to the effect, thus not part of the material cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about why things happen in your life, do you base your understanding on clear proof or on repeated experience? Could you be confusing inductive patterns with necessary causes? Will you take time today to examine one belief about how something works and ask whether it is based on proof or just habit?
?
The author corrects a respected scholar's mistake about conditions and material cause. Are you willing to be corrected when your reasoning about cause and effect is flawed? Recall a time you had to change your view because of evidence. How did it feel? Are you open to such changes now?
?
Conditions are external, yet we often treat them as essential. In your own journey of faith, have you confused external requirements (like certain circumstances) with the internal reality of your relationship with Allah? What is one condition you think you need to be a better Muslim? Is it truly necessary, or is it external? How will you distinguish the two from now on?
