Obligation vs Existence in Causal Analysis
Argues for obligation over existence in considering actuality/potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that actuality and potentiality should be considered in terms of obligation (wujūb) rather than existence (wujūd), and how this avoids logical contradictions in definitions of matter and form.

Core Idea
Obligation, not existence, preserves the logical precision of matter and form.

This passage delves into a technical debate about where to apply the concepts of actuality and potentiality in causal analysis. The author, following Ibn Sina, argues that considering these in 'obligation' is superior to considering them in 'existence,' as the majority do. He shows that using existence leads to contradictions in the definitions of matter and form, while obligation avoids them. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal English translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to terms like فعلیة and قوة.
2
Identify the two competing views
The author compares two approaches: considering actuality/potentiality in existence (view of the majority) versus in obligation (view of Ibn Sina followed by the author). Trace how each leads to different results.
3
Follow the objection and response
The passage includes an objection about the non-last part of a composite form. Understand the objection and how the author responds. Notice the final clarification about potentiality not being mere possibility.
This is a subtle point that many students misunderstand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ما ذكرنا من اعتبار الفعل والقوة في الوجوب، وهو الموافق لكلام ابن سينا أولى من اعتباره في الوجود على ما ذكره الجمهور، لأن المادة إذا لحقها الصورة يكون وجود المعلول معها بالفعل لا بالقوة، فيدخل في تعريف الصورة، فلا يكون مانعاً، ويخرج من تعريف المادة فلا يكون جامعاً بخلاف الوجوب، فإنه بالنظر إلى المادة لا يكون إلا بالقوة وبالنظر إلى الصورة لا يكون إلا بالفعل. وكان مرادهم أن الصورة ما يكون وجوداً لشيء معه بالفعل البتة والمادة ما يكون الوجود معه بالقوة في الجملة، وحينئذ لا انتقاض أن الجزء الغير الأخير من الصورة المركبة، يكون وجوب المعلول معه بالقوة لا بالفعل، فيدخل في تعريف المادية، ويخرج عن تعريف الصورة، فينتقض التعريفان جمعاً ومنعاً، ولا يجوز أن يراد بالقوة الإمكاني بحيث لا ينافي الفعل ، لأن الفساد حينئذ أظهر
That which we mentioned of considering actuality and potentiality in obligation (wujūb) - and it agrees with the statement of Ibn Sīnā - is more appropriate than considering it in existence (wujūd) as the majority stated, because when form attaches to matter, the existence of the effect with it is actual not potential, so it enters the definition of form and thus is not a preventer, and it exits the definition of matter and thus is not a unifier, contrary to obligation, for with respect to matter it is only potential and with respect to form it is only actual. And their intent was that form is that with which the existence of something is actually present, and matter is that with which existence is potentially present in general, and therefore there is no breach: the non-last part of a composite form: the obligation of the effect with it is potential not actual, so it enters the definition of materiality and exits the definition of form, and thus both definitions are breached collectively and preventively. And it is not permissible that potentiality be meant as possibility such that it does not conflict with actuality, because the corruption would then be more evident.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about whether actuality (fiʿl) and potentiality (quwwa) should be considered in terms of obligation (wujūb) or existence (wujūd). The author, following Ibn Sīnā, argues that obligation is preferable. He explains that if we use existence, when form joins matter, the effect's existence becomes actual, making it fit the definition of form and not matter, causing both definitions to fail. Using obligation avoids this: with matter, obligation is potential; with form, it is actual. Then an objection is addressed: the non-last part of a composite form – the obligation with it is potential, so it would seem to fall under materiality, not formality. The author replies that this does not cause a breach because the definitions as intended by the majority avoid this. He further clarifies that potentiality here is not mere possibility (imkān) that can coexist with actuality, because that would cause even more corruption.

Key Terms
فعل
قوة
وجوب
وجود
مادة
صورة
جزء غير أخير
إمكاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that potentiality (quwwa) in matter means mere possibility (imkān) that can coexist with actuality.
Correction: The author warns that potentiality in this context is not mere possibility; it is a genuine privation of actuality. If it were mere possibility, the distinction between matter and form would collapse.

Checkpoint
Why does the author prefer considering actuality and potentiality in 'obligation' rather than 'existence'?
A) Because existence is irrelevant to causation
B) Because using existence would make matter actual when form attaches, contradicting its definition
C) Because Ibn Sīnā said so
D) Because obligation is a more precise term

Key Takeaway
The author argues that obligation provides a consistent logical framework for analyzing causality without contradicting the definitions of matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own potential, do you treat it as a mere possibility that can coexist with inaction, or as a real capacity that demands actualization? How does this lesson's warning against confusing potentiality with possibility challenge the way you approach your own growth?
?
The author argues that obligation, not existence, provides a consistent framework for analyzing causality. In which areas of your life do you instinctively prioritize 'what exists' over 'what must be'? Will you reconsider the deeper necessities behind your actions?
?
This lesson uses a technical distinction to guard against logical corruption. When you reason about the world, are you careful to define your terms precisely, or do you accept fuzzy concepts? How might neglecting logical precision lead you away from truth?

Genus and Matter: Critiquing al-Ghazali's Identity View
Critique of al-Ghazali on genus/matter identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali's identification of genus with matter and differentia with form is rejected by verifiers, and how this relates to the definition of species.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia belong to definition, not to the physical parts of a species.

In this passage, the author takes up a subtle point about the relationship between logical terms (genus, differentia) and metaphysical concepts (matter, form). He critiques al-Ghazali for reducing them to mere subjective distinctions, which would have implications for how we understand simple immaterial beings. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author states that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but parts of its definition. This sets the stage for the critique.
Recall the earlier distinction between species and definition.
2
Grasp al-Ghazali's view
Al-Ghazali argued that genus and matter differ only by mental consideration. Understand his reasoning: what is taken 'with the condition of being alone' is matter; 'without that condition' is genus.
3
See the condition for his view
For al-Ghazali's view to be complete, genus must be taken from matter and differentia from form absolutely. This would imply that simple external entities (like immaterial intellects) have no genera or differentiae.
4
Recognize the verifiers' rejection
The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) have explicitly stated the contrary, affirming that genera and differentiae apply even to immaterial beings.
This shows that al-Ghazali's view is not upheld.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن حصر الجزء في المادة والصورة مبنى على أن الجنس والفعل ليسا جزأين من النوع بل من حده على ما سبق تحقيقه وجعله الإمام مبنياً على أنه لا تغاير بين الجنس والمادة، ولا بين الفعل والصورة إلا بمجرد الاعتبار لما مر من المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، ويكون كل ما يقارنه زائداً عليه، ولا يكون هو مقولاً على ذلك المجموع مادة والمأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، ويكون مقولاً على المجموع جنس وهو إنما يتم لو كان الجنس مأخوذاً من المادة، والفصل من الصورة البتة حتى لا يكون للبسائط الخارجية كالمجردات أجناس وفصول، وقد صرح المحققون بخلافه، إن من شروط ما هو عدمي كعدم المانع، فإن كان من جملة العلة الفاعلية لزم استناد المعلول إلى العلة المعدومة ضرورة انعدام الكل بانعدام الجزء وهو باطل، لأن امتناع تأثير المعدوم في الموجود ضروري، ولأنه يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع.
That the division of the part [of the composite] into matter and form is based on the fact that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but rather of its definition, as has been established previously. And the Imām [al-Ghazālī] made it based on the idea that there is no distinction between genus and matter, nor between differentia and form, except by mere consideration, due to what has passed: that what is taken with the condition that it be alone, and everything that accompanies it is additional to it, and it is not predicated of that aggregate, is matter; and what is taken without the condition that it be alone or not alone, and is predicated of the aggregate, is genus. But this is only complete if genus is taken from matter and differentia from form absolutely, so that simple external entities like immaterial intellects would have no genera and differentiae. And the verifiers have explicitly stated the contrary. And among the conditions is that which is privative, like the absence of an impediment. If it is made a part of the efficient cause, then the effect is necessarily attributed to a non-existent cause, since the whole ceases to exist by the cessation of the part, which is false, because the impossibility of the effect of a non-existent upon the existent is necessary, and because it would close the door to proving the Creator.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses two distinct issues. First, it discusses the basis for dividing the part (juzʾ) of a composite into matter and form with respect to genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl). The author states that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but of its definition. He critiques al-Ghazālī's view that genus and matter, and differentia and form, differ only by consideration, arguing that this would imply that simple entities like immaterial intellects (mujarradāt) have no genera or differentiae, which the verifiers reject. Second, he examines privative conditions (ʿadamī) like the absence of an impediment (ʿadam al-māniʿ). If such conditions were considered parts of the efficient cause, the effect would depend on a non-existent cause, because the whole cause ceases when the privative part is absent. This is impossible, as a non‑existent cannot affect an existent, and it would undermine the proof of the Creator (ithbāt al-Ṣāniʿ).

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
مادة
صورة
حد
بسائط
مجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Genus and matter are identical, differing only in mental consideration.
Correction: They are conceptually distinct; genus and differentia apply to definitions and are not reducible to physical composition.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ghazali's view imply about immaterial intellects (mujarradāt)?
A) They have both genus and differentia
B) They have no genera or differentiae
C) They are composed of matter and form
D) They are the same as material beings

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are parts of definition, not of species, and apply even to simple immaterial beings, contrary to al-Ghazali's narrow identification with matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you tend to define yourself by external attributes (like your body or possessions) rather than by what is essential to you? How might this lesson's distinction between physical parts and logical parts challenge you to reexamine your self-definition?
?
Al-Ghazali's error was conflating two distinct things (genus with matter). Where in your life have you mistakenly identified something as identical to what it merely accompanies? What would change if you saw the distinction clearly?
?
If simple immaterial beings like intellects can still be defined and known, what does that imply about the value of knowing things beyond physical reality? How does this lesson invite you to deepen your pursuit of intellectual knowledge over mere physical experience?

Privative Conditions and Efficient Causation
Argument against privative conditions as efficient causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a privative condition like the absence of an impediment cannot be considered part of the efficient cause, and why this is crucial for proving the Creator.

Core Idea
A non‑existent cannot cause an existent — privative conditions are not efficient causes.

This passage tackles a subtle issue in causal theory: the role of privative conditions (e.g., absence of impediment). Some might think such conditions are part of the efficient cause. The author argues this is impossible because it would make the effect depend on a non‑existent, which is both logically absurd and undermines the proof of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify privative conditions
Privative conditions are non‑existent states like the absence of an impediment. They are necessary for an effect but not efficient causes.
Example: for fire to burn, the absence of moisture is needed. But that absence does not cause the burning.
2
Understand the argument
If a privative condition is part of the efficient cause, then the effect would depend on a non‑existent cause because the whole cause (including the privative part) ceases when the privative part is absent. This is impossible.
The author uses the principle that a non‑existent cannot affect an existent.
3
See the larger implication
Including privative conditions in efficient causation would close the door to proving the Creator, because it would allow effects to arise from non‑existent causes.
This shows the theological importance of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن حصر الجزء في المادة والصورة مبنى على أن الجنس والفعل ليسا جزأين من النوع بل من حده على ما سبق تحقيقه وجعله الإمام مبنياً على أنه لا تغاير بين الجنس والمادة، ولا بين الفعل والصورة إلا بمجرد الاعتبار لما مر من المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، ويكون كل ما يقارنه زائداً عليه، ولا يكون هو مقولاً على ذلك المجموع مادة والمأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، ويكون مقولاً على المجموع جنس وهو إنما يتم لو كان الجنس مأخوذاً من المادة، والفصل من الصورة البتة حتى لا يكون للبسائط الخارجية كالمجردات أجناس وفصول، وقد صرح المحققون بخلافه، إن من شروط ما هو عدمي كعدم المانع، فإن كان من جملة العلة الفاعلية لزم استناد المعلول إلى العلة المعدومة ضرورة انعدام الكل بانعدام الجزء وهو باطل، لأن امتناع تأثير المعدوم في الموجود ضروري، ولأنه يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع.
That the division of the part [of the composite] into matter and form is based on the fact that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but rather of its definition, as has been established previously. And the Imām [al-Ghazālī] made it based on the idea that there is no distinction between genus and matter, nor between differentia and form, except by mere consideration, due to what has passed: that what is taken with the condition that it be alone, and everything that accompanies it is additional to it, and it is not predicated of that aggregate, is matter; and what is taken without the condition that it be alone or not alone, and is predicated of the aggregate, is genus. But this is only complete if genus is taken from matter and differentia from form absolutely, so that simple external entities like immaterial intellects would have no genera and differentiae. And the verifiers have explicitly stated the contrary. And among the conditions is that which is privative, like the absence of an impediment. If it is made a part of the efficient cause, then the effect is necessarily attributed to a non-existent cause, since the whole ceases to exist by the cessation of the part, which is false, because the impossibility of the effect of a non-existent upon the existent is necessary, and because it would close the door to proving the Creator.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses two distinct issues. First, it discusses the basis for dividing the part (juzʾ) of a composite into matter and form with respect to genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl). The author states that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but of its definition. He critiques al-Ghazālī's view that genus and matter, and differentia and form, differ only by consideration, arguing that this would imply that simple entities like immaterial intellects (mujarradāt) have no genera or differentiae, which the verifiers reject. Key Terms
عدم المانع
علة فاعلية
علة معدومة
اثبات الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Privative conditions like the absence of an impediment are efficient causes because they are necessary for the effect.
Correction: They are necessary but not efficient; a non‑existent cannot produce an existent effect.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of making a privative condition part of the efficient cause?
A) The effect depends on a non‑existent cause
B) The effect becomes impossible
C) The cause becomes eternal
D) The definition of cause becomes circular

Key Takeaway
Privative conditions are necessary but cannot be efficient causes; otherwise, effects would arise from non‑existence, which is absurd and undermines the proof of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a good outcome in your life to the 'absence of a problem' (like a close call) rather than to God’s active creation? How does this lesson challenge that habit of thought? Will you now consciously thank God as the efficient cause, not just the removal of obstacles?
?
If you truly believe that non‑existent things cannot cause existent ones, then how should that reshape your reliance on God for every single moment of existence? Is there any area of your life where you act as if luck or chance (which are non‑entities) determine events, rather than God’s will?
?
The proof of the Creator depends on the principle that every effect requires an efficient cause. Does your daily life reflect this principle, or do you sometimes behave as if things 'just happen'? What specific change in your attitude toward daily events will you make after understanding that privative conditions are not causes?

intermediate
5 min
Critique of Matter and Form Definitions
Sanusi critiques the definitions of matter and form using the example of a composite form's non-final part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem with the Aristotelian definitions of matter and form when applied to composite entities, and why potentiality cannot be interpreted as mere possibility.

Core Idea
The definitions of matter and form fail because they treat potentiality inconsistently.

Sanusi begins by examining the standard definitions of matter and form used in classical philosophy. He shows that these definitions lead to contradictions when applied to the non-final parts of a composite form. This lesson unpacks his critique and clarifies the precise meaning of 'potentiality' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the key terms: مادة, صورة, قوة, فعل. Try to see how the argument flows from the definitions to the contradiction.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if potentiality enters definition of form, then...'
2
Identify the definitions
Identify the definitions: form is that with which existence is actual; matter is that with which existence is potential. Note that potentiality (قوة) is the distinguishing feature.
3
Understand the contradiction
The non-final part of a composite form has necessity of the effect potentially, not actually. So it fits the definition of matter, not form. Both definitions are violated: form includes what should be matter, and matter excludes what should be matter.
This is the core of the critique.
4
Reject a misinterpretation
Sanusi rejects interpreting potentiality as mere possibility (الإمكاني) that does not contradict actuality, because that would make the problem worse.
Potentiality here is privation, not possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالقوة، فيدخل في تعريف الصورة، فلا يكون مانعاً، ويخرج من تعريف المادة فلا يكون جامعاً بخلاف الوجوب، فإنه بالنظر إلى المادة لا يكون إلا بالقوة وبالنظر إلى الصورة لا يكون إلا بالفعل. وكان مرادهم أن الصورة ما يكون وجوداً لشيء معه بالفعل البتة والمادة ما يكون الوجود معه بالقوة في الجملة، وحينئذ لا انتقاض أن الجزء الغير الأخير من الصورة المركبة، يكون وجوب المعلول معه بالقوة لا بالفعل، فيدخل في (۱) تعريف المادية، ويخرج عن (۲) تعريف الصورة، فينتقض التعريفان جمعاً ومنعاً، ولا يجوز أن يراد بالقوة الإمكاني بحيث لا ينافي الفعل، لأن الفساد حينئذ (۳) أظهر أن حصر الجزء في المادة والصورة مبنى على أن الجنس والفعل ليسا جزأين من النوع بل من حده على ما سبق تحقيقه وجعله الإمام مبنياً على أنه لا تغاير بين الجنس والمادة، ولا بين الفعل والصورة إلا بمجرد الاعتبار لما مر من المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، ويكون كل ما يقارنه زائداً عليه، ولا يكون هو مقولاً على ذلك المجموع مادة والمأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، ويكون مقولاً على المجموع جنس وهو إنما يتم لو كان الجنس مأخوذاً من المادة، والفصل من الصورة البتة حتى لا يكون للبسائط الخارجية كالمجردات أجناس وفصول، وقد صرح المحققون بخلافه، إن من شروط ما هو عدمي كعدم المانع، فإن كان من جملة العلة الفاعلية لزم استناد المعلول إلى العلة المعدومة ضرورة انعدام الكل بانعدام الجزء وهو باطل، لأن امتناع تأثير المعدوم في الموجود ضروري، ولأنه يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع.
By potentiality, so it enters the definition of form and does not prevent, and exits the definition of matter so it is not comprehensive, unlike necessity, for it with respect to matter is only potentially and with respect to form is only actually. Their intention was that form is that with which the existence of a thing is actually absolutely, and matter is that with which existence is potentially in general. Then there is no contradiction that the non-final part of the composite form has the necessity of the effect with it potentially not actually, so it enters the definition of materiality and exits the definition of form, so both definitions are violated in terms of inclusion and exclusion. It is not permissible that by potentiality is meant the possible such that it does not contradict actuality, because corruption then is more apparent. The restriction of the part to matter and form is based on the fact that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but of its definition, as previously verified, and the Imam (al-Ghazali) made it based on there being no distinction between genus and matter, nor between differentia and form, except by mere consideration, as previously mentioned: that which is taken with the condition of being alone, and everything that accompanies it is additional to it, and it is not predicated of that aggregate as matter; and that which is taken without the condition of being alone or not alone, and is predicated of the aggregate as genus. But this is only complete if genus is taken from matter and differentia from form absolutely, so that simple external entities like separate intellects have no genera and species. The verifiers have stated contrary to this: among the conditions is something non-existential like the absence of an obstacle. If it is part of the efficient cause, then the effect would be attributed to a non-existent cause necessarily because the whole is negated by the negation of the part, and this is false, because the impossibility of the non-existent having influence on the existent is necessary, and because it would lead to closing the door of proving the Creator.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage critiques the Aristotelian definitions of matter and form in the context of causality. The author first explains that if the non-final part of a composite form is considered as potentially necessary, it would fit the definition of matter rather than form, violating both definitions. He then discusses the distinction between genus and differentia, noting that some scholars (like al-Ghazali) treat genus and matter as merely conceptually distinct, but the author argues this only works if genus is always taken from matter and differentia from form, which fails for simple separate intellects. The main point is that verifiers have rejected this, leading to the introduction of non-existential conditions (like the absence of an obstacle) as part of the efficient cause. If such a non-existential condition were to be considered part of the efficient cause, then the effect would depend on a non-existent cause, which is impossible because a non-existent cannot influence an existent. Moreover, this would lead to the absurdity of closing the door for proving the Creator, because all effects would require a non-existent condition. The passage thus rejects the inclusion of non-existential conditions in the efficient cause.

Key Terms
مادة
صورة
قوة
فعل
وجوب
امكاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potentiality in the definition of form is the same as logical possibility.
Correction: Potentiality here means privation of actuality, not mere possibility. Sanusi explicitly rejects interpreting it as possible that does not contradict actuality.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say the definition of matter is not comprehensive?
A) Because it excludes the non-final part of a composite form.
B) Because it includes the final part of a composite form.
C) Because it confuses potentiality with actuality.
D) Because it defines matter as potential existence.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of matter and form are inconsistent because potentiality applies to parts of form, making them fit the definition of matter instead.

Causality and Created Things

6 min
Non-Existential Conditions and the Efficient Cause
Sanusi argues against including non-existential conditions in the efficient cause and highlights its theological consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existential conditions like the absence of an obstacle cannot be part of the efficient cause, as this would imply an effect depends on non-existence and would undermine the proof of the Creator.

Core Idea
A non-existential condition cannot be part of the efficient cause because non-existence cannot cause existence.

Sanusi extends his critique to a deeper metaphysical principle: conditions for causation. Some had argued that non-existential conditions (like absence of an obstacle) are part of the efficient cause. Sanusi shows this leads to absurdity—the effect would depend on a non-existent cause—and even threatens the proof of God's existence. This lesson explains his argument and why it matters for kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second part of the source passage. Focus on the part from 'أن حصر الجزء' to the end. Mark the key claim: 'إن من شروط ما هو عدمي كعدم المانع'.
The passage builds on the earlier discussion but shifts to a new objection.
2
Understand the background
The restriction of parts to matter and form is based on the idea that genus and differentia are not parts of the species but of its definition. Al-Ghazali's view reduces genus and matter to a conceptual distinction. Sanusi mentions that verifiers reject this.
This sets up the problem of non-existential conditions.
3
Identify the objection
Sanusi says: if a non-existential condition (like absence of obstacle) were part of the efficient cause, then the effect would depend on a non-existent cause, which is impossible because a non-existent cannot influence an existent.
4
Grasp the theological implication
Sanusi adds that accepting this would 'close the door of proving the Creator', because all effects would require a non-existent condition, making it impossible to trace effects back to a necessary existent.
This shows the argument's importance for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Their intention was that form is that with which the existence of a thing is actually absolutely, and matter is that with which existence is potentially in general. D) The existence of the Creator would be proven directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute the success of your actions to the absence of obstacles (good circumstances) rather than to the Creator who removed those obstacles? How does Sanusi's argument challenge that assumption in your daily life?
?
Sanusi warns that including non-existential conditions in the efficient cause 'closes the door of proving the Creator.' Think of a situation where you felt your success was due to 'luck' or 'absence of problems.' Did you trace that back to God as the true cause? What would change if you consistently saw God as the efficient cause behind every outcome?
?
This lesson argues that non-existence cannot influence existence. Consider your own reliance on 'what if' scenarios or fears of future obstacles. Are you living as though those non-existential 'what ifs' have power over you? How can this truth free you from anxiety?

The Agent vs. Conditions in Causality
Sanusi distinguishes the agent's essence from conditions to preserve the proof of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the true cause of an effect is the agent's essence alone, while conditions are mere prerequisites, and why this preserves the proof of the Creator.

Core Idea
The cause is the agent's essence; conditions are prerequisites, not parts of the cause.

Sanusi now provides a clear answer to the objection that including non-existential conditions in the efficient cause leads to absurdity. He makes a crucial distinction: the effective agent is not the entire efficient cause, but only the essence of the agent. Everything else is a condition for influence, not part of the cause itself. This distinction keeps the door open for proving the Creator, because contingent existence still depends on an existent cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the main answer
Read the Arabic passage from 'والجواب' to 'بشرائط عدمية' and its translation. Notice how Sanusi separates the agent's essence from the conditions.
2
Identify the key distinction
Sanusi says the effect depends on the existent agent, not on the conditions. Conditions are merely prerequisites. Make sure you see why this avoids the objection.
The objection claimed non-existential conditions would make the cause incomplete.
3
Connect to the proof of the Creator
The proof of the Creator relies on contingent things needing an existent cause. Even if that cause is accompanied by non-existential conditions, the need for an existent creator remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن المؤثر في وجود المعلول ليس هو العلة الفاعلية بحملتها، بل ذات الفاعل فقط، وسائر ما يرجع إلى الفاعل، إنما هي شرائط التأثير ولا امتناع في استناد المعلول إلى فاعل موجود مقرون بأمور عدمية، بمعنى أن العقل إذا لاحظه حكم بأنه لا يحصل بدونها مع القطع بأن الموجد هو الفاعل الموجود، وحينئذ لا ينسد باب إثبات الصانع، لأن وجود الممكن يحتاج إلى وجود موجد، وإن كان مقروناً بشرائط عدمية . وقد يجاب بأن الشرط إنما هو أمر وجودي خفي. وذلك الأمر العدمي الذي يظن كونه شرطاً لازم له كاشف عنه مثلاً شرط احتراق الخشبة ليس بزوال الرطوبة، وانعدامها بل وجود اليبوسة الذي ينبيء عنه زوال الرطوبة وكذا سائر الصور.
The answer: The effective agent in the existence of the effect is not the efficient cause in its entirety, but only the essence of the agent. Everything else pertaining to the agent are only conditions of influence, and there is no impossibility in the effect being dependent on an existent agent accompanied by non-existential matters, meaning that the intellect, when it considers it, judges that it does not occur without them, while being certain that the bringer-into-existence is the existent agent. Then the door of proving the Creator is not closed, because the existence of the contingent needs the existence of a bringer-into-existence, even if accompanied by non-existential conditions. And it may also be answered that the condition is only a hidden existential matter. That non-existential matter which is thought to be a condition is a necessary concomitant of it, revealing it. For example, the condition for the burning of wood is not the removal of moisture and its non-existence, but rather the existence of dryness which the removal of moisture indicates. And similarly for all other cases.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the objection raised earlier that including non-existential conditions in the efficient cause leads to absurdity. The author argues that the true effective agent in bringing about the effect is not the entire efficient cause (including conditions), but only the essence of the agent itself. The other things attributed to the agent, such as intentions, instruments, and conditions, are merely prerequisites (شرائط) for influence, not part of the cause. Therefore, it is not problematic for the effect to depend on an existent agent accompanied by non-existential conditions. The intellect recognizes that the effect requires these conditions to occur, but the actual bringer-into-existence is the existent agent. This preserves the proof of the Creator, because contingent existence still needs an existent creator, even if that creator's action is accompanied by non-existential conditions. The passage also mentions an alternative answer: that what appears to be a non-existential condition is actually a hidden existential condition. For example, the condition for burning wood is not the removal of moisture (a non-existential), but rather the presence of dryness (an existential) which the removal of moisture merely indicates. So all conditions are ultimately existential, not non-existential.

Key Terms
علة فاعلية
شرائط التأثير
أمور عدمية
فاعل موجود
باب إثبات الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The efficient cause includes all conditions, so non-existential conditions would make the cause incomplete and absurd.
Correction: The efficient cause properly is only the agent's essence; conditions are prerequisites, not part of the cause. Thus non-existential conditions are not part of the cause and do not create absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's answer, why does including non-existential conditions not close the door of proving the Creator?
A) Because the Creator is non-existent.
B) Because the effect depends only on the existent agent, not on the conditions.
C) Because conditions are always existential.
D) Because the effect has no need for a cause.

Key Takeaway
The true cause of an effect is the agent's essence alone; conditions are prerequisites, so non-existential conditions do not undermine the proof of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you subconsciously attribute the outcomes you experience to the conditions around you (health, wealth, circumstances) rather than recognizing Allah as the sole Agent behind every effect? How can you train your heart to see His hand directly, even when conditions seem favorable or unfavorable?
?
If non-existential conditions (like the absence of obstacles) are not causes but merely prerequisites, does your reliance on Allah (tawakkul) focus more on the conditions or on Him? What would change if you detached your trust from conditions and placed it entirely on the Agent?
?
The intellect judges that the effect cannot occur without certain conditions, yet affirms the existent agent as the true cause. Are there areas in your life where you prioritize the 'conditions' over the Creator—perhaps by worrying about means rather than calling upon Allah directly? What practical step can you take this week to shift your focus from conditions to the Agent?

Non-Existential Conditions as Hidden Existential Conditions
Sanusi reinterprets non-existential conditions as necessary concomitants of hidden existential conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative answer that apparent non-existential conditions are actually hidden existential conditions that are necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Non-existential conditions are signs of hidden existential causes.

Sanusi offers a second, complementary answer to the objection: what seems like a non-existential condition—such as the removal of moisture before wood burns—is actually a necessary concomitant of an underlying existential condition (dryness). This reinterprets all conditions as ultimately existential, avoiding the problem of non-existential factors entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative answer
Read the Arabic from 'وقد يجاب' to the end, along with the translation. Note the example of burning wood.
2
Understand the principle
Sanusi says the condition is actually a hidden existential matter. The non-existential thing that seems to be a condition is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) that reveals the real condition.
For burning, the removal of moisture indicates the presence of dryness, which is the real condition.
3
Apply the principle to other cases
Sanusi says 'and similarly for all other cases.' Think of another example where a non-existential appearance hides an existential reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن المؤثر في وجود المعلول ليس هو العلة الفاعلية بحملتها، بل ذات الفاعل فقط، وسائر ما يرجع إلى الفاعل، إنما هي شرائط التأثير ولا امتناع في استناد المعلول إلى فاعل موجود مقرون بأمور عدمية، بمعنى أن العقل إذا لاحظه حكم بأنه لا يحصل بدونها مع القطع بأن الموجد هو الفاعل الموجود، وحينئذ لا ينسد باب إثبات الصانع، لأن وجود الممكن يحتاج إلى وجود موجد، وإن كان مقروناً بشرائط عدمية . وقد يجاب بأن الشرط إنما هو أمر وجودي خفي. وذلك الأمر العدمي الذي يظن كونه شرطاً لازم له كاشف عنه مثلاً شرط احتراق الخشبة ليس بزوال الرطوبة، وانعدامها بل وجود اليبوسة الذي ينبيء عنه زوال الرطوبة وكذا سائر الصور.
The answer: The effective agent in the existence of the effect is not the efficient cause in its entirety, but only the essence of the agent. For example, the condition for the burning of wood is not the removal of moisture and its non-existence, but rather the existence of dryness which the removal of moisture indicates. Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the objection raised earlier that including non-existential conditions in the efficient cause leads to absurdity. The author argues that the true effective agent in bringing about the effect is not the entire efficient cause (including conditions), but only the essence of the agent itself. The other things attributed to the agent, such as intentions, instruments, and conditions, are merely prerequisites (شرائط) for influence, not part of the cause. Checkpoint
According to the alternative answer, what is the actual condition for the burning of wood?
A) The removal of moisture.
B) The existence of dryness.
C) The absence of water.
D) The presence of heat.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring struggle in your life where you've been trying to remove a negative trait (like impatience or pride) as a condition for becoming better. Based on this lesson, what hidden existential quality might that removal reveal? What positive trait must you actively cultivate instead? Be specific—write down one concrete action you will take this week to cultivate that hidden existential condition.
?
Sanusi teaches that what appears to be a non-existential condition is actually a sign of something existential within you. When you see a lack in yourself (e.g., lack of discipline, lack of gratitude), do you stop at the absence, or do you look for the hidden existential cause like the presence of laziness or ingratitude? What would it mean for you today to stop focusing on what you lack and start nurturing its opposite? Commit to one specific phrase of praise to Allah for a quality you want to embody.
?
If all conditions are existential—present realities—then your spiritual growth is not about removing negatives but about bringing positives into existence. What is one positive spiritual quality (like sincerity, trust, or love for Allah) that you know you need but have been neglecting to cultivate because you're busy fighting its absence? How will you start making it exist in your heart right now, even if just as a small intention?

8 min
Non-Existence is Not a Principle of Existence
Sanusi refutes the claim that non-existence is a principle of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the non-existence of an event cannot be a genuine principle of its existence, and the distinction between essential and accidental priority.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not a cause; only existent things are principles.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: that the non-existence of an event is a principle of its existence because the event needs an agent that exists at the same time as the event. Sanusi refutes this by distinguishing between temporal and essential priority. Let's read the passage to see how he argues.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection presented: 'The very non-existence of the event is among the principles of its existence because it needs the agent to be concurrent with it.' Notice what the objector is trying to prove.
The objector claims that since the agent must be concurrent, the prior non-existence is a condition or principle.
2
Understand the refutation
Then read Sanusi's response: need for a thing does not entail need for what is concurrent with it. The priority is merely temporal, not essential. Ask: why is it incoherent for existence to need non-existence?
Sanusi points out that temporal priority does not imply causal priority.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Finally, Sanusi concludes that non-existence is only accidentally considered a principle because it accompanies the true principle (the agent). Understand the 'accidental' (bil-'arad) qualification.
This preserves the principle that only existent things can be causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نفس عدم الحادث من مبادىء وجوده لافتقاره إلى الفاعل المقارن له . قلنا : الاحتياج إلى الشيء لا يقتضي الاحتياج إلى ما يقارنه، ولهذا كان تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده زمانياً محضاً لا ذاتياً، وكيف يعقل احتياج وجود الشيء إلى عدمه فهو ليس من المبادىء إلا بالعرض بمعنى أنه يقارن المبدأ.
If it is said: The very non-existence of the event is among the principles of its existence because it needs the agent to be concurrent with it. We say: Need for a thing does not entail need for what is concurrent with it. Therefore the priority of the non-existence of the event over its existence is purely temporal, not essential. How can the existence of a thing be in need of its non-existence? So it is not among the principles except accidentally, meaning that it accompanies the principle.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and its answer. The objection claims that the non-existence of an event is a principle (مبدأ) of its existence because the event requires an agent that is simultaneous with it (i.e., the agent must exist at the same time as the event, implying that the prior non-existence is a condition). The author answers by distinguishing between essential and accidental priority. The non-existence of the event is only temporally prior to its existence, not essentially prior. Need for a thing (e.g., the agent) does not imply need for what is concurrent with that thing (e.g., the prior non-existence). It is incoherent to say that existence depends on non-existence. Therefore, non-existence is not a true principle of existence; it is only accidentally considered a principle because it accompanies the actual principle (the agent) in the sense that the agent's action occurs after non-existence. This preserves the principle that only existent things can be causes.

Key Terms
عدم الحادث
مبادىء
فاعل مقارن
تقدم زماني
تقدم ذاتي
بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existence of an event is a genuine principle or cause of its existence because it is a prior condition.
Correction: Non-existence is only temporally prior, not essentially prior. Existence cannot depend on non-existence. Hence non-existence is not a cause but only accidentally accompanies the real cause (the agent).

Checkpoint
How does the author characterize the priority of the non-existence of an event over its existence?
A) Essential priority.
B) Temporal priority only.
C) Logical priority.
D) Causal priority.

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of an event is only temporally prior, not essentially, and thus is not a genuine principle of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the fact that you did not exist before you were born, do you ever treat that non-existence as a reason for your current life, rather than seeing it as merely temporally prior? How does this teaching force you to redirect your gratitude from 'circumstances' to the Creator?
?
In your daily life, where do you confuse 'first in time' with 'first in importance' or 'the cause'? For example, do you blame your upbringing for your actions, forgetting the present agent is you? How will you start distinguishing temporal order from causal responsibility starting today?
?
If non-existence cannot be a principle, then what does that say about your moments of spiritual 'non-existence'—times when you feel distant from Allah? Are you tempted to see that distance as a cause for your current state, or can you now see it as merely a temporal condition that does not determine your existence in Him?

Definition of the Complete Cause
Sanusi defines the complete cause and implies the distinction from the incomplete cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the complete cause and how it logically necessitates its effect, as well as the implied contrast with the incomplete cause.

Core Idea
A complete cause is one that, by its mere existence, forces its effect to exist.

Sanusi begins a discussion of causality by defining the most fundamental term: the complete cause. Understanding what makes a cause 'complete' is essential because it distinguishes between mere prerequisites and truly sufficient conditions. This short passage sets the stage for deeper kalam debates about the nature of divine and natural causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the passage starts with the heading 'The complete and incomplete cause' and then gives the definition.
2
Identify the key condition
Look at the phrase 'متى حصل وجد ذلك الشيء البتة' (whenever it occurs, that thing exists absolutely). This is the central criterion for a complete cause: necessary and sufficient condition.
The word البتة (absolutely) emphasizes the necessity.
3
Reflect on the implication for the incomplete cause
The passage only defines the complete cause, but by implication, an incomplete cause is something that is needed but not sufficient. Ask yourself what kind of things could be incomplete causes.
For example, a father is needed for a child but not sufficient; the mother's contribution and other conditions are also needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة التامة والناقصة . قال : ثم جميع ما يحتاج الشيء في وجوده يسمى علة تامة، سواء كان هو الفاعل في وجوده بمعنى أن ذلك المحتاج إليه إن كان بحيث إنه متى حصل وجد ذلك الشيء البتة فهو علة تامة .
The complete cause and the incomplete cause. He said: Then everything that a thing needs in its existence is called a complete cause, whether it is the agent in its existence, meaning that if that which is needed is such that whenever it occurs that thing exists absolutely, then it is a complete cause.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines the complete cause (العلة التامة) and by implication the incomplete cause (العلة الناقصة). The author states that everything a thing needs in its existence can be called a cause, but specifically a complete cause is that upon whose existence the effect necessarily and invariably exists. That is, if the cause occurs, the effect must occur. The phrase 'whether it is the agent in its existence' indicates that the complete cause may be the agent itself or something else (such as the material or formal cause), but the key criterion is the necessary conjunction. An incomplete cause, then, is one that is needed but not sufficient by itself; it requires additional conditions to produce the effect. This definition is foundational for discussions of causality in kalam, as it distinguishes between mere prerequisites and true efficient causes.

Key Terms
علة تامة
علة ناقصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any necessary condition qualifies as a complete cause.
Correction: In fact, a complete cause must be sufficient to necessitate the effect on its own; many necessary conditions are incomplete causes.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of a complete cause according to this passage?
A) It is always the agent.
B) It is that without which the effect cannot exist.
C) When it exists, the effect necessarily exists.
D) It must be simultaneous with the effect.

Key Takeaway
A complete cause is defined as that which, whenever it exists, necessitates the existence of its effect absolutely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your effort is the complete cause of your success? What would change if you truly believed that only God's will can make a cause complete?
?
Think of a time when you had all the conditions for something but it didn't happen. How does this passage help you accept your lack of control and rely on the true Sufficient Cause?
?
If every incomplete cause reminds you of your dependency, what in your life are you still trying to control as if you were complete? Will you surrender that today?

The Real Cause Is the Agent's Essence, Not Conditions
Sanusi refutes the objection that non-existential conditions negate causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existential conditions do not undermine the causal proof for the Creator.

Core Idea
Conditions accompany the cause; the agent's essence remains the true origin.

Sanusi addresses a key objection against causality-based theistic proofs. Critics argue that if an effect requires non-existential conditions, then the cause is not truly efficient. This passage clarifies that the real cause is the agent's essence itself; conditions are merely necessary concomitants that do not diminish the cause's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objection and the answer that follows.
Pay attention to the example of burning wood.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms 'illa fa'iliyya' (efficient cause) and 'shara'it al-ta'thir' (conditions of influence). Understand how they are distinguished.
3
Reflect on the proof
Consider why the presence of non-existential conditions does not block the proof of the Maker. The answer lies in the distinction between the essence of the agent and its conditions.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن المؤثر في وجود المعلول ليس هو العلة الفاعلية بحملتها، بل ذات الفاعل فقط، وسائر ما يرجع إلى الفاعل، إنما هي شرائط التأثير ولا امتناع في استناد المعلول إلى فاعل موجود مقرون بأمور عدمية، بمعنى أن العقل إذا لاحظه حكم بأنه لا يحصل بدونها مع القطع بأن الموجد هو الفاعل الموجود، وحينئذ لا ينسد باب إثبات الصانع، لأن وجود الممكن يحتاج إلى وجود موجد، وإن كان مقروناً بشرائط عدمية . وقد يجاب بأن الشرط إنما هو أمر وجودي خفي. وذلك الأمر العدمي الذي يظن كونه شرطاً لازم له كاشف عنه مثلاً شرط احتراق الخشبة ليس بزوال الرطوبة، وانعدامها بل وجود اليبوسة الذي ينبيء عنه زوال الرطوبة ) وكذا سائر الصور. فإن قيل : نفس عدم الحادث من مبادىء وجوده لافتقاره إلى الفاعل المقارن له . قلنا : الاحتياج إلى الشيء لا يقتضي الاحتياج إلى ما يقارنه، ولهذا كان تقدم عدم الحادث على وجوده زمانياً محضاً لا ذاتياً، وكيف يعقل احتياج وجود الشيء إلى عدمه فهو ليس من المبادىء إلا بالعرض بمعنى أنه يقارن المبدأ .
And the answer: that the effective factor in the existence of the effect is not the efficient cause in its entirety, but only the essence of the agent, and all that pertains to the agent are only conditions of influence, and there is no impossibility in the effect being dependent on an existing agent accompanied by non-existential matters, meaning that when the intellect observes it, it judges that it does not occur without them while being certain that the creator is the existing agent. At that point, the door of proving the Maker is not closed, because the existence of the possible requires an existing creator, even if it is accompanied by non-existential conditions. And it may be answered that the condition is only a subtle existential matter. And that non-existential matter which is thought to be a condition is a necessary concomitant and indicator of it; for example, the condition for the burning of wood is not the removal of moisture and its absence, but rather the existence of dryness which is indicated by the removal of moisture. And similarly for all cases. If it is said: The very non-existence of the event is among the principles of its existence because of its need for an agent concurrent with it. We say: Need for something does not necessitate need for what is concurrent with it, and therefore the priority of the non-existence of the event over its existence is purely temporal, not essential. And how can the existence of a thing need its non-existence? It is not among the principles except accidentally, meaning that it accompanies the principle.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section refuting two arguments that would allegedly block the proof of the Creator. The first objection claims that if the efficient cause requires non-existential conditions, then causal proof fails. The answer explains that the real cause is the agent's essence; conditions are necessary but do not diminish the agency. The second objection argues that the non-existence of an event before it occurs is a principle of its existence because it needs an agent at the moment. The answer clarifies that existential need is temporal, not essential, so non-existence is only accidentally a principle.

Key Terms
علة فاعلية
شرائط التأثير
أمور عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existential conditions imply the cause is insufficient and thus the proof of a Creator fails.
Correction: The agent's essence is the sufficient cause; conditions are merely necessary prerequisites that do not diminish the cause's power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the presence of non-existential conditions not close the door of proving the Maker?
A) Because non-existential conditions are unreal.
B) Because the real cause is the agent's essence, and conditions are merely necessary concomitants.
C) Because all conditions are actually existential.
D) Because the proof of the Maker does not rely on causality.

Key Takeaway
The real cause of an effect is the agent's essence; non-existential conditions are merely necessary requirements that do not diminish the need for a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed your success or failure to external circumstances—like the right conditions or their absence—while forgetting the One who alone is the real Cause of every outcome? What one condition have you been relying on that should instead deepen your trust in Allah?
?
When you face a barrier that seems impossible because the 'conditions' are missing, do you focus on the missing condition or on the Agent who can create it? How can you train your mind to see behind every absence a hidden invitation to rely on Him alone?
?
This lesson teaches that non-existential conditions are merely indicators of something existent. What 'absence' in your life (e.g., lack of wealth, health, or opportunity) might actually be pointing to a hidden existential reality—a quality of your soul or a lesson from Allah? How will you respond to that sign?

The Non-Existence of an Event Is Not a Principle of Its Existence
Sanusi refutes the claim that non-existence is a principle of existence.

Temporal priority is not essential priority.

8 min
Complete and Incomplete Cause: Definitions and Priority
Defining complete and incomplete cause with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of complete and incomplete causes and the conditions under which a complete cause precedes its effect.

Core Idea
Not every complete cause precedes its effect; matter-form causes are simultaneous.

This lesson explores the technical definitions of complete and incomplete causes in Islamic philosophy. The author, Sanusi, clarifies that a complete cause includes everything needed for the effect, but this does not always imply temporal priority, especially when the cause includes matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the repetition in the opening; it may be a scribal emphasis.
Focus on the classification of causes.
2
Identify the two types of cause
The passage defines complete cause (علة تامة) and incomplete cause (علة ناقصة). Note the defining characteristic: complete cause includes everything needed.
3
Examine the subtypes of complete cause
The passage lists subtypes: agent alone, agent with end, and for composites, agent with matter and form (with or without end). Understand each scenario.
Examples involve simple (بسيط) and composite (مركب) effects.
4
Understand the priority condition
The passage argues that when a complete cause includes matter and form, it cannot temporally precede the effect because the effect's parts are those same matter and form. Only the parts are prior, not the whole cause.
This refutes the idea that every complete cause must precede.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم جميع ما يحتاج الشيء يسمى علة تامة، ما يحتاج الشيء يسمى علة تامة، سواء كان هو الفاعل وحده، أو مع الغاية كالبسيط للبسيط إيجاباً أو اختياراً أو مع البواقي، المركبات، وحينئذ لا يتصور تقدمها والاحتياج إليها، إذ فيها جميع الأجزاء التي هي نفس الشيء. هي العلة إما تامة هي جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء بمعنى أنه لا يبقى هناك أمر آخر يحتاج إليه، لا بمعنى أن تكون مركبة من عدة أمور البتة، وإما ناقصة بعض ذلك، والتامة قد تكون هي الفاعل وحده كالبسيط الموجد للبسيط إيجاباً وقد تكون هي مع الغاية كالبسيط الموجد للبسيط اختياراً، فإن فعل المختار قد يكون لغرض يدعو إليه، وقد يكون هو مع المادة والصورة أيضاً كالموجد للمركب عنهما، إما الغاية أو بدونها، وإذا كانت العلة التامة مشتملة على المادة والصورة يمتنع تقدمها على المعلول، واحتياج المعلول إليها أجزاء الشيء نفسه، وإنما التقدم لكل جزء منها، فما يقال أن العلة التامة التي يجب تقدمها على المعلول ليس على إطلاقه، بل العلة الناقصة أو هي الفاعل وحده، أو مع الشرط والغاية .
He said: Then everything that a thing needs is called a complete cause, everything a thing needs is called a complete cause, whether it is the agent alone, or with the end, as the simple [cause] for the simple effect necessarily or voluntarily, or with the remaining [causes] for composites, and in that case priority and need cannot be conceived, since it contains all the parts that are the same as the thing. The cause is either complete: it is everything the thing needs in the sense that there remains no other thing it needs, not in the sense that it is composed of several matters absolutely; or incomplete: some of that. The complete cause may be the agent alone, like the simple cause that brings about a simple effect necessarily, or it may be with the end, like the simple cause that brings about a simple effect voluntarily, for the voluntary agent's action may be for a purpose that calls for it, and it may be with matter and form as well, like what produces a composite from them, either with the end or without it. And if the complete cause includes matter and form, its priority over the effect is impossible, and the effect's need for it is because they are parts of the thing itself; priority belongs only to each part of it. So what is said that the complete cause must precede the effect is not absolute; rather, it is the incomplete cause, or the agent alone, or with the condition and end.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines the complete and incomplete cause. It begins by stating that everything a thing needs is called a complete cause, whether it is the agent alone or with other causes like the end. For simple causes and effects, the complete cause may be the agent alone (necessity) or with the end (voluntary). For composites, it includes matter and form. If the complete cause includes matter and form, it cannot precede the effect because the effect's parts are identical to those of the cause; only each part can be prior. Therefore, the claim that every complete cause must precede its effect is not universal; it applies only to the incomplete cause, the agent alone, or the agent with condition and end.

Key Terms
علة تامة
علة ناقصة
فاعل
غاية
مادة
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every complete cause must temporally precede its effect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that when a complete cause includes matter and form, it cannot precede the effect because the effect's essence consists of those same matter and form.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the complete cause NOT precede the effect?
A) When it is the agent alone.
B) When it includes matter and form.
C) When it is an incomplete cause.
D) When it involves a voluntary agent.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause includes all that is needed for the effect, but its temporal priority is not universal; when it comprises matter and form, it cannot precede the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the complete cause of your existence includes matter and form that are identical to you, does that mean you are reduced to a mere composite of physical parts? How does this lesson challenge your sense of self as only a body? Commit to reflecting on your true identity as a creation of God that is sustained each moment simultaneously with its cause.
?
The author argues that priority belongs only to parts of the cause, not the complete cause. How does this affect your understanding of God's action in your life? Do you often think of God as a cause that is prior and then leaves you, or as a simultaneous sustainer? What would change in your daily reliance if you truly believed God's causing of you is continuous right now? Decide one concrete way to acknowledge God's simultaneous causation in your life today.

Divisions of the Four Causes
Enumerates seven categories of causes and their subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the four causes can be divided into seven (or fourteen) categorical pairs, providing a precise vocabulary for causal analysis.

Core Idea
Each cause is not a single concept but can be analyzed through multiple binary lenses.

This passage presents a systematic classification of causes that is foundational in Islamic philosophy and theology. The author lists several pairs of contrasting attributes that apply to each of the four causes: material, formal, efficient, and final. Understanding these divisions sharpens one's ability to reason about causality and avoid conflating different types of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the list of pairs and the footnote clarifying the count.
2
Identify the six common pairs
The passage lists six pairs that apply to all four causes: simple/composite, universal/particular, essential/accidental, proximate/remote, general/specific, shared/exclusive. Find each pair in the text.
3
Understand the extra pair for efficient cause
The efficient cause adds a seventh pair: potential/actual. Note that the footnote explains the total count: seven pairs (counting each pair as one) or fourteen (counting each member separately).
This extra pair reflects the dynamic nature of the efficient cause.
4
Apply the classification
Ask yourself: For a given thing, what are its efficient cause's attributes? Is it simple or composite? Essential or accidental? This helps in precise causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم أقسام العلة (قال) وكل من الأربع ينقسم إلى بسيطة ومركبة، وإلى كلية وجزئية، و إلى ذاتية ،وعرضية وإلى قريبة وبعيدة إلى عامة وخاصة، وإلى مشتركة ومختصة. (0) إلى سبعة أقسام إن عُد كل متقابلين قسماً وإلا فأربعة عشر. وذلك أن الفاعلية منها تنقسم إلى بسيطة ومركبة وإلى كلية وجزئية وإلى ذاتية وعرضية وإلى قريبة وبعيدة وإلى عامة وخاصة وإلى مشتركة ومختصة وإلى ما بالقوة وما بالفعل .
Division of the divisions of the cause. He said: And each of the four [causes] divides into simple and composite, into universal and particular, into essential and accidental, into proximate and remote, into general and specific, into shared and exclusive. (0) Into seven divisions if each pair is counted as one division, otherwise fourteen. That is because the efficient [cause] among them divides into simple and composite, universal and particular, essential and accidental, proximate and remote, general and specific, shared and exclusive, and potential and actual.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of causes. It states that each of the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final) can be divided into several pairs of contrasting categories: simple vs. composite, universal vs. particular, essential vs. accidental, proximate vs. remote, general vs. specific, and shared vs. exclusive. The footnote clarifies that if each pair is taken as a single division, there are seven divisions; if each member is counted separately, there are fourteen. The efficient cause is further subdivided into potential and actual.

Correction: These are not different categories of causes but attributes that can be applied to any of the four causes. For example, a cause can be both essential and accidental in different respects.

Perfect and Imperfect Cause Defined
Author defines perfect and imperfect cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of perfect and imperfect cause, and what distinguishes them beyond mere composition.

Core Idea
A perfect cause is self-sufficient; an imperfect cause supplies only part of what is needed.

The author begins by setting out the two fundamental categories of cause: perfect and imperfect. This distinction is not about simplicity or complexity, but about whether the cause provides everything the effect requires. Let's read this definition carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic sentence slowly. Notice the key terms 'علة تامة' and 'علة ناقصة'. Pay attention to the qualifying phrase 'لا بمعنى أن تكون مركبة'.
The author is careful to exclude the idea that perfect cause must be composite.
2
Understand the meaning of perfect cause
A perfect cause is 'all that the thing needs'. This does not mean it is composed of multiple parts; rather, nothing else is required for the effect to exist.
3
Understand the imperfect cause
An imperfect cause is 'some of that' – it provides only a portion of what the effect needs, leaving a remaining dependency.
4
Reflect on common confusion
Many beginners think 'perfect' means 'made of many parts'. The author explicitly corrects this: it means 'sufficient', not 'composite'.
This is a key nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي العلة إما تامة هي جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء بمعنى أنه لا يبقى هناك أمر آخر يحتاج إليه، لا بمعنى أن تكون مركبة من عدة أمور البتة، وإما ناقصة بعض ذلك، والتامة قد تكون هي الفاعل وحده كالبسيط الموجد للبسيط إيجاباً وقد تكون هي مع الغاية كالبسيط الموجد للبسيط اختياراً، فإن فعل المختار قد يكون لغرض يدعو إليه، وقد يكون هو مع المادة والصورة أيضاً كالموجد للمركب عنهما، إما الغاية أو بدونها، وإذا كانت العلة التامة مشتملة على المادة والصورة يمتنع تقدمها على المعلول، واحتياج المعلول إليها أجزاء الشيء نفسه، وإنما التقدم لكل جزء منها، فما يقال ضرورة أن العلة التامة يجب تقدمها على المعلول ليس على إطلاقه، بل العلة الناقصة أو هي الفاعل وحده، أو مع الشرط والغاية.
It (the cause) is either a perfect cause, which is all that the thing needs, meaning that there remains no other thing it needs, not meaning that it is composed of several things absolutely, or an imperfect cause, which is some of that. And the perfect cause may be the agent alone, like the simple (cause) bringing the simple into existence necessarily, and may be with the goal, like the simple bringing the simple into existence by choice, for the act of the chooser may be for a purpose that calls to it, and may be with the matter and form as well, like the one bringing the composite into existence from them, either with the goal or without it. And when the perfect cause includes the matter and form, it is impossible for it to precede the effect, and the effect's need of it is the parts of the thing itself; rather, the priority is for each of its parts. So what is said necessarily that the perfect cause must precede the effect is not absolute; rather, the imperfect cause is either the agent alone, or with the condition and the goal.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the perfect cause (علة تامة) and imperfect cause (علة ناقصة). The author explains that a perfect cause encompasses everything the effect requires, not meaning it is composite but that nothing else is needed. An imperfect cause provides only part of what is needed. Examples are given: a simple cause acting necessarily (like the Intellect in philosophy) is the agent alone; when acting by choice, the goal is also part of the perfect cause. For composites, the perfect cause includes matter and form. Importantly, when the perfect cause contains the effect's own material and formal parts, it cannot temporally precede the effect because those parts constitute the effect itself. Thus, the common claim that the perfect cause must precede is not universal; the imperfect cause — whether the agent alone or with condition and goal — does precede.

Key Terms
علة تامة
علة ناقصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfect cause is always made of many parts (composite).
Correction: The author explicitly states 'not meaning that it is composed of several things absolutely'. Perfect cause means sufficient, not multi-part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'perfect cause' NOT mean?
A) That it is composed of several things absolutely
B) That it is all that the thing needs
C) That it leaves no other need
D) That it is a cause

Key Takeaway
Perfect cause is sufficient by itself; imperfect cause is only a part of what is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What imperfect causes (علة ناقصة) do you rely on that can never complete what you truly need? How does this realization push you to turn to the Perfect Cause alone?
?
Examine your life: which causes have you mistaken as perfect when they are merely partial? How does this awareness change your reliance on them and your trust in the One who is truly sufficient?
?
If the perfect cause is that which needs nothing else, what does that say about your own dependence? Are you content to be completely dependent on the Perfect Cause, or do you secretly desire to be self-sufficient like Him?

Types of Perfect Cause and Precedence
Author lists types of perfect cause and clarifies temporal precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different types of perfect cause (agent alone, with goal, with matter/form) and the special case where the perfect cause does not precede its effect.

Core Idea
A perfect cause can include the effect's own parts, making temporal precedence impossible.

After defining perfect and imperfect cause, the author now gives concrete examples. These examples illustrate how the perfect cause can vary depending on whether the effect is simple or composite, and whether the agent acts necessarily or by choice. A crucial point emerges: when the perfect cause contains the matter and form of the effect, it cannot temporally precede the effect, because those parts constitute the effect itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'والتامة قد تكون' to the end. Notice the different cases: agent alone, agent+goal, agent+matter+form.
The examples use philosophical terminology like 'بسيط' (simple) and 'مركب' (composite).
2
Identify the three types of perfect cause
List them: (1) agent alone for necessary simple, (2) agent+goal for volitional simple, (3) agent+matter+form±goal for composite.
3
Understand the precedence issue
When the perfect cause includes the matter and form (essence) of the effect, it cannot come before the effect temporally; rather, the effect's need for the cause is intrinsic, not prior.
The author corrects the common saying that the perfect cause must always precede.
4
Distinguish perfect and imperfect cause in this context
The imperfect cause (agent alone or with condition and goal) does precede; the perfect cause that includes essence does not.
5
Reflect on the philosophical implications
This nuance is important for understanding the relationship between Creator and creation, especially in kalam arguments about temporal origination.
Think about how this applies to Allah as cause of the world.

The Seven Binary Divisions of Causes
Author enumerates seven binary divisions for each of the four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven binary divisions that apply to each of the four causes in kalam causality.

Core Idea
Every cause can be analyzed through seven binary pairs.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic classification of the four causes (efficient, material, formal, final) into seven binary divisions. This framework is essential for precise causal analysis in Kalam. Understanding these divisions allows you to describe any causal relationship in terms of simple/compound, universal/particular, essential/accidental, proximate/remote, general/specific, common/exclusive, and potential/actual. Let's explore each division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice that it lists seven pairs of terms.

2
Identify the seven binary pairs

3
Understand the scope
Realize that these divisions apply to all four causes, not just one. The passage explicitly says 'each of the four is divided' and 'each of the four causes is divided.'
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why such a classification is useful. It allows scholars to precisely describe the nature of a cause in terms of these attributes, aiding in causal analysis.
This framework is foundational in Kalam discussions of causality.

Then he rephrases the same division, emphasizing that each cause is considered under each of these seven pairs.
