Necessity of the Effect When the Cause is Complete
Sanusi proves the mutual necessary connection between the complete efficient cause and its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutual necessity between a complete cause and its effect, and why the cause's priority is essential, not temporal.

Core Idea
A complete cause makes its effect necessary; they are mutually dependent in existence.

This passage establishes a foundational principle in Islamic causal reasoning: if a cause is fully complete, its effect must exist, and vice versa. The author, Sanusi, proves this using the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderant and the nature of contingency. We will see how this leads to the conclusion that the cause is prior by essence, not by time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then follow with the English translation. Let the author's precise argument sink in without over-analyzing yet.
Notice the two 'يجب' statements: one for the effect, one for the cause.
2
Identify the two directions of necessity
The passage argues both that if the complete cause exists, the effect must exist; and if the effect exists, the complete cause must exist. Find the proof for each direction.
The first uses the principle of no preponderance without a preponderant. The second uses the concept of contingency.
3
Understand essential vs temporal priority
The passage concludes that the cause does not precede the effect in time, but only by essence (need). Reflect on why temporal priority would contradict mutual necessity.
This resolves the apparent problem of how an eternal creator relates to temporal events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : يجب وجود المعلول عند تمام الفاعل لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح، وبالعكس لكون الاحتياج من لوازم الإمكان، فتقدمه لا يكون إلا بالذات واستناد الحادث إلى القديم لا يكون إلا بشرط حادث يقارنه لتعلق الإرادة. يعني إذا وجد الفاعل بجميع جهات التأثير من الشرط والآلات والقابل يجب وجود المعلول، إذ لو جاز عدمه لكان وجوده بعد ذلك ترجحاً بلا مرجح، لأن التقدير حصول جميع جهات التأثير من غير أن يبقى معه شيء مرجح، يجب الترجيح، وإذا وجد المعلول يجب وجود الفاعل بجميع جهات التأثير، لأن الاحتياج إلى المؤثر التام من لوازم الإمكان والإمكان من لوازم المعلول فلو لم يجب وجود المؤثر التام عند وجود المعلول، لزم جواز وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم هذا خلف، وإذا كان بين المؤثر التام ومعلوله تلازم في الوجود، لم يكن للمؤثر تقدم عليه بالزمان، بل بالذات بمعنى الاحتياج.
The second investigation: The existence of the effect is necessary when the agent is complete, because preponderance without a preponderant is impossible; and conversely [the existence of the agent is necessary when the effect exists] because the need [for the agent] is among the necessary concomitants of contingency. So its priority is only by essence, and the dependence of the originated upon the eternal is only with an originated condition that is simultaneous with it due to the attachment of will. Meaning: When the agent exists with all aspects of influence—condition, instruments, and recipient—the existence of the effect is necessary. For if its non-existence were possible, its existence thereafter would be a preponderance without a preponderant, because the assumption is that all aspects of influence have been obtained without any preponderant remaining with it; preponderance must occur. And when the effect exists, the existence of the agent with all aspects of influence is necessary, because the need for the complete efficient cause is among the necessary concomitants of contingency, and contingency is among the necessary concomitants of the effect. So if the existence of the complete efficient cause were not necessary when the effect exists, then the existence of the necessary condition without its necessary concomitant would be possible—this is a contradiction. And when there is a mutual necessity in existence between the complete efficient cause and its effect, then the cause does not have temporal priority over it, but only essential priority meaning the need.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the mutual necessity between the complete efficient cause and its effect. It states two directions: (1) If the complete cause exists, the effect must exist, because otherwise its later existence would be a preponderance without a preponderant, which is impossible. (2) If the effect exists, the complete cause must also exist with all aspects of influence, because the effect is essentially contingent, and contingency requires a cause; if the cause were not necessary, the contingent could exist without its necessary concomitant, which is absurd. The passage further concludes that the priority of the cause over the effect is not temporal but essential (priority of need). The explanation (يعني) clarifies that when the agent is complete with all conditions, instruments, and the recipient, the effect must exist; otherwise, the delay would be arbitrary. Similarly, when the effect exists, the agent must exist because the effect's contingency entails a cause. Thus, there is a two-way necessary connection (تلازم) in existence, and the cause is only prior by essence (that is, the effect depends on the cause, not temporally).

Correction: The passage argues that priority is essential (dependency), not temporal. Cause and effect are simultaneous in existence; the cause is prior only in the sense that the effect depends on it.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause and its effect necessarily coexist, with the cause being prior only in terms of the effect's dependency.

The Necessity of the Effect Upon the Complete Cause and Its Converse
Taftazani states and explains the principle of cause-effect necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the complete cause necessarily produces its effect and why the effect necessarily entails the complete cause, based on the principles of preponderance and need.

Core Idea
The complete cause and its effect are mutually necessary: one cannot exist without the other.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid lays down a foundational principle of causality: the complete cause necessarily brings about its effect, and conversely, the effect cannot exist without its complete cause. Taftazani provides two key justifications: the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and the fact that need for an effective cause is inherent to possibility. As you read, notice how he explains the logical relationship between cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold in his own terms.
Pay attention to the key terms like تمام الفاعل and الترجيح بلا مرجح.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two central claims: (1) when the agent is complete, the effect must exist; (2) when the effect exists, the agent must be complete. Highlight these in the text.
3
Understand the justifications
Taftazani justifies the first claim by the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and the second by the fact that need for an effective cause is a necessary concomitant of possibility. Work through each justification step by step.
Consider why these justifications are logically compelling.
4
Reflect on essential vs. temporal priority
The passage emphasizes that the cause's priority over the effect is essential, not temporal, because a temporal effect from an eternal cause requires a coincident condition (like will). Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : يجب وجود المعلول عند تمام الفاعل لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح، وبالعكس لكون الاحتياج من لوازم الإمكان، فتقدمه لا يكون إلا بالذات واستناد الحادث إلى القديم لا يكون إلا بشرط حادث يقارنه لتعلق الإرادة. يعني إذا وجد الفاعل بجميع جهات التأثير من الشرط والآلات والقابل يجب وجود المعلول، إذ لو جاز عدمه لكان وجوده بعد ذلك ترجحاً بلا مرجح، لأن التقدير حصول جميع جهات التأثير من غير أن يبقى معه شيء مرجح، يجب الترجح، وإذا وجد المعلول يجب وجود الفاعل بجميع جهات التأثير، لأن الاحتياج إلى المؤثر التام من لوازم الإمكان والإمكان من لوازم المعلول فلو لم يجب وجود المؤثر التام عند وجود المعلول، لزم جواز وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم هذا خلف.
The second investigation: The existence of the effect is necessary when the agent is complete, due to the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and conversely due to need being among the necessary concomitants of possibility. So its priority is only by essence, and the dependence of the originated on the eternal is only with a originated condition that coincides with it, due to the attachment of will. Meaning, if the agent exists with all its aspects of influence—condition, instruments, and receiver—the existence of the effect is necessary, for if its non-existence were permissible, then its existence after that would be a preponderance without a preponderator, because it is assumed that all aspects of influence have occurred with nothing remaining as a preponderator, so preponderance would be necessary. And if the effect exists, the existence of the agent with all its aspects of influence is necessary, because the need for the perfect effective cause is among the necessary concomitants of possibility, and possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the effect. So if the existence of the perfect effective cause were not necessary when the effect exists, then the existence of the necessary concomitant (the effect) without the necessary condition (the perfect cause) would be permissible—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines and explains a fundamental principle of causal necessity in kalam: the complete cause (al-fāʿil al-tāmm) necessitates its effect, and conversely the effect necessitates the complete cause. The author begins by stating the principle: 'The existence of the effect is necessary when the agent is complete.' The justification is the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator (imtināʿ al-tarjīḥ bi-lā murjīḥ). If the cause is complete with all conditions and instruments, the effect cannot fail to exist, otherwise its later existence would be arbitrary. Then he states the converse: if the effect exists, the complete cause must exist, because the effect is possible (mumkin), and possibility entails need for an effective cause (muʾaththir tāmm). He emphasizes that the priority of the cause is not temporal but essential (bi-l-dhāt): the cause precedes the effect in essence, not in time, because the origin of the temporal (al-ḥādith) from the eternal (al-qadīm) requires a coincident condition (sharṭ ḥādith), such as the attachment of will (taʿalluq al-irāda). The explanation ('yaʿnī') elaborates: if the agent exists with all aspects of influence (sharṭ, ālāt, qābil), the effect must exist; otherwise its existence would be a preponderance without a preponderator. Conversely, if the effect exists, the agent with all aspects must exist, otherwise the concomitant (effect) would exist without its necessary condition (perfect cause), which is impossible. This establishes a necessary concomitance (talāzum) between the complete cause and its effect.

الاحتياج من لوازم الإمكان
الشرط
الآلات
القابل
المؤثر التام
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the cause must precede the effect in time.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the priority is essential (bi-l-dhāt), not temporal, because the temporal effect from an eternal cause requires a coincident condition like will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the effect exist when the agent is complete?
A) Because the agent is eternal and the effect is temporal.
B) Because otherwise the existence of the effect would be a preponderance without a preponderator.
C) Because the effect is necessary in itself.
D) Because the agent has will.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause and its effect are mutually necessary: the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator and the entailment of need by possibility establish this fundamental causal principle.

The First Objection and Answer: Temporal Priority and the Coincident Condition
Taftazani presents an objection about temporal priority and answers by citing the coincident condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection of temporal priority resolves the apparent problem of an eternal cause producing a temporal effect through an originated condition that coincides with the effect.

Core Idea
An eternal cause can produce a temporal effect by including an originated condition that coincides with the effect.

At this point in the discussion, a significant objection arises: if the complete cause must be simultaneous with its effect, how can an eternal cause produce a temporal effect, which is posterior in time? Taftazani presents this objection and then provides the standard theological answer, which invokes an originated condition (sharṭ ḥādith) that coincides with the effect. This key concept, familiar from discussions of divine will, resolves the apparent contradiction. Let's examine the objection and its rebuttal carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening sentence of the passage: 'If it is said: If this is correct, then it would not be permissible for the originated to depend on the eternal because it is temporally posterior to it.' This is the core objection: temporal posteriority seems to break the causal link.
2
Identify the answer's key move
The answer introduces an 'originated condition' (شرط حادث) that is part of the complete cause and coincides with the effect. This condition bridges the temporal gap.
The condition is not part of the eternal essence but is a temporal aspect of the causation.
3
Note the examples given
According to theologians, this condition is the attachment of will (تعلق الإرادة); according to philosophers, it is movements and positions (الحركات والأوضاع). These are temporal items that come into existence with the effect.
These examples show that the condition is not the eternal essence but something that arises in time.
4
Grasp the distinction
The temporal priority belongs only to the essence of the agent (ذات الفاعل), not to the agent with all its aspects of influence (جهات التأثير). The complete cause, including the condition, is simultaneous with the effect.
This distinction resolves the objection: the complete cause does not temporally precede the effect.
5
Confirm the resolution
The objection is thus answered: there is no temporal priority of the complete cause over the effect because the complete cause includes a temporal condition that arises at the same moment as the effect.
Thus, the principle that the complete cause necessitates the effect simultaneously is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو صح هذا لما جاز استناد الحادث إلى القديم لتأخره عنه بالزمان . قلنا من جملة جهات تأثير القديم في الحادث شرط حادث يقارن الأثر الحادث كتعلق الإرادة عندنا والحركات والأوضاع عند الفلاسفة، فيكون التقدم بالزمان لذات الفاعل، ولا نزاع فيه لا للفاعل مع جميع جهات التأثير.
If it is said: If this is correct, then it would not be permissible for the originated to depend on the eternal because it is temporally posterior to it. We say: Among the aspects of influence of the eternal upon the originated is an originated condition that coincides with the originated effect, such as the attachment of will according to us, and movements and positions according to the philosophers. Thus the temporal priority belongs to the essence of the agent, and there is no dispute about that, not to the agent with all its aspects of influence.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection argues: if the complete cause necessitates the effect as claimed, then it would be impossible for the originated (al-ḥādith) to depend on the eternal (al-qadīm) because the originated is temporally posterior to the eternal, yet the principle seems to require simultaneity. The answer clarifies that among the aspects of influence (jihat al-taʾthīr) of the eternal upon the originated is an originated condition (sharṭ ḥādith) that coincides with the originated effect. According to the theologians (ʿindanā), this condition is the attachment of will (taʿalluq al-irāda); according to the philosophers, it is movements and positions (al-ḥarakāt wa al-awḍāʿ). Thus, the temporal priority belongs to the essence of the agent (dhāt al-fāʿil), not to the agent with all its aspects of influence. The agent's essence is eternal, but the actual causation involves a condition that arises with the effect, so there is no temporal gap; the effect is simultaneous with that condition. The author notes that there is no dispute about the temporal priority of the essence—it is agreed that the eternal essence precedes the temporal effect in time—but the complete cause (with all conditions) does not precede temporally.

Key Terms
استناد الحادث إلى القديم
جهات التأثير
الشرط الحادث
تعلق الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entire cause of a temporal effect must be eternal.
Correction: The eternal essence is prior, but the complete cause includes an originated condition that coincides with the effect, so the effect is not temporally posterior to the complete cause.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's answer, what enables the originated effect to depend on the eternal cause despite temporal posteriority?
A) The eternal cause is timeless.
B) There is an originated condition that coincides with the effect.
C) The effect is itself eternal.
D) The cause causes the effect at all times.

Key Takeaway
The temporal priority of the eternal cause is resolved by an originated condition that coincides with the effect.

The Act of Causing and Its Temporal Relation
Taftazani refutes the claim that the act of causing must be temporally after the cause's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of causing (ījād) by the complete cause is simultaneous with the cause's existence, not temporally posterior.

Core Idea
Causation by a complete cause is concurrent, not sequential.

This passage presents a second objection against the Ashʿarī view of causation, followed by a crisp refutation. The objector claims that common sense demands that causing occurs after the cause exists. Taftazani denies the temporal posteriority for the complete cause, asserting simultaneity. Let's unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that causing must be after the cause's existence. The objector appeals to necessity (al-ḍarūra).
2
Notice the concession
The objector concedes that the effect may be immediate (ʿaqīb) without temporal interval, but still insists it is posterior.
The phrase 'عقيبه من غير تخلل زمان' is key.
3
Examine the refutation
Taftazani denies that the causing is temporally posterior to the cause with all aspects of influence (مع جميع جهات التأثير). This means for the complete cause, causation is simultaneous.
The denial is a simple 'ممنوع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الضرورة قاضية بأن إيجاد العلة للمعلول لا يكون إلا بعد وجودها، ووجود المعلول، إما مقارن للإيجاد أو متأخر عنه فيكون متأخراً عن وجود العلة . غاية الأمر أن يكون عقيبه من غير تخلل زمان لئلا يلزم الترجح بلا مرجح. قلنا : كون الإيجاد بعد وجود العلل مع جميع جهات التأثير بعدية زمانية ممنوع.
If it is said: Necessity dictates that the causing of the effect by the cause does not occur except after its existence, and the existence of the effect is either simultaneous with the causing or posterior to it, so it is posterior to the existence of the cause. At most, it is immediately after it without an intervening time, to avoid preponderance without a preponderator. We say: That the causing is after the existence of the causes with all aspects of influence is temporally posterior—this is denied.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection claims that common sense (al-ḍarūra) dictates that the act of causing (ījād) by the cause can only occur after the cause itself exists. Then the effect's existence is either simultaneous with the causing or after it, so in either case the effect is temporally posterior to the cause's existence. The objector concedes that the effect may be immediate (ʿaqīb) without temporal interval, but still posterior. The answer is a denial: the claim that the causing occurs temporally after the existence of the cause with all its aspects of influence (maʿa jamīʿ jihat al-taʾthīr) is not accepted (mamnūʿ). The complete cause, including all conditions and instruments, does not have temporal posteriority for its act of causing. In other words, when the cause is complete, the causing and the effect are simultaneous; there is no temporal gap between the existence of the complete cause and its act of causing. The objection incorrectly assumes that the cause's existence precedes its causation, but for the complete cause, causation is concurrent with its existence.

Key Terms
إيجاد العلة
بعدية زمانية
عقيبه من غير تخلل زمان
الترجح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that causation always involves a temporal sequence, i.e., the cause first exists, then it causes, then the effect exists.
Correction: The answer clarifies that for the complete cause, causation is simultaneous with the cause's existence. There is no temporal posteriority for the act of causing.

Checkpoint
What is Taftazani's response to the objection that causing must be after the cause's existence?
A) He accepts it and says the effect is immediate.
B) He denies that the causing is temporally posterior for the complete cause.
C) He argues that the cause is eternal so no temporal sequence applies.
D) He says the causing is before the cause's existence.

Key Takeaway
Causation by a complete cause is simultaneous with the cause's existence, not temporally posterior.

The objector made a small concession—immediate causation—to avoid a bigger problem. In your own faith, have you ever compromised on a truth to avoid a difficult question? Are you willing to accept the full truth of God's power, even if it feels uncomfortable? What might that cost you?
?
Taftazani simply denies the false premise. When you encounter doubts about your beliefs, do you have the courage to reject the underlying assumption outright, or do you try to accommodate it? Will you, starting today, train yourself to identify and deny false premises with the same clarity?

Objection on Temporal Precedence and Answer Regarding Simultaneous Condition
Objection on temporal precedence and answer with simultaneous condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternity of the cause is reconciled with the temporal origination of effects through the concept of a simultaneous temporal condition.

Core Idea
The eternal cause acts through a temporal condition, not by its essence alone.

This lesson presents a key objection in causal reasoning: if an eternal cause is truly eternal, how can it bring about a temporal effect without temporal priority? The answer introduces the notion of a simultaneous temporal condition, such as will, that is created at the moment of the effect. This preserves both the eternity of the cause and the temporality of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if the cause is eternal and the effect is temporal, the effect is later in time, so how can the eternal cause be its source? This is the problem of temporal precedence.
The objection assumes that causation requires temporal proximity.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer distinguishes between the essence of the cause and its aspects of influence. The eternal cause's essence is pre-eternal, but it brings about the effect through a temporal condition that coincides with the effect.
Key term: شرط حادث (originated condition).
3
Reflect on the examples
Notice the examples given: the attachment of will (in Islamic theology) and movements/positions (in philosophy). These are temporal conditions that occur simultaneously with the effect.
This shows how different schools use similar reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو صح هذا لما جاز استناد الحادث إلى التقديم لتأخره عنه بالزمان . قلنا من جملة جهات تأثير القديم في الحادث شرط حادث يقارن الأثر الحادث كتعلق الإرادة عندنا والحركات والأوضاع عند الفلاسفة، فيكون التقدم بالزمان لذات الفاعل، ولا نزاع فيه لا للفاعل مع جميع جهات التأثير.
For if it is said: If this were valid, then it would not be permissible for the originated event to be attributed to the pre-eternal cause due to its temporal posteriority. We say: Among the aspects of influence of the eternal upon the originated is an originated condition that coincides with the originated effect, like the attachment of will according to us and the movements and positions according to the philosophers, so the temporal precedence is for the essence of the agent, and there is no dispute about that, not for the agent with all aspects of influence.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if the effect depends entirely on the cause, then an originated event cannot be attributed to an eternal cause because the event occurs later in time. The answer distinguishes between the cause's essence and its aspects of influence. The eternal cause, by its essence, is pre-eternal, but it brings about the effect through a temporal condition (like will or movement) that coincides with the effect. Thus the temporal precedence belongs to the essence, not to the complete cause with all its aspects. The answer preserves both the eternity of the cause and the temporal origination of the effect.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The answer shows that the eternal cause can have a temporal condition (like will) that is created at the moment of the effect, so the cause's essence remains eternal while the effect is temporally originated.

Objection and Answer on Temporal Posteriority
Objection and answer on temporal posteriority of effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that effects must be temporally posterior to causes and the response that temporal posteriority is denied when all aspects of the cause are considered.

Core Idea
Temporal posteriority of effects is not necessary; logical order suffices.

This passage presents a subtle objection and answer concerning causation. The objector argues that the act of bringing into existence must occur after the cause's existence, making the effect temporally later. The author denies that this posteriority is temporal when the complete cause with all its influencing aspects is taken into account. This preserves the possibility of simultaneous causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: 'if it is said... we say...'.
The objection is a common one in kalām.
2
Analyze the objection's logic
The objector claims that bringing into existence (إيجاد) must be after the cause's existence, and effect existence is either simultaneous with or after الإيجاد, so effect is after cause. Even if immediate, still after.
3
Grasp the answer's key move
The author denies that the posteriority is temporal (بعدية زمانية) when considering all aspects of influence. This means logical priority, not temporal delay.
This distinction is crucial for simultaneous causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الضرورة قاضية بأن إيجاد العلة للمعلول لا يكون إلا بعد وجودها، ووجود المعلول، إما مقارن للإيجاد أو متأخر عنه فيكون متأخراً عن وجود العلة . غاية الأمر أن يكون عقيبه من غير تخلل زمان لئلا يلزم الترجح بلا مرجح . قلنا : كون الإيجاد بعد وجود العلل مع جميع جهات التأثير بعدية زمانية ممنوع .
For if it is said: Necessity judges that the bringing into existence of the effect by the cause is only after the existence of the cause, and the existence of the effect is either simultaneous with the bringing into existence or posterior to it, so it is posterior to the existence of the cause. At most, it is immediately after it without temporal interval to prevent preponderance without a preponderator. We say: That the bringing into existence being after the existence of the causes with all aspects of influence is a temporal posteriority is denied.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the act of bringing into existence (إيجاد) necessarily occurs after the cause's existence, so the effect is temporally posterior to the cause. Even if it is immediate, it is still after. The answer denies that this posteriority is temporal when considering the complete cause with all its aspects. This preserves the possibility of simultaneous causation without temporal gap.

Key Terms
إيجاد
بعدية زمانية
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any posteriority of the effect to the cause must be temporal.
Correction: The author denies that the posteriority of bringing into existence to the cause's existence is temporal; it is logical posteriority only.

Checkpoint
What does the author deny in his answer?
A) That the effect exists after the cause
B) That the bringing into existence is after the cause's existence
C) That the posteriority is temporal when considering all aspects of influence
D) That the cause exists before the effect

Key Takeaway
The author rejects the necessity of temporal posteriority for causation, allowing for simultaneous causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something, do you expect an answer only after a certain amount of time? How does the rejection of necessary temporal posteriority challenge your expectation? Will you now see God's response as possibly immediate at the level of existence, not just delayed?
?
Consider a moment you attributed a result to a natural cause, like medicine healing you. Did you assume the cause and effect were temporally separate? How does this lesson invite you to reconsider the role of Allah as the immediate Creator without temporal delay?
?
The objector's argument seems intuitive: cause first, effect later. But the author denies this as a necessity. Are there areas of your life where you unconsciously assume that time must pass between your action and Allah's decree? How can you live with the certainty that Allah's power is not bound by time?

The Dependence of Effect's Non-Existence on Cause's Non-Existence
The author states the principle of non-existence dependence and explains via contrapositive and preparative/efficient distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the non-existence of an effect depends on the non-existence of its cause, including the contrapositive relationship and the distinction between preparative and efficient causes.

Core Idea
The non-existence of an effect requires the non-existence of its complete cause.

This lesson establishes a key principle of causal reasoning: not only does existence depend on the cause, but non-existence does as well. The author uses the logical tool of contrapositive to explain this, and he carefully distinguishes between preparative causes (where the effect can outlast the cause) and efficient causes (where it cannot). This principle is crucial for understanding how God's agency relates to all contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that the non-existence of the effect depends on the non-existence of the cause, even for non-stationary things like motion.
Notice the phrase 'ولو في غير القار'.
2
Understand the contrapositive
The author explains that from the causal rule (cause exists → effect exists), it follows by contrapositive that if the effect is absent, the cause is absent. This is a logical necessity.
The term 'عكس النقيض' is used.
3
Identify the distinction between preparative and efficient causes
The author clarifies that survival of an effect after the cause's removal is only possible in preparative causes (معدات), not efficient causes (مؤثرات). Examples: son after father, building after builder, heat after fire.
This distinction answers the final objection later.
4
Examine the clarification on non-stationary accidents
The author addresses a potential misunderstanding about motion and time, which seem to cease naturally even if the cause remains. He delays full discussion but notes that their essence involves renewal.
This shows the principle is still valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فعدم المعلول ولو في غير القار كالحركة يفتقر إلى عدم العلة ولو ببعض الشروط، وعدم الثبوت لا ينافي الشرطية بهذا المعنى، فالفاعل في طرفي الممكن واحد يجب بوجوده وجوده وبعدمه عدمه، إن سابقاً فسابق وإن لاحقاً فلاحق، وبقاء المعلول عند انعدام العلة إنما يتصور في المعدات كالابن بعد الأب والبناء بعد البناء، وسخونة الماء بعد النار لا في المؤثرات) . يعني لما ثبت أنه كلما وجدت العلة بتجميع جهات التأثير وجد المعلول لزمه بحكم عكس النقيض أنه كلما انتفى المعلول انتفت العلة التامة إما بذاتها أو ببعض جهات تأثيرها وأكد الحكم بقوله : ولو في غير القار لأنه قد يتوهم أن الأعراض الغير القارة كالحركة والزمان قد ينعدم أجزاؤها مع بقاء العلة بتمامها لكونها بحسب ذاتها على التجدد والانصرام بمعنى أن ذاتها تقتضي عدم كل جزء بعد الوجود، وإن بقيت علته، وستطلع على حقيقة الحال في بحث الحركة فإن قيل كل من العدمين نفي محض لا ثبوت له فكيف يكون أثراً أو مؤثراً . قلنا بل عدم مضاف لا يمتنع كون أحدهما محتاجاً والآخر محتاجاً إليه، وهذا معنى المعلولية والعلية ههنا لا التأثر والتأثير، وإذا ثبت أن وجود الممكن يفتقر إلى وجود علته وعدمه إلى علته ظهر أن الفاعل في طرفي الممكن أعني وجوده وعدمه واحد، يجب بوجوده وجوده وبعدمه عدمه، أما عدمه السابق فبعدمه السابق بمعنى أن عدم حدوث الحادث محتاج إلى عدم حدوث فاعله بجميع جهات التأثير وأما عدمه اللاحق فبعدمه اللاحق يعني أن زوال وجوده يحتاج إلى زوال وجود الفاعل بجميع جهات التأثير. فإن قيل : ما ذكرتم من انعدام المعلول عند انعدام العلة باطل لما نشاهد من بقاء الابن بعد الأب، والبناء بعد البناء، وسخونة الماء بعد النار .
He said: So the non-existence of the effect, even in non-stationary things like motion, depends on the non-existence of the cause, even with some conditions. And the lack of establishment does not contradict conditionality in this sense. So the agent in the two aspects of the possible is one: by its existence its existence is necessary, and by its non-existence its non-existence is necessary, whether earlier then earlier, later then later. And the survival of the effect when the cause is absent is only conceivable in preparatives, like the son after the father, the building after the builder, and the heat of water after the fire, not in the efficient causes. That is, since it is established that whenever the cause exists with the totality of aspects of influence, the effect exists, it follows by the law of contrapositive that whenever the effect is absent, the complete cause is absent, either in its essence or in some aspects of its influence. And he emphasized the judgment by saying: 'even in non-stationary things', because it might be imagined that non-stationary accidents like motion and time have their parts ceasing to exist while the complete cause remains, because they are by their essence in a state of renewal and cessation, meaning that their essence necessitates the non-existence of each part after existence, even if its cause remains. And you will come to know the true nature of the matter in the discussion of motion. For if it is said: Each of the two non-existences is a pure negation with no positive reality, so how can one be an effect and the other a cause? We say: Rather it is a relative non-existence; it is not impossible for one to be in need and the other to be needed, and this is the meaning of effecthood and causality here, not being influenced and influencing. And when it is established that the existence of the possible depends on the existence of its cause and its non-existence on its cause, it becomes clear that the agent in the two aspects of the possible, i.e. its existence and non-existence, is one; by its existence its existence is necessary, and by its non-existence its non-existence is necessary. As for its prior non-existence, it is by the prior non-existence, meaning that the non-occurrence of the originated event depends on the non-occurrence of its agent with all aspects of influence. And as for its posterior non-existence, it is by its posterior non-existence, meaning that the extinction of its existence depends on the extinction of the existence of the agent with all aspects of influence. For if it is said: What you have mentioned about the non-existence of the effect when the cause is non-existent is false, because we observe the survival of the son after the father, the building after the builder, and the heat of water after the fire.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of the dependence of the effect's non-existence on the cause's non-existence. It begins by stating that even for non-stationary things like motion, the non-existence of the effect requires the non-existence of the cause, possibly with some conditions. The agent is the same for both existence and non-existence. Survival of an effect after the cause's removal only occurs in preparatives (معدات), not in efficient causes (مؤثرات). The author then explains via contrapositive that if the effect is absent, the complete cause is absent. He addresses a potential misunderstanding about non-stationary accidents by noting that their parts cease by their nature, not necessarily due to the cause's absence. Then an objection is raised: non-existences are pure negations, so how can one be dependent on another? The answer is that they are relative non-existences; dependency is possible. The passage then reiterates that the agent is one for both existence and prior/posterior non-existence. Finally, an objection is raised based on observed cases like son surviving father, but the answer is already given: these are preparatives, not efficient causes.

Key Terms
عدم
معلول
علة
معدات
مؤثرات
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an effect survives after the cause is removed (e.g., son after father), then the effect does not depend on the cause for its existence.
Correction: The author clarifies that survival after cause removal only occurs in preparative causes, not efficient causes. For efficient causes, the effect depends on the cause continuously.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the survival of an effect after the cause's removal occurs only in which type of causes?
A) Efficient causes
B) Preparative causes
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of an effect depends on the non-existence of its complete cause, with the important exception of preparative causes where the effect can outlast the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on many causes daily—your health, income, relationships—as if their presence guarantees your well-being. When any of these causes fail, do you immediately recognize that the true Complete Cause (Allah) has withdrawn His sustaining grace? How would your life change if you saw every loss as a sign from the Real Agent rather than a mere breakdown of means? Pause and identify one thing you are currently clinging to as if it were an efficient cause instead of a preparative one—then admit that you cannot survive its loss without the One who alone can make it continuous.
?
The lesson distinguishes between preparative and efficient causes. Think of an area in your life—your job, your health, a relationship—where you act as if the cause (e.g., your effort) is efficient and thus the effect must continue. But the source says only in preparatives does the effect outlast the cause. Are you assuming that your past righteousness or good deeds are efficient causes that guarantee future reward? Or do you treat them as preparatives that require Allah's continuous activation? Write down one deed you have done that you hope will benefit you after death, and ask yourself: 'Do I believe it works by itself (efficient) or only if Allah keeps it accepted (preparative)?' Let this lesson change how you pray for Allah to sustain your good works.
?
The author admits that motion and time seem to cease even while causes remain—a puzzle. Do you have something in your life—a spiritual high, a moment of closeness to Allah—that faded even when you felt the cause was still there (e.g., consistent worship)? Instead of dismissing this as a flaw in the causal principle, the author promises a deeper explanation in the discussion of motion. How willing are you to submit your own puzzlements to the scholars' explanation, rather than rejecting the principle? Commit right now to seeking knowledge on this topic if you truly want to understand, and do not let your limited experience shake your certainty in the causal order that points to Allah.

The Nature of Non-Existence: Relative Non-Existence as Cause and Effect
The author responds to objections about non-existence being pure negation and about observed survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that non-existence is a pure negation and how the author responds by defining non-existence as relative, thereby allowing it to be a cause or effect.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a cause or effect when considered as relative non-existence.

A potential problem arises: if non-existence is just nothing, how can it be a cause or effect? The author explains that the non-existence we are talking about is 'relative non-existence' (عدم مضاف), which is not a pure negation but a meaningful relation. This resolution is essential for understanding how God's agency extends to both the existence and non-existence of contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about pure negation
Read the objection that non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be a cause or effect.
The objector says: 'كل من العدمين نفي محض لا ثبوت له'.
2
Understand the answer: relative non-existence
The author replies that non-existence here is relative (مضاف), not absolute. Relative non-existence can enter into causal relations of need.
The term 'عدم مضاف' is key.
3
See the elaboration on agent unity
The author reiterates that the agent is one for both existence and non-existence, explaining prior and posterior non-existence.
Prior non-existence: the event never occurred; posterior: the event ceased.
4
Consider the second objection and its answer
The second objection cites observed survival (son after father, etc.). The answer is implicit in the earlier distinction between preparative and efficient causes.
Refer back to lesson 1 for the full answer.

The Contrapositive of the Causal Principle and Non-Permanent Accidents
Author explains contrapositive of causal principle and addresses non-permanent accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rule of contrapositive applies to the causal principle and why it holds even for non-permanent accidents like movement and time.

Core Idea
If the cause is present, the effect is present; thus if the effect is absent, the cause must be absent.

This passage extends the causal principle to its contrapositive and addresses a subtle objection regarding non-permanent accidents. The author explains that even when an accident like movement ceases part by part, the complete cause must have ceased in some aspect. We'll unpack this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the contrapositive
Read the Arabic passage and locate where the author states the contrapositive: 'كلما انتفى المعلول انتفت العلة التامة'. Notice the logical structure.
The contrapositive flips the original implication.
2
Distinguish preparatory from efficient causes
The author begins by listing preparatory causes (e.g., father, builder, fire) to clarify that the principle applies to efficient causes, not preparatory ones.
Preparatory causes are not the complete cause.
3
Understand non-permanent accidents
The author considers movement and time as examples. Although they cease part by part while the cause seems to remain, the cause's influence must have ceased in that aspect.
Their essence entails renewal, so partial cessation reflects a change in the cause's influence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for kalam arguments: causality is a universal principle that applies even to transient phenomena.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The preparatory causes like the son after the father, and the building after the builder, and the hotness of water after the fire, not in the efficient causes. It is imagined? meaning when it is established that whenever the cause is present with the combination of aspects of influence, the effect is present, it follows by the rule of contrapositive that whenever the effect is absent, the complete cause is absent, either in itself or in some of its aspects of influence. And he emphasized the rule by saying: even in non-permanent [accidents] because non-permanent accidents like movement and time may have their parts cease to exist while the cause remains complete, because they are by their essence subject to renewal and cessation, meaning that their essence necessitates the non-existence of every part after existence, even if its cause remains. And you will learn the true state of the matter in the discussion of movement. For if it is said: each of the two non-existences is pure negation, no positivity, so how can it be an effect or an agent? We say: rather it is a relative non-existence, it is not impossible that one is needful and the other is needed, and this is the meaning of effecthood and causality here, not being affected and affecting. And when it is established that the existence of the contingent depends on the existence of its cause, and its non-existence on its cause, it becomes apparent that the agent in the two aspects of the contingent, namely its existence and its non-existence, is one; by its existence, its existence is necessary, and by its non-existence, its non-existence. As for its prior non-existence, it is by its prior non-existence, meaning that the non-occurrence of an event depends on the non-occurrence of its agent with all aspects of influence. And as for its posterior non-existence, it is by its posterior non-existence, meaning that the cessation of its existence requires the cessation of the existence of the agent with all aspects of influence.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about causality. It begins by distinguishing preparatory causes (e.g., father for son, builder for building, fire for water heat) from efficient causes. The author then explains that the principle 'when the cause is present, the effect is present' implies, by contrapositive, that when the effect is absent the cause must be absent either in itself or in some aspect. This holds even for non-permanent accidents like movement and time, which can cease partially while their cause remains because their essence involves renewal. An objection is raised: non-existence is pure negation, so how can it be an effect or cause? The answer is that it is relative non-existence, which can be a term of relation. The conclusion is that both the existence and non-existence of a contingent depend on the same agent: by its existence the contingent exists, by its non-existence it ceases. The prior non-existence (before creation) depends on the agent's not creating, and the posterior non-existence (perishing) depends on the agent's ceasing to sustain.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
عكس النقيض
أعراض غير قارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-permanent accidents can cease without any change in the cause.
Correction: Their cessation implies either the cause itself or its influence has ceased, because the accident's essence requires continual renewal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the rule of contrapositive imply about the complete cause when the effect is absent?
A) The cause must be absent in itself or in some aspect.
B) The cause may still be present.
C) The effect was never real.
D) The cause must be efficient.

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of the causal principle applies universally, including to non-permanent accidents, because their renewal and cessation imply a change in the cause's influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of your life do you rely on as if it were self-sustaining, ignoring that every moment—like your next heartbeat—is a non-permanent accident that requires constant renewal from its Cause? How can you remind yourself of this dependency daily?

If you find a lack of spiritual effect (e.g., peace, taqwa) in your heart, the contrapositive says the cause is absent. What aspect of your cause (intention, sincerity, reliance on Allah) might be missing or weak, and what will you do to restore it tonight?
?
The passage shows that even when a cause seems to remain (like a fire), the ceasing of its effect (water no longer hot) means the cause's influence has ceased in that aspect. In your relationships or worship, when you see effects fading, will you honestly examine whether your internal cause (e.g., love, devotion) has ceased in that aspect, rather than blaming external factors?

Can Non-Existence Be an Effect? Resolving the Objection
Author resolves objection about non-existence as effect and concludes agent is one for both existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existence can be considered an effect in the context of causal dependence, and how the same agent is responsible for both the existence and non-existence of a contingent.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be an effect when understood as relative non-existence dependent on a cause.

This passage addresses a critical objection: how can non-existence, which is pure negation, be an effect or a cause? The author clarifies that relative non-existence can serve as a term of dependence, and that the same agent is responsible for both the existence and non-existence of a contingent being. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that non-existence (عدم) is pure negation and cannot be an effect or cause. Understand the intuition behind this doubt.
Negation seems to lack the positivity needed for causation.
2
Grasp the author's answer
The author distinguishes absolute non-existence from relative non-existence (عدم مضاف). Relative non-existence can be a term of need or dependence.
Causality here is about dependence, not efficient causation.
3
See the conclusion
The same agent is responsible for both existence and non-existence of a contingent: by its existence, the contingent exists; by its non-existence, it ceases.
This unifies the causal framework.
4
Distinguish prior and posterior non-existence
The author differentiates between non-existence before creation (prior) and after perishing (posterior), both dependent on the agent's non-existence in some sense.

Preparatory vs. Efficient Causes: Answering an Objection
Author refutes an objection by distinguishing between preparatory and efficient causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to distinguish between preparatory causes (علل معدّة) and efficient causes (علل مؤثرة) to resolve apparent counterexamples in causal reasoning.

Core Idea
Efficient causation ceases, but preparatory causation does not – that's why effects sometimes survive.

The principle that an effect ceases when its cause ceases seems contradicted by everyday examples: sons outlive fathers, buildings stand without builders, hot water stays hot after the fire is gone. In this passage, the author defends the principle by drawing a crucial distinction. You are about to see how he turns these apparent counterexamples into evidence for his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's argument carefully. It uses three examples to challenge the claim that effects vanish when causes vanish.
Notice the pattern: son/father, building/builder, hot water/fire.
2
Identify the author's answer
The author responds by distinguishing two types of causes. Find where he states the distinction and how he applies it to the father/son example.
The key terms are 'علة معدّة' and 'علة مؤثرة'.
3
Analyze the analogy
See how the author extends the same reasoning to the builder and the fire. Why does he say the building persists? What about on the Ash'ari view?
The last sentence references two different theological frameworks.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader argument about causality and divine power. The author is defending a specific causal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ذكرتم من انعدام المعلول عند انعدام العلة باطل لما نشاهد من بقاء الابن بعد الأب ، والبناء بعد البناء، وسخونة الماء بعد النار . قلنا : ذاك في العلل المعدة، وكلامنا في العلل المؤثرة. فالأب بالنسبة إلى الابن ليس إلا معداً للمادة لقبول الصورة، وإنما تأثيره في حركات وأفعال تفضي إلى ذلك وتنعدم بانعدام قصده ومباشرته وفي هذا قياس سائر الأمثلة. فإن البناء إنما يؤثر في حركات تفضي إلى ضم أجزاء البناء بعضها بالجواز إلى بعض، ووجوده إنما هو أثر التمسك المعلول ليس العنصر هذا على رأي الفلاسفة ، وأما على رأي القائلين باستناد الكل إلى الواجب بطريق الاختيار وتعلق الإرادة فالأمر بين.
For it is said: what you mentioned of the non-existence of the effect upon the non-existence of the cause is false, because we observe the survival of the son after the father, and the building after the builder, and the hotness of water after the fire. We say: that is in preparatory causes, and our discussion is in efficient causes. The father with respect to the son is only a preparer of the matter to receive the form, and his influence is only in movements and actions that lead to that, and they cease with the cessation of his intention and direct action. And in this is the analogy of the rest of the examples. For the builder only influences movements that lead to joining the parts of the building to each other by passing? and its existence is only the effect of holding? this is according to the philosophers. And as for according to those who say that everything depends on the Necessary Being by way of choice and attachment of will, the matter is clear.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector points to examples (son surviving father, building surviving builder, hot water after fire) that seem to contradict the claim that the effect ceases when the cause ceases. The author answers by distinguishing preparatory causes ('علل معدّة') from efficient causes ('علل مؤثرة'). The father is only preparatory, providing matter; his efficient causation lies in acts that cease when he stops intending. Similarly, the builder's efficient acts cease, and the building persists due to other causes. The author notes that on the philosophers' view this distinction holds, and on the Ash'ari view of constant divine creation it is even clearer.

Key Terms

علة مؤثرة
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think that if a son survives his father, the father cannot be a cause, or that the effect (son) can exist without a cause.
Correction: The father is a preparatory cause, not an efficient cause. The efficient cause (the father's actions) ceases, but the effect persists due to other causes.

Checkpoint

B) The father is only a preparatory cause.
C) The father is not a cause at all.
D) The father is a necessary cause.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between preparatory and efficient causes resolves apparent counterexamples and upholds the principle that the effect must cease when its efficient cause ceases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Cause of Existence vs. Cause of Persistence
Author states that the cause of existence may or may not be the same as the cause of persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the cause of a thing's existence and the cause of its persistence, and how they may differ.

Core Idea
The cause that brings something into being may not be the same as the cause that keeps it in being.

Have you ever wondered why a fire keeps burning? The initial spark causes it to start, but the fire continues only as long as fuel and oxygen are present. This lesson introduces a foundational concept in causal reasoning: the cause of origination (علة الحدوث) can be distinct from the cause of persistence (علة البقاء). The author illustrates this with two clear examples, one where they are identical and one where they are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully, noting the two examples given.
The passage is short but packed.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the example of the sun and light, and the example of fire contact and ignition. Determine which demonstrates sameness and which demonstrates difference.
The sun example shows the cause of existence is also cause of persistence.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters. How does it affect our understanding of causality and dependence?
This distinction is used in theological arguments about created things and their dependence on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المؤثر في الوجود مؤثر في البقاء (قال) : والمؤثر في الوجود قد يكون هو المؤثر في البقاء كالشمس للضوء وقد يكون غيره كمماسة النار للاشتعال واستمرارها بمعونة الأسباب لبقائه) . يريد أن ما يفيد وجود الشيء قد يفيد بقاءه من غير افتقار إلى أمر آخر كالشمس تفيد ضوء المقابل وبقاءه، وقد يفتقر البقاء إلى أمر آخر، وهذا ما يقال : إن علة الحدوث غير علة البقاء كمماسة النار تفيد الاشتعال ثم يفتقر بقاء الاشتعال إلى استدامة المماسة واستمرارها بتعاقب الأسباب.
The one who influences existence may be the one who influences persistence, like the sun for light, and may be other than it, like the contact of fire for ignition and its continuation with the assistance of causes for its persistence. He intends that that which gives existence to a thing may give it persistence without need for another, like the sun gives the light of the facing object and its persistence; and persistence may depend on another, and this is what is said: the cause of origination is different from the cause of persistence, like the contact of fire causes ignition, then the persistence of ignition depends on the continuation of contact and its continuation by successive causes.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains a distinction in causality: the cause that brings something into existence may also be the cause of its continued existence, or it may not. Two examples are given. The sun is both the cause of light's existence and persistence—as long as the sun shines, the light remains. Fire contact, however, causes ignition (the start of burning), but the persistence of burning requires continued contact and other supporting causes. Thus, the cause of origination ('علة الحدوث') can differ from the cause of persistence ('علة البقاء').

بقاء

علة البقاء

C) The builder for the building.

Key Takeaway
The cause that brings a thing into existence may be the same as or different from the cause that keeps it in existence, as illustrated by the sun (same) and fire contact (different).

The Cause of Existence and Continuance: Same or Different?
The author explains the relationship between the cause of existence and the cause of continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the cause of a thing's existence may also be its cause of continuance, or a different cause may be needed.

Core Idea
The cause of existence may not be the cause of persistence.

In studying causality, a key question is whether the same cause that brings something into being also keeps it in being. The author presents two possibilities: sometimes the same cause suffices, like the sun for light; sometimes a different cause is required, like the touch of fire for ignition needing sustained contact. This lesson breaks down this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. The author states a general rule about causality.
Note the key terms: مؤثر (cause), وجود (existence), بقاء (continuance).
2
Examine the first example
The sun is the cause of both the existence and continuance of light on an opposite object. When the sun disappears, the light ceases. Identify this as the case where the cause is the same.
3
Examine the second example
Fire's contact causes ignition, but the continuance of ignition requires sustained contact and other causes. This shows the cause of origin and persistence may differ.
4
Understand the terminology
The author uses 'علة الحدوث' (cause of origination) and 'علة البقاء' (cause of persistence). Understand that they can be different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المؤثر في الوجود قد يكون هو المؤثر في البقاء (١) كالشمس للضوء (۲) وقد يكون غيره (۳) كمماسة النار للاشتعال ) واستمرارها بمعونة الأسباب لبقائه (٥)) . يريد أن ما يفيد وجود الشيء قد يفيد بقاءه من غير افتقار إلى أمر آخر كالشمس تفيد ضوء المقابل وبقاءه، وقد يفتقر البقاء إلى أمر آخر، وهذا ما يقال : إن علة الحدوث غير علة البقاء كمماسة النار تفيد الاشتعال ثم يفتقر بقاء الاشتعال إلى استدامة المماسة واستمرارها بتعاقب الأسباب. (1) أي في بقاء ذلك الموجود . (۲) لحاصل في مقابلها فإنها هي المؤثرة في وجود ذلك الضوء، وهي المؤثرة في بقائه على المقابل. لأنها إذا غابت عن المقابل انتفى بقاء ضوئها عليه . (۳) أي غير المؤثر في الوجود. (٤) الذي هو أخذ النار المشتعل فيه وشروعها في حروقه فإن تلك المماسة علة الاشتعال أولاً . (ه) أي لبقاء ذلك الاشتعال .
The one who influences in existence may be the one who influences in continuance (1) like the sun for light (2) and may be other than it (3) like the touching of fire for ignition and its continuation with the aid of causes for its persistence (5). He means that what benefits the existence of a thing may benefit its persistence without needing another matter, like the sun benefits the light of the opposite and its persistence, and persistence may need another matter. This is what is said: the cause of origination is other than the cause of persistence, like the touching of fire benefits ignition then the persistence of ignition needs the continuation of touching and its continuity by the succession of causes. (1) i.e. in the persistence of that existent. (2) For what occurs in its opposite, for it is the one influencing in the existence of that light, and it is the one influencing in its persistence on the opposite. Because when it disappears from the opposite, the persistence of its light upon it ceases. (3) i.e. other than the one influencing in existence. (4) Which is the taking of the fire that is ignited and its beginning in its burning, for that touching is the cause of ignition first. (5) i.e. for the persistence of that ignition.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains a causal principle: the cause that brings something into existence may also be the cause of its continuance (like the sun for light), or a different cause may be required for continuance (like fire's contact causes ignition, but the continuance of ignition requires sustained contact with the help of other causes). The author then clarifies through numbered notes: (1) explains continuance, (2) explains that the sun is the cause of both existence and continuance of light on an opposite object, (3) indicates 'other' means a different cause, (4) defines the process of ignition, and (5) specifies the persistence of ignition. The overall point is that the cause of existence (علة الحدوث) may differ from the cause of persistence (علة البقاء).

Correction: The author shows that sometimes the cause of existence is not sufficient; continuance may require a different cause.

The Principle of One Effect Requires One Cause
The author argues that a single individual effect requires a single cause, contrary to some Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that a single individual effect cannot have two independent causes, and the distinction between causality at the level of individuals versus types.

Core Idea
One unique effect demands one unique cause.

This passage presents a foundational principle of kalam causality: a unique individual effect must be traced back to a single cause. The author defends this against Mu'tazila who allow two independent causes for one effect, and clarifies that the converse—a single cause producing a single effect—is not necessarily true. The lesson unpacks the logical proof and the nuance of causality at the level of individuals versus types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once continuously, then read the full translation. Notice the numbered notes clarifying key points.
The passage includes both the main text and explanatory notes.
2
Focus on the proof against two independent causes
The author presents a dilemma: if an individual effect had two independent causes, it would either need each partially or be independent of one. Both lead to contradiction. Identify this step in the argument.
This directly refutes the Mu'tazila position.
3
Distinguish between individual and type causality
The author explains that a type (النوع) can have multiple causes across its individuals, but a specific individual (شخص) requires a specific cause. See the example of heat from multiple fires.
This distinction is key to understanding the scope of the principle.
4
Consider the hesitation on individuation
The passage ends with a question: can a specific individual be caused by a generic cause on alternation without changing its individuation? The author notes hesitation (تردد) on this point.
This shows the depth of kalam reasoning about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث وحده المعلول بالشخص توجب وحدة الفاعل (۱) خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة (۲) ) ولا عكس (۳) خلافاً للفلاسفة، حيث منعـوا صدور الكثير عن الواحد الحقيقي ) . أما الأول : فلأن الشخص لـو علل بمستقلتين الاحتاج إلى كل لعلتيها واستغنى عنها العلية الأخرى، ولأنه إما أن يحتاج إلى كل منهما فيكون جزء علة، أو إلى إحداهما فقط فيكون هو العلة بخلاف النوع، فإن المحتاج إلى كل منهما فرد مغاير للمحتاج إلى الأخرى كأفراد الحرارة الواقعة في نيران متعددة فالفرد بعينه يحتاج إلى علة بعينهـا وفـرد مـا إلى علة ما مع امتناع الاجتماع والنوع إلى علة (ما) مع جواز الاجتماع نظراً إلى تعدد الأفراد، وهل يستند الفرد بعينه إلى علة ما بأن يقع بهذه كما يقع بتلك على البدل ولا يتبدل التشخص فيه تردده) (1) أي كون وحدة واحد بالشخص يقتضي كون علته واحدة فلا يجوز أن يكون لشخص واحد علتان مستقلتان بحيث يوجد بكل منهما على الانفراد. (۲) فإنه يقول بالجواز إذ الجوهر الفرد يجوز أن يلتصق بيد زيد ويد عمرو مثلاً، فإذا جذبته يد زيد ودفعته يد عمرو الصوب الجذب في آن واحد فلا محالة تحصل حركته حينئذ منهما . (٣) أي لا توجب وحدة العلة وحدة المعلول. (٤) وهو الذي وحدته شخصية مع انتفاء تعدد الشروط والآلات . (٥) أي نظر واحتمال فيصح أن يقال بالاستناد نظراً إلى أن تشخص الشيء يتحقق بدون فاعله وأن يقال بعدمه نظراً إلى أن التشخص لا يتحقق في الشيء إلا باختيار وتعلق تأثير المؤثر الخاص به .
The third topic: The oneness of the individual effect necessitates the oneness of the agent (1), contrary to some Mu'tazila (2), and not the converse (3), contrary to the philosophers, as they prevented the emanation of the many from the true one (4). As for the first: because if the individual were caused by two independents, it would need each of its two causes and be independent of the other causality. And because it either needs each of them, so it is part of a cause, or only one of them, so that is the cause. Unlike the type, for the one that needs each of them is an individual different from the one that needs the other, like individuals of heat occurring in multiple fires. So the individual specifically needs a specific cause, and an individual in general needs a cause in general, with impossibility of simultaneous existence [of both causes for the same individual?], while the type needs a cause in general with possibility of simultaneous existence due to multiplicity of individuals. And whether the individual specifically is based on a cause in general such that it occurs by this one just as it occurs by that one on alternation and the individuation does not change? There is hesitation regarding it (5). (1) i.e. the oneness of an individual necessitates that its cause is one, so it is not permissible for a single individual to have two independent causes such that it exists by each of them independently. (2) For he [the Mu'tazili] says permissibility, because the indivisible atom may adhere to the hand of Zayd and the hand of 'Amr for example. So if Zayd's hand pulls it and 'Amr's hand pushes it towards the direction of the pull at the same moment, then inevitably its motion occurs then from both of them. (3) i.e. the oneness of the cause does not necessitate the oneness of the effect. (4) He whose oneness is personal with the absence of multiplicity of conditions and instruments. (5) i.e. there is consideration and probability, so it is correct to say based on [the idea that] the individuation of a thing is realized without its agent, and to say that it is not realized except by choice and the attachment of the influence of the specific agent.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument about causality and individuation. The author presents a principle: a unique individual effect (شخص) must have a single cause. This is opposed by some Mu'tazila who allow two independent causes for one individual, using the example of an atom being pulled by one hand and pushed by another simultaneously. The converse—that a single cause produces a single effect—is not necessarily true, contrary to philosophers who deny that one simple being can produce multiple effects. The argument for the first part uses a dilemma: if an individual had two independent causes, it would need each, leading to either each being a partial cause or one being sufficient, both absurd. The author distinguishes between types (النوع) and individuals: many individuals of a type can have different causes. The passage ends with a question about whether a specific individual can be caused by a generic cause without affecting its individuation, noting hesitation (تردد) on this point. The numbered notes clarify: (1) the principle, (2) the Mu'tazila objection, (3) the rejection of the converse, (4) the definition of the true one, and (5) the hesitation regarding the basis of individuation.

نوع
علة مستقلة
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single cause must produce a single effect (the converse of the thesis).
Correction: The author explicitly denies the converse: the oneness of the cause does not necessitate the oneness of the effect, contrary to the philosophers.

Checkpoint
What is the author's position on whether a single individual effect can have two independent causes?
A) It is possible.
B) It is impossible.
C) It depends on the type of cause.
D) It is only possible in the case of atoms.

Key Takeaway
A unique individual effect must have a single unique cause, but a single cause can produce multiple effects; the distinction between individual and type is crucial for understanding causality in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you instinctively seek multiple human or material causes to solve it, forgetting that every individual outcome ultimately depends on a single unique Cause? How can this lesson reshape your reliance on Allah alone?
?
Consider a recent event you attributed to multiple factors (e.g., your success due to your effort and others' help). Does this lesson challenge you to see beyond secondary causes and affirm Allah's exclusive agency over that specific outcome? What would change if you truly lived this belief?
?
The author hesitates whether an individual's individuation can be preserved with alternating generic causes. How does this subtle point affect your understanding of your own unique existence? If only one Cause can bring about your specific self, what does that demand from you?
