Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Unity of an Individual Effect and Its Cause
Taftazani presents two theses on causality with proof and refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an individual effect must have a single independent cause, and why a single cause can produce multiple effects.

Core Idea
One effect requires one cause; one cause may have many effects.

This lesson from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes two foundational principles of causality in Islamic theology. The first is that an effect that is one in identity cannot be produced by two independent causes, a position that corrects some Mu'tazilite views. The second is that a cause that is one in all respects can still produce many effects, opposing the philosophical claim that the simple can only produce one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Taftazani's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a declaration of the topic and then provides proof.
2
Identify the two theses
Find the two main claims: (1) an individual effect necessitates a single cause, (2) the converse is not true. Note which groups are contradicted for each.
3
Analyze the proof for the first thesis
The proof has two arguments. Understand why two independent causes cannot coexist for one effect. Pay attention to the distinction between individual and species.
The species example (heat) helps clarify the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث
وحدة المعلول توجب وحدة الفاعل
قال: المبحث الثالث وحده المعلول بالشخص توجب وحدة الفاعل خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة ولا عكس خلافاً للفلاسفة، حيث منعوا صدور الكثير عن الواحد الحقيقي.
أما الأول: فلأن الشخص لو علل بمستقلتين لاحتياج إلى كل لعلتيها واستغنى عنها العلية الأخرى، ولأنه إما أن يحتاج إلى كل منهما فيكون جزء علة، أو إلى إحداهما فقط فيكون هو العلة بخلاف النوع، فإن المحتاج إلى كل منهما فرد مغاير للمحتاج إلى الأخرى كأفراد الحرارة الواقعة في نيران متعددة فالفرد بعينه يحتاج إلى علة بعينها وفرد ما إلى علة ما مع امتناع الاجتماع والنوع إلى علة ما مع جواز الاجتماع نظراً إلى تعدد الأفراد، وهل يستند الفرد بعينه إلى علة ما بأن يقع بهذه كما يقع بتلك على البدل ولا يتبدل التشخص فيه تردده.
يريد أن الواحد الشخص لا يكون معلولاً لعلتين تستقل كل منهما بإيجاده خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة والواحد من جميع الوجوه لا يلزم أن يكون معلوله واحداً بل قد يكون كثيراً خلافاً للفلاسفة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن الواحد المحض من جميع الوجوه لا يكون علة إلا لمعلول واحد.
أما الأول: وهو امتناع اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد فلوجهين.
١ - أنه يلزم احتياجه إلى كل من العلتين المستقلتين لكونهما علة واستغناؤه عن كل منهما لكون الأخرى مستقلة بالعلية.
٢ - أنه إن توقف على كل منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة مستقلة بل جزء علة، لأن معنى استقلال العلة أن لا يفتقر في التأثير إلى شيء آخر، وإن توقف على إحداهما فقط كانت هي العلة دون الأخرى وإن لم تتوقف على شيء منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة وهذا بخلاف الواحد بالنوع، فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماع العلتين عليه بمعنى أن يقع بعض أفراده بهذه وبعضها بتلك فيكون المحتاج إلى كل منهما أمراً مغايراً للمحتاج إلى الأخرى، وحينئذ لا يلزم احتياج شيء إلى شيء واستغناؤه عنه بعينه ولا يلزم من احتياج النوع إلى كل من العلتين عدم استقلالهما بالعلية للفرد، وذلك كجزئيات الحرارة التي يقع بعضها بهذه النار وبعضها بتلك فنوع الحرارة يكون معلولاً لهذه النيران وقد تمثل بنوع الحرارة الواقع بعض جزئياتها بالنار وبعضها بالشمس وبعضها بالحركة والمناقشة في كون هذه الحرارات من نوع واحد تدفع بأن المراد بالنوع ما هو أعم من الحقيقي.
The third topic: The unity of the effect necessitates the unity of the agent. He said: The third topic: The oneness of an individual effect necessitates the oneness of the agent, contrary to some Mu'tazila, and not the converse, contrary to the philosophers, for they prevented the emanation of many from the truly one. As for the first: Because if the individual were caused by two independent [causes], it would need each for its causation and be independent of it due to the other's causality, and because either it needs each of them, so each would be part of the cause, or it needs only one of them, so that one is the cause, unlike the species, for what needs each of them is an individual different from what needs the other, like individuals of heat occurring in multiple fires. So the specific individual needs a specific cause, and some individual needs some cause, with the impossibility of conjunction, and the species needs some cause with the possibility of conjunction, considering the multiplicity of individuals. And whether the specific individual is attributed to some cause such that it occurs by this as it occurs by that on alternation and its individuation does not change, there is hesitation in that. He intends that the individual one is not caused by two causes each of which is independent in producing it, contrary to some Mu'tazila, and the one in all respects does not necessitate that its effect be one, rather it may be many, contrary to the philosophers, for they held that the pure one in all respects is not a cause except for one effect. As for the first, which is the impossibility of two independent causes coming together upon one effect, it has two aspects: 1. That it would necessitate its need for each of the two independent causes because they are causes, and its independence from each because the other is independent in causality. 2. That if it depends on each of them, then neither of them is an independent cause but rather a part of the cause, because the meaning of the independence of the cause is that it does not need anything else in its effect. And if it depends on only one of them, then that one is the cause without the other, and if it does not depend on either, then neither is a cause. And this is unlike the one in terms of species, for the coming together of two causes upon it is not impossible, meaning that some of its individuals occur by this and some by that, so what needs each of them is something different from what needs the other. Then it does not necessitate a thing's needing something and being independent of the very same thing, nor does the species' need for each of the two causes necessitate their not being independent in causality for the individual. For example, individuals of heat: some occur by this fire and some by that, so the species of heat is caused by these fires. And it is exemplified by the species of heat, some of whose individuals occur by fire, some by the sun, and some by motion. And the objection that these heats are of one species is refuted by saying that what is meant by species here is more general than the true species.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of two theses and a detailed proof for the first. Taftazani first declares the third topic: the unity of an individual effect (individual in identity) necessitates the unity of its agent (cause), contrary to some Mu'tazila who apparently allowed two independent causes for a single effect. Then he adds that the converse is not true: a single cause does not necessitate a single effect; it can produce many effects, contrary to the philosophers who held that the simple and truly one can only produce one effect. He then provides the proof for the first thesis (impossibility of two independent causes for one effect). The proof is given in two forms. First, an earlier summary: if an effect were caused by two independent causes, it would need each (because it is caused by it) and be independent of each (because the other suffices), which is contradictory. Second, a more detailed two-part argument: (a) If the effect depends on both causes, then neither is independent—they become parts of a cause. (b) If it depends on one only, that one is the cause; if on neither, neither is cause—so no two independent causes can coexist for a single effect. This is contrasted with the species (universal), where multiple causes can produce different individuals, e.g., heat from different fires. The species as a whole requires multiple causes without contradiction. The passage ends by addressing an objection from Imam (likely al-Razi) about heat being of one species; the response is that 'species' here is more general than the logical species.

Key Terms
المعلول
الفاعل
العلتان المستقلتان
الواحد بالشخص
الواحد بالنوع
استقلال العلة
جزء العلة
الواحد الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument against two independent causes also applies to species (universals), making it impossible for multiple causes to produce individuals of the same species.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that the argument only applies to an individual effect. For a species, different causes can produce different individuals without contradiction, as seen in heat from different sources.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why is it impossible for a single individual effect to have two independent causes?
A) Because the effect would need both and be independent of both, which is contradictory.
B) Because the cause must be one and only one for every effect without exception.
C) Because the philosophers proved that the one cannot produce many.
D) Because the Mu'tazila rejected the idea of multiple causes.

Key Takeaway
A single effect requires a single cause, but a single cause can produce many effects; the distinction between individual and species is crucial in causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute the good and bad in your life to independent causes like luck, people, or your own efforts, rather than seeing Allah as the sole independent cause? How does it change your reliance on Him when you internalize that every individual effect—every breath, every trial, every joy—has only one independent cause? Will you consciously trace each moment back to Him today?
?
The philosophers claimed that a simple being can only produce one effect, limiting God's power. Is there an area of your life where you subconsciously limit Allah, thinking He cannot handle multiple things at once or that He is too 'simple' to care about details? How does Taftazani's refutation free you to trust in Allah's absolute power over every detail of your life without contradiction?
?
Taftazani distinguishes between an individual event and its general type. Do you confuse the two? When you face a specific hardship, do you attribute it to a general cause (e.g., 'life is hard') instead of seeing the individual hand of Allah in that exact moment? What would change if you saw every individual event as directly from Allah, not just a random instance of a general pattern? Will you practice seeing His specific will in your next difficulty?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Thesis and First Proof: The Contradiction of Need and Independence
Taftazani states the thesis and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that an individual effect cannot have two independent causes, based on a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An effect cannot need and be independent of the same cause simultaneously.

Taftazani begins by stating the thesis: a single individual effect cannot have two causes each sufficient on its own. He then offers the first proof: the effect would need each cause because it is a cause, yet be independent of each because the other suffices—a clear contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part of the passage, focusing on the thesis and the first proof.
Notice the two parts of the proof.
2
Identify the contradiction
Identify why needing each cause and being independent of each is impossible for the same thing.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does this prove that two independent causes cannot coexist for one effect? The commentary will help.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد أن الواحد الشخص لا يكون معلولاً لعلتين تستقل كل منهما بإيجاده خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة والواحد من جميع الوجوه لا يلزم أن يكون معلوله واحداً بل قد يكون كثيراً خلافاً للفلاسفة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن الواحد المحض لا يكون علة إلا لمعلول واحد. أما الأول: وهو امتناع اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد فلوجهين. ١ - أنه يلزم احتياجه إلى كل من العلتين المستقلتين لكونهما علة واستغناؤه عن كل منهما لكون الأخرى مستقلة بالعلية. ٢ - أنه إن توقف على كل منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة مستقلة بل جزء علة، لأن معنى استقلال العلة أن لا يفتقر في التأثير إلى شيء آخر، وإن توقف على إحداهما فقط كانت هي العلة دون الأخرى وإن لم تتوقف على شيء منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة وهذا بخلاف الواحد بالنوع، فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماع العلتين عليه بمعنى أن يقع بعض أفراده بهذه وبعضها بتلك فيكون المحتاج إلى كل منهما أمراً مغايراً للمحتاج إلى الأخرى، وحينئذ لا يلزم احتياج شيء إلى شيء واستغناؤه عنه بعينه ولا يلزم من احتياج النوع إلى كل من العلتين عدم استقلالهما بالعلية للفرد، وذلك كجزئيات الحرارة التي يقع بعضها بهذه النار وبعضها بتلك فنوع الحرارة يكون معلولاً لهذه النيران وقد مثل بنوع الحرارة الواقع بعض جزئياتها بالنار وبعضها بالشمس وبعضها بالحركة والمناقشة في كون هذه الحرارات من نوع واحد تدفع بأن المراد بالنوع ما هو أعم من الحقيقي.
He intends that the individual one (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣ) is not an effect (ma‘lūl) of two causes each of which is independent (mustaqillah) in producing it, contrary to some Mu‘tazila. And the one in all respects (al-wāḥid min jamī‘ al-wujūh) does not necessarily have its effect as one; rather it may be many, contrary to the philosophers, who held that the pure one (al-wāḥid al-maḥḍ) is not a cause except for one effect. As for the first: it is the impossibility of the conjunction of two independent causes upon one effect, and it has two proofs. 1. That it necessitates its need for each of the two independent causes because they are causes, and its independence from each because the other is independent in causality. 2. That if it depends on each of them, then neither of them is an independent cause but rather a part of a cause, because the meaning of the independence of the cause is that it does not need another thing in its effect. And if it depends on only one of them, then that one is the cause and not the other; and if it does not depend on either of them, then neither of them is a cause. And this is different from the specific one (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘), for the conjunction of two causes upon it is not impossible, meaning that some of its individuals occur by this cause and some by that cause, so that which needs each cause is a thing different from that which needs the other. In that case, a thing's need for something and its independence from it do not both apply to the same thing, nor does the species' need for each cause entail the lack of independence of the two causes in causality for the individual. This is like the particulars of heat, some of which occur by this fire and some by that fire; so the species of heat is an effect of these fires. And he gave the example of the species of heat, some of whose particulars occur by fire, some by the sun, and some by motion. And the discussion about whether these heats are of one species is rebutted by saying that what is meant by species is more general than the real (genus).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the first main point: the impossibility of two independent causes for one individual effect. It begins by stating the thesis: an individual (numerically one) effect cannot have two causes each independently sufficient to produce it, contrary to some Mu‘tazila. Then it distinguishes the individual from the type: a type (universal) can have multiple causes because its instances are produced by different causes. The author then gives two proofs for the impossibility: (1) The effect would require each cause (since each is a cause) and yet be independent of each (since the other is sufficient), leading to contradiction. (2) If the effect depends on both, neither is independent; if on one only, the other is not a cause; if on none, neither is a cause. This does not apply to a type because different instances depend on different causes. The author illustrates with heat: different particular heats come from different fires, so the type 'heat' depends on multiple fires without contradiction. A potential objection about the type being univocal is dismissed by noting that 'type' here is broader than the real genus.

Key Terms
الواحد الشخص
العلة المستقلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first proof show?
A) The effect needs both causes equally.
B) The effect would need each cause and yet be independent of each, a contradiction.
C) The effect depends on only one cause.
D) The effect does not need any cause.

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that two independent causes for one effect lead to a logical contradiction of simultaneous need and independence.

Second Proof and the Distinction between Individual and Type
Taftazani completes the second proof and distinguishes the individual from the type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against two independent causes for one effect and why the type (naw‘) can have multiple causes.

Core Idea
An effect cannot depend on two independent causes because neither would be independent, or only one is truly the cause.

Taftazani presents the second proof, which examines the dependence relations. If the effect depends on both, neither is independent; if on one, the other is superfluous; if on none, neither is a cause. He then clarifies that this does not apply to a type, whose instances can have different causes, as illustrated by the heat example.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof, which has three possibilities.
Note the three cases: dependence on both, on one, or on none.
2
Understand the distinction
Read the explanation about the type (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘) and how it differs from the individual.
3
Consider the heat example
Examine the example of heat from different sources. See how the type can have multiple causes.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why this distinction is important in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد أن الواحد الشخص لا يكون معلولاً لعلتين تستقل كل منهما بإيجاده خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة والواحد من جميع الوجوه لا يلزم أن يكون معلوله واحداً بل قد يكون كثيراً خلافاً للفلاسفة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن الواحد المحض لا يكون علة إلا لمعلول واحد. أما الأول: وهو امتناع اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد فلوجهين. ١ - أنه يلزم احتياجه إلى كل من العلتين المستقلتين لكونهما علة واستغناؤه عن كل منهما لكون الأخرى مستقلة بالعلية. ٢ - أنه إن توقف على كل منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة مستقلة بل جزء علة، لأن معنى استقلال العلة أن لا يفتقر في التأثير إلى شيء آخر، وإن توقف على إحداهما فقط كانت هي العلة دون الأخرى وإن لم تتوقف على شيء منهما لم يكن شيء منهما علة وهذا بخلاف الواحد بالنوع، فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماع العلتين عليه بمعنى أن يقع بعض أفراده بهذه وبعضها بتلك فيكون المحتاج إلى كل منهما أمراً مغايراً للمحتاج إلى الأخرى، وحينئذ لا يلزم احتياج شيء إلى شيء واستغناؤه عنه بعينه ولا يلزم من احتياج النوع إلى كل من العلتين عدم استقلالهما بالعلية للفرد، وذلك كجزئيات الحرارة التي يقع بعضها بهذه النار وبعضها بتلك فنوع الحرارة يكون معلولاً لهذه النيران وقد مثل بنوع الحرارة الواقع بعض جزئياتها بالنار وبعضها بالشمس وبعضها بالحركة والمناقشة في كون هذه الحرارات من نوع واحد تدفع بأن المراد بالنوع ما هو أعم من الحقيقي.
He intends that the individual one (al-wāḥid al-shakhṣ) is not an effect (ma‘lūl) of two causes each of which is independent (mustaqillah) in producing it, contrary to some Mu‘tazila. And the one in all respects (al-wāḥid min jamī‘ al-wujūh) does not necessarily have its effect as one; rather it may be many, contrary to the philosophers, who held that the pure one (al-wāḥid al-maḥḍ) is not a cause except for one effect. As for the first: it is the impossibility of the conjunction of two independent causes upon one effect, and it has two proofs. 1. That it necessitates its need for each of the two independent causes because they are causes, and its independence from each because the other is independent in causality. 2. That if it depends on each of them, then neither of them is an independent cause but rather a part of a cause, because the meaning of the independence of the cause is that it does not need another thing in its effect. And if it depends on only one of them, then that one is the cause and not the other; and if it does not depend on either of them, then neither of them is a cause. And this is different from the specific one (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘), for the conjunction of two causes upon it is not impossible, meaning that some of its individuals occur by this cause and some by that cause, so that which needs each cause is a thing different from that which needs the other. In that case, a thing's need for something and its independence from it do not both apply to the same thing, nor does the species' need for each cause entail the lack of independence of the two causes in causality for the individual. This is like the particulars of heat, some of which occur by this fire and some by that fire; so the species of heat is an effect of these fires. And he gave the example of the species of heat, some of whose particulars occur by fire, some by the sun, and some by motion. And the discussion about whether these heats are of one species is rebutted by saying that what is meant by species is more general than the real (genus).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the first main point: the impossibility of two independent causes for one individual effect. It begins by stating the thesis: an individual (numerically one) effect cannot have two causes each independently sufficient to produce it, contrary to some Mu‘tazila. Then it distinguishes the individual from the type: a type (universal) can have multiple causes because its instances are produced by different causes. The author then gives two proofs for the impossibility: (1) The effect would require each cause (since each is a cause) and yet be independent of each (since the other is sufficient), leading to contradiction. (2) If the effect depends on both, neither is independent; if on one only, the other is not a cause; if on none, neither is a cause. This does not apply to a type because different instances depend on different causes. The author illustrates with heat: different particular heats come from different fires, so the type 'heat' depends on multiple fires without contradiction. A potential objection about the type being univocal is dismissed by noting that 'type' here is broader than the real genus.

Key Terms
الواحد بالنوع
العلة المستقلة
الواحد الشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two causes produce the same type of effect, they must be independent causes for that individual effect.
Correction: The distinction shows that causality differs for the individual and the type; multiple causes can produce instances of the same type but not the same individual.

Checkpoint
Why can a type (naw‘) have multiple independent causes?
A) Because the type is not a real entity.
B) Because different instances of the type depend on different causes.
C) Because the type does not need any cause.
D) Because the type is one in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that two independent causes cannot both be truly independent for one individual effect, but a type can have multiple causes due to different instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever rely on multiple secondary causes as if they were independent, forgetting that all effects ultimately depend on Allah alone? How does this lesson's distinction between individual and type help you refocus your trust on the One True Cause?
?
When you see good things coming from different people or circumstances, do you mistakenly assume they each have independent power? How will you remind yourself that each instance is caused by Allah's decree, even if through different means?
?
This lesson shows that a single effect cannot have two independent causes. Are you trying to serve two masters—seeking approval from people while claiming submission to Allah? How does this proof challenge you to examine the independence of your devotion?

Resolving the Type's Need for a Specific Cause
Taftazani responds to an objection on the type's need for a specific cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction between a type's need for a cause and its ability to be caused by different specific causes.

Core Idea
The type needs a cause in general, but not a specific one; determination comes from the cause's side.

Taftazani addresses an objection: if a type essentially needs a specific cause, it cannot be produced by another. If it is independent, need cannot arise. He offers a nuanced answer: the type's need is absolute (for some cause), while the specific cause is determined by the cause's own nature. This preserves both the type's universality and causality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if the type needs a specific cause, it cannot have other causes; if not, it cannot become needy. Grasp the dilemma.
2
Taftazani's key distinction
Notice the answer: the type needs some cause absolutely, but the specific cause is determined by the cause, not the effect. This distinction resolves the dilemma.
3
The conclusion
Read the conclusion: the type's essence is not inherently needy or independent of a specific cause; both are accidental. Understand the implications for causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد الإمام أن المعلول النوعي إن احتاج لذاته إلى العلة المعينة امتنع استناده إلى غيرها، وهو ظاهر وإن لم كان غنياً عنها لذاته فلا يعرض له الاحتياج إليها. فأجاب بأنه لا يلزم من عدم الاحتياج لذاته إلى العلة المعينة استغناؤه عن العلة مطلقاً بل يجوز أن يحتاج لذاته إلى علة ما، ويكون الاستناد إلى العلة المعينة لا من جهة المعلول بل من جهة أن تلك العلة المعينة تقتضي ذلك المعلول. فالحاجة المطلقة من جانب المعلول وتعين العلة من جانب العلة. والحاصل: أن الماهية النوعية بالنظر إلى ذاتها ليست محتاجة إلى العلة المعينة ولا غنية عنها. بل كل من ذلك بالعارض.
And the Imām objected that the type-effect (al-ma‘lūl al-naw‘ī), if it essentially needs the specific cause, then its attribution to other than it is impossible—this is obvious; and if it is essentially independent of it, then need for it does not occur to it. So he answered: that from the lack of essential need for the specific cause, its independence from the cause absolutely does not follow. Rather, it is possible that it essentially needs some cause, and the attribution to the specific cause is not from the side of the effect but from the side that that specific cause necessitates that effect. So the absolute need is from the side of the effect, and the determination of the cause is from the side of the cause. The conclusion: that the specific essence (al-māhiyyah al-naw‘iyyah) with respect to itself is neither needy of the specific cause nor independent of it; rather, both of that are accidental.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The objector (the Imām) argues that if a type (such as heat as a universal) needs a specific cause (e.g., this fire) for its existence, then it cannot be produced by another cause, which contradicts its universal nature. But if it is independent of that specific cause, it cannot later become dependent. The author answers: The type's need is absolute—it needs some cause, but not a specific one. The specific cause is determined not by the effect's side but by the cause's side. So the type can be produced by different causes because its need is for a cause in general, and each particular cause brings about a particular instance. The conclusion: The specific nature (essence of the type) is not inherently needy or independent of a specific cause; both need and independence are accidental.

Key Terms
المعلول النوعي
العلة المعينة
الحاجة المطلقة
تعين العلة
الماهية النوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a type is independent of a specific cause, it is independent of all causes.
Correction: The type is independent of any specific cause but still needs some cause absolutely; its need is for a cause in general.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, where does the determination of the specific cause come from?
A) From the side of the effect, because the effect requires it.
B) From the side of the cause, because that cause necessitates the effect.
C) From both sides equally.
D) From neither side; it is arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
The type's essence is not attached to any specific cause; its need is for a cause in general, while the particular cause is determined by the cause's own nature.

This lesson says the type's essence is neutral regarding specific causes. How can you reframe your reliance on Allah while still using those means without the attachment? Commit to one practical step to detach from that specific cause today.

The Impossibility of Two Independent Causes for One Effect
Taftazani refutes the analogy between type and individual causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an individual effect cannot have two independent causes, unlike a type, and how the concept of 'one of them' does not entail conjunction for individuals.

Core Idea
An individual effect must have a specific cause; any other proposed cause would be superfluous.

Taftazani addresses an objection from al-Mawaqif that if an effect only needs 'some cause', then it could be produced by either of two causes, like a type. The answer shows that for an individual, the occurrence with one cause makes the other unnecessary, so it cannot be a cause. This lesson unpacks the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand al-Mawaqif's reasoning: if the effect needs a cause not in particular (concept of one of them), then two causes could each produce it, as with a type.
Focus on the phrase 'لا بعينها' (not in particular).
2
Identify the key concept
The objection relies on the idea that the effect needs 'one of them' not specifically, which for a type allows multiple causes. But does this apply to individuals?
The concept is مفهوم أحدهما.
3
Learn the answer's distinction
The answer says that the concept does not entail conjunction; for an individual, its specific occurrence entails a specific cause, making the other superfluous.
Superfluity means the other cause is 'mustaghni 'anhu' (dispensed with).
4
Apply the principle to examples
Consider a specific instance of heat: it has a specific cause. If another cause were claimed, that cause would be unnecessary because the heat already has a cause.
This shows why the individual case fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض صاحب المواقف بأن فيما ذكر من احتياج المعلول إلى علة ما بحيث يكون التعين من جانب العلة التزام أن يحتاج المعلول المعين إلى علة لا بعينها فيجوز أن يكون الواحد بالشخص معلولاً لعلتين من غير احتياج إلى كل منهما ليلزم المحال بل إلى مفهوم أحدهما لا بعينه الذي لا ينافي في الاجتماع كما هو شأن المعلول النوعي. والجواب: أن مفهوم أحدهما، وإن لم يناف الاجتماع، لكن لا يستلزمه فيمتنع فيما إذا كان المعلول شخصياً، لأن وقوعه بهذه الصورة يستلزم تلك، والمستغنى عنه لا يكون علة. ويجوز فيما إذا كان نوعاً لأن الواقع لكل منهما فرد آخر فلا يكون شيء منهما في معرض الاستغناء، ولهذا قال فالفرد بعينه يحتاج إلى علة بعينها، بمعنى أن الفرد المعين من الحرارة مثلاً يحتاج إلى علته المعينة التي أوجبتها ضرورة احتياج المعلول إلى
And the author of al-Mawāqif objected that what was mentioned of the effect's need for some cause such that the determination is from the side of the cause entails that the specific effect needs a cause not in particular, so it would be permissible for the individual one to be an effect of two causes without needing each of them—which would entail absurdity—but rather [it needs] the concept of one of them not in particular, which does not preclude conjunction, as is the case with the type-effect. The answer: that the concept of one of them, although it does not preclude conjunction, does not entail it; so it is impossible when the effect is individual, because its occurrence in this form entails that [specific cause], and that which is dispensed with is not a cause. And it is permissible when it is a type, because what occurs by each of them is another individual, so neither of them is subject to being dispensed with. For this reason he said: the very individual needs its very cause, meaning that the specific individual of heat, for example, needs its specific cause that necessitated it, due to the necessity of the effect's need for
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer. Al-Mawaqif objects that if the effect needs a cause in general (i.e., the concept of one of the two causes, not specifically this or that), then it is like the type: it can be produced by either cause without contradiction. The answer: The concept of one of them, although not precluding conjunction, does not entail conjunction. For an individual effect, its occurrence in this specific form (from this cause) entails that specific cause; the other cause, if also claimed, would be superfluous (since the effect does not need it). So the second cause cannot be a cause. For a type, however, different instances are produced by different causes, so no cause is superfluous. Therefore, the individual effect must have a specific cause. The answer concludes with a statement that the very individual (like a specific heat) needs its very cause.

Key Terms
مفهوم أحدهما
المعلول الشخصي
الاستغناء
المعلول النوعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the concept 'one of the two causes' allows two causes for a type, it should also allow two causes for an individual.
Correction: The concept does not entail conjunction; for an individual, the specific occurrence requires a specific cause, making the second cause superfluous and thus not a cause.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for an individual effect to have two independent causes according to the answer?
A) Because two causes cannot exist simultaneously.
B) Because the individual's occurrence with one cause makes the other cause superfluous, and what is superfluous is not a cause.
C) Because the concept of 'one of them' always leads to both causes being needed.
D) Because the type and individual are identical.

Key Takeaway
An individual effect requires a specific cause; if two causes were possible, one would be dispensable and thus not a cause.

This passage shows that an individual effect requires its very cause. The Effect's Need for a Cause
The author presents an objection and answer about whether an effect needs a specific cause or any cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection about an effect's need for a specific cause by distinguishing between the effect's need for any cause and the cause's own specification.

Core Idea
An effect needs a cause, but not a specific one; the cause itself determines which cause it is.

The author now addresses a subtle objection about causation: if an effect needs a specific cause, it couldn't be caused by anything else; but if it doesn't need that specific cause, it seems to need no cause at all. The answer lies in distinguishing between the effect's inherent need for some cause and the cause's own specificity. This passage explains that distinction clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the dilemma: either the effect inherently needs the specific cause, or it does not. If it does, it cannot be caused by anything else. If it does not, it seems independent of that cause.
The objector presents a binary scenario.
2
Identify the author's answer
The author argues that the effect does not need a specific cause in itself; it only needs some cause. The specification of which cause comes from the cause's own nature, not from the effect.
This resolves the dilemma by separating need from specification.
3
Understand the two sides
The effect's side: it has an absolute need for a cause (any cause). The cause's side: it necessitates a specific effect. Thus, the specificity is not demanded by the effect.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this principle avoids the false dichotomy. The effect's need is generic; the cause's nature is specific. This is a key distinction in kalam causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحتاج احتاج لذاته إلى العلة المعينة امتنع استناده إلى غيرها، وهو ظاهر وإن لم كان غنياً عنها لذاته فلا يعرض له الاحتياج إليها. فأجاب بأنه لا يلزم من عدم الاحتياج لذاته إلى العلة المعينة استغناؤه عن العلة مطلقاً بل يجوز أن يحتاج لذاته إلى علة ما، ويكون الاستناد إلى العلة المعينة لا من جهة المعلول بل من جهة أن تلك العلة المعينة تقتضي ذلك المعلول. فالحاجة المطلقة من جانب المعلول وتعين العلة من جانب العلة.
If [the effect] inherently needs the specific cause, then its attribution to other than it is impossible, and this is apparent even if it is independent of it in its essence, then it does not become subject to need for it. He answered that it does not follow from the lack of inherent need for the specific cause that it is independent of the cause absolutely, but rather it is permissible that it inherently needs some cause, and the attribution to the specific cause is not from the aspect of the effect but from the aspect that that specific cause necessitates that effect. So the absolute need is from the side of the effect, and the specification of the cause is from the side of the cause.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning whether an effect requires a specific cause or any cause. The objector argues: if the effect inherently needs a particular specific cause, then it cannot be caused by any other cause; but if it is inherently independent of that specific cause, then it never needs it. The answer clarifies that the effect's need is for a cause in general, not a specific one; the specificity comes from the cause's own nature, not from the effect. Thus, the effect's absolute need (for some cause) is from its own side, while the particularity of the cause is from the cause's side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an effect does not need a specific cause, it needs no cause at all.
Correction: The effect still needs some cause; the specificity is not from the effect but from the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the effect not need a specific cause in itself?
A) Because the effect is independent of all causes.
B) Because the effect only needs any cause, not a particular one.
C) Because the cause necessitates the effect regardless of the effect's need.
D) Because the effect's need is for the cause's essence, not its specificity.

Key Takeaway
The effect's need is for any cause, but the identity of the cause is determined by the cause itself, not the effect.

Objection of al-Mawaqif and the Distinction Between Individual and Type Effects
The author objects that a specific effect needs a non-specific cause, and answers by distinguishing individuals from types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām distinction between individual and type effects in causality and how it resolves the objection that a specific effect needs a non-specific cause.

Core Idea
An individual effect needs a specific cause; a type effect can be caused by any of multiple causes alternatively.

The author of al-Mawāqif raised a powerful objection: if an effect needs some cause but not a specific one, then the same individual could have two different causes, which seems absurd. The answer introduces a critical distinction between an individual effect (a specific thing) and a type effect (a kind of thing). This passage unpacks that distinction and shows how it saves the coherence of causality in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully to see the problem: if an effect needs any cause unspecified, the same individual could be caused by two different causes, leading to impossibility.
The objection targets the idea that specification comes from the cause.
2
Grasp the answer's core distinction
The answer says the concept of a non-specific cause does not necessitate conjunction (simultaneous causality). So it does not automatically allow two causes for one individual.
The key is that 'one of them' does not imply both can act together.
3
Apply the distinction to individual vs. type
Notice how the rule differs: a specific individual (e.g., a particular heat) needs a specific cause. A type (e.g., heat in general) can be caused by alternative causes, each producing a different individual.
This prevents simultaneous causality for an individual but allows alternative causality.

والحاصل: أن الماهية النوعية بالنظر إلى ذاتها ليست محتاجة إلى العلة المعينة ولا غنية عنها. بل كل من ذلك بالعارض. واعترض صاحب المواقف بأن فيما ذكر من احتياج المعلول إلى علة ما بحيث يكون التعين من جانب العلة التزام أن يحتاج المعلول المعين إلى علة لا بعينها فيجوز أن يكون الواحد بالشخص معلولاً لعلتين من غير احتياج إلى كل منهما ليلزم المحال بل إلى مفهوم أحدهما لا بعينه الذي لا ينافي في الاجتماع كما هو شأن المعلول النوعي. والجواب: أن مفهوم أحدهما، وإن لم يناف الاجتماع، لكن لا يستلزمه فيمتنع فيما إذا كان المعلول شخصياً، لأن وقوعه بهذه الصورة يستلزم تلك، والمستغنى عنه لا يكون علة. ويجوز فيما إذا كان نوعاً الاستغناء عن لأن الواقع لكل منهما فرد آخر فلا يكون شيء منهما في معرض الاستغناء، ولهذا قال فالفرد بعينه يحتاج إلى علة بعينها، بمعنى أن الفرد المعين من: الحرارة مثلاً يحتاج إلى علته المعينة التي أوجبتها ضرورة احتياج المعلول إلى علته وفرد ما أي الفرد لا بعينه يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، بل بحيث يحتمل أن يكون هذه أو تلك، لكن يمتنع اجتماعهما عليه لما سبق، وهذا ما يقال أن الواحد بالشخص يجوز أن تكون له علتان على سبيل البدل، دون الاجتماع والنوع يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، لكن لا يمتنع الاجتماع بالنظر إلى النوع، لأن الواقع بكل منهما فرد مغاير للواقع بالأخرى، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إن القول بالاحتياج إلى علة ما إما أن يكون قولاً بتعدد العلة أو لا وأياً ماكان فلا فرق بين النوع والفرد.
The summary: that the specific essence (māhiyya al-nawʿiyya) with respect to itself is not in need of the specific cause nor independent of it. Rather, each of these is accidental. And the author of al-Mawāqif objected that in what was mentioned of the effect's need for some cause such that specification is from the side of the cause, it entails that the specific effect needs a non-specific cause, so it would be permissible for the same individual to be an effect of two causes without needing each of them — which would lead to impossibility — but rather [it needs] the concept of one of them not specifically, which does not preclude conjunction as is the case for a type effect. The answer: that the concept of one of them, even if it does not preclude conjunction, does not necessitate it; so it is impossible when the effect is individual because its occurrence in this form necessitates that [i.e., a specific cause], and what is dispensed with is not a cause. And it is permissible when it is a type to dispense with [a specific cause] because what occurs from each [cause] is another individual, so nothing of them is subject to being dispensed with. For this reason it is said: the specific individual needs a specific cause, meaning that the particular individual, e.g., heat, needs its specific cause that necessitated it due to the effect's need for its cause; and some individual, i.e., a non-specific individual, needs a non-specific cause, but such that it could be this or that, but their conjunction on it is impossible for the reason previously mentioned. And this is what is said: that the same individual can have two causes on an alternative basis (badal), not conjointly. And the type needs a non-specific cause, but conjunction is not impossible with respect to the type because what occurs from each is a different individual from what occurs from the other. And with this is repelled what is said: that the statement of need for some cause either implies multiplicity of cause or not, and in either case there is no difference between type and individual.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the author of al-Mawāqif and its answer, establishing a key distinction between individual (shakhṣī) and type (nawʿī) effects. The summary states that the specific essence is not inherently in need of or independent from a specific cause; such attributes are accidental. The objection argues that if an effect needs a non-specific cause, then the same individual could be caused by either of two causes alternatively, which is impossible. The answer clarifies that the concept of one cause does not necessitate conjunction, so for an individual effect, a specific cause is necessary because its occurrence requires that specific cause. For a type effect, however, a non-specific cause suffices because each cause produces a different individual. Therefore, an individual can have two causes on an alternative basis (badal), but not together. A type can have multiple causes without contradiction because the effects are distinct individuals. This refutes the claim that there is no difference between type and individual in this regard.

واحد بالشخص
معلول شخصي
معلول نوعي
بدل
اجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an effect needs any cause, it allows two causes for the same individual simultaneously.
Correction: The passage shows that for an individual, only alternative causality is possible, not simultaneous. For a type, multiple causes can coexist because they produce distinct individuals.

Checkpoint
Why can a type effect have two causes without contradiction, but an individual effect cannot?
A) Because a type effect is not a real effect.
B) Because each cause produces a different individual of the type.
C) Because an individual effect needs both causes together.
D) Because type effects have no specific essence.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that an individual effect requires a specific cause, while a type effect can have alternative causes, resolving the paradox of non-specific causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you hold that secondary causes are real yet remain deeply reliant on Allah alone as the ultimate Creator?
?
When you see a pattern of blessings in your life (e.g., repeated provision or guidance), do you tend to think of them as a 'type' of blessing that comes from multiple sources? Does this lesson change how you view the consistency of Allah’s action in the world? Ask yourself: am I truly attributing each individual blessing to its specific Divine cause, or am I lazily generalizing?
?
The passage shows that for a type effect, multiple causes can produce distinct individuals of the same type. Consider your own good deeds: are you trying to produce a 'type' effect (general righteousness) through one specific method, or are you open to different means (various acts of worship) that each produce unique spiritual fruits? Which attitude reflects deeper trust in Allah’s wisdom and diversity of causation?

Can Two Alternative Causes Produce the Same Individual Effect?
Investigation about alternative causality for the same individual effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the question of whether the same individual effect can be caused by two alternative causes, and how the unity of the agent relates to the individuation of the effect.

Core Idea
Does the identity of the cause determine the identity of the effect?

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced investigation in causation: can the very same individual motion be produced by either of two different agents? This question hinges on whether the unity of the agent (tawḥīd al-fāʿil) has any role in individuating the effect (tashakhkhuṣ al-maʿlūl). We will examine the example of a stone's motion and the beginning of the opponent's argument against the possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a new investigation, an example, a distinction, and the start of a proof.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the opponent's argument.
2
Identify the central question
The author asks whether the same individual effect (wāḥid bi-ʿaynihi) can be caused by a non-specific cause (ʿilla lā bi-ʿaynihā) on an alternative basis (badal). The example is the motion of a stone caused by Zayd or ʿAmr.
This is different from simultaneous multiple causes; it is about alternative causality.
3
Understand the distinction made
The author distinguishes this case from a later one where a single motion is caused partly by different agents. Here, the whole motion is considered as being caused entirely by one agent or another.
The key factor is whether the agent's unity (ittiḥād al-fāʿil) influences the individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) of the effect.
4
Examine the opponent's proof introduction
The text begins to present the opponent's proof (dalīl al-mukhālif) that multiple causes are impossible. It mentions a specific example: the motion of an atom (jawhar fard) that Zayd pushes while pulling it.
The proof is incomplete, but we can see the opponent uses a concrete physical example.

بقي ههنا بحث، وهو أن الواحد بعينه، وإن كان من حيث وقوعه بالعلة المعينة محتجاً إليها، لكن هل يصح إلى علة لا بعينها بأن يقع بكل منهما على سبيل البدل بأن يكون الواقع بهذه هو بعينه الواقع بتلك مثلا حركة هذا الحجر في مسافة معينة في زمان معين، إذا وقعت بتحريك زيد فلو فرضناها واقعة بتحريك عمرو هل تكون هي بعينها؟ فيه تردد بناء على أن اتحاد الفاعل هل له مدخل في تشخص المعلول، وهذا غير ما سيجيء من أنه لا مدخل في تشخص الحركة لوحدة الفاعل، حيث تقع الحركة المعينة بعضها بتحريك زيد، وبعضها بتحريك عمرو، وإنما الكلام في أنا لو فرضناها في ذلك الزمان في تلك المسافة، واقعة بتحريك بكر وخالد بدل زيد، وعمرو هل تكون تلك بالشخص المعين. دليل المخالف بامتناع تعدد العلة قال: تمسك المخالف بأن حركة جوهر يدفعه زيد حين يجذبه
There remains here an investigation, which is that the same individual, even if from the perspective of its occurrence by the specific cause it is in need of it, is it valid [to be attributed] to a non-specific cause such that it occurs by each cause on an alternative basis, such that what occurs by this [cause] is the very same as what occurs by that [cause]? For example, the motion of this stone in a specific distance in a specific time, if it occurred by Zayd's pushing, then if we suppose it occurred by ʿAmr's pushing, would it be the very same [motion]? There is hesitation based on whether the unity of the agent has an input into the individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) of the effect. And this is different from what will come that there is no input into the individuation of motion from the unity of the agent, where a specific motion occurs partly by Zayd's pushing and partly by ʿAmr's pushing. Rather, the discussion is: if we suppose it in that time in that distance, occurring by Bakr and Khālid's pushing instead of Zayd and ʿAmr, would it be that very specific individual? The proof of the opponent [against] the impossibility of multiple causes: he said, the opponent adheres to the view that the motion of an atom that Zayd pushes while he pulls it...

Commentary
This passage is a new investigation (baḥth) into whether the same individual effect can be caused by two different causes on an alternative basis. The author asks whether the motion of a stone, if caused by Zayd's push, could be the very same motion if caused by ʿAmr's push instead. Individual and Type in Causality: The Substitution Basis
Explains the basic distinction between individual and type in causality with its logical rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a particular individual effect can have two causes on a substitution basis, while a type of effect allows multiple causes simultaneously, and how this refutes a common objection.

Core Idea
An individual can have two possible causes, but only one at a time; a type can have many causes each producing a distinct individual.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in kalam causality: the difference between a specific individual effect (fard) and a general type (nawʿ) in relation to their causes. The author explains that an individual does not require a specific cause, but can be produced by any one of two causes on a substitution basis, whereas a type can be produced by multiple causes simultaneously since each yields a different individual. This distinction refutes the objection that invoking a non-specific cause implies multiple causes for the same thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the two categories: individual (fard) and type (nawʿ).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the rule for the individual
The individual may have two causes on a substitution basis (sabīl al-badal), but not simultaneously. Understand why: two causes cannot produce the same individual together.
This is a key point: substitution, not conjunction.
3
Identify the rule for the type
The type may have multiple causes simultaneously because each cause produces a different individual that falls under the type.
Contrast with the individual rule.
4
Understand the refutation
The author refutes the objection that saying 'need for a cause' must imply either one cause or many, and that there is no difference between individual and type. The refutation shows the distinction is real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استناده علته وفرد ما أي الفرد لا بعينه يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، بل بحيث يحتمل أن يكون هذه أو تلك، لكن يمتنع اجتماعهما عليه لما سبق، وهذا ما يقال أن الواحد بالشخص يجوز أن تكون له علتان على سبيل البدل، دون الاجتماع والنوع يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، لكن لا يمتنع الاجتماع بالنظر إلى النوع، لأن الواقع بكل منهما فرد مغاير للواقع بالأخرى، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إن القول بالاحتياج إلى علة ما إما أن يكون قولاً بتعدد العلة أو لا وأياً ماكان فلا فرق بين النوع والفرد بقى ههنا بحث، وهو أن الواحد بعينه، وإن كان من حيث وقوعه بالعلة المعينة محتجاً إليها، لكن هل يصح إلى علة لا بعينها بأن يقع بكل منهما على سبيل البدل بأن يكون الواقع بهذه هو بعينه الواقع بتلك مثلا حركة هذا الحجر في مسافة معينة في زمان معين، إذا وقعت بتحريك زيد فلو فرضناها واقعة بتحريك عمرو هل تكون هي بعينها؟ فيه تردد بناء على أن اتحاد الفاعل هل له مدخل في تشخص المعلول، وهذا غير ما سيجيء من أنه لا مدخل في تشخص الحركة لوحدة الفاعل، حيث تقع الحركة المعينة بعضها بتحريك زيد، وبعضها بتحريك عمرو، وإنما الكلام في أنا لو فرضناها في ذلك الزمان في تلك المسافة، واقعة بتحريك بكر وخالد بدل زيد وعمرو هل تكون تلك بالشخص المعين.
Its attribution (to a cause): a cause and a certain individual (fard), meaning the individual not specifically [determined] needs a cause not specifically [determined], but rather in such a way that it can be this or that, but their conjunction on it is impossible due to what preceded. And this is what is said: that the individual (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) may have two causes on a substitution basis (sabīl al-badal), not [on the basis of] conjunction. And the type (nawʿ) needs a cause not specifically [determined], but conjunction is not impossible with respect to the type, because what occurs by each cause is a different individual from what occurs by the other. And by this is refuted what is said: that the statement 'need for a cause' either is a statement of multiplicity of cause or not, and whichever it is, there is no difference between the type and the individual. There remains here a research (baḥth): whether the particular individual, although from the perspective of its occurrence by a specific cause it needs that cause, is it valid [to attribute it] to a non-specific cause, in the sense that it occurs by each of them on a substitution basis, such that what occurs by this one is exactly the same as what occurs by that one? For example, the motion of this stone in a specific distance at a specific time, if it occurs by Zayd's pushing, then if we suppose it occurs by ʿAmr's pushing, would it be the very same motion? There is hesitation (taraddud) based on whether the unity of the agent has an influence on the particularization (tashakhkhuṣ) of the effect. And this is different from what will come: that there is no influence on the particularization of motion from the unity of the agent, where the particular motion occurs partly by Zayd's pushing and partly by ʿAmr's pushing. The discussion here is: if we suppose it at that time and that distance, occurring by Bakr and Khālid instead of Zayd and ʿAmr, would it be that particular individual?
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion about causality, distinguishing between an individual effect (fard bi-ʿaynihi) and a type of effect (nawʿ). It begins by stating that a particular individual does not require a specific cause, but rather a cause that is not predetermined, such that it could be this or that, but it is impossible for two causes to simultaneously produce that same individual (due to the impossibility of two likes meeting). This is called 'on a substitution basis' (sabīl al-badal). For the type, multiple causes can coexist because each cause produces a different individual instantiating the type. This refutes the objection that claiming a need for a cause necessarily implies multiple causes for the same individual. The passage then introduces a research question: Can a particular individual effect be produced by a non-specific cause in the sense that the same token effect could be produced by either of two distinct agents? The example of a stone's motion pushed by Zayd or by ʿAmr is given. Whether the motion is identical depends on whether the unity of the agent affects the particularization of the effect, which is a separate issue from the later discussion where the agent's unity does not affect motion's particularization. The research is left unresolved with hesitation (taraddud).

Key Terms
فرد (fard)
نوع (nawʿ)
علة (illa)
بدل (badal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an individual effect cannot have more than one possible cause at all.
Correction: The text clarifies that an individual can have two causes on a substitution basis, just not simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, can a particular individual effect have two causes simultaneously?
A) Yes, absolutely.
B) Yes, but only on a substitution basis, not simultaneously.
C) No, never.
D) Only for types, not individuals.

Key Takeaway
An individual effect may have two possible causes on a substitution basis, while a type can have multiple causes simultaneously because each yields a distinct individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often attribute your circumstances to a single cause—whether it be your own actions, others' treatment, or fate. But the lesson teaches that an individual can have multiple possible causes, though only one actualizes. Are you limiting your understanding of Allah's decree by insisting on a single cause? How could embracing substitutional causality deepen your tawakkul?
?
The passage refutes an objection by distinguishing between type and individual. In your theological inquiries, have you fallen into the error of conflating the two, leading to false conclusions? Ponder a specific belief you hold that might actually be a confusion of type and individual. Commit to clarifying one such distinction today.
?
The individual's cause is 'not specifically determined'—it could be this or that. In your life, when you face uncertainty about why something happened, do you patiently accept the ambiguity or do you rush to assign a definitive cause? How does the logical possibility of substitution affect your patience and trust in Allah's wisdom?

Can the Same Token Effect Be Produced by Different Agents?
Investigates whether a particular token effect can be produced by different agents on a substitution basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unresolved research question of whether a particular token effect (e.g., a specific motion) can be attributed to different agents on a substitution basis, and the role of agent unity in the particularization of effects.

Core Idea
Whether the same exact effect can be produced by different agents depends on whether agent unity affects the particularization of the effect.

After establishing the basic distinction between individual and type, the author introduces a deeper research problem: can a particular individual effect (like the motion of a specific stone at a specific time) be produced by either of two agents on a substitution basis? The example contrasts Zayd and Amr pushing the stone. The answer is unresolved and hinges on whether the unity of the agent influences the particularization (tashakhkhuṣ) of the effect. This lesson explores this question and its connection to later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the research question
Read the Arabic passage from 'بقى ههنا بحث' to the end. Understand the question: can a particular token effect be caused by a non-specific cause on a substitution basis?
The author presents this as a remaining research (baḥth).
2
Analyze the example
Consider the example: the motion of a stone pushed by Zayd. If we suppose it is pushed by Amr instead, would it be the very same motion? The answer is not straightforward.
This tests whether the agent's identity matters.
3
Examine the hesitation
The author notes hesitation (taraddud) based on whether the unity of the agent has an influence on the particularization of the effect. This is separate from the later discussion where agent unity does not affect motion particularization when parts come from different agents.

استناده علته وفرد ما أي الفرد لا بعينه يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، بل بحيث يحتمل أن يكون هذه أو تلك، لكن يمتنع اجتماعهما عليه لما سبق، وهذا ما يقال أن الواحد بالشخص يجوز أن تكون له علتان على سبيل البدل، دون الاجتماع والنوع يحتاج إلى علة لا بعينها، لكن لا يمتنع الاجتماع بالنظر إلى النوع، لأن الواقع بكل منهما فرد مغاير للواقع بالأخرى، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إن القول بالاحتياج إلى علة ما إما أن يكون قولاً بتعدد العلة أو لا وأياً ماكان فلا فرق بين النوع والفرد بقى ههنا بحث، وهو أن الواحد بعينه، وإن كان من حيث وقوعه بالعلة المعينة محتجاً إليها، لكن هل يصح إلى علة لا بعينها بأن يقع بكل منهما على سبيل البدل بأن يكون الواقع بهذه هو بعينه الواقع بتلك مثلا حركة هذا الحجر في مسافة معينة في زمان معين، إذا وقعت بتحريك زيد فلو فرضناها واقعة بتحريك عمرو هل تكون هي بعينها؟ فيه تردد بناء على أن اتحاد الفاعل هل له مدخل في تشخص المعلول، وهذا غير ما سيجيء من أنه لا مدخل في تشخص الحركة لوحدة الفاعل، حيث تقع الحركة المعينة بعضها بتحريك زيد، وبعضها بتحريك عمرو، وإنما الكلام في أنا لو فرضناها في ذلك الزمان في تلك المسافة، واقعة بتحريك بكر وخالد بدل زيد وعمرو هل تكون تلك بالشخص المعين.
Its attribution (to a cause): a cause and a certain individual (fard), meaning the individual not specifically [determined] needs a cause not specifically [determined], but rather in such a way that it can be this or that, but their conjunction on it is impossible due to what preceded. And this is what is said: that the individual (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) may have two causes on a substitution basis (sabīl al-badal), not [on the basis of] conjunction. And the type (nawʿ) needs a cause not specifically [determined], but conjunction is not impossible with respect to the type, because what occurs by each cause is a different individual from what occurs by the other. And by this is refuted what is said: that the statement 'need for a cause' either is a statement of multiplicity of cause or not, and whichever it is, there is no difference between the type and the individual. There remains here a research (baḥth): whether the particular individual, although from the perspective of its occurrence by a specific cause it needs that cause, is it valid [to attribute it] to a non-specific cause, in the sense that it occurs by each of them on a substitution basis, such that what occurs by this one is exactly the same as what occurs by that one? For example, the motion of this stone in a specific distance at a specific time, if it occurs by Zayd's pushing, then if we suppose it occurs by ʿAmr's pushing, would it be the very same motion? There is hesitation (taraddud) based on whether the unity of the agent has an influence on the particularization (tashakhkhuṣ) of the effect. And this is different from what will come: that there is no influence on the particularization of motion from the unity of the agent, where the particular motion occurs partly by Zayd's pushing and partly by ʿAmr's pushing. The discussion here is: if we suppose it at that time and that distance, occurring by Bakr and Khālid instead of Zayd and ʿAmr, would it be that particular individual?
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion about causality, distinguishing between an individual effect (fard bi-ʿaynihi) and a type of effect (nawʿ). It begins by stating that a particular individual does not require a specific cause, but rather a cause that is not predetermined, such that it could be this or that, but it is impossible for two causes to simultaneously produce that same individual (due to the impossibility of two likes meeting). This is called 'on a substitution basis' (sabīl al-badal). For the type, multiple causes can coexist because each cause produces a different individual instantiating the type. This refutes the objection that claiming a need for a cause necessarily implies multiple causes for the same individual. The passage then introduces a research question: Can a particular individual effect be produced by a non-specific cause in the sense that the same token effect could be produced by either of two distinct agents? The example of a stone's motion pushed by Zayd or by ʿAmr is given. Whether the motion is identical depends on whether the unity of the agent affects the particularization of the effect, which is a separate issue from the later discussion where the agent's unity does not affect motion's particularization. The research is left unresolved with hesitation (taraddud).

Key Terms
بدل (badal)
تشخص (tashakhkhuṣ)
علة (illa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unity of the agent definitely determines the particularization of the effect, so a token cannot have multiple possible agents.
Correction: The text leaves this open as an unresolved research question, with hesitation based on whether agent unity influences particularization.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the question whether a particular motion produced by Zayd could be the very same motion if produced by ʿAmr?
A) It is definitively answered: yes, it can be the same.
B) It is definitively answered: no, it cannot be the same.
C) There is hesitation (taraddud) based on whether agent unity affects particularization.
D) The passage does not address this question.

Key Takeaway
Whether a particular token effect can be produced by different agents on a substitution basis remains an open question, depending on the role of agent unity in effect particularization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a good deed to your own effort, do you think that very same deed could have been produced by someone else? How does that possibility affect your pride or sense of ownership over your actions?
?
The passage hesitates on whether the agent's identity changes the particularity of the effect. In your life, do you treat actions done by you as fundamentally different from the same actions done by others? How might this affect your view of others' contributions or your own accountability before Allah?

Refutation of the Opponent's Argument Against Multiple Causes
Presents and refutes the opponent's argument using the atom example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument against multiple causes for a single effect and how it is refuted by the concept of partial causes and ultimate attribution to God.

Core Idea
Two agents can jointly cause one effect as partial causes without contradiction.

In this passage, Sanusi presents an objection raised by opponents of multiple causes, using the example of a single atom pushed and pulled simultaneously. He then provides a clear refutation that upholds the possibility of multiple causes without attributing independent causation to each. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Notice the key terms like jawhar fard and istinād.
2
Identify the opponent's scenario
The opponent imagines a single atom pushed by Zayd and pulled by ʿAmr with equal force. Understand why they think this proves impossibility.
The key is that two likes (two motions) cannot coexist in one atom.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation denies that the motion is independently attributed to each agent. Instead, it is attributed jointly or to God.
The concept of partial cause (juzʿ ʿilla) is introduced.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the refutation preserves the unity of the effect while allowing multiple causes. Ask: Does composition of cause require composition of effect?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل المخالف بامتناع تعدد العلة. قال: تمسك المخالف بأن حركة جوهر يدفعه زيد حين يجذبه عمرو مستندة إلى كل. قلنا: بل إلى الكل أو إلى الواجب تعالى. أي تمسك القائل بجواز اجتماع العلتين على معلول واحد بالشخص، بأنا لو فرضنا جوهراً فرداً ملتصقاً بين زيد وعمرو يدفعه زيد، ويجذبه عمرو في زمان واحد على حد واحد من القوة والسرعة فالحركة مستندة إلى كل منهما بالاستقلال لعدم الرجحان مع أنها واحدة بالشخص ضرورة امتناع اجتماع المثلين ولذا فرضناها في الجوهر الفرد دون الجسم حيث يمكن تعدد الحل. والجواب: منع استنادها إلى كل واحد بالاستقلال، بل إليهما جميعاً بحيث يكون كل منهما جزء علة وليس من ضرورة تركب العلة تركب المعلول، وتوزيع أجزائه على أجزائها أو إلى الواجب تعالى كما هو الرأي الحق.
The proof of the opponent for the impossibility of multiplicity of cause. He said: The opponent argues by [saying] that the motion of an atom that Zayd pushes while ʿAmr pulls is attributed to each. We said: Rather, to both together or to the Necessary Most High. That is, the proponent of the permissibility of the conjunction of two causes on one particular effect argues: if we suppose a single atom attached between Zayd and ʿAmr, Zayd pushes it and ʿAmr pulls it at one time, on one measure of force and speed, then the motion is attributed to each of them independently due to lack of preponderance, while it is one particular effect necessarily because of the impossibility of two likes meeting. Hence we supposed it in the atom, not in the body where multiplicity of location is possible. The answer: It is denied that it is attributed to each independently; rather, it is attributed to both together, such that each is a partial cause (juzʿ ʿilla), and it is not necessary that the composition of the cause entails composition of the effect and distribution of its parts among its parts, or [it is attributed] to the Necessary Most High, as is the correct view.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (shubha) from the opponent who denies the possibility of multiple causes for a single effect, followed by its refutation. The opponent's argument uses the hypothetical scenario of a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard) located between two agents: Zayd pushes it from one side and ʿAmr pulls it from the other, both exerting equal force. The resulting motion would be attributed independently to each agent, yet it is a single motion, which is impossible because two likes (two motions) cannot coexist in the same atom. Therefore, the opponent claims, multiple causes for one effect are impossible. The answer (jawāb) denies that the motion is independently attributed to each agent. Instead, the motion is attributed jointly to both as partial causes (juzʿ ʿilla). It is not necessary that the composition of the cause implies composition of the effect. Alternatively, the motion may be attributed directly to God (al-wājib taʿālā), which is the position of the ḥaqq. Thus the objection is repelled.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
استناد (istinād)
استقلال (istiqlāl)
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimāʿ al-mithlayn)
جزء علة (juzʿ ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's example shows that two independent causes cannot produce one effect, proving multiple causes impossible.
Correction: The refutation shows that causes can be partial, not independent, or the effect ultimately comes from God. Thus multiple causes are possible.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's main example to argue against multiple causes?
A) A stone moved by two persons consecutively.
B) A single atom pushed and pulled simultaneously by two agents.
C) A type of cause producing many effects.
D) A substitution of causes over time.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of independent multiple causes for one effect does not rule out partial causes or divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something in life, do you instinctively credit your own effort (as an independent cause) or do you see it as a partial cause, with God as the ultimate source? Which attribution would better cultivate humility and gratitude?
?
Have you ever felt that your work and another person's work were in conflict, yet produced a single outcome? How does the concept of juzʿ ʿilla (partial cause) help you see your role differently—not as a full cause but as part of God's greater plan?
?
The opponent’s argument fails because it assumes independence. In your relationship with others, do you often assume you are the sole cause of success or failure, ignoring the contributions of others and of God? What would it take for you to truly internalize that you are only a partial cause?
