Refutation of Two Independent Causes Producing One Effect
Al-Taftazani refutes a thought experiment claiming two independent causes can produce one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single effect cannot have two independent causes, and how composite causation resolves the thought experiment.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot be independently caused by two agents.

Al-Taftazani addresses a challenge to the principle that one effect has one cause. A thought experiment involving two agents acting on an atom seems to show independent causation. He refutes this by explaining that both agents together form a composite cause, not two independent ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the proponent's argument
First, read the passage describing the thought experiment: imagine an atom between Zayd and 'Amr, one pushing, one pulling, with equal force. According to the proponent, each independently causes the movement.
Notice the assumption that no preponderance forces independent attribution.
2
Understand the problem
The movement is numerically one, yet seems to have two independent causes. This challenges the principle that one effect has one cause.
3
Al-Taftazani's denial
Read the refutation: al-Taftazani denies that the movement is attributed to each independently. Instead, the movement is attributed to both together, each being part of a composite cause.
He also notes that a composite cause does not necessitate a composite effect.
4
Ultimate recourse to God
The passage ends by referring the ultimate cause to the Necessary Existent, as is the correct view. This reinforces the primacy of divine causation.
This aligns with the Ash'ari emphasis on God as the sole creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي تمسك القائل بجواز اجتماع العلتين على معلول واحد بالشخص، بأنا لو فرضنا جوهراً فرداً ملتصقاً بين زيد وعمرو يدفعه زيد، ويجذبه عمرو في زمان واحد على حد واحد من القوة والسرعة فالحركة مستندة إلى كل منهما بالاستقلال لعدم الرجحان مع أنها واحدة بالشخص ضرورة امتناع اجتماع المثلين ولذا فرضناها في الجوهر الفرد دون الجسم حيث يمكن تعدد الحل . والجواب : منع استنادها إلى كل واحد بالاستقلال، بل إليهما جميعاً بحيث يكون كل منهما جزء علة وليس من ضرورة تركب العلة تركب المعلول، وتوزيع أجزائه على أجزائها أو إلى الواجب تعالى كما هو الرأي الحق
That is the argument of the proponent of the permissibility of two causes concurring on one individual effect: that if we suppose a single atom attached between Zayd and 'Amr, such that Zayd pushes it and 'Amr pulls it in one time and with one degree of force and speed, then the movement is attributed to each of them independently due to no preponderance, while it is one in individual due to the necessary impossibility of two identical things co-existing; and therefore we hypothesized it in the atom, not in the body where multiplicity of course is possible. The answer: we deny its attribution to each one independently; rather, it is attributed to both together such that each is a part of the cause, and it is not necessary from the composition of the cause that the effect be composed, nor [necessarily] the distribution of its parts over its parts; alternatively, [it is attributed] to the Necessary Existent (al-wajib) as is the correct view.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument for two independent causes producing one effect. The proponent gives a thought experiment: two agents acting on a single atom with equal force, so the movement must be independently caused by each. Al-Taftazani denies that each causes it independently. Instead, the movement is caused by both together as a composite cause, and it is not necessary that a composite cause yields a composite effect. The ultimate cause, however, is God alone. This preserves the principle that a single effect cannot have two independent efficient causes.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
استقلال (independence)
الواجب تعالى (the Necessary Existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two agents can independently cause the same effect if they act with equal force on the same object.
Correction: The movement is not independent; it results from a composite cause where each agent contributes partially. True independent causation would require the effect to be separable, which it is not.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani deny that the movement is attributed to each cause independently?
A) Because the movement is one effect and cannot have two independent causes.
B) Because each cause is too weak to produce the movement alone.
C) Because the movement is attributed to God alone.
D) Because the atom is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
A single effect cannot have two independent efficient causes; causal attribution must ultimately be to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute your successes to multiple independent factors—your effort, luck, or other people—while forgetting that ultimately God is the sole Creator of every outcome? How can you begin to see every effect, no matter how small, as directly from Him?
?
Think of a time when you felt torn between two competing influences or motivations, believing both equally caused your action. According to this lesson, neither alone is the independent cause. How does realizing that God is the ultimate cause change the way you understand your own choices and responsibilities?
?
If you truly internalized that no effect, from the movement of an atom to the circumstances of your life, can have two independent causes, how would it reshape your reliance on created means and your reliance on the Creator?

Refuting the Philosophers' First Argument: Source-Relation as Mental Consideration
Al-Taftazani refutes the philosophers' first argument for multiplicity from the One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani rejects the philosophers' first argument for multiplicity by showing that the source-relation is a mental consideration, not a real entity.

Core Idea
The philosophers mistake mental relations for real ones, leading to a flawed argument.

The philosophers argued that the One cannot be the source of multiple effects without introducing composition or regress. Al-Taftazani responds by exposing a critical flaw: they treat the source-relation as real, whereas it is merely a mental consideration. This lesson walks through his refutation, including a reductio and an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes the philosophers' argument, al-Taftazani's response, and an objection.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Find the claim that the source-relation for different effects must be distinct, otherwise composition or infinite regress would follow.
The philosophers base this on the idea that the accident (al-'arid) is an effect and has an issuing.
3
Follow al-Taftazani's response
Note how he first states that the source-relation is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). Then see the consequences if it were real: it would compromise God's unity and lead to infinite effects. Finally, catch the reductio: if the argument were valid, nothing could issue from the One at all.
Each point builds on the previous to dismantle the philosophers' reasoning.
4
Understand the objection and its answer
The objection says negations are merely mental, but issuing appears real. Al-Taftazani's implied answer is that issuing can be both mental and real, but the real aspect does not affect the argument.
This shows that the philosophers cannot distinguish between mental and real aspects of relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت الفلاسفة بوجوه الأول: إن مصدريته لهذا غير مصدريته لذلك فإن دخل فيه شيء منهما ترك وإلا تسلسل ضرورة أن العارض معلول وله صدور ورد بأنها أمر اعتباري، لو كانت متحققة، لم تتمحض وحدة الفاعل، ولزم تكثر المعلولات بل لا تناهيها إذا صدر عن الواجب شيء، إذ معلولية العارض هناك مسلمة، على أنه لو هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يصدر عن الواحد شيء أصلاً، لكون صدوره مغايراً، وأن لا يسلب عنه إلا واحد، ولا يتصف لا بواحد ولا يقبل إلا واحد . فإن قيل : السلوب اعتبارات لا تحقق لها، ولا تمايز في الأعيان، وكذا الاتصاف والقابلية، بخلاف الصدور فإنه كما يطلق على اعتباري، يعرض للعلة والمعلول من حيث هما معاً، يطلق على حقيقي
The philosophers argued with [several] aspects: first, that His being the source (masdariyya) for this is not the same as being the source for that; if something from them [i.e., the source or the effect] entered into it, then composition [would result]; otherwise there would be infinite regress, because the accident (al-'arid) is an effect and has an issuing. The response: it is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari); if it were actual, then the oneness of the agent would not be pure, and multiplicity of effects would be necessary – rather, infinite [multiplicity] if something issued from the Necessary, since the accidentality of the accident there is granted; moreover, based on this argument, nothing would issue from the One at all, because its issuing is other [than the source], and nothing can be negated from it except one, and it cannot be described [even] with one, nor can it accept except one. If it is said: negations are considerations with no reality, nor distinction in exteriors, and likewise description and receptivity, unlike 'issuing' which is, just as it is used for a mental consideration, occurring to cause and effect as a pair, is also used for a real [issuing].
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage refutes the first of the philosophers' arguments for multiplicity from the One. The philosophers argued that the source-relation for different effects must be distinct, otherwise composition or regress occurs. Al-Taftazani responds that the source-relation is a mental consideration, not something real. If it were real, it would destroy God's simplicity and lead to infinite regress. He also offers a reductio: if the argument were valid, nothing could issue from the One because the act of issuing would itself be distinct. Then an objection is raised: negations are merely mental, but issuing seems real. The answer (implied) is that issuing can be considered both mental and real, but the real aspect does not affect the argument against the philosophers. The passage thus defends the possibility of multiplicity by showing the philosophers' argument is based on a mistaken reification of relations.

Key Terms
مصدريّة (source-relation)
عارض (accident)
أمر اعتباري (mental consideration)
صُدُور (issuing)
السلب (negation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument that the source-relation must be distinct for each effect is valid.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows the source-relation is a mental consideration, not a real entity, so the argument fails.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani reject the philosophers' argument that the source-relation for different effects must be distinct?
A) Because the source-relation is a mental consideration, not a real entity.
B) Because God cannot be the source of multiple effects.
C) Because the argument leads to an infinite regress of causes.
D) Because the source-relation is identical for all effects.

Key Takeaway
The source-relation is not a real attribute; it is a mental construct, so the philosophers' argument for multiplicity collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the different blessings in your life, do you ever subconsciously imagine that God has a 'separate source' for each one, like a human manager dividing tasks? How can you actively remind yourself that His unity is not composed of parts, even in your mind?
?
Consider a time you argued about God's attributes with someone. Were you treating mental concepts like they were real divisions in God? What would you change in that conversation now, after learning that relations like 'source' are mere considerations?
?
If you truly believed that every relation between God and creation is a mental construct, how would that change the way you pray or ask for something? Would it increase your confidence that a single, simple God can respond to every need without inner conflict?

The First Proof of the Philosophers and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the philosophers' first proof that from the One only one proceeds, and how al-Taftazani refutes it by showing the sourcehoods are merely mental considerations.

Core Idea
Sourcehoods are mental, not real; otherwise unity breaks and effects multiply infinitely.

The philosophers assert that if the One were the source of multiple distinct effects, its sourcehood for each would be different, leading either to composition in the One or an infinite regress of causes. Al-Taftazani refutes this by arguing that these sourcehoods are merely mental considerations, not real attributes, and that the proof would actually prevent any effect from proceeding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the key terms: مصدرية, اعتباري, العارض, تسلسل.
2
Identify the proof's two alternatives
The proof presents a dilemma: either the sourcehoods are distinct, leading to composition in the One, or they are identical, leading to an infinite regress. Find these in the text.
Composition (تركيب) and infinite regress (تسلسل) are the two unacceptable outcomes.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that sourcehoods are mental considerations (أمر اعتباري). If they were real, the agent's unity would be compromised and effects would multiply infinitely.
The term 'اعتباري' is key: it means something that exists only in the mind, not externally.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The passage ends by showing that the proof would lead to absurdities: nothing could proceed from the One at all, and only one negation could apply. Consider why this undermines the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إن مصدريته لهذا غير مصدريته لذلك فإن دخل فيه شيء منهما تركب وإلا تسلسل ضرورة أن العارض معلول وله صدور ورد بأنها أمر اعتباري، لو كانت متحققة، لم تتمحض وحدة الفاعل، ولزم تكثر المعلولات - بل لا تناهيها إذا صدر عن الواجب شيء، إذ معلولية العارض هناك مسلمة، على أنه لو هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يصدر عن الواحد شيء أصلاً، لكون صدوره مغايراً، وأن لا يسلب عنه إلا واحد، ولا يتصف لا بواحد ولا يقبل إلا واحد.
The first: that his being the source for this is other than his being the source for that. If something of both enters into him, he would be composed; otherwise, there would be an infinite regress necessarily because the accident is an effect and has proceeding. It is refuted by [the response] that they are a mental consideration; if they were real, the unity of the agent would not be pure, and multiplicity of effects would be necessary—rather, their infinity [would follow] if something proceeded from the Necessary, since the effectness of the accident there is conceded. Moreover, on this proof, it would follow that nothing at all proceeds from the One, because its proceeding is other [than it], and that nothing except one is negated of Him, and He is not described by anything nor does He accept anything except one.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the philosophers who assert that from the One only one can proceed, followed by a refutation. The proof is: if the One is the source (maṣdariyya) for two different things (A and B), then these two sourcehoods must be either identical or distinct. If they are distinct, the One would be composed of multiple aspects, breaking its simplicity. If they are identical, then only one effect can proceed, so multiple effects cannot arise—otherwise infinite regress would occur because the accident (ʿāriḍ) of being an effect would itself require a source. The refutation (radd) states that these sourcehoods are merely mental considerations (iʿtibārī), not real attributes. If they were real (muḥaqqiqa), the unity of the agent would be compromised, and the effects would multiply infinitely. Moreover, if the proof were sound, nothing could proceed from the One at all—since even a single proceeding would require a distinct sourcehood, leading to the same problem—and only one negation could apply to Him, which is false.

Key Terms
مصدرية
اعتباري
العارض
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle 'from one only one proceeds' is an absolute logical necessity that cannot be refuted.
Correction: The refutation shows that the principle only holds if the sourcehoods are real; but they are merely mental considerations, so the principle is not absolute and the proof fails.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first proof, what is the nature of the sourcehoods (maṣdariyyāt) that the philosophers posit?
A) Real external attributes
B) Mere mental considerations
C) Essential aspects of the divine essence
D) Accidental qualities that can be separated

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof fails because the sourcehoods are merely mental considerations, not real attributes, and the argument would lead to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's actions in your life, do you unconsciously imagine multiple 'parts' or 'aspects' in His will? How does knowing that His sourcehood is merely a mental consideration change the way you approach your duas?
?
If the philosophers' proof had been sound, nothing could proceed from Allah—including you. What does it mean for you personally that the refutation of this proof affirms that Allah can create without complexity or limitation?
?
When you attribute multiple intentions to Allah (e.g., He created you and also sustains you), do you ever feel uneasy about how to reconcile His unity? How does the concept of 'mental consideration' free you from that unease and deepen your reliance on Him?

Objection to the Mental Nature of Proceeding and Its Refutation
An objection to the first refutation and its brief answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that proceeding (ṣudūr) can be real rather than merely mental, and the author's brief dismissal of this objection.

Core Idea
Proceeding is not always mental; it can be real, but the objection fails to produce a genuine doubt.

This lesson presents an objection to the earlier claim that the cause's attributes are mere mental considerations. The objector argues that proceeding (ṣudūr) is different: it can be real, not just mental. The author responds sharply, dismissing the objection as baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the structure: 'If it is said...' introduces the objection, and 'We say...' gives the answer.
Pay attention to the distinction between mental (iʿtibārī) and real (ḥaqīqī).
2
Analyze the objector's argument
The objector claims that negations are mental, but proceeding can be real. Understand the logic: a real proceeding implies a particularity in the cause that necessitates the effect.
The objector uses the idea that multiplicity of effects would require multiplicity in the cause's particularity.
3
Examine the author's response
The author dismisses the objection as 'baseless assertions' that do not even create a doubt. Reflect on why this dismissal is sufficient.
This shows the author's confidence in the earlier refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : السلوب اعتبارات لا تحقق لها، ولا تمايز في الأعيان، وكذا الاتصاف والقابلية، بخلاف الصدور، فإنه كما يطلق على اعتباري، بعرض للعلة والمعلول من حيث هما معاً، يطلق على حقيقي، هو كون العلة بحيث يصدر عنها المعلول. أعني خصوصية بحسبها يجب المعلول. فإن تعدد المعلول فهو متعدد وإلا فواحد وحينئذ إن كانت العلة علة لذاتها، فهو ذات العلة، وإلا فحالة تعرض لها فلزوم تعدد الجهات، إنما يكون عند صدور الكثير دون الواحد . قلنا : تحكمات لا يقتضي بها شبهة .
If it is said: Negations are considerations having no reality, and no distinction in individuals, and likewise description and receptivity—unlike proceeding (ṣudūr). For just as it is applied as a mental consideration occurring between cause and effect in terms of both together, it is also applied as a real thing (ḥaqīqī) — that is, the cause being such that the effect proceeds from it. I mean, a particularity by virtue of which the effect is necessary. If the effect multiplies, it is multiple; otherwise it is one. And if the cause is a cause by its essence, it is the essence of the cause; otherwise it is a state that occurs to it. Thus the multiplicity of aspects only occurs when there is multiplicity of effects, not with a single effect. We say: Baseless assertions that do not require a doubt to be refuted.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces an objection (fa-in qīl) to the preceding refutation. The objector argues that the philosophers' notion of proceeding (ṣudūr) is not merely a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) but can also be real (ḥaqīqī). Negations (sulūb) are indeed mental, but proceeding is a real relation requiring the cause to have a particularity that necessitates the effect. If multiple effects proceed, that particularity multiplies; otherwise it remains one. So the cause's unity is not compromised by multiple mental aspects but by real multiplicity in the effect. The answer (qulnā) is brief: these are baseless assertions (taḥakkumāt) that do not even produce a plausible suspicion (shubha).

Key Terms

اعتباري
حقيقي
سلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'proceeding' is always a real external relation.
Correction: The author maintains that proceeding can be considered mentally as well, and the objection that it is always real is baseless.

Key Takeaway
The author dismisses the objection that proceeding is real, asserting that it is baseless and does not even create a doubt.

Multiplicity of Effect and the Simple Agent
Objection and its refutation regarding multiplicity of effect and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that multiplicity in effect forces multiplicity in the agent fails, and why mental aspects do not violate the real unity of the simple.

Core Idea
Conceptual multiplicity does not break real unity.

The philosophers face a second objection: if the effect multiplies, doesn't the agent have to multiply too? The objector argues even a mental aspect (ḥaythiyya) would imply multiplicity. But this objection backfires, as the answer will show—it undermines the philosophers' own principle about the simple. Let's read the exchange closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on what the objector claims: that any multiplicity in effect forces at least a mental multiplicity in the agent, and if there is no such multiplicity, the effect is one.
The objector is trying to force the philosophers into a dilemma.
2
Understand the refutation
Notice how the answer dismisses the objection as empty and self-defeating. It says the reasoning would also prevent the simple from producing even one effect, because that too requires a mental aspect. So the objection actually demolishes the philosophers' own foundation.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Identify the distinction between real unity (waḥda ḥaqīqiyya) and mental aspects (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya). The answer asserts that mental aspects do not harm real unity. This is the core of the defense.
Without this distinction, tawḥīd would be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مرادهم أنه كلما تكثر المعلول تكثر الفاعل ولو بالحيثية ضرورة أن فاعليته لهذا اعتبار مغاير لفاعليته لذلك، ويلزم أنه كلما لم يكن تكثر في الفاعل، ولو بالحيثية اتحد المعلول. قلنا : كلام خال عن التحصيل هادم أساس قواعدهم المبنية على امتناع تعدد أثر البسيط. فإن تعدد الحيثيات العقلية لا يقدح في الوحدة الحقيقية، وإلا لما أمكن أن يصدر عنه الواحد أيضاً لأن مصدريته له اعتبار مغاير لـه بحسب العقل ضرورة.
If it is said: What they intend is that whenever the effect multiplies, the agent multiplies, even if by aspect, necessarily because his agency (fāʿiliyya) for this is a consideration different from his agency for that; and it follows that whenever there is no multiplicity in the agent, even by aspect, the effect is one. We say: Speech devoid of substance, demolishing the foundation of their principles built on the impossibility of multiple effects of the simple (basīṭ). For multiplicity of mental aspects does not harm real unity; otherwise, it would not be possible for the One to produce even one thing, because his sourcehood (maṣdariyya) for it is a consideration different from him mentally, necessarily.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is the second objection (fa-in qīl) raised after the first one. The objector restates the philosophers' position more precisely: whenever effects multiply, the agent must multiply, even if only by a mentally distinguishable aspect (ḥaythiyya). This means that if the agent has no such internal multiplicity, the effect must be one. The answer (qulnā) is sharp: this line of reasoning is empty and actually undermines the philosophers' own fundamental principle (qawāʿid) that the simple (basīṭ) cannot produce multiple effects. The reason is that even the production of a single effect requires that the agent have some particular aspect by which it produces that effect. If mental aspects were problematic, then even one effect would be impossible. Thus the objection fails because mental aspects do not compromise real unity.

Key Terms
فاعلية
حيثية
بسيط
وحدة حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any differentiation in the agent, even conceptual, breaks its real unity.
Correction: Mental aspects (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) do not affect real unity (waḥda ḥaqīqiyya). The simple can have multiple mental considerations without compromising its oneness.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say about the philosophers' principle that the simple cannot have multiple effects?
A) It is correct and must be accepted
B) It is contradicted by the objection itself
C) It is demolished by the objection's reasoning
D) It is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Mental aspects do not compromise real unity; the simple can have multiple effects through mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Commit to reflecting on your essential unity in worship today.
?
The objector assumed that a difference in consideration implies a real division. Where in your relationship with Allah do you confuse mere conceptual distinctions (like His attributes) with real separation? Ask yourself: Do I truly believe that Allah's knowledge, power, will are not separate from His essence? If not, what doubt lingers? Resolve to study His unity more deeply.
?
If even a single effect from the One requires a mental aspect, then every action of yours is a sign of His power without dividing Him. Does your heart feel awe at His simplicity? Or do you unconsciously limit His ability by imagining that multiple creations exhaust Him? Repent of any subtle anthropomorphism and praise Him as al-Ahad.

The Impossibility of Two Contradictory Effects and Its Refutation
Second proof of the philosophers and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof that the One cannot produce two different things without multiple aspects, and its refutation that distinguishes between truth and existence.

Core Idea
A genuine contradiction requires the same proposition to be both true and false.

The philosophers argue that if the One, being simple, produced two distinct effects, that would imply a logical contradiction. However, the refutation shows this is only an apparent contradiction caused by confusing truth with existence. This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial logical distinction in kalām arguments about divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument before breaking it down.
Notice the technical terms: نقيضان (contradictories), جهة (aspect), صدق (truth), وجود (existence).
2
Identify the proof structure
The proof has three parts: a premise (if A and not-A both proceed), a conclusion (contradiction follows), and an escape clause (unless aspects differ). Find each part in the text.
The escape clause is mentioned but then refuted.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues there is no contradiction between the proceeding of A and the proceeding of not-A. The true contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed', not 'not-A proceeds'. Grasp this distinction.
This hinges on the difference between existence (a thing being true of something) and truth (a proposition being true).
4
Apply the key distinction
The real impossibility is that the same proposition be both true and false. Here, the two propositions 'A proceeds' and 'not-A proceeds' can both be true in existence; the contradiction only arises if we claim 'A proceeds' and 'A does not proceed' together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه إذا صدر عنه ( أ ) فلو صدر عنه (ب) وهو ليس (أ) اجتمع النقيضان بخلاف ما إذا تعددت الجهة، فإن كلا يستند إلى جهة . ورد بأن صدور (أ) لا يناقض صدور ما ليس (أ) بل عدم صدور (أ) وهو وإن صدق على صدور ما ليس (أ) لكن لا امتناع في اجتماع الشيء، وما يصدق عليه نقيضه، إذا كان بحسب الوجود دون الصدق، وإنما الممتنع إن يصدق عليه، أنه يصدر عنه (أ) ولا يصدر عنه (أ)
The second: that if from Him proceeds (A), and if from Him proceeds (B) while it is not (A), then two contradictories would come together, unlike when the aspect is multiple, for each is attached to an aspect. It is refuted by saying: Proceeding of (A) does not contradict the proceeding of what is not (A); rather, the non-proceeding of (A) contradicts it. And even if it is true of the proceeding of what is not (A) [that it is the non-proceeding of (A)], there is no impossibility in a thing being true together with its contradictory being true of it, if it is in terms of existence rather than truth. What is impossible is that it be true that from Him proceeds (A) and does not proceed (A).
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-thānī) of the philosophers. The proof argues: if the One produces two things, say A and B, and B is not A, then the propositions 'A proceeds from Him' and 'non-A proceeds from Him' are both true, which is a contradiction (i.e., 'A proceeds' and ~'A proceeds' are true together). The only escape is if the aspects are multiple—meaning the cause has distinct aspects for each effect. The refutation (radd) states that there is no genuine contradiction. The proceeding of A does not contradict the proceeding of non-A; it contradicts only the non-proceeding of A. The statement 'non-A proceeds from Him' can be true while 'A proceeds from Him' is also true, because there is no impossibility in a thing and its logical contradictory both being true of something if the contradiction is in terms of existence (bi-ḥasab al-wujūd) rather than in terms of truth (al-ṣidq). The real impossibility would be if the same proposition 'A proceeds from Him' were both true and false at the same time.

Key Terms
نقيضان
جهة
صدق
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that having two different effects from the One implies a logical contradiction because 'A proceeds' and 'non-A proceeds' are contradictory.
Correction: They are not true contradictories. The true contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed'. 'Non-A proceeds' is a different proposition that can coexist with 'A proceeds' in terms of existence. The only impossible situation is the same proposition being both true and false.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the real impossibility regarding proceeding from the One?
A) That two different things proceed
B) That the same thing both proceeds and does not proceed
C) That two contradictory propositions are true in terms of existence
D) That aspects are necessary

Key Takeaway
The real impossibility is that the same proposition be both true and false; two different things proceeding from the One do not constitute a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two different blessings or trials from God that seem to pull in opposite directions, do you ever assume that God is inconsistent? How does this lesson's distinction between existence and truth help you trust His oneness despite apparent contradictions? Will you consciously recall this distinction next time you feel spiritual confusion?
?
Think of a time you doubted God's wisdom because of a situation that seemed contradictory (e.g., hardship after prayer). Did you fall into the philosophers' fallacy by assuming that multiple effects from one cause must conflict? How can you train your heart to see that God's actions are never contradictory in truth, even if they seem so in existence? Commit to repeating the key takeaway this week.

The Third Causal Argument and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the third argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for 'from one only one proceeds'—that differing effects require differing causes—and why it is refuted as relying on unproven premises about causality.

Core Idea
Different effects don't always mean different causes.

We now encounter the third argument that attempts to prove that from one cause only one effect can proceed. This argument appeals to a seemingly natural intuition: if effects differ, then their causes must differ. But is this intuition always reliable? Taftazani presents the argument and then immediately subjects it to a sharp refutation, exposing its hidden assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
The argument states a principle: inferring the difference of causes from the difference of effects is ingrained in minds. Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim.
Notice that the argument does not provide any proof—it simply asserts an intuition.
2
Identify the two premises in the refutation
The refutation claims the argument is built on two premises: (1) an effect cannot lag behind its cause, and (2) a necessary condition cannot exist without its necessary concomitant. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on why these premises are problematic
Ask yourself: are these premises self-evident? Could an effect be delayed? Could a necessary condition exist without its concomitant in some cases? The refutation suggests the argument is not conclusive.
4
Connect to the larger debate
Consider how this argument and refutation fit into the overall discussion of 'from one only one proceeds'. This is one of multiple attempts to prove that doctrine, each refuted.
The discussion shows the complexity of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الاستدلال باختلاف الآثار على اختلاف المؤثرات مركوز في العقول ورد بأنه مبنى على امتناع تخلف المعلول عن علته، وتحقق الملزوم بدون لازمه .
The third: That inferring from the difference of effects to the difference of causes is ingrained in minds. It is refuted by saying that it is based on the impossibility of the effect lagging behind its cause, and the existence of the necessary condition without its necessary concomitant.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument and its refutation. The argument appeals to a natural intuition: if effects differ, then their causes must differ. This principle is said to be firmly rooted in human reason. The refutation points out that this argument implicitly assumes two premises: (1) that an effect cannot be delayed relative to its cause (i.e., causality is immediate), and (2) that the necessary condition (الملزوم) cannot exist without its necessary concomitant (اللازم). Both premises are questionable and would need further justification. Thus the argument from common-sense intuition is not conclusive.

Key Terms
الآثار (effects)
المؤثرات (causes)
التخلف (lagging)
الملزوم (necessary condition)
اللازم (necessary concomitant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different effects always require different causes, and this is self-evident.
Correction: This inference is not self-evident; it depends on assumptions about the immediacy of causation and the necessary relationship between a condition and its concomitant.

Checkpoint
On what assumption does the third argument rely according to its refutation?
A) That causes must be multiple if effects are multiple.
B) That effects cannot lag behind their causes and the necessary condition cannot exist without its concomitant.
C) That effects are identical to their causes.
D) That the mind naturally knows all causal relations.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive link between different effects and different causes is not necessarily valid because it assumes immediate causation and necessary concomitance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something in your life has multiple effects, it must have multiple causes? How does this lesson challenge you to pause before drawing such conclusions about your own experiences and relationships?
?
Consider an area of your life where you've been certain about a cause-effect relationship. Are you willing to admit that your certainty might rest on unexamined assumptions? What would it take for you to re-evaluate that conviction today?
?
The lesson points out that even intuitive inferences need to be justified. Is there a belief you hold about yourself or about God’s actions in your life that you have never questioned because it seemed obvious? Will you commit to examining the hidden premises behind that belief?

Objections 5 and 6 to the One-to-One Principle and Their Answer
Two objections and their refutation regarding the one-to-one principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections to the principle that from one only one proceeds, and the sophisticated answer that distinguishes mental considerations from real particularity.

Core Idea
Mental multiplicity does not imply real composition in the Divine.

Sanusi presents two powerful objections to the principle that from a simple cause only one effect can proceed. The first objection focuses on the concept of source-ness (maṣdariyya) leading to infinite regress; the second broadens the attack to negation, description, and receptivity. The answer provides a crucial distinction: mental considerations require two relata, but the real meaning of 'issuing' requires only one relatum, and introduces the concept of particularity (khuṣūṣiyya).

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answer as a continuous argument.
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the logical flow from objections to answer. Notice how the objections are formulated as hypotheticals leading to absurd consequences.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the first objection about source-ness.
The first objection argues that if source-ness (maṣdariyya) is a real attribute, then the first effect would itself be a source, leading to infinite regress. Note the chain: source-ness → multiplicity → regress.
3
Identify the second objection about negation, description, and receptivity.
The second objection extends the argument: if the principle holds, then God cannot negate, describe, or receive multiple things without composition or regress, because each concept is different.
This objection aims to show the principle leads to absurd consequences.
4
Understand the answer's core distinction.
The answer states that negation, description, and receptivity are mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) that require two relata and thus multiplicity, but 'issuing' can be a real meaning requiring only one relatum (the cause).
This is the pivotal point.
5
Explore the concept of particularity (khuṣūṣiyya).
The answer further explains that when a cause produces many effects, it requires distinct particularities; but for a single effect, only one particularity (the cause's essence or a state) is needed.
This defuses the objections for the case of the Simple One producing exactly one effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه - أنه لو تحققت المصدرية لزم تكثر المعلولات، بل لا تناهيها فيما إذا صدر عن الواجب شيء فإن المصدرية حينئذ بعد ما تكون خارجة، لا يجوز أن تكون معلولاً لأمر آخر، بل تكون معلولاً للواجب، صادراً عنه، فتتحقق مصدرية أخرى بالنسبة إليه، ويتسلسل . ٦ - وأنه لوصح هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يصدر عن الواحد المحض شيء أصلاً وإلا لكانت هناك مصدرية داخلة، فيتركب أو خارجة فيتسلسل، وأن لا يسلب عنه أشياء كثيرة ، كسلب الحجر والشجر عن الإنسان، وأن لا يتصف بأشياء كثيرة، كاتصاف زيد بالقيام والقعود، وأن لا يقبل أشياء كثيرة كقبول الجسم للحركة والسواد لأن مفهوم سلب هذا مغاير لمفهوم سلب ذاك ، وكذا الاتصاف والقابلية، فيلتزم إما التركب أو التسلسل . وقد يجاب عن هذه الاعتراضات كلها بأن سلب الشيء عن الشيء، واتصاف الشيء بالشيء وقابلية الشيء للشيء من الاعتبارات العقلية التي تحقق لها، ولا تمايز بينها في الأعيان ولو سلم فهي لا تلحق الواحد من حيث هو واحد، بل تستدعي كثرة تلحقها هي ، باعتبارات مختلفة، فإن السلب يفتقر إلى مسلوب ومسلوب عنه يتقدمانه، ولا يكفي ثبوت المسلوب عنه فقط، وكذا الاتصاف يفتقر إلى موصوف وصفة ، والقابلية إلى قابل ومقبول، أو إلى قابل وشيء يوجد المقبول فيه ، بخلاف الصدور ، فإنه كما يطلق على الأمر الإضافي الذي يعرض للعلة والمعلول من حيث يعتبر العقل نسبة أحدهما إلى الآخر، وليس كلامنا فيه كذلك يطلق على معنى حقيقي هو كون العلة، بحيث يصدر عنها المعلول وكلامنا فيه ويكفي في تحققه فرض شيء واحد هو العلة، وإلا امتنع استناد المعلولات إلى مبدأ واحد ولما كان الظاهر من كون الشيء بحيث يصدر عنه شيء أيضاً أمراً إضافياً اعتبارياً، زعموا أن المراد به خصوصية بالقياس إلى الأثر بحسبها يجب الأثر، وأنه وجودي بالضرورة، فإنا إذا أصدرنا حركات متعددة فما لم يحصل لنا خصوصيته بالقياس إلى كل حركة، وأقلها إرادتها لم يصدر عنا تلك الحركة وهكذا سائر العلل الفاعلية لا يصدر عنها الأشياء الكثيرة، إلا إذا كان لها مع كل منها خصوصية لا تكون مع الآخر. وإذا صدر الشيء الواحد، لم يلزم تعدد الخصوصية، بل لم يجز ، وحينئذ إن كانت العلة علة لذاتها، فتلك الخصوصية ذات العلة، وإن كانت علة لا لذاتها، بل بحسب حالة أخرى، فتلك الخصوصية حالة تعرض لذات العلة . فلزوم تعدد الجهات وتكثر المعلولات إنما يكون عند صدور الكثير، وأما عند صدور الواحد، فلا يكون إلا ذات العلة أو حالة لها، وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات
5. That if source-ness were realized, it would necessitate multiplicity of effects, indeed their infinity, in that when something issues from the Necessary, then the source-ness at that point is external; it is not permissible that it be an effect of something else, but rather it is an effect of the Necessary, issuing from it, so another source-ness is realized in relation to it, and there would be an infinite regress. 6. And that if this proof were valid, it would necessitate that nothing at all issue from the Simple One — otherwise there would be an internal source-ness, so composition would follow, or an external one, so infinite regress; and that many negations not be negated of Him, like the negation of stone and tree from a human; and that He not be described by many attributes, like Zayd's being described by standing and sitting; and that He not receive many things, like a body's receiving motion and blackness — because the concept of negating this is different from the concept of negating that, and likewise description and receptivity. Thus either composition or infinite regress would be entailed. And it may be answered against all these objections: that the negation of a thing from a thing, the description of a thing by a thing, and the receptivity of a thing to a thing are mental considerations that have realization, but there is no distinction among them in external entities. Even if granted, they do not apply to the One qua one, but rather require multiplicity that attaches to them, due to different considerations. For negation requires a negated thing and a thing from which it is negated, which precede it, and mere existence of the thing from which it is negated is insufficient; likewise description requires a described thing and an attribute; receptivity requires a receptive thing and a thing received, or a receptive thing and a thing in which the received exists. This is unlike 'issuing', for just as it is applied to the relative matter that occurs to the cause and effect from the perspective of the intellect considering the relation of one to the other — and this is not our concern — so too it is applied to a real meaning, which is that the cause is such that the effect issues from it — and this is our concern. And its realization is sufficient by positing a single thing, namely the cause; otherwise, the dependence of effects on a single principle would be impossible. And since the apparent meaning of 'a thing being such that something issues from it' is also a relative, mental matter, they claimed that what is meant by it is a particularity with respect to the effect, by which the effect is necessary, and that it is existent by necessity. For when we produce multiple movements, unless we acquire a particularity with respect to each movement — the least of which is intending it — that movement does not issue from us. Likewise all agent causes do not produce many things unless they have with each of them a particularity not had with the other. And when a single thing issues, multiplicity of particularities is not entailed; indeed it is not possible. In that case, if the cause is a cause by its essence, then that particularity is the essence of the cause; and if it is a cause not by its essence but by another state, then that particularity is a state that occurs to the essence of the cause. So the necessity of multiplicity of aspects and multiplicity of effects only occurs when many things issue. As for when a single thing issues, there is only the essence of the cause or a state of it. On this basis, none of the objections apply to it.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (5 and 6) to the principle that from a simple, non-composite cause only one effect can proceed, followed by a detailed answer. The first objection argues that if source-ness (maṣdariyya) is a real attribute, then the first effect from God would itself be a source, leading to infinite regress. The second objection broadens the attack: if the principle holds, then God cannot negate, describe, or receive multiple things without composition or regress. The answer distinguishes between real external attributes and mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya). Negation, description, and receptivity are mental — they require two relata and thus multiplicity. But 'issuing' (ṣudūr) can be a real meaning requiring only one thing (the cause). To further clarify, the answer introduces the idea of 'particularity' (khuṣūṣiyya): when a cause produces many effects, it requires a distinct particularity for each; but when a single effect issues, only one particularity (the cause’s essence or a state) is needed. Thus the objections do not apply when the cause produces exactly one effect.

Key Terms
المصدرية (al-maṣdariyya)
الواحد المحض (al-wāḥid al-maḥḍ)
الصدور (al-ṣudūr)
الاعتبارات العقلية (al-iʿtibārāt al-ʿaqliyya)
الخصوصية (al-khuṣūṣiyya)
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute that involves relation to multiple things makes God composite.
Correction: The passage distinguishes mental considerations (which require two relata but are not real) from real attributes; ṣudūr as a real meaning requires only one relatum.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what distinguishes negation, description, and receptivity from 'issuing' (ṣudūr)?
A) Negation, description, and receptivity are real external attributes, while ṣudūr is a mental consideration.
B) Negation, description, and receptivity are mental considerations requiring two relata, while ṣudūr can be a real meaning requiring only one relatum.
C) Ṣudūr is impossible for the Simple One, while the others are possible.
D) All four are mental considerations and have no distinction.

Key Takeaway
The objections to the 'one from one' principle are deflected by distinguishing mental considerations from real particularity: God's producing one effect requires only one aspect, not multiple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God's attributes as if they were separate 'parts' added to His essence, forgetting that in His case, they are identical to His essence? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your conception of divine simplicity?
?
When you reflect on the causes and effects in your own life—your actions, your sustenance, your trials—do you truly see them as issuing directly from the One Simple Cause, or do you unconsciously imagine intermediaries that compete with God's absolute unity? What one habit can you change today to deepen your reliance on Him alone?
?
The lesson teaches that mental multiplicity does not imply real multiplicity in God. But do you treat your own thoughts about God—your theological concepts and mental images—as if they are the reality itself? In what specific way might you be mistaking your limited mental consideration for the transcendent reality of the Divine, and how will you guard against that today?

The Final Proof: Why a Simple Cause Cannot Produce Two Effects
A concise demonstration that two effects from a simple cause entail composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that from a truly simple cause only one effect can proceed, and why multiple effects would imply composition in the cause.

Core Idea
Multiple effects from a single cause would force the cause to be composite – a contradiction.

The principle 'from one only one proceeds' is a cornerstone of Islamic theology. In this passage, the author provides a decisive proof: if two distinct effects were to issue from the One, the causal relationship for each would be conceptually different, thereby introducing multiplicity into the supposedly simple cause. Let's examine this elegant argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the key terms: المفهوم (concept), العليّة (causality), الواحد الحقيقي (the truly One), الخلف (contradiction).
The argument is short but dense.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: assume two effects → then concepts differ → cause cannot be simple → contradiction.
This is a classic kalam proof.
3
Reflect on why this matters
This proof safeguards divine simplicity. Without it, one might mistakenly attribute multiplicity to Allah.
4
Consider potential objections
Some might argue that a cause can have multiple aspects. Why does this proof reject that?
The proof shows that even aspects would be conceptually distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بين المطلوب بوجه لا يرد عليه الاعتراضات يدعى أنه زيادة تنبيه وتوضيح ، وإلا فامتناع صدور الكثير عن الواحد الحقيقي واضح، لأنه لو صدر عنه شيئان فمفهوم عليته لأحدهما مغاير لمفهوم عليته للآخر بالضرورة، والشيء مع أحد المتغايرين لا يكون هو فالمفروض لا يكون شيئاً واحداً محضاً، بل شيئين أو شيئاً موصوفاً بصفتين، هذا خلف
And the desired point has been demonstrated in a manner that admits no objections, claiming that it is added as a reminder and clarification. Otherwise, the impossibility of many things issuing from the truly One is clear: for if two things issued from Him, then the concept of His causality for one is necessarily different from the concept of His causality for the other, and a thing with one of two different things is not it [i.e., the same]. Thus the supposed thing would not be a single simple thing, but rather two things or a single thing described by two attributes — this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own rigorous proof for the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. He asserts that if two distinct effects were to proceed from a single, simple cause, then the causality (ʿilliyya) of that cause for the first effect would be conceptually different from its causality for the second effect. Since the cause is one, it cannot be both of these distinct concepts simultaneously without composition or multiplicity arising within it. Therefore, the cause would either be two things (compound) or one thing with multiple attributes — both contradicting the assumption of true simplicity. The argument is direct: it reduces the supposed scenario to a contradiction (khalaf). The author claims this proof is immune to the earlier objections.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhūm)
العليّة (al-ʿilliyya)
الواحد الحقيقي (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī)
الخلف (al-khalaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple cause can have multiple effects if it has multiple aspects or attributes.
Correction: The proof shows that even different causal concepts would introduce multiplicity (at least conceptually) into the cause, violating its absolute simplicity. The cause would then be either two things or one thing with two attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the final proof that from the truly One only one can proceed?
A) The cause must be distinct from its effects.
B) The concept of causality for each effect is necessarily different, so the cause would have multiple concepts.
C) Effects must be similar to their cause.
D) The cause is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The truly One cannot be the source of multiple effects, because that would require distinct causal concepts, undermining absolute simplicity.

The Impossibility of Many from the One
Author refutes a defense of multiplicity from one cause and provides a clear proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a truly simple cause cannot produce multiple distinct effects, and why the opponents' defense using 'particularity' is insufficient.

Core Idea
A truly simple cause cannot have multiple distinct causal aspects.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental objection against monotheistic causality: how can one God create a diverse world? He first presents a common defense—that causes have particularities for each effect—then shows it is arbitrary. Finally, he provides a crisp logical proof: if two effects issued, the concept of causation for each would differ, so the cause would not be truly one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice that the author first describes an opposing view and then gives his own proof.
The passage contains two main parts: the opponents' claim and the author's refutation.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
The opponents claim that a cause can have multiple 'particularities' (khuṣūṣiyyāt) for different effects, and that this allows multiplicity without compromising simplicity. Look for how they explain the production of multiple movements through distinct volitions.
The opponents use the example of voluntary movements to illustrate their point.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author dismisses the opponents' premises as arbitrary and unsupported. Note his sharp language: 'most of these premises are arbitrary, not supported by a semblance of proof'.
This critique sets the stage for a more rigorous proof.
4
Grasp the final proof
The author's proof is simple: if two different effects issued, the concept of causation for each would be different. A truly simple cause cannot have two different concepts of causation—that would imply it is either two things or a thing with two attributes, which is a contradiction.
This proof relies on the principle that a simple thing cannot have diverse causal relations without internal multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع امتنع استناد المعلولات إلى مبدأ واحد ولما كان الظاهر من كون الشيء بحيث يصدر عنه شيء أيضاً أمراً إضافياً اعتبارياً، زعموا أن المراد به خصوصية بالقياس إلى الأثر بحسبها يجب الأثر، وأنه وجودي بالضرورة، فإنا إذا أصدرنا حركات متعددة فما لم يحصل لنا خصوصيته بالقياس إلى كل حركة، وأقلها إرادتها لم يصدر عنا تلك الحركة وهكذا سائر العلل الفاعلية لا يصدر عنها الأشياء الكثيرة، إلا إذا كان لها مع كل منها خصوصية لا تكون مع الآخر. وإذا صدر الشيء الواحد، لم يلزم تعدد الخصوصية، بل لم يجز، وحينئذ إن كانت العلة علة لذاتها، فتلك الخصوصية ذات العلة، وإن كانت علة لا لذاتها، بل بحسب حالة أخرى، فتلك الخصوصية حالة تعرض لذات العلة. فلزوم تعدد الجهات وتكثر المعلولات إنما يكون عند صدور الكثير، وأما عند صدور الواحد، فلا يكون إلا ذات العلة أو حالة لها، وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات لكن لا يخفى أن أكثر هذه المقدمات تحكمات لا يعضدها شبهة فضلا عن حجة. وقد بين المطلوب بوجه لا يرد عليه الاعتراضات يدعى أنه زيادة تنبيه وتوضيح، وإلا فامتناع صدور الكثير عن الواحد الحقيقي واضح، لأنه لو صدر عنه شيئان فمفهوم عليته لأحدهما مغاير لمفهوم عليته للآخر بالضرورة، والشيء مع أحد المتغايرين لا يكون هو الآخر فالمفروض لا يكون شيئاً واحداً محضاً، بل شيئين أو شيئاً موصوفاً بصفتين، هذا خلف.
All [agree] that it is impossible for effects to be based on a single principle. And since the apparent meaning of a thing being such that something also issues from it is a relational, mental consideration, they claimed that what is intended by it is a particularity in relation to the effect by which the effect is necessitated, and that it is existential necessarily. For when we produce multiple movements, if we do not obtain a particularity of ours in relation to each movement — the least of which is its willing — that movement does not issue from us. Likewise, all other efficient causes do not produce many things unless for each of them they have a particularity that is not with the other. And when the single thing issues, multiplicity of particularity is not necessary; rather it is not permissible. Then, if the cause is a cause by its own essence, that particularity is the essence of the cause; and if it is a cause not by its own essence but by another state, then that particularity is a state that attaches to the essence of the cause. Thus, the necessity of multiplicity of aspects and multiplicity of effects only occurs when many things issue. But when one thing issues, there is nothing but the essence of the cause or a state of it. And on this basis, no objection applies to it. However, it is not hidden that most of these premises are arbitrary, not supported by a semblance [of proof], let alone a proof. And the desired point has been demonstrated in a way that no objections apply, which is claimed to be an additional warning and clarification. Otherwise, the impossibility of many things issuing from the true one is clear, because if two things issued from it, then the concept of its causation for one of them is necessarily different from the concept of its causation for the other, and a thing together with one of two differing things is not the other. So the assumed thing is not a single pure thing, but two things or a thing described by two attributes — this is a contradiction (khalaf).
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the principle that from a truly simple cause only one effect can proceed, followed by a critique of a previous defense. The author begins by noting that everyone agrees it is impossible for effects to depend on a single principle without further qualification. He then recounts the opponents' view: the particularity (khuṣūṣiyya) of the cause in relation to each effect is merely a relational mental consideration; they claim it is an existential property that necessitates the effect. The author illustrates with an example: when we perform multiple voluntary movements, each movement requires a distinct volition (irāda) — a particularity in us toward that movement. Similarly, every efficient cause produces many effects only if it has a distinct particularity for each. If only one effect issues, multiple particularities are neither necessary nor permissible. If the cause is cause by its very essence, that particularity is its essence; if by a state, it is that state. So multiplicity of aspects (jihāt) is necessary only when many effects issue; for a single effect only the essence or one state is involved. This, they claim, avoids objections. The author then sharply criticizes this position: most of these premises are arbitrary (taḥakkumāt), lacking even a semblance of proof. He then provides what he considers a clearer proof: if two different effects issued from a truly simple cause, the very concept of causation (mafhūm al-‘illiyya) for each would be different — but a single simple thing cannot have two different concepts of causation; it would have to be either two things or one thing with two distinct attributes, which is a contradiction (khalaf). Thus the impossibility of multiplicity from the true one is clear.

Key Terms
الواحد الحقيقي
العلة
المعلول
الخصوصية
الجهات
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a cause can be truly simple yet have multiple distinct aspects or relations without any real multiplicity in its essence.
Correction: The author shows that if a cause has multiple distinct causal relations (concepts of causation), it cannot be truly simple; it would either be multiple things or have multiple attributes, contradicting its simplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for a single, true one to produce multiple distinct effects?
A) Because the concept of causation for each effect must be identical, but the effects are different.
B) Because the concept of causation for one effect would be different from the concept for another, so the cause would have multiple aspects or attributes, contradicting its absolute unity.
C) Because the cause would need to be composed of parts to produce multiple effects.
D) Because the effects would have to be identical if they come from one cause.

Key Takeaway
A truly simple cause cannot have two distinct causal concepts, so it cannot produce two different effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson teaches that a simple cause cannot have two different causal intentions. How does this truth expose the contradiction in dividing your intention? Take a moment to repent and renew your sincerity.

What would it cost you to let go of these secondary 'causes' in your heart?

The author argues that if a cause had two causal concepts, it would not be a single pure thing. Consider your own heart: are you trying to serve both Allah and your worldly ambitions through the same actions? This is a spiritual contradiction. What is one specific area where you must cut away a secondary 'causal concept' to return to single-hearted devotion? Commit to a concrete change today.

Critique of the Contrapositive Argument for Unity of Effect
Author critiques the contrapositive argument for unity of effect and defends conceptual multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contrapositive argument for unity of effect is considered weak and how conceptual multiplicity does not undermine divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Conceptual multiplicity in God's causation does not imply real multiplicity.

This passage challenges a popular argument for the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'. The argument uses contrapositive logic to claim that unity of agent necessitates unity of effect. The author shows this argument is self-defeating, because it would prevent any effect at all from a simple agent. We will explore his reasoning and the distinction between real and conceptual multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense, technical style of the critique.
2
Identify the contrapositive argument
The passage begins by presenting the argument: if multiplicity of effect implies multiplicity in agent, then unity of agent implies unity of effect by contrapositive. Understand that this is the target of the critique.
Contrapositive is a logical rule: if P implies Q, then not-Q implies not-P.
3
Understand why the author dismisses it
The author says this argument is 'of little benefit' and 'destroys the foundation' of the principle. Ask yourself why he says that. His key point is that it would also prevent any effect from issuing from a simple cause.
He uses the example of 'knowledge' (ʿilmiyya) as a conceptual relation distinct from the essence.
4
Grasp the distinction between real and conceptual
The author explains that a simple cause can have multiple conceptual relations (mafāhīm iʿtibāriyya) without compromising its real unity. If you reject that, nothing could issue from it. Reflect on how this preserves divine simplicity while allowing multiple effects.
This is a core insight in Islamic philosophy and kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان تكثر المعلول مستلزماً التكثر في الفاعل، كان وحدة الفاعل مستلزمة لوحدة المعلول بحكم عكس النقيض، ولا خفاء في أن هذا كلام قليل الجدوى، بعيد عن أن يجعل من معارك الآراء، وتفسيره على هذا الوجه يهدم أساس المسائل المبنية على أنه لا يصدر من البسيط شيئان. فانه يجوز أن يصدر عنه أشياء، ويكون عليته لكل منها مفهوماً اعتبارياً مغايراً لعليته للآخر ولا يقدح ذلك في وحدته وبساطته الحقيقية، وإلا لما جاز أن يصدر عنه شيء أصلاً لأن علميته لذلك الشيء مفهوم مغاير لذات العلة بحسب التعقل ضرورة كونه نسبة له إلى المعلول.
And if the multiplicity of the effect necessitates multiplicity in the agent, then the unity of the agent necessitates the unity of the effect by the rule of contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ). There is no hiding that this speech is of little benefit, far from being considered a battlefield of opinions. Its interpretation in this manner destroys the foundation of the issues built on [the principle] that two things do not issue from the simple. For it is permissible that things issue from it, and its causation for each of them is a mental concept (mafhūm iʿtibārī) different from its causation for the other, and that does not harm its real unity and simplicity. Otherwise, it would not be permissible that anything issue from it at all, because its knowledge (ʿilmiyya) of that thing is a concept different from the essence of the cause according to intellection, by necessity of it being a relation of it to the effect.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific argument that attempts to prove the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' by contrapositive. The argument runs: if multiplicity of effect implies multiplicity in the agent, then conversely, unity of agent implies unity of effect. The author dismisses this as 'of little benefit' and warns that it undermines the very basis of the issues that depend on the principle. His reasoning is that if we accept this strict logical contrapositive, then we would have to conclude that a simple cause cannot produce any effect at all — because even a single effect requires a conceptual distinction (the knowledge or causation of that effect) which is different from the simple essence. In other words, if any conceptual multiplicity in the cause (such as different relations to effects) were taken as a real multiplicity, then even one effect would be impossible. But the principle itself is about real, not merely conceptual, multiplicity. The author argues that it is perfectly possible for a simple cause to have multiple conceptual relations (mafāhīm iʿtibāriyya) without any real division in its essence, and therefore the contrapositive argument is too simplistic and self-defeating.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
البسيط
المفهوم الاعتباري
العلمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any conceptual plurality in God's knowledge or causation implies real multiplicity in His essence.
Correction: Conceptual plurality (e.g., different relations to effects) does not entail real multiplicity; it is merely a mental consideration and does not compromise real simplicity.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the argument that unity of agent necessitates unity of effect by contrapositive?
A) It is sound and supports the principle.
B) It is beneficial and must be accepted.
C) It is of little benefit and undermines the principle because it would prevent any effect from issuing from a simple agent.
D) It is the only valid proof for the principle.

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive argument for unity of effect is self-defeating because it fails to distinguish between real and conceptual multiplicity, which is essential for understanding the principle of divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God's simplicity means He cannot be intimately involved in the details of your life? The author argues that conceptual multiplicity allows Him to create many things without division. How does that challenge your assumptions? Will you now trust that God's unity is not a barrier to His direct causation of every moment of your existence?
?
The author dismisses the contrapositive argument as 'of little benefit'. Have you ever relied on a neat logical argument about God only to find it reduces faith to a formula? How does this warning against over-reliance on human reasoning stir you to approach theological mysteries with more humility? What will you do the next time you are tempted to prove something about God with mere logic?
?
If even one effect from God requires a conceptual distinction in His causation, then every prayer of yours, every breath, is an effect that depends on a specific relationship between God and that event. Does this make you feel your complete dependence on Him? How should this awareness change the way you live this day?

The First Face: Refuting 'From the One Only One Proceeds'
Al-Taftazani presents the first face refuting the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the principle that only one thing can proceed from a simple cause by introducing the idea of conceptual multiplicity.

Core Idea
Conceptual multiplicity does not compromise real simplicity.

Al-Taftazani presents the first of several arguments against the famous kalam principle that from the one only one proceeds. He begins by dismissing a standard logical defense of that principle, then offers a subtle distinction between real and conceptual attributes. This distinction allows a simple cause to be the source of multiple effects without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses technical terms like 'mafhum i'tibari'.
2
Identify the contrapositive argument
Find where al-Taftazani states the logical contrapositive and then dismisses it as 'of little benefit'. Understand why he thinks this approach is harmful.
The contrapositive form: if multiplicity of effect implies multiplicity of agent, then oneness of agent implies oneness of effect.
3
Grasp the core refutation
Al-Taftazani argues that a simple cause can have multiple effects because the causality for each effect is a distinct conceptual meaning. This does not affect real simplicity.
The key is the distinction between real (haqiqi) and conceptual (i'tibari) multiplicity.
4
Examine the supporting argument
The author strengthens his case by noting that even the cause's knowledge of each effect is a distinct concept relative to its essence. This shows conceptual multiplicity is inherent.
If knowledge were real composition, nothing could proceed from a simple cause at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان تكثر المعلول مستلزماً التكثر في الفاعل، كان وحدة الفاعل مستلزمة لوحدة المعلول بحكم عكس النقيض، ولا خفاء في أن هذا كلام قليل الجدوى، بعيد عن أن يجعل من معارك الآراء، وتفسيره على هذا الوجه يهدم أساس المسائل المبنية على أنه لا يصدر من البسيط شيئان. فانه يجوز أن يصدر عنه أشياء، ويكون عليته لكل منها مفهوماً اعتبارياً مغايراً لعليته للآخر ولا يقدح ذلك في وحدته وبساطته الحقيقية، وإلا لما جاز أن يصدر عنه شيء أصلاً لأن علميته لذلك الشيء مفهوم مغاير لذات العلة بحسب التعقل ضرورة كونه نسبة له إلى المعلول.
And if the multiplicity of the effect necessitates multiplicity in the agent, then the oneness of the agent necessitates the oneness of the effect by the rule of contrapositive. And it is not hidden that this statement is of little benefit, far from being made a battlefield of opinions. And its interpretation in this manner destroys the foundation of the questions built upon the principle that nothing more than one thing proceeds from the simple. For it is possible that multiple things proceed from it, and its causality for each of them is a conceptual meaning different from its causality for the other, and that does not detract from its oneness and real simplicity. Otherwise, it would not be possible for anything to proceed from it at all, because its knowledge of that thing is a concept different from the essence of the cause in terms of intellection, by necessity of it being a relation of it to the effect.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is the first face (al-wajh al-awwal) of a counter-argument against the kalam principle 'from the one only one proceeds'. The author begins by stating the logical contrapositive: if multiplicity of effect entails multiplicity of agent, then oneness of agent entails oneness of effect. But he dismisses this reasoning as trivial and harmful to the principle it tries to support. He then argues that it is possible for a simple cause to produce multiple effects because the causality for each effect is a conceptual meaning (mafhum i'tibari) distinct from that for another, without compromising the cause's real simplicity. To strengthen this, he argues that even the cause's knowledge of each effect is a distinct concept relative to the cause's essence. Thus, the principle is not necessary.

صدور
عليت
مفهوم اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conceptual multiplicity implies real composition in the divine essence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that conceptual multiplicity does not entail real multiplicity; distinct conceptual relations do not compromise real simplicity.

Checkpoint

A) It is identical.
B) It is a conceptual difference.
C) It is a real difference.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani argues that a simple cause can produce multiple effects through distinct conceptual relations, without compromising its real oneness.

Al-Taftazani argues that conceptual multiplicity does not harm real simplicity.
