The Third Face: Inferring Agent from Effect
Al-Taftazani examines the third face about inferring agent from effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third face of the argument concerning inference from effect to agent and how Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that it requires the 'from one only one' principle.

Core Idea
Inferring cause from effect does not need 'from one only one' but requires the complete agent and its effect.

In this passage, Al-Taftazani examines a crucial step in the argument for the unity of the agent. He presents an objection that if multiple effects could come from one agent, then we could not infer multiplicity of agents from diversity of effects. Then he offers a sharp reply, clarifying that the inference is actually based on the presence or absence of the effect of a complete agent. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the third face clearly.
2
Identify the objection
The passage presents an objection: if multiple effects could proceed from one agent, then you cannot infer multiplicity of agents from diversity of effects. Find where this objection is stated.
3
Understand the reply
The author replies that the inference is not based on the necessity of multiplicity of effect implying multiplicity of agent, but on the existence of the complete agent and its effect, and of the malzum and its lazim. Note the key terms.
The reply uses the concepts of 'athar' (effect), 'mu'aththir' (agent), 'malzum' (necessary condition), and 'lazim' (necessary concomitant).
4
Analyze the example
The author gives the example of fire heating and water cooling. How does this illustrate the reply? Consider what is observed and what is inferred.
The presence of heating indicates the nature of fire; its absence from water indicates a different nature.
5
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this face defends the inference from effects to the unity of the agent without relying on the controversial principle 'from one only one'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أنه لو جاز صدور الكثير عن الواحد، لما كان تعدد الأثر، واختلافه مستلزماً لتعدد المؤثر واختلافه، فلم يصح الاستدلال منه عليه، لكن مثل هذا الاستدلال مركوز في العقول مشهور بين العقلاء كما إذا وجدوا النار تسخن المجاور، والماء يبرده ، حكموا قطعاً بأن اختلافهما في الحقيقة. ورد بأنا لا نسلم ابتناء ذلك على استلزام تعدد الأثر، تعدد المؤثر، بل على استلزام وجود المؤثر التام، ووجود أثره ، ووجود الملزوم وجود لازمه، فحين لم يجدوا من الماء أثر طبيعة النار ، ولازمها الذي هو سخونة المجاور، حكموا بأن طبيعته غير طبيعة النار .
The third face: that if the proceeding of many from the one were possible, then multiplicity of effect and its difference would not necessitate multiplicity and difference of the agent, so inferring from it (the effect) to it (the agent) would not be valid. But such inference is ingrained in minds and well-known among rational people, as when they find fire heating what is adjacent and water cooling it, they judge decisively that their difference is in reality. And it was replied: We do not concede that this is based on multiplicity of effect necessitating multiplicity of agent, but rather on necessitating the existence of the complete agent and the existence of its effect, and the existence of the necessary concomitant of the existence of the necessary condition (al-malzum). So when they did not find from water the effect of the nature of fire and its concomitant which is the heating of what is adjacent, they judged that its nature is other than the nature of fire.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is the third face (al-wajh al-thalith) of the argument. It challenges the inference from the diversity of effects to the diversity of agents, which is a common rational inference. The objection argues that if multiple effects could proceed from one agent, then we could not use the diversity of effects to infer the diversity of agents. But the author replies that the inference is not based on the necessity of multiplicity of effect implying multiplicity of agent, but rather on the necessity that the existence of a complete agent implies the existence of its effect, and the existence of a necessary condition (malzum) implies the existence of its necessary concomitant (lazim). So when we see fire heating water, we infer the nature of fire from the effect; when we see water cooling, we do not find the effect of fire's nature, so we judge it is different. Thus, the principle of 'from one only one' is not needed for this inference.

Key Terms
أثر
مؤثر
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inference from effect to cause requires the principle that from one only one proceeds.
Correction: The author shows that the inference is based on the existence of the complete agent and its effect, not on that principle.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the inference from effect to agent is based on?
A) The necessity of multiplicity of effect requiring multiplicity of agent.
B) The existence of the complete agent and its effect.
C) The principle that from one only one proceeds.
D) The diversity of effects.

Key Takeaway
The inference from effect to agent does not depend on the principle that from one only one proceeds, but on the presence or absence of the effect of a complete agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world and see contradictions like good and evil, do you ever doubt that there is a single Creator? How does this lesson's logic—that opposite effects require different natures—either strengthen or challenge your belief in tawheed?

The passage says that rational people judge decisively about fire and water based on their effects. What would it take for that conviction to be as concrete as the example of fire and water?
?
You have just learned that the inference from effect to agent is based on the complete agent and its effect, not on the number of effects. Have you ever assumed that because something is complicated, it must have many causes, forgetting that a single complete agent could produce many effects? How can you apply this correction to your understanding of Allah's power and oneness starting today?

Two Refutations of the Corporeality Objection
First objection and response regarding the unity of corporeality and its effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways scholars refuted the objection that corporeality contradicts the principle 'from the one only one proceeds.'

Core Idea
Corporeality is not simple, and its effects are not existential.

This lesson examines a key objection in kalam: if corporeality is a single entity, how can it produce two effects? The response offers two incisive refutations: one denying the unity of corporeality, the other denying that the effects are real. These arguments safeguard the principle of divine unity without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that corporeality (al-jismiyya) is a single principle yet produces two effects: occupying space and receiving accidents. This seems to violate the rule 'from the one only one proceeds.'
Keep the objection in mind as you read the response.
2
First refutation: deny unity
The response first denies that corporeality is a simple unity. It is composed of matter (hayula) and form, which allows it to receive accidents and dimensions. Thus it is not truly one.
This shows corporeality is composite, not simple.
3
Second refutation: deny existentiality
The response denies that the two effects (occupying space and being qualified) are real existential matters. They are merely mental considerations (i'tibariyan) and non-existential ('adamiyan), so they do not require an external cause.
Thus the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' is not violated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بمنع وحدة الجسمية ووجودية الأمرين . رد هذا الوجه بشيئين أحدهما إنا نمنع وحدة الجسمية لأن فيها مادة هي الهيولي وبها تقبل الأعراض وصور الأبعاد . وثانيهما : أنا لا نسلم أن التحيز والاتصاف أو قابليتهما من الأمور الوجودية فتفتقر إلى علة مؤثرة في وجودها، بل الأمران اعتباريان عدميان لا علة لهما خارجاً وإنما عللا ذهناً والكلام في التأثير الخارجي .
It was replied by preventing the unity of corporeality and the existentiality of the two matters. This aspect was replied with two things: one of them is that we deny the unity of corporeality because it contains a matter which is hyle (al-hayula), and by it it receives accidents and forms of dimensions. The second is that we do not concede that occupying space (al-tahayyuz) and being qualified, or their potentiality, are existential matters requiring an effective cause for their existence; rather, the two matters are mental considerations and non-existential, having no cause externally, but are caused mentally, and the discourse concerns external influence.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its response. The objection is based on the principle that 'from the one only one proceeds' (al-wahid la yasdur minhu illa wahid). The objector claims that corporeality (al-jismiyya) is a single thing yet it produces two effects: occupying space (al-tahayyuz) and receiving accidents (qabul al-a'rad). If from the one only one proceeds, then corporeality should only produce one effect. The response (wa bihi) is given with two counter-arguments. First, we deny the unity of corporeality because it is composed of matter (hayula) which receives accidents and dimensions, so it is not truly simple. Second, we do not concede that occupying space and being qualified (or the potentiality for them) are existential matters that need an external cause. Instead, they are merely mental considerations (i'tibariyan) and non-existential ('adamiyan); they have no cause in external reality, only in the mind. The discussion is about external causality (al-ta'thir al-khariji), so these considerations do not refute the principle.

Key Terms
وحدة الجسمية
التحيز
قبول الأعراض
الهيولي
اعتباري
عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection is valid because corporeality appears simple and its effects are real existential attributes requiring a cause.
Correction: The response shows corporeality is composite (contains matter and form) so it is not truly one, and the effects are mere mental considerations, not real external existents.

Checkpoint
What are the two ways the response refutes the objection from corporeality?
A) Denying that corporeality has any effects, and denying the principle of one from one.
B) Denying the unity of corporeality due to its composition, and denying that the effects are existential matters.
C) Affirming that corporeality is simple, and that both effects are existential.
D) Claiming that the effects are actually one, and that corporeality is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
This passage refutes the corporeality objection by denying both the unity of corporeality (due to composition) and the existentiality of its effects (they are mental considerations).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your heart truly believe that everything you perceive as a 'cause'—your efforts, your plans, your intentions—has real existential power? Or can you feel the weight of this lesson's claim that many things are only mental considerations? How would your daily reliance on Allah change if you internalized that occupying space and receiving accidents are not even real properties needing a cause?
?
You may have felt a sense of intellectual pride in understanding this refutation. But ask yourself: Have you ever used such subtle theological distinctions to avoid submitting to the sheer power and freedom of Allah? When you say 'this happened because of that,' do you really mean it, or could you instead see each moment as a direct act of creation? This lesson strips away the assumed reality of secondary causes—are you willing to live with that stripping?
?
The objector assumed corporeality is simple. You might assume your own identity is simple—that you are a unified self. But like corporeality, you are composed of many parts given by Allah. Can you point to one part of yourself that you created? What would change if you saw yourself not as a self-caused agent but as a bundle of contingent accidents sustained by Allah alone?

Second Objection: Essence and Existence Both Caused
Second objection and response regarding essence and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that every caused thing has both essence and existence, and the response that only existence is the caused effect.

Core Idea
Only existence is caused; essence is mentally distinguished.

The second objection challenges the principle 'from the One only one can proceed' by arguing that every effect has both essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud), both of which are caused. This would mean two things proceed, violating the principle. The response concedes multiplicity in external existence but limits the caused to existence or being qualified by it, preserving the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'كل ما يصدر فله ماهية ووجود كلاهما معلول'. Note the claim that both essence and existence are caused.
2
Analyze the response
The response concedes multiplicity externally but says the caused is only existence or the attribute of being qualified by it. Understand this distinction.
The response does not deny multiplicity in the outside world but clarifies what is directly caused.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this response defends the principle that only one proceeds from the One. The caused is one reality (existence), and essence is a mental consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن كل ما يصدر فله ماهية ووجود كلاهما معلول . ورد بعد تسليم تعددهما في الخارج بأن المعلول هو الوجود أو الاتصاف به . الثالث : أن المركز مبدأ محاذياته لنقط المحيط . ورد بأنها اعتبارات . الرابع : أنه لو لم يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد لاتحدت سلسلة الموجودات ولزم في كل شيئين علية أحدهما للآخر، ولو بوسط. ورد بأن وحدة الذات لا تنافي كثرة الاعتبارات، فيجوز أن يصدر عن المعلول الأول الواحد كثرة بحسب ما يعقل له من الوجود والماهية والإمكان . وتَعَقُّلِ مبدئه، وأن يصدر عن الواحد الحق مع معلوله الأول معلول ثان ويتوسطه ثالث ويتوسطهما رابع وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى من المعلولات وحينئذ لا تنحصر السلاسل . أي لا ينحصر جنس سلسلة الموجودات في سلسلة.
The second: that everything that proceeds has an essence and an existence, both caused. It was replied, after conceding their multiplicity externally, that the caused is existence or being qualified by it. The third: that the center is the principle of its coincidences with the points of the circumference. It was replied that they are considerations. The fourth: that if from the one only one proceeds, the chain of existents would be singular, and necessary in every two things is that one is the cause of the other, even if through an intermediary. It was replied that the unity of essence does not contradict the multiplicity of considerations, so it is possible that from the first caused which is one, multiplicity proceeds according to what is conceived for it of existence, essence, and contingency; and the conception of its principle; and that from the one Truth with its first caused there proceeds a second caused, and a third mediates between them, and a fourth mediates between the two, and so on to infinite caused things, and then the chains are not limited. That is, the genus of the chain of existents is not limited to one chain.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents three more objections and their responses. The second objection states that every originated thing has both essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud), both are caused. This would mean the cause produces two things, but the objector wants to show multiplicity. The response concedes the multiplicity in external existence but argues that the caused effect is only existence or the attribute of being qualified, not both as independent realities. The third objection uses the analogy of a center and its radii to the circumference: one center is the principle of multiple lines. The response dismisses these as mental considerations (i'tibarat) without external reality. The fourth objection argues that if only one could proceed from the one, the chain of existents would be linear and every pair would have one causing the other. This would limit the chain. The response explains that the unity of the essence does not preclude multiple aspects (i'tibarat) in the first intellect. Thus the first caused, being one, can be the source of multiplicity based on what is conceived in it: existence, essence, and contingency (imkan), and its principle. Furthermore, from the One Truth (al-wahid al-haqq) together with the first caused, a second caused can proceed, then a third mediates, and so on infinitely, so chains are not limited to one line.

Key Terms
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both essence and existence are real and caused seperately.
Correction: The response clarifies that only existence is directly caused; essence is a mental distinction, not a separate caused reality.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the second objection, what is actually caused?
A) Both essence and existence
B) Existence or being qualified by it
C) Essence only
D) Neither essence nor existence

Key Takeaway
Only existence is directly caused; essence is a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your identity (your 'essence') is fixed and independent, forgetting that your existence itself is an ongoing gift from Allah? How can this lesson change your daily reliance on Him?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you sense a separation between 'who you are' and 'that you exist'? How does recognizing that only your existence is directly from Allah affect your gratitude and humility?
?
If only existence is caused, and essence is mental, what does that say about the permanence of your traits? Are you willing to let go of rigid self-definitions and see yourself as constantly sustained by Allah's act of giving existence?

Third Objection: Center and Its Radii
Third objection and response using geometric center

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a single center produces multiple lines, and the response that these are mental considerations.

Core Idea
Multiple relations from one point are mental, not real.

The third objection uses a geometric example: a single center is the principle of multiple lines to the circumference, seemingly producing multiplicity. The response dismisses this as mere mental considerations (i'tibarat) that have no external existence, thus not violating the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read: 'المركز مبدأ محاذياته لنقط المحيط'. Understand the geometric analogy: one center corresponds to many points.
2
Understand the response
The response says 'they are considerations' (اعتبارات). This means the multiple lines are mental aspects, not real effects.
Considerations exist in the mind, not externally.
3
Connect to the principle
Reflect on why this does not break the rule: mental multiplicity does not contradict real unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن كل ما يصدر فله ماهية ووجود كلاهما معلول . ورد بعد تسليم تعددهما في الخارج بأن المعلول هو الوجود أو الاتصاف به . الثالث : أن المركز مبدأ محاذياته لنقط المحيط . ورد بأنها اعتبارات . الرابع : أنه لو لم يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد لاتحدت سلسلة الموجودات ولزم في كل شيئين علية أحدهما للآخر، ولو بوسط. ورد بأن وحدة الذات لا تنافي كثرة الاعتبارات، فيجوز أن يصدر عن المعلول الأول الواحد كثرة بحسب ما يعقل له من الوجود والماهية والإمكان . وتَعَقُّلِ مبدئه، وأن يصدر عن الواحد الحق مع معلوله الأول معلول ثان ويتوسطه ثالث ويتوسطهما رابع وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى من المعلولات وحينئذ لا تنحصر السلاسل . أي لا ينحصر جنس سلسلة الموجودات في سلسلة.
The second: that everything that proceeds has an essence and an existence, both caused. It was replied, after conceding their multiplicity externally, that the caused is existence or being qualified by it. The third: that the center is the principle of its coincidences with the points of the circumference. It was replied that they are considerations. The fourth: that if from the one only one proceeds, the chain of existents would be singular, and necessary in every two things is that one is the cause of the other, even if through an intermediary. It was replied that the unity of essence does not contradict the multiplicity of considerations, so it is possible that from the first caused which is one, multiplicity proceeds according to what is conceived for it of existence, essence, and contingency; and the conception of its principle; and that from the one Truth with its first caused there proceeds a second caused, and a third mediates between them, and a fourth mediates between the two, and so on to infinite caused things, and then the chains are not limited. That is, the genus of the chain of existents is not limited to one chain.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents three more objections and their responses. The second objection states that every originated thing has both essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud), both are caused. This would mean the cause produces two things, but the objector wants to show multiplicity. The response concedes the multiplicity in external existence but argues that the caused effect is only existence or the attribute of being qualified, not both as independent realities. The third objection uses the analogy of a center and its radii to the circumference: one center is the principle of multiple lines. The response dismisses these as mental considerations (i'tibarat) without external reality. The fourth objection argues that if only one could proceed from the one, the chain of existents would be linear and every pair would have one causing the other. This would limit the chain. The response explains that the unity of the essence does not preclude multiple aspects (i'tibarat) in the first intellect. Thus the first caused, being one, can be the source of multiplicity based on what is conceived in it: existence, essence, and contingency (imkan), and its principle. Furthermore, from the One Truth (al-wahid al-haqq) together with the first caused, a second caused can proceed, then a third mediates, and so on infinitely, so chains are not limited to one line.

Key Terms
مركز
اعتبارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the third objection about the center?
A) By denying that the center is the cause
B) By stating that the lines are mental considerations
C) By claiming the principle does not apply to geometry
D) By saying the center is not one

Key Takeaway
Mental considerations do not constitute real multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat the mental distinctions you draw (e.g., between 'my effort' and 'God's provision') as if they were separate realities, forgetting that all causes are unified in Allah's single will? How can this lesson's concept of iʿtibārāt help you realign your view of cause and effect?
?
If the multiple radii from the center are mere considerations, what does that imply about the multiple 'causes' you see in your life—your successes, failures, trials, and blessings? Are you willing to attribute them all to the one true Cause, without seeing them as independent?
?
When you face a situation that seems to have many contributing factors, do you instinctively multiply agents and lose sight of the one real Agent? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself that apparent multiplicity is often just a mental consideration?

Fourth Objection: Chain of Existents
Fourth objection and response regarding chain of existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the principle leads to a single linear chain of existents, and the response that multiplicity arises from considerations and intermediaries.

Core Idea
Unity of essence allows multiplicity of aspects and infinite intermediaries.

The fourth objection argues that if only one can proceed from the One, then the entire chain of existents would be a single line where every two things are directly or indirectly causally related. The response explains that the unity of the essence does not preclude the first caused from having multiple aspects (existence, essence, contingency) and that further intermediaries can produce infinite chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: 'لو لم يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد لاتحدت سلسلة الموجودات...' Understand the worry that the chain would be singular.
2
Examine the first part of the response
The response says unity of essence does not contradict multiplicity of considerations. The first caused, though one, can give rise to multiplicity based on its conceived aspects: existence, essence, and contingency.
This shows multiplicity can arise from one thing via mental aspects.
3
Examine the second part of the response
The response also allows that from the One Truth together with the first caused, a second caused can proceed, then a third mediates, and so on to infinity. This creates multiple chains.
Thus the chain of existents is not limited to one series.
4
Synthesize
Understand that the principle does not forbid multiple intermediaries or aspects within the first caused. Infinite chains are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second: that everything that proceeds has an essence and an existence, both caused. The third: that the center is the principle of its coincidences with the points of the circumference. It was replied that they are considerations. This would mean the cause produces two things, but the objector wants to show multiplicity. ?

Objection from Divine Attributes and Circularity
Objection about divine attributes and the response of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that divine negations and relations could allow God to be a source of multiplicity is answered by pointing to circularity.

Core Idea
Divine attributes cannot be direct causes of multiplicity because that leads to circular dependence.

In the ongoing discussion about how multiplicity arises from the One, an objector suggests that if mere considerations suffice, then God's own negations and relations could make Him a source of multiplicity without intermediaries. The answer is that these attributes depend on the existence of something else, leading to a vicious circle. This lesson examines that objection and its dismissal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how the objector builds on earlier arguments about mental considerations.
The objector is trying to extend the same logic to God's attributes.
2
Identify the response
Find the answer in the passage. It is very short: 'they depend on the existence of the other, so dependence on them is a circle'. Understand the concept of circularity (dawr).
Circularity is a logical impossibility where A depends on B and B depends on A.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does the objector's proposal fail? Because the negations and relations themselves require something else to exist first, so they cannot be independent causes of multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لو كفى مثل هذه الاعتبارات فللواحد الحق أيضاً كثرة سلوب وإضافات فيصلح مبدأ لكثرة من غير توسط المعلولات . ويجاب بأنها تتوقف على ثبوت الغير فالتوقف عليها دور . أي الشبه المذكورة بوجوه : الأول: أن الجسمية وهي أمر واحد تقتضي أثرين هما التحيز أي الحصول في حيز ما وقبول الأعراض أي الاتصاف بها فإن نوقش في استنادهما إلى مجرد الجسمية، وجعل للحيز والأعراض مدخل في ذلك ينقل الكلام إلى قابلية الجسم للتحيز، وقابليته للاتصاف بالأعراض، فإنهما يستندان إلى الجسمية لا محالة. وإن نوقش في وحدة الجسمية بأن لها وجوداً أو ماهية وإمكاناً وجنساً وفصلاً، وغير ذلك . قلنا : هي بجميع معنى لاستناد الكثير إلى الواحد سوى هذا . ما فيها، ولها شيء واحد يستند إليه كل من الأمرين. ولا : وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن التحيز وقبول الأعراض أو القابلية لهما من الأمور الوجودية التي تقتضى مؤثراً إما تحقيقاً فظاهر وإما لزاماً، فلأن الفلاسفة وإن قالوا بوجود النسب والإضافات لم يعمموا ذلك، بحيث يتناول قابلية التحيز مثلاً . ولو سلم فلا نسلم استناد كل من الأمرين إلى الواحد المحض، بل أحدهما باعتبار الصورة، والأبعاد والآخر باعتبار المادة .
It may be said: if such considerations suffice, then for the one Truth also there is a multiplicity of negations and relations, so He would be a principle for multiplicity without the mediation of caused things. It is answered that they depend on the existence of the other, so dependence on them is a circle. That is, the mentioned doubts are in aspects: the first: that corporeality, which is one thing, necessitates two effects: occupying space, i.e., being in some place, and receiving accidents, i.e., being qualified by them. If one objects to their being based solely on corporeality, and makes space and accidents have an input in that, then the discussion shifts to the body's potentiality for occupying space and its potentiality for being qualified by accidents, and they are based on corporeality inevitably. And if one objects to the unity of corporeality by saying it has existence or essence and contingency and genus and differentia, etc., we say: it is in all its meanings [such that] the attribution of multiplicity to the one is except this. There is in it, and it has one thing to which each of the two matters is based. And it was answered: we do not concede that occupying space and receiving accidents or the potentiality for them are existential matters that require an effective cause, either actually or necessarily, because the philosophers, even if they affirmed the existence of relations and additions, did not generalize that to include potentiality for occupying space, for example. And if conceded, we do not concede that each of the two matters is based on the one pure [cause], but rather one of them is by consideration of form and dimensions, and the other by consideration of matter.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and then restates the objection from corporeality with a more detailed answer. First, an objector says: if mere mental considerations (i'tibarat) suffice to allow multiplicity from the one, then the One Truth (al-wahid al-haqq) also has many negations (sulub) and relations (idafat) that could make Him a source of multiplicity without intermediaries. The response is that those considerations depend on the existence of something else (ghayr), leading to circularity (dawr): the other's existence depends on the considerations, and vice versa, which is impossible. Then the text says 'that is, the mentioned doubts are in aspects' and presents the first aspect again, which is the corporeality objection. The answer here is more elaborate. It first shifts the discussion to potentiality (qabiliyya) if the opponent denies that the effects come from corporeality alone. Then if the opponent questions the unity of corporeality due to its having existence, essence, contingency, genus, differentia, etc., the response says that despite all that, there is a single aspect in corporeality to which both effects are attributed. But the final answer (wa ujiba) is that we do not concede that occupying space and receiving accidents (or the potentiality for them) are existential matters requiring a cause, because philosophers do not generalize relations to include such potentialities. Even if conceded, the two effects do not both originate from the one pure cause; rather, one is from form (sura) and dimensions, and the other from matter (madda). This preserves the principle that from the one only one proceeds in the same respect.

Key Terms
سلوب
إضافات
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine attributes like negations and relations could directly cause multiplicity in creation.
Correction: The response shows that these attributes depend on the existence of others, leading to circularity, so they cannot be independent causes.

Checkpoint
Why does the response reject the objection that divine negations and relations could make God a source of multiplicity without intermediaries?
A) Because God has no negations or relations.
B) Because those negations and relations depend on the existence of something else, causing circularity.
C) Because negations are not real attributes.
D) Because the philosophers deny divine relations.

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine attributes cause multiplicity fails because it involves circular dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector thought divine attributes (negations and relations) could be multiple aspects causing multiplicity. In your own life, do you ever treat your relationship with God (e.g., calling yourself 'servant') as if it gives you independence? How does the idea that such relations depend on something else expose a hidden arrogance? What will you do to replace that arrogance with true dependence on God?
?
The response uses the logical flaw of circularity (dawr). Have you ever held a belief about God that, if examined, required the very thing it was trying to explain? What would it take for you to surrender such circular reasoning to the simple truth of tawhid, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Restated Corporeality Objection and Its Answer
Restated objection about corporeality and its three-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed answer to the objection that corporeality can produce two effects from a single cause.

Core Idea
Corporeality's two effects are not independent existential realities but are attributed to different aspects of form and matter.

The corporeality objection is restated here with a more detailed answer. The objector argues that corporeality, though one, produces two effects: occupying space and receiving accidents. The answer shifts the discussion to potentiality and then denies that these effects are existential matters requiring a cause, or if conceded, they are attributed to different aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its structure
Read the passage. Note that it presents the objection first, then gives three layers of response. The objection: corporeality is one but produces two effects.
The passage mentions 'the first' doubt, so this is a restatement.
2
Understand the shift to potentiality
If the opponent denies that effects stem from corporeality alone, the discussion shifts to the body's potentiality (qabiliyya) for those effects, which still depends on corporeality.
This is a defensive move.
3
Examine the argument on unity
If the opponent questions the unity of corporeality due to its multiple aspects (existence, essence, etc.), the response says there is still one aspect to which both effects are attributed.
4
Analyze the final answer
The final answer (wa ujiba) denies that the effects are existential matters requiring a cause. If conceded, each effect is attributed to different principles: form and matter.
This preserves the rule that from the one only one proceeds in the same respect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single corporeal substance can directly cause two distinct existential effects.
Correction: The answer shows that the effects are not existential or are attributed to different aspects of form and matter.

Checkpoint
According to the final answer, why does occupying space and receiving accidents not require a single effective cause from corporeality alone?
A) Because they are not real effects.
B) Because they are not existential matters requiring a cause, or if they are, they are from different aspects.
C) Because corporeality is not one.
D) Because the philosophers deny potentiality.

Key Takeaway
The corporeality objection is answered by denying the existential nature of the effects or by attributing them to different principles.

The One's Attributes and Circularity
Objection and answer about the One's attributes and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the One's negative attributes and relations cannot serve as an independent source of multiplicity without circularity.

Core Idea
The One's attributes depend on effects, causing circularity if used to produce them.

In this passage, the author presents an objection that the One (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqq) itself has many negative attributes and relations, so why could these not directly produce multiplicity without the need for created effects? The answer exposes a logical flaw: those attributes and relations themselves depend on the existence of the effects, leading to circular reasoning. Let's examine this subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection: the One's own attributes (negative and relative) could suffice for multiplicity without effects. Compare to the earlier case of intelligible considerations.
The objector is drawing an analogy to a previous argument.
2
Identify the answer
The answer shows that these attributes depend on the existence of another (i.e., the effects), so they cannot be the source. This introduces the concept of circularity (dawr).
3
Reflect on the logic
Why is this circular? If the attributes depend on the effects to exist, then using the attributes to explain the effects would mean the effects depend on themselves. Recognize the flaw.
This is a standard refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لو كفى مثل هذه الاعتبارات فللواحد الحق أيضاً كثرة سلوب وإضافات فيصلح مبدأ لكثرة من غير توسط المعلولات. ويجاب بأنها تتوقف على ثبوت الغير فتوقفه عليها دور.
It might be said: If such considerations suffice, then the One True also has many negative attributes (sulūb) and relations (iḍāfāt), so it could serve as a principle of multiplicity without the mediation of effects. The answer is: They depend on the existence of another, so their dependence on it would involve circularity (dawr).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the One (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqq) itself has many negative attributes (like being necessary, eternal, etc.) and relations (like being the cause, being prior, etc.), so why cannot these directly produce multiplicity without needing effects? The answer points out that these negative attributes and relations depend on the existence of something else (i.e., the effects themselves), so if we try to derive multiplicity from them, we get a circular dependence.

Key Terms
الواحد الحق
سلوب
إضافات
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The One's negative attributes and relations are independent sources of multiplicity.
Correction: They depend on the existence of effects, so deriving multiplicity from them leads to circularity.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that the One's own attributes could serve as sources of multiplicity?
A) Those attributes are negative and relative so they cannot produce multiplicity.
B) Those attributes depend on the existence of others, leading to circularity.
C) The One's attributes are identical to His essence.
D) The objector misunderstands the meaning of multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
The One's negative attributes and relations depend on the existence of others, so they cannot independently produce multiplicity without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself relying on attributes or relationships that themselves depend on what you are trying to achieve—like seeking validation from others when your worth already depends on their opinion? Where in your life do you see this circular pattern, and what one step will you take today to break it?
?
The objector thought the One's attributes could directly produce multiplicity, but the answer shows they depend on the effects. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes assume your deeds or worship are self-sufficient, when they actually depend on God's prior grace? How will you acknowledge this dependence in your next prayer?
?
This lesson reveals that even God's negative attributes (like 'necessary') are relative to creation. Have you ever thought of God's attributes as independent realities? How does this change your understanding of God's absolute self-sufficiency? What specific thought or action can you adopt today to align your heart with this truth?

First Objection: A Single Body Producing Two Effects
Objection and two-part answer regarding bodiliness producing two effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from bodiliness challenges the rule 'from the one only one proceeds' and how it is answered by distinguishing real effects from capacities or attributing effects to different aspects.

Core Idea
The body's unity does not refute the rule because its effects arise from different aspects.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the first objection to the principle that from a single thing only one effect can originate. The objector uses the example of a body, which is one yet produces two effects: occupying a place and receiving accidents. The answer involves two key moves. Understanding this objection clarifies the logical foundation of the rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial counter
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the objection that a single body produces two effects, and the first response that shifts the discussion to capacities.
The objector claims bodiliness alone necessitates both effects.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The answer has two distinct parts: first, denying that the effects are real existents requiring a cause; second, even if they are, attributing each to a different aspect of the body.
Look for the transition: 'وأجيب' begins the answer.
3
Understand the first part of the answer
The first part argues that occupying a place and receiving accidents (or the capacity for them) are not among existent things that need an effect. The philosophers do not generalize relations to such capacities.
This attacks the premise that the effects are real.
4
Understand the second part of the answer
The second part concedes for argument and shows that even if real, the two effects are not from a single aspect: one is from form/dimensions, the other from matter.
Thus, the body does not produce two effects from one and the same aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: أن الجسمية وهي أمر واحد تقتضي أثرين هما التحيز أي الحصول في حيز ما وقبول الأعراض أي الاتصاف بها. فإن نوقش في استنادهما إلى مجرد الجسمية، وجعل للحيز والأعراض مدخل في ذلك ينقل الكلام إلى قابلية الجسم للتحيز، وقابليته للاتصاف بالأعراض، فإنهما يستندان إلى الجسمية لا محالة. وإن نوقش في وحدة الجسمية بأن لها وجوداً أو ماهية وإمكاناً وجنساً وفصلاً، وغير ذلك . قلنا : هي بجميع ما فيها، ولها شيء واحد يستند إليه كل من الأمرين. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن التحيز وقبول الأعراض أو القابلية لهما من الأمور الوجودية التي تقتضى مؤثراً إما تحقيقاً فظاهر وإما لزاماً، فلأن الفلاسفة وإن قالوا بوجود النسب والإضافات لم يعمموا ذلك، بحيث يتناول قابلية التحيز مثلاً . ولو سلم فلا نسلم استناد كل من الأمرين إلى الواحد المحض، بل أحدهما باعتبار الصورة، والأبعاد والآخر باعتبار المادة.
The first aspect: Bodiliness (al-jismiyya) — which is a single thing — necessitates two effects: occupying a place (al-taḥayyuz), i.e., being located in some place, and receiving accidents (qubūl al-aʿrāḍ), i.e., being qualified by them. If it is objected that these are not based solely on bodiliness, and that place and accidents have a role, then the discussion shifts to the body's capacity for occupying a place and its capacity for being qualified by accidents, for these two are necessarily based on bodiliness. And if it is objected that bodiliness is not one because it has existence, quiddity, contingency, genus, differentia, etc., we say: It is one in all its aspects (bi-jamīʿ mā fīhā), and it has a single thing to which each of the two is attributed. And it is answered: We do not accept that occupying a place and receiving accidents, or the capacity for them, are among the existent things that require an effect, either actually or necessarily. For the philosophers, although they affirm the existence of relations (nisab) and additions (iḍāfāt), they do not generalize that to include, for example, the capacity for occupying a place. And if that is granted, we do not accept that each of the two is attributed to the pure one; rather, one is in respect of form (ṣūra) and dimensions (abʿād), and the other in respect of matter (mādda).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is the first objection to the rule 'from the one only one proceeds'. It uses the example of a body (jism), which is one yet produces two effects: occupying a place (taḥayyuz) and receiving accidents (qubūl al-aʿrāḍ). The objector argues that this disproves the rule. The answer has two parts: first, it denies that these are real effects requiring a cause, because the capacity for occupying a place is not an existent thing that needs an effect. Second, even if they were, they would not both be attributed to a single aspect of the body; rather, occupying a place is attributed to the body's form and dimensions, while receiving accidents is attributed to its matter. Thus, the body is not a case of one thing producing two effects from one aspect.

Key Terms
الجسمية
التحيز
قبول الأعراض
القابلية
الصورة
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fact that a single body has multiple properties refutes the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'.
Correction: The principle applies only to a single aspect; the body's properties arise from different aspects (form and matter), so the principle is not violated.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, why does the body not produce two effects from one aspect?
A) Because the effects are not real.
B) Because the effects are attributed to different aspects of the body.
C) Because the body is not one.
D) Because the effects are not created by the body.

Key Takeaway
The objection from bodiliness fails because the alleged effects are either not real or proceed from different aspects of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the diverse effects in your own life – your actions, thoughts, and circumstances – do you attribute them to multiple causes, forgetting that they all ultimately proceed from the One through different aspects? How will you remind yourself of this unity today?
?
The answer distinguishes between form and matter. In your worship, are you sometimes preoccupied with the outward form (actions) while neglecting the inward matter (intention)? How can you bring both into alignment to serve the One in a unified way?
?
The philosophers acknowledged relations but not as existents needing a cause. In your own understanding, do you treat the connections between things (e.g., cause-effect in daily life) as independent realities, or do you anchor them in the sole agency of Allah? What would change if you truly saw every relation as contingent on His will?

Second Objection: Quiddity and Existence as Two Effects
Objection and two-part answer about quiddity and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the causal principle (that every cause produces only one effect) and the two-part answer that preserves the rule.

Core Idea
The distinction between quiddity and existence is mental, not external, so only one thing is caused.

The second objection challenges the idea that a cause produces only one effect by pointing out that every effect consists of both quiddity (what it is) and existence (that it is), both of which seem caused. The answer, however, clarifies that quiddity and existence are not two separate things in external reality; their distinction is only in the mind. Even if they were distinct, only existence is directly caused, not the quiddity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the objection claims every effect has quiddity and existence, each caused, so even the First Cause would produce multiplicity.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The answer consists of two distinct points. First, it denies that quiddity and existence are multiple externally. Second, it denies that both are caused.
3
Understand the mental distinction
The first part of the answer relies on the earlier conclusion that existence is added to quiddity only in the mind. This means that in external reality, they are one thing, not two.
This is a key doctrine: the addition of existence to quiddity is mental.
4
Analyze the second part of the answer
Even if they were distinct externally, the answer says only existence (or the qualification of quiddity by existence) is caused by the agent, not the quiddity itself. Thus, only one effect.
5
Reflect on the significance
This objection and answer reinforce the causal principle: a cause produces one effect. The two-step answer shows how the apparent multiplicity is resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن كل ما يصدر عن العلة فله ماهية ووجود ضرورة كونه أمراً موجوداً وكل منهما معلول فيكون الصادر عن كل علة حتى الواحد المشخص المحض متعدداً. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم كون الوجود مع الماهية متعدداً بحسب الخارج لما سبق من أن زيادته على الماهية إنما هو بحسب الذهن فقط. ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أن كلا منهما معلول بل المعلول هو الوجود، أو اتصاف الماهية به لأن هذا هو الحاصل من الفاعل.
The second aspect: Every effect emanating from a cause has quiddity (māhiyya) and existence (wujūd), necessarily because it is an existent thing, and each of them is caused, so what emanates from every cause, even the pure individual one (al-wāḥid al-mushakhkhaṣ al-maḥḍ), is multiple. The answer is: We do not accept that existence together with quiddity is multiple externally, because it has already been established that its addition (ziyāda) to quiddity is only in the mind. And if that is granted, we do not accept that each of them is caused; rather, the caused is existence, or the qualification of quiddity by it, because that is what is actualized by the agent.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the second objection. It argues that every effect consists of both quiddity (whatness) and existence (thatness), both of which are caused by the agent, so even the First Cause produces two things. The answer has two steps: First, it denies that quiddity and existence are two distinct things externally; rather, the existence added to quiddity is only a mental distinction. Second, even if they were distinct, only existence (or the qualification) is directly caused by the agent, not the quiddity itself. Thus, only one thing is caused, preserving the rule.

Key Terms
الماهية
الوجود
زيادة الوجود على الماهية
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quiddity and existence are separate external realities each caused by the agent.
Correction: Their distinction is only mental; externally they are one, and only existence (or the qualification) is directly caused.

Checkpoint
The answer to the second objection denies that quiddity and existence are multiple externally. On what basis?
A) Because quiddity is the same as existence.
B) Because the addition of existence to quiddity is only mental.
C) Because existence is not caused.
D) Because quiddity is not an effect.

Key Takeaway
The second objection is refuted by showing that quiddity and existence are not two separate caused things externally, and even if they were, only existence is directly caused, preserving the rule of one effect per cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you tend to separate 'who you are' from 'that you are'? How does this lesson's teaching that the distinction is only mental challenge you to see yourself as a single, indivisible act of divine creation?
?
The objection tried to see multiplicity in God's creation, but the answer restores unity. In which areas of your life do you perceive God's actions as divided or conflicting? How can the simple causal unity taught here reshape your trust in His decree?
?
If your existence is directly caused by God while your quiddity is not a separate cause, what does that say about your dependence on Him for every moment of your being? How should this affect your daily awareness of His presence?

The Center Point Objection and Its Answer
Objection using geometric point and three-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from the geometric center point is refuted through the concepts of mental consideration, relation, and difference in aspect.

Core Idea
Correspondences are mental relations, not real effects.

This lesson tackles a clever objection that uses geometry to challenge the principle that one cause produces one effect. The objection points to a single point at the center of a circle that has multiple correspondences to points on the circumference. The answer, however, shows that such correspondences are not real external features but mental considerations, relations, or aspects that differ. By following the three steps of the answer, you will see how classical logic dissolves the apparent problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection claims the center point is the origin (mabda') of its correspondences to many points on the circumference.
This seems to suggest one thing produces many effects.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The answer begins by stating that correspondence (muḥādhāt) is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) with no external reality. Ask: why does this refute the objection?
If it's not real, it doesn't need a real cause.
3
Examine the second part of the answer
Even if correspondence were real, it is a relation (iḍāfa) subsisting in the two points, not an effect produced by the point as an agent. Identify what the relation depends on.
A relation is between things, not a separate effect.
4
Analyze the third part of the answer
Even if it were an effect, the aspect (ḥaythiyya) of the point's relation to different points differs, so there is no single effect from a single aspect. Understand how this preserves the principle of one cause producing one effect.
The aspects are multiple, so the effects can be multiple.
5
Summarize the logic
Write a short summary of how the three steps progressively refute the objection. Check that you can explain why the objection fails without appealing to theology.
This is a purely rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن النقطة التي هي مركز الدائرة مبدأ محاذياته للنقط المفروضة على المحيط . وأجيب بأن المحاذاة أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، فلا يكون معلولاً لشيء ولو سلم فمحاذاة النقطتين إضافة قائمة بهما، أو بكل منهما إضافة قائمة بها، فلا يكون فاعلاً للمحاذيات على ما هو المتنازع . ولو سلم فاختلاف الحيثية ظاهر لا مدفع له .
The third aspect: The point that is the center of the circle is the principle of its correspondences to the assumed points on the circumference. The answer is: Correspondence (muḥādhāt) is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) with no external reality, so it is not an effect of anything. Even if it is granted, the correspondence of two points is a relation (iḍāfa) subsisting in them, or in each of them a relation subsisting in it, so it is not an agent (fāʿil) for the correspondences as is disputed. Even if that is granted, the difference in aspect (ikhtilāf al-ḥaythiyya) is manifest and cannot be rejected.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the third objection. It uses a geometric example: a single central point has multiple correspondences to many points on the circumference, suggesting one thing produces many relations. The answer has three steps: First, correspondence is a mental consideration, not a real thing in the external world, so it does not need a real cause. Second, even if it were real, the correspondence between two points is a relation that subsists in them, not something produced by the point as an agent. Third, even if it were produced, the aspects (ḥaythiyyāt) of the point's relation to different points differ, so there is no single effect from a single aspect.

Key Terms
النقطة
المحاذاة
أمر اعتباري
الإضافة
الفاعل
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The center point's multiple correspondences are real effects, so one cause can produce many effects.
Correction: Correspondences are mental considerations or relations, not real effects; even if they were, the aspects differ, preserving one cause per aspect.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute the objection from the center point?
A) By denying that the point is one.
B) By showing that correspondence is a mental consideration.
C) By arguing that the point does not exist.
D) By saying the point produces the circumference directly.

Key Takeaway
The geometric objection fails because correspondence is not a real external effect; it is either a mental consideration, a relation, or differs in aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken a mental concept—like a label or association you've created—for objective reality? How does recognizing that correspondence is an amr iʿtibārī challenge the way you think about your own assumptions? What will you do this week to identify one such assumption and examine it critically?
?
When you see connections and relationships in the world around you, do you instinctively treat them as real, independent things? What would change in your perspective if you remembered they are merely relations (iḍāfa) subsisting in what relates? Is there a specific relationship in your life you have been objectifying that you now need to approach differently?
?
Consider an area of your life where you feel pulled in different directions by a single responsibility or role (e.g., parent, spouse, worker). Are these really multiple effects from one cause, or are they one reality viewed under different aspects (ḥaythiyyāt)? How might this reframe your struggle and lead you to seek God's help in managing the diverse aspects of your life?

The Chain of Single Causality and Its Refutation
Objection that rule forces a single chain, refuted by multiplicity of aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the principle 'from one only one' leads to a single chain of causes is false, and how the multiplicity of aspects in the first effect allows for multiple effects.

Core Idea
The first effect's internal multiplicity breaks the single chain.

This lesson presents the fourth objection to the kalam principle that from the One only one proceeds. The objection argues that this leads to a single linear chain of causality, contradicting observed reality. The answer, though partially preserved, points to the multiplicity of aspects in the first effect as the key to generating many effects. We will examine both sides.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that if only one effect can proceed from a single cause, then the first effect can only produce one effect, leading to a chain. This is claimed to be manifestly false.
This is a hypothetical deduction based on the principle.
2
Examine the answer
The answer denies the inevitable chain by pointing out that the first effect, though one in essence, has multiple aspects (jihāt) and considerations (iʿtibārāt), so it can cause many effects.
The answer is cut off but the core idea is clear.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
This shows that the principle does not necessitate monolinear causality; the universe can have multiple causal lines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أنه لو لم يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد، لما صدر عن المعلول الأول إلا واحد هو الثاني، وعنه واحد هو الثالث وهلم جراً فتكون الموجودات سلسلة واحدة، ويلزم في كل موجودين فرضاً أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر، والآخر معلولاً له بوسط أو بغير وسط، وهذا ظاهر البطلان . وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يلزم لو لم يكن في المعلول الأول مع وحدته بالذات كثرة بحسب الجهات والاعتبارات ولو لم يصدر عن الواجب مع المعلول الأول أو يتوسطه شيء آخر وهكذا إلى ما لا يحصى بيانه على ما ...
The fourth aspect: If from the one only one proceeds, then from the first effect only one proceeds, which is the second, and from it only one is the third, and so forth, so existents would be a single chain, and for any two existents it would be necessary that one is the cause of the other and the other is its effect, either directly or indirectly. This is manifestly false. The answer is: This would follow only if in the first effect (al-maʿlūl al-awwal) there was no multiplicity in terms of aspects (jihāt) and considerations (iʿtibārāt) despite its essential unity, and if nothing else proceeded from the Necessary (al-wājib) along with the first effect or through its mediation, and so on ad infinitum... its explanation is as follows...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is the fourth objection. It states that if only one effect proceeds from the One, then the entire universe would be a single linear chain of cause and effect, which is clearly false because we see many things not in a single chain. The answer (which is cut off) says that this consequence would only follow if the first effect had no internal multiplicity of aspects. In reality, the first effect (e.g., the first intellect) has multiple aspects (form, matter, possibilities), so it can produce multiple effects. Additionally, other things may proceed directly from God without mediation. The full answer would elaborate on this.

Key Terms
المعلول الأول
السلسلة
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد
الجهات والاعتبارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle that from the One only one proceeds forces the entire universe to be a single linear chain of cause and effect.
Correction: The first effect has internal multiplicity of aspects, allowing it to produce many effects, so the chain is not forced.

Checkpoint
What condition does the answer give under which the chain of single causality would not follow?
A) If the first effect had only one aspect.
B) If the first effect had internal multiplicity of aspects.
C) If God directly created everything.
D) If the chain was infinite.

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of aspects in the first effect prevents the universe from being a single chain of causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to see events as part of a rigid chain of causes, forgetting that God's creative power involves multiple aspects (jihāt) that can produce many outcomes from a single source? How can you train your mind to recognize the richness of divine causation beyond linear narratives?
?
This lesson shows that even the first created being has internal multiplicity. Consider your own sense of self: are you a simple, single cause of your actions, or do you recognize a complex interplay of intentions, motives, and circumstances (aspects) that shape your choices? How does this shift your accountability before God?
?
The objection assumed a single chain, but the answer opened up a world of multiplicity. When you face difficulties or successes, do you attribute them to a single cause (e.g., your effort) or do you see the myriad divine considerations (iʿtibārāt) at play? Pause and name one situation where you overlooked multiple aspects—what might you have missed?

The Second Objection: Essence and Existence as Multiple Effects
Sanusi presents the second objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against 'from one only one proceeds' and the Ash'ari answer that existence is only mentally distinct from essence.

Core Idea
Existence is not an external addition; only the mind separates it from essence.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the second objection to the principle that from a single cause only one effect proceeds. The objector claims that every effect has both essence and existence, which would make the effect multiple. Sanusi's answer hinges on a key Ash'ari doctrine: existence is not a real, external property added to essence, but merely a mental distinction. This preserves the unity of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that each effect consists of essence and existence, both caused, so from one cause multiple things proceed.
Notice the assumption that existence is a distinct entity externally.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
Sanusi rejects that existence is multiple externally; it is only mentally additional to essence.
3
Consider the conditional answer
Even if existence were external, only one of the two is really caused, not both.
This is a secondary defense.
4
Reflect on the defense
See how the answer preserves the principle that from one cause only one effect proceeds.
The key is the mental distinction of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن كل ما يصدر عن العلة فله ماهية ووجود ضرورة كونه أمرا موجودا وكل منهما معلول فيكون الصادر عن كل علة حتى الواحد المشخص المحض متعددا . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم كون الوجود مع الماهية متعددا بحسب الخارج لما سبق من أن زيادته على الماهية إنما هو بحسب الذهن فقط ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أن كلا منهما معلول بل المعلول هو الوجود، أو اتصاف الماهية به لأن هذا هو الحاصل من الفاعل .
The second aspect: That everything that issues from a cause has an essence and an existence by necessity of being an existing thing, and each of them is caused; thus what issues from every cause, even the single, individuated, pure one, is multiple. And it was answered: We do not grant that existence is multiple with essence in respect of the external, due to what preceded: that its addition to essence is only in respect of the mind. And if it were granted, we do not grant that each of them is caused; rather the caused is either existence or the attribute of essence with it, because this is what is obtained from the agent.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. The objector argues that every effect consists of both essence and existence, so from a single cause, multiple things (essence and existence) must proceed. Sanusi answers that existence is not a distinct external addition to essence; it is only mentally distinct. So from the cause, only one thing (either existence or the attribute of essence) proceeds. This preserves the principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is an external attribute added to essence.
Correction: In Ash'ari thought, existence is only a mental distinction, not a real additional attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, why does existence not make the effect multiple?
A) Existence is separate from essence externally.
B) Existence is identical to essence in all respects.
C) Existence is only mentally additional to essence, not externally.
D) The cause only produces existence, not essence.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because existence is not an external addition to essence, so the effect remains one.

How does that affect your view of your own accountability? Are you living as if your actions are separate from your existence, or do you see each action as an expression of your contingent being before Allah?
?
The objection claims multiplicity from a single cause. In your life, do you sometimes act as if the various blessings come from different sources, forgetting that they all come from Allah alone? How can you train your heart to see the unity behind every provision?

The Third Objection: The Center Point's Alignments
Sanusi presents the third objection from the circle's center point and answers it in three levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that a single cause (the center point) could produce multiple effects (alignments) by distinguishing between mental relations and real causal effects.

Core Idea
Relations are mental entities, not real effects.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection from the analogy of a circle's center. The objector claims that a single point has many alignments, suggesting multiple effects from one cause. Sanusi's response clarifies the nature of relations and respects, preserving the principle that a single cause cannot directly produce multiple distinct effects in the external world.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the initial answer
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the first part of the passage. Note that the objection claims the center point is a 'source of alignments'.
The term 'muḥādhāt' (alignment) is the key contested relation.
2
Identify the three layers of the answer
Sanusi gives three successive answers, each introduced by 'And it was answered' or 'And if it were granted'. Trace how each answer builds on the previous one.
The structure is: (1) alignment is i'tibārī, (2) even if real, it's an attribute of both points, (3) even then, different respects explain multiplicity.
3
Understand the key technical terms
Look up the terms i'tibārī, iḍāfa, and ḥaythiyya. Reflect on how each term helps Sanusi's argument.
These terms are central to kalam discussions on causality and relations.
4
Reflect on the overall defense
Consider how this objection and answer fit into the larger debate on causality. Why is it important to show that mental relations are not real effects?
This protects the principle that one cause cannot directly produce multiple distinct external effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن النقطة التي هي مركز الدائرة مبدأ محاذياته للنقط المفروضة على المحيط . وأجيب بأن المحاذاة أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، فلا يكون معلولا لشيء ولو سلم فمحاذاة النقطتين إضافة قائمة بهما، أو بكل منهما إضافة قائمة بها، فلا يكون فاعلا للمحاذيات على ما هو المتنازع . ولو سلم فاختلاف الحيثية ظاهر لا مدفع له .
The third aspect: That the point which is the center of a circle is a source of its alignments with the points assumed on the circumference. And it was answered: The alignment is a relational matter (amr i'tibārī) having no realization in the external, so it is not caused by anything. And if it were granted, the alignment of two points is an attribute (iḍāfa) subsisting in them, or in each of them an attribute subsisting in it, so there is no agent for the alignments in the sense disputed. And if it were granted, the difference of respects (ikhtilāf al-ḥaythiyya) is apparent and cannot be repelled.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection using the analogy of a circle's center point: the center point is said to have multiple alignments with all points on the circumference, thus it seems to produce multiple effects. Sanusi answers that alignment is a mental consideration, not a real external relation. Even if it were real, it would be an attribute subsisting in the two points, not a separate effect caused by the center. Finally, even if that were granted, the difference in respects (the center's relation to each point) explains multiplicity without violating the principle.

Key Terms
نقطة مركز
محاذاة
اعتبار
إضافة
حيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alignments of the center point with circumference points are real external effects caused by the center.
Correction: Sanusi shows that alignments are merely mental relations (i'tibārī), and even if considered real, they are attributes of the two related points, not separate effects produced by one cause alone.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the third objection, what is the status of the alignment (muḥādhāt) of the center point?
A) It is a real effect caused by the center point.
B) It is a relational matter with no external realization.
C) It is a substance that subsists independently.
D) It is a necessary attribute of the center point.

Key Takeaway
The center point example shows that relational multiplicity does not imply causal multiplicity because relations are mental constructs, not real effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship in your life where you feel you are the 'center' causing multiple reactions in others. Are you confusing mental expectations (what you think you cause) with real effects? How can you distinguish between your own mental constructs and what actually emanates from you?
?
Think of a time you attributed a conflict to a single cause (e.g., a person's action). Did you assume that one cause produced a uniform effect? How does the lesson's third answer (difference of respects) push you to see multiple respects in a single action?
?
When you pray for many things, do you expect that your single act of prayer must produce separate effects? How does the distinction between mental consideration and real effect free you from demanding that God respond in a way that matches your mental categories?
