The Six Considerations and the Multiplicity of Chains
Sanusi presents the fourth objection and its answer using the six considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth objection against the principle 'from one only one proceeds' is refuted by appealing to the six considerations of the first effect.

Core Idea
The first effect has multiple aspects, allowing it to produce many chains.

The fourth objection argues that if only one proceeds from the one, existence would be a single linear chain, which is absurd. Sanusi's answer introduces a key nuance: the first effect, though one in essence, has multiple 'considerations' (i'tibārāt). This allows it to be the source of multiple causal chains. Let's unpack this crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Understand the objection: if only one proceeds from the one, all existents form a single chain, which is false.
Notice how the objection is presented as a conditional argument.
2
Identify the answer's structure
The answer begins by stating what would be necessary for the objection to hold. Look for the conditional 'إنما يلزم لو لم يكن...' and see how it introduces the six considerations.
The answer does not reject the principle but rather explains why it does not lead to a single chain.
3
List the six considerations
Find the six things that are conceivable for the first effect: existence, identity, contingency, necessity by another, conception of its essence, and conception of its principle. Memorize them.
These six are the pivot on which the refutation turns.
4
See how multiplicity arises
The passage explains that from each consideration, a different effect can proceed, leading to multiple chains. Also, chains can branch through intermediaries.
This shows that the first effect is not a simple point but a source of diverse products.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: the principle 'from one only one proceeds' is preserved, but the unity of the first effect is consistent with it being the source of a diverse world.
This is a subtle point in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أنه لو لم يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد، لما صدر عن المعلول الأول إلا واحد هو الثاني، وعنه واحد هو الثالث وهلم جرا فتكون الموجودات سلسلة واحدة، ويلزم في كل موجودين فرضا أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر، والآخر معلولا له بوسط أو بغير وسط، وهذا ظاهر البطلان . وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يلزم لو لم يكن في المعلول الأول مع وحدته بالذات كثرة بحسب الجهات والاعتبارات ولو لم يصدر عن الواجب مع المعلول الأول أو يتوسطه شيء آخر وهكذا إلى ما لا يحصى بيانه على ما ذكروه : أنه إذا صدر عن المبدأ الأول الذي ليس فيه تكثر جهات واعتبارات شتى كان ذلك الشيء واحدا بالحقيقة والذات، لكن يعقل له بحسب الاعتبارات المختلفة أمور ستة هي الوجود والهوية والإمكان والوجوب بالغير وتعقل ذاته وتعقل مبدئه. فيجوز أن يصدر عنه بحسب تلك الاعتبارات أمور متكثرة. ويظهر ابتداء سلاسل متعددة. وكذا يجوز أن يصدر عن ذلك الشيء الواحد الذي هو المعلول الأول معلول ثان وعن المبدأ الأول يتوسطه معلول ثالث ويتوسط المعلول الأول والثاني والثالث معلول رابع وهكذا عن كل معلول يتوسط ما فوقه أو ما تحته. وعن الواجب يتوسط ما تحته جملة أو فرادى فيكون هناك سلاسل غير محصورة ولبعض المحققين رسالة في تفصيل ذلك، وأورد نبذا منه في شرحه للإشارات
The fourth aspect: That if only one proceeds from the one, then from the first effect only one would proceed, which is the second, and from it one which is the third, and so on (wa halumma jarran), so existents would be a single chain, and it would follow that for any two supposed existents, one of them is a cause of the other and the other is an effect of it with or without an intermediary, and this is clearly false. And it was answered: That would only follow if the first effect, despite its unity in essence, did not have multiplicity in respects (jihat) and considerations (i'tibārāt), and if nothing else proceeded from the Necessary along with the first effect or through it as intermediary, and so on ad infinitum. Its explanation is as they mentioned: That when something proceeds from the First Principle which has no multiplicity of respects and various considerations, that thing is one in reality and essence, but in accordance with different considerations, six things are conceivable for it: existence (al-wujūd), identity (al-huwiyya), contingency (al-imkān), necessity by another (al-wujūb bi al-ghayr), conception of its essence (ta'aqqul dhātih), and conception of its principle (ta'aqqul mabda'ih). So it is possible that multiple things proceed from it according to those considerations, and multiple chains begin to appear. Similarly, it is possible that from that single thing which is the first effect, a second effect proceeds, and from the First Principle through its intermediation a third effect proceeds, and through the intermediation of the first, second, and third a fourth effect proceeds, and so on from each effect through what is above it or below it. And from the Necessary through what is below it, collectively or individually, so there are unlimited chains. And one of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqīn) has a treatise detailing that, and he included a summary of it in his commentary on al-Ishārāt.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: if only one proceeds from the one, then all existents would be a single linear chain where each is caused by the one before it, which is absurd (e.g., two unrelated things would have to be causally linked). Sanusi answers by introducing the concept of the first effect having multiple 'aspects' or 'considerations' (i'tibārāt) while remaining one in essence. He lists six such considerations: existence, identity, contingency, necessity by another, conception of its essence, and conception of its principle. Because of these multiple aspects, the first effect can be the source of multiple distinct chains of causes and effects. The answer also explains that from the first effect many effects can proceed, not just one, and from the Necessary Being through various intermediaries many chains can arise. Thus the fourth objection is refuted.

الوجوب بالغير
تعقل الذات
تعقل المبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first effect is simple in every respect, so it can only produce one effect.
Correction: The first effect has multiple considerations (existence, identity, etc.) that allow it to be the source of multiple chains, preserving the principle 'from one only one' while still enabling diversity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fourth objection, how many considerations (i'tibārāt) are conceivable for the first effect?
A) Three: existence, essence, and contingency.
B) Six: existence, identity, contingency, necessity by another, conception of its essence, conception of its principle.
C) Five: essence, existence, contingency, necessity, and possibility.
D) Infinite, since each relation is a distinct consideration.

Key Takeaway
The six considerations of the first effect answer the objection of a single chain by showing multiplicity within unity.

?

Imam's Objection: Mental Considerations as Causes
Imam al-Razi objects and author answers with condition/respect distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the six considerations against the objection that they are merely mental concepts and not real causes.

Core Idea
Six considerations are not independent causes but conditions that differentiate the one cause's effects.

The objection that the six considerations are merely mental concepts threatens to undermine the entire framework of causal explanation in kalam. The author's response shows that they do not need to be independent real causes; they serve as conditions and aspects that allow a single existing cause to produce multiple effects. In this lesson, we examine the objection and its concise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the brevity: it contains both an objection and an answer in one sentence.
The passage is a single sentence but contains two distinct parts: the objection and the response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the six considerations (existence, necessity, contingency) are mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) and cannot be real causes for external entities. Understand why this objection is serious: if they are merely mental, they cannot have causal power in the external world.
Imam al-Rāzī is often associated with this kind of critical objection in kalām.
3
Understand the answer's key distinction
The author concedes they are not independent causes (ghayr mustaqilla) but reclassifies them as conditions (shurūṭ) and respects (ḥaythiyyāt) that differentiate the states of the existing cause. This preserves their explanatory role without requiring them to be independent agents.
This distinction is crucial: a condition does not cause but enables or specifies the causal act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض الإمام بأن الوجود والوجوب والإمكان اعتبارات عقلية، لا تصلح علة للأعيان الخارجية، ولما كان ظاهراً أنها ليست عللا مستقلة بل شروطا وحيثيات تختلف بها أحوال العلة الموجودة .
And the objection of the Imām (al-Rāzī) that existence, necessity, and contingency are mental considerations, not suitable to be causes for external entities. And since it was apparent that they are not independent causes but conditions and respects by which the states of the existing cause differ.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Imam al-Razi (or perhaps another Imam) against the previous answer. The objection argues that the six considerations (existence, necessity, contingency, etc.) are merely mental concepts and cannot serve as real causes for external things. The author's response is implied in the phrase 'since it was apparent that they are not independent causes but conditions and respects by which the states of the existing cause differ.' In other words, the author concedes they are not independent agents but nonetheless they are relevant as conditions and aspects that differentiate the causal power of the existing cause. Thus the objection is answered by clarifying that the multiplicity of considerations does not require them to be real causes; they are merely relational aspects that allow the one cause to produce multiple effects.

Key Terms

اعتبارات عقلية
علة
شرط
حيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the six considerations are proposed as real causes that directly produce effects in the external world.
Correction: The answer clarifies they are only conditions and respects that differentiate the states of the existing cause, not independent agents.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Imām's objection that the six considerations are mental?
A) He rejects the objection entirely, insisting they are real external causes.
B) He agrees they are mental but argues they are conditions and aspects of the existing cause.
C) He argues that mental considerations can directly cause external things.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant because the six considerations are not needed.

Key Takeaway
The six considerations are not independent causes but conditions and respects that differentiate the states of the existing cause.

Objection: Negations and Relations as Causes for Multiplicity
Objection and answer regarding negations and relations as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negations and relations cannot serve as direct causes for multiplicity from the One Necessary Being without leading to circularity.

Core Idea
Negations and relations presuppose the existence of another, so they cannot cause that other.

After establishing that multiplicity in effects requires intermediaries, an objection arises: why not simply attribute multiplicity to God's many negations and relations? This lesson examines that objection and the decisive answer that reveals a logical circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the objection: if mere multiplicity of aspects allows a single entity to produce many effects, then God's own negations and relations could directly produce all possible things without intermediaries.
Notice the objection challenges the need for intermediaries.
2
Identify the answer's key point
The answer states that negations (sulūb) and relations (iḍāfāt) are only conceivable after the existence of another (al-ghayr). That means they depend on that other's existence.
This dependency is crucial for the refutation.
3
Understand the circularity (dawr)
If negations and relations depend on another yet are supposed to cause that same other, that would be a circular reasoning (dawr), which is invalid. Therefore, they cannot be direct causes.
This shows that God's negations and relations do not suffice to avoid intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترض : بأنه لو كفى مثل هذه الكثرة في أن يكون الواحد مصدرا للمعلولات الكثيرة. فذات الواجب تعالى تصلح أن تجعل مبدأ للممكنات باعتبار ماله من كثرة السلوب والإضافات من غير أن يجعل بعض معلولاته واسطة في ذلك . ويحكم بأن الصادر الأول عنه ليس إلا واحدا . وأجيب بأن السلوب والإضافات لا تعقل إلا بعد ثبوت الغير، فلو كان لها دخل في ثبوت الغير لكان دورا .
It was objected: That if such multiplicity sufficed for the one to be a source for many effects, then the essence of the Necessary, exalted is He, would be suitable to be made a principle for possible things by virtue of His having many negations (sulūb) and relations (iḍāfāt) without making any of His effects an intermediary in that, yet it is judged that the first proceeding from Him is only one. And it was answered: The negations and relations are not conceivable except after the establishment of the other (ghayr), so if they had a role in establishing the other, it would be a circle (dawr).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This final objection argues that if mere multiplicity of aspects allows a single entity to produce many effects, then God Himself, who has numerous negative attributes and relations, could directly produce all possible things without needing intermediaries—yet it is agreed that only one thing proceeds from Him first. The answer is that negations (sulūb) and relations (iḍāfāt) are only conceivable after the existence of something else (al-ghayr). Therefore, if these negations and relations were to play a role in bringing that other into existence, it would be circular (dawr). Thus God's negations and relations cannot serve as the basis for multiplicity without intermediaries.

Key Terms
سلوب
إضافات
دور
الواجب تعالى
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's many negations and relations could directly cause multiplicity without intermediaries.
Correction: Negations and relations presuppose the existence of another, so they cannot cause that other without circularity.

Checkpoint
Why can't God's negations and relations directly produce multiple effects without intermediaries?
A) Because negations and relations are non-existent and cannot cause anything.
B) Because negations and relations are only conceivable after the existence of another, causing circularity.
C) Because God's essence is too simple to have relations.
D) Because negations and relations are mental, not real.

Key Takeaway
Negations and relations cannot be direct causes of multiplicity because they presuppose the existence of another, leading to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever argued for something that actually depends on the very thing you are trying to prove? How can you ensure your understanding of God does not involve such circular reasoning?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you find yourself subtly relying on created things to define them? What would it take to purify your conception of God from such dependencies?
?
This lesson shows that negations and relations cannot be causes because they presuppose the other. Is there any area of your faith where you unknowingly treat God's attributes as if they were independent causes? How can you humble yourself and accept the limitations of human reasoning?

Can One Be Both Agent and Recipient? The Philosophers' Claim and Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the philosophers' claim that one cannot be both agent and recipient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that a single entity cannot be both agent and recipient, their arguments, and how Al-Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing aspects.

Core Idea
One thing can be both agent and recipient under different aspects.

This passage from Al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a key philosophical objection to divine attributes: if God is absolutely one, how can He be both agent (creating) and recipient (possessing attributes)? The philosophers argue this is impossible. Al-Taftazani presents their case and then dismantles it with a simple yet profound distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Begin by reading the passage where Al-Taftazani states the philosophers' position: 'the one is not both recipient and agent.' Notice the two arguments presented.
2
Understand the two arguments
Identify the two arguments: (1) agency and receptivity are two distinct effects and cannot come from one cause; (2) agency is by necessity, receptivity by contingency, and these are incompatible.
3
Analyze the response
Read Al-Taftazani's response: he concedes the difference in relations but argues that no impossibility arises when considering different aspects (bi-jihatayn).
He also mentions the general vs. particular contingency distinction.
4
Note the example and first proof
Consider the fire example: fire produces heat by its form and receives heat by its matter—one thing acting differently under different aspects. Then note the philosophers' first proof: reception and action are two effects, so they cannot issue from one.
This proof is the same as the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً عند الفلاسفة (قال : المبحث الرابع (۱): زعمت الفلاسفة أن الواحد لا يكون قابلا(۲) وفاعلاً لأنهما أثران (۳) وقد مر ، ولأن نسبة الفاعل بالوجوب (٤) والقابل بالإمكان (ه) . والجواب بعد تسليم كونها بالإمكان الخاص المنافي للوجوب أو بالإمكان العام الذي يمكن أن يتحقق بدون الوجوب أنه لا امتناع في الوجوب، واللاوجوب بجهتين بحيثية (٦) . من حيث هو واحد ، لا يكون قابلا للشيء ، وفاعلا له، وبنوا على ذلك امتناع اتصاف الواجب بصفات حقيقية ، واحترز بقيد حيثية الوحدة عن مثل النار تفعل الحرارة بصورتها وتقبلها (۷) بمادتها وتمسكوا في ذلك بوجهين : الأول : أن القبول والفعل أثران فلا يصدران عن واحد لما مر .
The fourth inquiry: The one is not both recipient and agent according to the philosophers. (He said: The fourth inquiry: The philosophers claimed that the one is not a recipient and an agent because they are two effects (3) and it has passed, and because the relation of the agent is by necessity (4) and the recipient by contingency (5). The answer, after conceding that it is by particular contingency contrary to necessity or by general contingency which can be realized without necessity, is that there is no impossibility in necessity and non-necessity by two aspects under a condition (6). Insofar as it is one, it is not a recipient of a thing and an agent of it. And they built upon that the impossibility of the Necessary Being being qualified with real attributes. And they restricted by the condition of the aspect of unity against things like fire: it produces heat by its form and receives it by its matter. And they held to that by two proofs: The first: that reception and action are two effects, so they cannot issue from one, as has passed.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the fourth inquiry, which addresses the philosophers' claim that one entity cannot simultaneously be both a recipient (qābil) and an agent (fāʿil). The author presents their two arguments: (1) agency and receptivity are two distinct effects, and one cause cannot produce two distinct effects; (2) the relation of the agent to its act is by necessity, while the relation of the recipient is by contingency, and these are incompatible. The author responds by accepting the premise that the relations differ but denying the impossibility: it is possible for a single thing to be a giver and receiver under different aspects (bi-jihatayn). The philosophers further argue based on this that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) cannot have real attributes, because if He were both agent and recipient of attributes, that would violate unity. However, the author notes that they restrict this impossibility to the aspect of unity, not absolutely, as illustrated by the example of fire which produces heat by its form and receives it by its matter. The philosophers then give two formal proofs; the first is restated: reception and action are two effects, so they cannot issue from one cause.

Key Terms
قابل (recipient)
فاعل (agent)
أثران (two effects)
وجوب (necessity)
إمكان (contingency)
حيثية (aspect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is one, He cannot have multiple attributes because that would make Him both agent and recipient simultaneously.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that unity does not preclude having different aspects; a single entity can be agent under one aspect and recipient under another, as the fire example shows.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the philosophers' first proof as presented in this passage?
A) That an agent must be necessary and a recipient contingent
B) That reception and action are two effects, so they cannot issue from a single cause
C) That the Necessary Being cannot have attributes
D) That fire produces heat by its form and receives it by its matter

Key Takeaway
Unity does not prevent a single entity from having different aspects; one thing can be both agent and recipient under different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers claimed that God cannot have real attributes because that would make Him both agent and recipient in the same respect. Have you ever unconsciously believed that God's oneness means He is distant and uninvolved? How does the truth that He can be both agent and recipient under different aspects change how you relate to Him today? Will you now approach Him as both the one who gives and the one who receives your worship, without feeling that compromises His unity?
?
Think of an area in your life where you feel pulled between two roles—for example, being both a servant who submits and a person with free will. How does the principle of 'different aspects' (جهتين) from this lesson free you from seeing these as contradictions? What is one specific situation this week where you will consciously embrace both roles, knowing that you are one person under different aspects?
?
The fire example shows that one thing can produce heat by its form and receive heat by its matter. In your spiritual journey, where are you only focusing on being an 'agent' (trying to act for God) while neglecting to be a 'recipient' (open to receive from God)? What is one practical step you can take today to become more receptive to God's grace, even as you continue to act faithfully?

Refuting the Agent-Recipient Unity Argument
Taftazani refutes philosophers' first argument against one thing being both agent and recipient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani refutes the philosophers' claim that one thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and how the distinction of aspects resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
A single entity can be both agent and recipient from different aspects.

The philosophers argued that if something is both agent and recipient, it would have contradictory relations of necessity and contingency. Taftazani shows that this argument fails because the two roles apply from different perspectives, much like a person treating themselves or fire heating and being heated by its matter. This passage is a key moment in Islamic theology that defends the possibility of God having real attributes without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim
Read the opening sentence where the philosophers assert that the one cannot be both agent and recipient, and note their two reasons: (1) they are two effects, (2) agent implies necessity, recipient implies contingency.
2
See Taftazani's general answer
Taftazani concedes the contingency distinction but argues that necessity and non-necessity can coexist from different aspects under a qualification of unity.
This is the key move: the two roles are from different aspects.
3
Examine the first argument and refutation
The philosophers argue that two effects cannot proceed from one. Taftazani responds by denying that only one effect can proceed from one, and by showing the logical consequence would be absurd.
Pay attention to the dialectical exchange.
4
Follow the objections and replies
The opponents try to rejoin by distinguishing aspects. Taftazani turns the distinction back on them, and when they object to self-affection, he uses the example of self-treatment.
5
Conclude the futility of unity of aspect
Taftazani ends by stating that the assumption of a single aspect is false; there is never unity of aspect in such cases.
This seals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: زعمت الفلاسفة أن الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلاً لأنهما أثران وقد مر، ولأن نسبة الفاعل بالوجوب والقابل بالإمكان. والجواب بعد تسليم كونها بالإمكان الخاص المنافي للوجوب أو بالإمكان العام الذي يمكن أن يتحقق بدون الوجوب أنه لا امتناع في الوجوب، واللاوجوب بجهتين بحيثية من حيث هو واحد، لا يكون قابلا للشيء، وفاعلا له، وبنوا على ذلك امتناع اتصاف الواجب بصفات حقيقية، واحترز بقيد حيثية الوحدة عن مثل النار تفعل الحرارة بصورتها وتقبلها بمادتها وتمسكوا في ذلك بوجهين: الأول: أن القبول والفعل أثران فلا يصدران عن واحد لما مر. ورد بعد تسليم كون القبول أثراً بأنا لا نسلم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، على أنه لو صح ذلك لزم أن لا يكون الواحد قابلاً لشيء وفاعلاً لآخر. فإن دفع باختلاف الجهة، فإن الفاعلية لذاته وقابليته، باعتبار تأثره عما يوجد المقبول. قلنا: فليكن حال القابلية والفاعلية للشيء الواحد أيضاً كذلك. فإن قيل: الشيء لا يتأثر عن نفسه. قلنا: أول المسألة، ولم لا يجوز باعتبارين كالمعالج لنفسه. فإن قيل: الكلام على تقدير اتحاد الجهة. قلنا: فيكون لغواً إذ لا اتحاد جهة أصلاً.
Fourth investigation: The philosophers claimed that the one cannot be both recipient and agent because they are two effects, as has passed, and because the relation of the agent is by necessity and the recipient by contingency. The answer, after conceding that it is by particular contingency opposed to necessity or by general contingency which can be realized without necessity, is that there is no impossibility in necessity and non-necessity from two aspects by a qualification of unity, so that from the perspective of being one, it is not both recipient of something and agent of it. And they built upon that the impossibility of the Necessary being described with real attributes. And they guarded by the restriction of the qualification of unity against the likes of fire which produces heat by its form and receives it by its matter. And they clung in that to two arguments: The first: that reception and action are two effects, so they cannot proceed from one, as has passed. And it was refuted, after conceding that reception is an effect, by saying: we do not concede that only one proceeds from one; moreover, if that were sound, it would entail that the one cannot be recipient of something and agent of another. If it is repelled by difference of aspect—for the agency is by its essence and its receptivity by consideration of its being affected by what produces the received thing—we say: then let the case of receptivity and agency for one thing also be likewise. If it is said: the thing is not affected by itself, we say: that is the very issue; why is it not permissible from two considerations, like someone treating himself? If it is said: the discourse is on the assumption of unity of aspect, we say: then it is futile, for there is no unity of aspect at all.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' claim that one thing cannot be both agent (faʿil) and recipient (qabil). Taftazani begins by stating the philosophers' claim and their two reasons: (1) agency and receptivity are two distinct effects (atharān), so they cannot come from a single cause, as previously argued; (2) the agent's relation to its effect is one of necessity (wujūb), while the recipient's relation is one of contingency (imkān). He then gives a general answer: even if conceding the contingency is particular (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) which excludes necessity, or general (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) which can coexist with necessity, there is no impossibility in necessity and non-necessity coexisting from two different aspects (bi-jahatayn) qualified by unity (bi-ḥaythiyyat al-waḥda). The philosophers built on this the denial that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) can have real attributes, and they used the example of fire which produces heat by its form and receives it by its matter, but they restricted it by the qualification of unity. Taftazani then explains they have two arguments: the first is that reception and action are two effects, so they cannot proceed from one. The refutation: we do not concede that only one proceeds from one; moreover, if that were true, then the one could not be recipient of one thing and agent of another—but that is false. If the opponent objects by distinguishing aspects (agency by essence, receptivity by being affected), Taftazani replies that the same distinction applies to the one thing. If the opponent says a thing cannot be affected by itself, Taftazani says that is the very point at issue; why not allow two considerations like a person treating himself? If the opponent insists on unity of aspect, Taftazani says that is futile because there is never unity of aspect (the agent and recipient roles are always from different aspects).

Key Terms
الفاعل
القابل
الأثر
الوجوب
الإمكان
الجهة
بحيثية الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument is logically airtight and cannot be refuted.
Correction: Taftazani shows the argument fails because it ignores the possibility of different aspects. The coexistence of necessity and contingency from different aspects is not contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why does the refutation of the first argument succeed?
A) Because the philosophers concede that effects can be multiple.
B) Because the one thing can act and receive from different aspects.
C) Because the philosophers fail to define 'effect' properly.
D) Because the example of fire proves the point.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's key move is to show that agency and receptivity can coexist when considered from different aspects, thus the philosophers' argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes—like His being both All-Knowing and All-Powerful—do you ever feel a tension, as if these attributes might somehow conflict? How does Taftazani's argument about different aspects (جهتين) give you a tool to resolve that tension in your own heart and mind? Will you actively remind yourself of this principle when you encounter doubts about God's unity?
?
This lesson shows that even seemingly airtight logical arguments can fail when they ignore the possibility of different aspects. Is there an area in your own life where you have been too quick to accept a logical conclusion without considering alternative perspectives? How can you cultivate the habit of asking 'What aspect am I not seeing?' before making judgments?

Refuting the Incompatibility of Necessity and Contingency
Taftazani refutes the second argument based on necessity and contingency incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the second argument by demonstrating that necessity and contingency are not incompatible when considered under different aspects (agency vs. receptivity).

Core Idea
Necessity and contingency can coexist in one thing under different aspects.

The philosophers argued that agency and receptivity cannot coexist because agency entails necessity and receptivity entails contingency, which are opposites. Taftazani challenges this by refining the notion of contingency and showing that the same thing can be necessary as an agent and contingent as a recipient. This lesson unpacks the argument, the objections, and the final resolution.

Guided Path
1
1. Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Taftazani sets up the philosophers' claim that agency and receptivity are mutually exclusive due to their concomitants of necessity and contingency.
The argument hinges on the idea that the perfect agent necessitates its effect, while the recipient only allows it.
2
2. Analyze the first objection
The first objection argues that the contingency here is general contingency (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), meaning non-impossibility, which does not contradict necessity. Understand why this objection challenges the incompatibility claim.
General contingency only requires that something is not impossible, not that it is not necessary.
3
3. Examine the second objection
The second objection suggests that contingency means particular contingency (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ), which includes both possibility and non-necessity. Even if we take it as general contingency, its meaning is broader than necessity. Consider how this further undermines the original argument.
The text becomes unclear about 'determination of necessity', but the key point is that contingency does not equal necessity.
4
4. Grasp Taftazani's final answer
After conceding the objections, Taftazani states that it is permissible for a thing to be necessary qua agent and not necessary qua recipient. This dissolves the incompatibility by distinguishing aspects.
The resolution is that there is no conflict because the necessity and contingency apply to different relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن نسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب، ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان. لأن الفاعل التام للشيء من حيث هو فاعل يستلزمه، والقابل له لا يستلزمه بل يمكن حصوله فيه، فيكون قبول الشيء للشيء، وفاعليته له متنافيين لتنافي لازميهما أعني الوجوب والإمكان. واعترض بأنه إنما هي إمكان عام، لأن معنى قابلية الشيء للشيء أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه، وهو لا ينافي الوجوب. وقيل: بل معناه أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه ولا عدم حصوله. وهو معنى الإمكان الخاص، ولو فرضناه الإمكان العام، فليس معناه أحد نوعيه، أعني الوجوب بل معناه مفهومه الأعم بحيث يحتمل الإمكان الخاص فينا في تعين الوجوب الذي لا يحتمله. والجواب بعد تسليم ذلك: أنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء واجباً للشيء من حيث كونه فاعلاً، له غير واجب من حيث كونه قابلاً له.
The second: that the relation of the agent to the effect is by necessity, and the relation of the recipient to the received is by contingency. Because the perfect agent of a thing, insofar as it is an agent, necessitates it, and the recipient of it does not necessitate it but rather its occurrence in it is possible. So the receptivity of a thing for a thing and its agency for it are mutually incompatible due to the incompatibility of their two concomitants, namely necessity and contingency. And it was objected that it is only general contingency, because the meaning of a thing's receptivity for a thing is that its occurrence in it is not impossible, and this does not contradict necessity. And it was said: rather its meaning is that its occurrence in it is not impossible nor its non-occurrence—and that is the meaning of particular contingency. Even if we assume it is general contingency, its meaning is not one of its two types, namely necessity, but rather its more general concept such that it admits particular contingency, then necessity is determined [or: in us determination of necessity] which it does not admit. And the answer, after conceding that: it is permissible that a thing be necessary for a thing insofar as it is an agent of it, and not necessary insofar as it is a recipient of it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the philosophers' second argument. The argument: the agent's relation to its effect is one of necessity (wujūb), because the perfect agent necessarily brings about its effect; the recipient's relation is one of contingency (imkān), because the recipient does not necessitate the effect—it merely allows it. Since necessity and contingency are opposites, agency and receptivity cannot coexist in one thing regarding the same effect. An objection is raised: the contingency here is general contingency (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), which means non-impossibility. Non-impossibility does not contradict necessity, so there is no incompatibility. Another response: the meaning is particular contingency (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ), which includes both possibility and non-necessity. Even if we take it as general contingency, its meaning is not necessity; it only means non-impossibility. Then necessity may become determined as a subtype? (The text is somewhat unclear at this point.) The final answer, after conceding the objector's points: it is permissible for a thing to be necessary for something as an agent and not necessary as a recipient—i.e., the aspect of agency necessitates, while the aspect of receptivity does not. Thus, there is no real incompatibility; agency and receptivity are from different aspects.

Key Terms
الفاعل
القابل
الوجوب
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessity and contingency are absolutely incompatible in one thing.
Correction: Taftazani shows they can coexist under different aspects (e.g., agency vs. receptivity).

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani resolve the apparent incompatibility between necessity and contingency in one thing?
A) By denying that necessity applies to the agent.
B) By distinguishing the aspects of agency and receptivity.
C) By redefining contingency as impossibility.
D) By accepting that the thing cannot be both.

Key Takeaway
Necessity and contingency are not contradictory because they pertain to different aspects: agency necessitates, receptivity does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something in your life must be either absolutely necessary or completely impossible, ignoring the possibility that it depends on different aspects? Where might you be missing this nuance in your relationship with Allah—for example, in how you view your own actions versus Allah's decree? Take time today to identify one such assumption and reflect on how the lesson's insight could change your understanding.

When you make plans, do you treat your own agency as if it necessitates outcomes, forgetting that you are also a recipient of Allah's will? How does this lesson help you balance reliance on Allah with personal responsibility? Commit to reciting a supplication that acknowledges both your effort and Allah's ultimate control.
?
The passage shows that necessity and contingency can coexist under different aspects. How often do you rush to label things as contradictory in your faith or life? What is one theological or personal conflict you have avoided reconciling? This week, write down one contradiction you can resolve by distinguishing aspects.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Different Aspects Allow Agency and Receptivity in One Thing
First argument refuting the principle through difference of aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the principle 'from one only one proceeds' does not prevent one thing from being both agent and recipient under different aspects.

Core Idea
Different aspects allow one thing to be both agent and recipient.

The philosophers claim that from one only one can proceed, which would prevent a single entity from being both cause and recipient. The author challenges this by showing that the same entity can function in both roles under different considerations, just as a physician treats himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the dialectical style: the author first concedes a point then denies the principle.
2
Understand the initial denial
The author denies the principle itself and argues that if it were true, one thing could not be both agent and recipient, which is false.
Highlight the phrase 'لزم أن لا يكون الواحد قابلاً لشيء وفاعلاً لآخر'.
3
Follow the dialectical exchange
The philosopher defends by appealing to difference of aspect (agency by essence, receptivity by being affected). The author retorts that the same can apply to one thing. Then the philosopher says a thing cannot affect itself; the author rebuts with the example of self-treatment.
Notice the author's use of analogy.
4
Grasp the resolution
When the philosopher insists on unity of aspect, the author declares the discussion futile because there is no unity of aspect at all. The key point: one thing can be both agent and recipient under different considerations.
This refutes the principle's implication.

ورد بعد تسليم كون القبول أثراً بأنا لا نسلم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، على أنه لو صح ذلك لزم أن لا يكون الواحد قابلاً لشيء وفاعلاً لآخر. فإن دفع باختلاف الجهة، فإن الفاعلية لذاته وقابليته باعتبار تأثره عما يوجد المقبول. قلنا : فليكن حال القابلية والفاعلية للشيء الواحد أيضاً كذلك. فإن قيل : الشيء لا يتأثر عن نفسه. قلنا : أول المسألة، ولم لا يجوز باعتبارين كالمعالج لنفسه. فإن قيل : الكلام على تقدير اتحاد الجهة. قلنا : فيكون لغواً إذ لا اتحاد جهة أصلاً. الثاني : أن نسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب، ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان. لأن الفاعل التام للشيء من حيث هو فاعل يستلزمه، والقابل له لا يستلزمه بل يمكن حصوله فيه، فيكون قبول الشيء للشيء، وفاعليته له متنافيين لتنافي لازميهما أعني الوجوب والإمكان. واعترض بأنه إنما هي إمكان عام، لأن معنى قابلية الشيء للشيء أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه، وهو لا ينافي الوجوب. وقيل : بل معناه أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه ولا عدم حصوله. وهو معنى الإمكان الخاص، ولو فرضناه الإمكان العام، فليس معناه أحد نوعيه، أعني الوجوب بل معناه مفهومه الأعم بحيث يحتمل الإمكان الخاص فينا في تعين الوجوب الذي لا يحتمله. والجواب بعد تسليم ذلك : أنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء واجباً للشيء من حيث كونه فاعلاً له غير واجب من حيث كونه قابلاً له.
After conceding that receptivity is an effect, [we say:] we do not accept that from one only one proceeds. Moreover, if that were true, it would necessitate that one thing cannot be receptive to something and agent for another. If it is rebutted by [appeal to] difference of aspect, that agency is due to its essence and receptivity is due to being affected by that from which the received thing comes, we say: then let the case of receptivity and agency for one thing also be likewise. If it is said: a thing is not affected by itself. We say: that is the very matter at issue; why is it not permissible under two considerations, like one treating oneself? If it is said: the discussion is on the assumption of unity of aspect. We say: then it is futile, since there is no unity of aspect at all. Second: that the relation of the agent to the act is by necessity, and the relation of the recipient to the received is by possibility. For the complete agent of a thing, insofar as it is agent, necessitates it, while the recipient of it does not necessitate it but rather its occurrence in it is possible. Thus, a thing's being a recipient of something and its being an agent for it are contradictory due to the contradiction of their concomitants, namely necessity and possibility. And it was objected that it is only general possibility, because the meaning of a thing's being receptive of something is that its occurrence in it is not impossible, and that does not contradict necessity. And it was said: rather its meaning is that its occurrence in it is not impossible and its non-occurrence is not impossible. That is the meaning of specific possibility. Even if we assume it is general possibility, its meaning is not one of its two types, namely necessity, but rather its meaning is the broader concept that admits specific possibility, unlike definite necessity that does not admit it. The answer, after conceding that, is that it is permissible for a thing to be necessary for something insofar as it is an agent for it, but not necessary insofar as it is a recipient for it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the philosophers' principle 'from one only one proceeds.' The author first concedes that receptivity is an effect but denies the principle itself, arguing that if it held, one thing could not be both agent and recipient of different things. The philosopher may defend by saying agency is by essence and receptivity by being affected from another; the author retorts that the same can be said for one thing being both agent and recipient under different aspects. When the philosopher objects that a thing cannot be affected by itself, the author says 'that's the issue' and gives an example of self-treatment. When the philosopher demands unity of aspect, the author calls it futile since aspects are different. Then the author provides a second argument: agency implies necessity (wujub) of the effect from the agent, while receptivity implies possibility (imkan) of the received in the recipient—these two relations are incompatible. The objector counters that receptivity only implies general possibility (imkan 'amm), which does not contradict necessity. Another says it is specific possibility (imkan khas). The author answers that even if general possibility, it is a broader concept that does not exclude specific possibility, but necessity does. The final resolution: it is permissible for one thing to be necessary in terms of being an agent for something but not necessary as a recipient—so the contradiction is resolved.

القبول
الفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle 'from one only one proceeds' is self-evident and cannot be challenged.
Correction: The principle is not self-evident; it can be refuted by showing that one thing can act and receive under different aspects.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author say the discussion is futile when the philosopher demands unity of aspect?
A) Because the principle is true only for abstract entities.
B) Because there is no unity of aspect at all, so the assumption is false.
C) Because the author agrees that unity of aspect is necessary.
D) Because the philosopher's objection is not logical.

Key Takeaway
One thing can be both agent and recipient under different aspects, refuting the claim that from one only one can proceed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been assuming that one cause can only produce one effect, thereby limiting how you see Allah's power working through multiple means? Will you today begin to look for the different aspects through which Allah acts in a single situation?
?
The passage uses the example of a person treating themselves. Where in your own spiritual life have you neglected that you are both the one who acts and the one who is affected? How can you intentionally 'treat yourself' through repentance or worship, acknowledging both your agency and your need to be changed?
?
If there is no unity of aspect at all—every perception involves multiple considerations—how does that challenge your tendency to simplify Allah, others, or yourself into a single label? Will you this week ask Allah to show you the different aspects of a person or situation you have judged too quickly?
